Luling Watermelon Thump has drawn as many as
40,000 visitors and agriculture remains important to the town. After the Luling oil field, Davis proceeded
to discover the nearby Buckeye and Darst Oil Fields. Despite his success and philanthropy, Davisâ
company eventually folded and Davis died in 1951, having nearly paid back all the debts he had incurred.
His grave is at his homesite, now the grounds of the local hospital named for him.
The Darst Oil Field (Figure 11) lies generally to the south and east of LCRAâs T-538 transmission line
but crosses it in some places. In the 1930s, this oil field was bustling with derricks and workers. The
workers lived on the oil fields in camps with their families. The families lived in houses with large fenced
yards and the children played among the derricks and slush pits (Limmer 2012b). The families took pride
in their community and kept the land groomed, even whitewashing the trees, and some kept livestock on
the land. Kids frequented a swimming hole on Nash Creek and families went into Kingsbury, Luling and
35

Seguin to socialize (Limmer 2012a). Increased mechanization eventually ended the need for oil camps
and workers moved into the nearby towns.
Just east of the T-538 line on Dowdy road are the remains of the Dowdy school, which served the
children of the oil camps (Figure 12). Originally opened in 1916, oil royalties allowed the school to
expand to include a school house, a residence for teachers, a library, and a gymnasium. By 1951 the oil
camps were gone and the Dowdy School closed as the area was consolidated into the Seguin school
district. The foundations and stairs are still visible.
The area today remains heavily dependent on agriculture and oil. Outside the nearby towns, the
population remains rural and relatively dispersed. A significant portion of the population lives in the area
but works in nearby cities such as San Antonio, Austin, and San Marcos.

Figure 12: A first grade class at Dowdy School circa 1933. Seguin Gazette.

METHODOLOGY
The methodology for this project consisted of a file search, followed by field survey. The file search
involved research on the THC archaeological Site Atlas, in LCRA internal archives, and of topographical
maps of the area, from both the United States Geological Survey and the Army Corps of Engineers,
dating from 1911 to 1964. The purpose of the file search was to find areas of high probability for
archaeological sites, both historic and prehistoric. Historic high probability areas were determined based
on structure locations plotted on topographic maps near the T-538 transmission line. Prehistoric high
probability areas were determined by various factors, including: presence of Holocene deposits,

36

landforms, proximity to water sources, etc. The result of this process was 32 historic high probability
areas and 7.39 miles of prehistoric high probability areas to be surveyed in the field.
LCRA Cultural Resources staff followed CTA field survey standards, which require 16 shovel tests per
mile of high probability areas for linear projects, except where ground surface visibility is greater than 30
percent. In accordance with CTA standards, LCRA Cultural Resources staff excavated shovel tests, 30-35
cm in diameter, down to either 100 cm bs or to the end of Holocene deposits, in 20 cm levels. Shovel tests
were generally concentrated in areas where the ground will be disturbed by construction, eg structure
locations, except where local conditions obviated the need for such tests.
Additionally, in areas with alluvium and high potential for buried deposits, LCRA Cultural Resources
staff used a skid-steer mounted auger to excavate subsurface tests 40 cm in diameter, in 20 cm levels and
to a depth of 120 cm to 140 cm bs. Auger tests were placed near proposed new structure locations and at
100 m intervals, except where buried utilities or petroleum pipelines made it dangerous to do so. All
deposits from both shovel and auger tests were screened in the field using Â¼-inch mesh screens. No
materials were collected during this survey.

RESULTS
The field survey was completed from January through March 2016. During the survey, ground surface
visibility was found to be variable and ranged from very good to poor. In many areas having deep sandy
soils, numerous pocket gopher burrow backdirt piles were present (see Figure 13). These backdirt piles
were always examined by the field crew as an additional method of evaluating subsurface deposits in
addition to shovel testing. Overall, a total of 97 shovel and auger tests were excavated. Although this
number is less than the 118 subsurface tests that would be expected for a project having 7.39 miles of
high probability areas, the field crew was aided in evaluating some areas by cutbank exposures and the
fact that in some areas bare patches of ground surface showed that topsoils had completely eroded away
leaving basal clay visible on the surface (Figure 13). The soil descriptions and artifact recovery from the
subsurface tests are presented in Appendix 1.

37

Figure 13. View of typical terrace with good ground surface visibility.

The 2016 survey of LCRAâs T-538 transmission line upgrade project produced seven new recorded sites,
of which two are historic, four are prehistoric, and one is multi-component. Six sites are in Guadalupe
County and one is in Caldwell County... The four prehistoric sites are all sparse lithic scatters near creek
channels. The two historic sites are both early to mid-twentieth century African-American occupations,
one of which is a farmstead in Guadalupe County, and the other is a possible farmstead inside the Luling
city limits in Caldwell County. The multi-component site consists of a sparse prehistoric lithic scatter and
a sparse historic artifact scatter. Additionally, one previously recorded historic period site, 41CW28 was
revisited during the survey. The sites are described in detail in the following paragraphs.
Site Descriptions
41GU183
Site 41GU183 is a multi-component site consisting of a prehistoric campsite and a historic debris scatter
(Figure 14). The site measures120 m north-south by 55 m east-west. The site is located on the lower part
of a terrace slope that overlooks the Darst Creek floodplain. The terrace has been previously disturbed by
land clearing for the construction of two separate electrical transmission line corridors and by oil drilling
38

activities. The site is less than 200 m east of the southernmost extent of Darst Oil Field. The soil is Darst
very gravelly sandy loam at the north end of the site and Demona loamy fine sand in the south (Ramsey
and Bade 1977). The sandy soils contain much gravel that is predominantly chert. The soil is also heavily
bioturbated by pocket gopher burrows.

Figure 14. Site 41GU183 sketch map.

In 2013, this area was partially investigated by LCRA Cultural Resources staff for the Deer Creek
substation project. The survey of this area ceased when the substation location was moved approximately
500 m south to abut ALT-90. Four shovel tests from this project are within 30 m of the T-538 ROW
corridor with ST#2 and 4 laying just outside the ROW. ST#3 and 4 on the west side of the ROW were
negative. ST# 1 and 2 on the east side of the ROW were positive, as 1 primary and several secondary and
tertiary flakes and chips were observed between 0 and 20 cm bs.
The 2016 project began with a surface survey in the T-538 ROW corridor. North of the 2013 shovel tests,
the very shallow soil of the ROW was heavily disturbed and only one chert flake was observed. Negative
ST#7 was dug to confirm the north boundary of the site. To the south of the 2013 shovel tests (Figure 15),
39

LCRA Cultural Resources staff observed a number of cultural materials, including: about 12 pieces of
chert debitage, some burned rocks (mostly burned chert), 3 stoneware glazed ceramic sherds, 1 piece of
porcelain dish, 4 pieces of glass, and some rusted metal that probably is old oil drilling equipment. Most
of the observed material rested on the ubiquitous pocket gopher mounds in the survey area. LCRA
Cultural Resources staff excavated a shovel test (ST#6) in the least bioturbated location available near a
surficial tertiary flake. This shovel test was excavated down to 75 cm bs in the sandy loam before
terminating at clay but was completely sterile. No shovel tests were placed farther south, though burned
chert and one modern ceramic fragment were observed as far as 24 m from shovel test #6. The site
boundary was drawn to encompass the burned chert scatter in the south and the chert flake to the north.
Within the LCRA electrical transmission line corridor, both the prehistoric and historic components of
site 41GU183 are diffuse and lacking in features. Much of the burned rock could be related to the burning
of vegetation when the transmission line corridor was originally cleared for construction. More
concentrated prehistoric remains may be expected east of the LCRA transmission line corridor on a slight
projection of a terrace into the Darst Creek floodplain. Additionally, a former historic farmstead may be
present somewhere nearby outside the transmission line corridor in areas not explored during the current
project. Site 41GU183 is assessed as being of low research value and no further investigations are
recommended. LCRA Cultural Resources staff recommends this site to be ineligible for formal
designation as an SAL within the ROW. No special considerations or avoidance measures are
recommended at this time.

Site 41GU184
Site 41GU184 is a small lithic scatter, 20 m north-south x 10 m west-east, situated on a sandy upland
terrace top within the cleared T-538 corridor (Figure 16 and Figure 17). Darst Creek and a tributary
thereof are, respectively, located about 165 meters to the east and 200 m to the south. The soil is Demona
loamy fine sand and, at the hill crest, Crockett fine sandy loam (Ramsey and Bade 1977). Numerous
pocket gopher backdirt piles visible on the ground surface provide a sample of the subsurface rock
materials.
Surface survey revealed one thick core biface on a gopher backdirt pile at the eastern ROW edge. A
shovel test (ST#9) was placed in the immediate vicinity and yielded one tertiary chert chip in the 20 to 40
cm level and one tertiary chert chip in the 60-80 cm level. Shovel tests placed north and south were
negative. A shovel test (ST#57) placed 25 m west, on the opposite edge of the ROW, was positive,
yielding one chert primary flake in the 60-80 cm level. Otherwise, some burned pieces of chert are
present on the surface but it is difficult to determine if these are burned rock fragments from the Native
American occupation or if they are just the result of brush burning activities associated with the
transmission line corridor vegetation clearing activities. The site boundary was drawn to include the
positive shovel tests and some burned chert upslope.

40

Figure 15. Looking south at site 41GU183.

Figure 16. View of site 41GU184, note numerous pocket gopher burrow backdirt piles on ground surface.

41

The cultural material at site 41GU184 is very sparse but there is potentially more material outside the
ROW to the northwest at the highest point of this low interfluvial ridge or, perhaps, to the southeast closer
to the creek. An old transmission line structure is inside the site boundaries and scheduled for removal but
should have no negative impact on the site. No new structure is being placed within the site boundaries.
The portion of site 41GU184 within the ROW corridor is of low research value and no further
investigations are recommended. The LCRA Cultural Resources team recommends that the part of this
site within the ROW is ineligible for formal SAL designation. No special considerations or avoidance
measures are recommended at this time.

Figure 17. Site 41GU184 sketch map.

42

Site 41GU185
Site 41GU185 is a small buried lithic scatter which runs 50 m southwest to northeast within a 20 m wide
ROW corridor (Figure 18). The site sits just below the crest of a gently sloping interfluvial hill situated
between Darst Creek and a small intermittent tributary thereof, 160 m to the east and 350 m to the south,
respectively. The Darst Oil Field is only 1 km to the east. The soil at the site location is Crockett gravelly
sandy loam (Ramsey and Bade 1977) with considerable gravel at the A-B horizon boundary.

Figure 18. Site 41GU185 sketch map.

Site 41GU185 consists of a small artifact assemblage observed in one positive shovel test (ST#16) and
includes: one unifacial expedient tool, one primary and one secondary flake, one burned chert potlid, and
one possible multi-directional core fragment. ST#16âs soil profile consisted of 20 cm of sandy loam over
sandy clay mixed with dense gravel/cobbles (40 percent +). A shovel test was placed 30 m southwest in

43

the ROW but was negative and struck the B horizon clay at 10 cm bs. A final shovel test was placed 75 m
northeast but was negative and just as shallow as the previous test. This test pit was placed so far away
from the original because of a gravel drive and a petroleum pipeline ROW in the intervening space. No
tests were placed to the east and west because of the narrow ROW and a fence to the west along the drive.
Additionally, the site sits between two houses just off the gravel drive and the ground was badly disturbed
all around. No surface material was observed due to the poor surface visibility.
Site 41GU185 is bounded on three sides by intermittent streams but sits high enough upslope to avoid
flooding, which fits well with established settlement patterns of hunter-gatherers in Central Texas (Collins
2004). It is speculated that the site may extend to the northwest to the crest of the hill, which seems to be
the most likely spot for occupation and looked to be relatively undisturbed. Site 41GU185 has very low
research value within the ROW, limited to locational data only, and no further investigation is
recommended. LCRA Cultural Resources staff recommends the site to be ineligible for SAL status within
the ROW corridor. No special considerations or avoidance measures are recommended at this time.

Site 41GU186
Site 41GU186 is a sparse, mostly surficial lithic scatter of chert flakes and burned chert and sandstone,
which is 30 m wide and runs 80 m southwest-northeast centered on the ROW corridor (Figure 19). The
site sits in an open cow pasture on a gently sloping, low interfluvial ridge between Smith Creek 200 m
east and a small tributary thereof 100-200 m to the north and northwest. The site is situated about 400 m
downslope and to the northeast of the highest point of the lobe. The site is located roughly 1 km north of
the Darst Oil Field. The soil is Windthorst fine sandy loam over sandy clay (Ramsey and Bade 1977). The
soil is heavily eroded in the site location and vegetation is sparse, leaving large patches of bare earth.
Pocket gopher mounds are frequent.
At site 41GU186, surface survey remained within the 20 m wide ROW, though there were several
surficial artifacts easily visible from the ROW corridor, resting on bare patches of soil up to 20 m outside
the ROW to the south, concentrated near the eastern site boundary. Shovel tests showed the surface
sandy loam to be generally around 20 cm deep before bedrock equivalent clay was encountered. The first
shovel test (ST#30) was placed about 10 m outside of the artifact scatter to the northeast and was
negative. ST#31 was placed 20 m upslope, inside the scatter area and was likewise negative. ST#32 was
placed 60 m upslope and to the west where soil looked to be deeper and was positive. Artifactual material
observed included a tertiary flake and a primary chip as well as some burned chert. No further tests were
placed to the west due to visibly shallow soil and disturbances. ST#33 was then placed half way between
the previous two tests but was negative. Surface survey and shovel tests were then conducted in the ROW
farther afield near the creeks. To the southwest, the soil was visibly shallow and clay was visible in many
places at the surface. One negative shovel test was placed in the only area of testable soil depth. To the
northeast, the soil was also visibly eroded down to clay. Based on survey and testing the site boundary
was drawn to include the surface scatter and the positive shovel test within the ROW only.

44

Figure 19. Site 41GU186 sketch map.

Site 41GU186 may extend to the southwest along the ridge to the crest but this area was outside the scope
of this project. An old transmission line structure is inside the site boundaries and scheduled for removal
but should have no negative impact on the site. One new structure is being placed within the site
boundaries but should have a negligible impact on the site. The portion of site 41GU186 located within
the ROW is of low research value, providing only locational data, and no further investigations are
recommended. LCRA Cultural Resources staff recommends this site to be SAL ineligible within the
ROW. No special considerations or avoidance measures are recommended at this time.

Site 41GU187
Site 41GU187 is an early to mid-twentieth century farmstead, through the center of which runs the T-538
ROW corridor (Figure 20). The site is 130 m west to east within the approximately 20 m wide ROW. The
farmstead sits at the crest of a gently sloping interfluvial hill between two tributaries of Smith Creek
located 300 m to the northeast and the southwest. The site is located roughly 1 km north of the Darst Oil
Field. The soil is Windthorst fine sandy loam over sandy clay (Ramsey and Bade 1977). Cleared pasture
and crop lands lie to the east and south.

45

Site 41GU187 is a small farmstead with the portion of the site within the transmission line ROW
consisting of a drive, a portion of a porch foundation, a small trash scatter, and a part of a corral. Outside
the ROW, approximately 10 outbuildings associated with the home are visible. The drive enters the site
from the southwest directly in front of a house and porch foundation. The house was of pier and beam
construction and is no longer present, but the outline can be discerned from the remaining concrete steps
and the concrete porch, which faces southeast toward the drive, then wraps around to face northeast. A
partially buried open concrete cistern is about 16 ft west of the rear of the house. Between the drive and
the porch is a scatter of historic trash, including a cast iron enameled bathtub made by Standard Sanitary
Mfg Co in Louisville (Kentucky), possibly in 1932, as indicated by faint manufacturerâs marks on the
underside. At the eastern end of the site is the south fence of a relatively large corral, including a cattle
neck and body clamp device.

Figure 20. Site 41GU187 sketch map.

Outside of the ROW, to a distance of up to 120 feet, numerous structures are visible both to the north and
the south. The following describes what was visible from the ROW corridor and distances were measured
using aerial imagery. The majority of the outbuildings lie to the north and northeast. The buildings are
typically wood plank and corrugated metal and are generally in ruin and collapsing. A multi-use shed and
chicken coop sits 65 ft directly north of the house. Sixty-five ft northeast of the shed is a small structure

46

which may have been a pump house. Sixty-five ft east-northeast of the possible pump house is a larger
building that appears to be a small animal shed of some kind. The structure 85 ft south of the animal shed
is likely a garage, as indicated by a ramped concrete slab floor. Just 30 ft northeast across a truck path
from the garage sits a relatively large pole barn, 26 x 40 ft, open to the southwest and enclosed on the
other three sides. Some of the wood used is salvaged, as one of the wood planks used in the roof is from
an airplane hearse service advertisement. Attached to the pole barn to the southeast is a small fenced
animal pen which connects to a larger corral to the east. One hundred and five ft southeast of the larger
corral, across the ROW and truck path, is another small outbuilding of indeterminate use. Approximately
65 ft southwest of this building is a completely collapsed structure. This last structure is about 100 ft
south of the large corral. The LCRA electrical transmission line follows the truck path running through
the site from west to east. A hand-dug brick lined well, at least 20 feet deep and retaining water and likely
associated with the farmstead, sits 750 ft south of the house and just east of the drive, which is the access
road.
Observed materials are consistent with an early to mid-twentieth century occupation age. Some cursory
internet research yielded information about some observed time-diagnostic objects, consisting of: a
Firestone brand refrigerator, which was only produced by that company in the 1940s and very early
1950s; an electric washing machine with a wringer/mangle attachment, which style dates generally to the
1930s and 1940s; and a Parmak brand electric fence control box which dates to the 1940s; as well as the
aforementioned cast iron tub.
Shovel testing found no subsurface cultural material. The first shovel test was sandy loam over a layer of
gravel fill from an outside source over clay. The gravel seems obviously to be a drive constructed over a
surface graded down to clay. Both a second and third shovel test were placed to the northeast, at the
boundary between the farmstead and a pasture, and both were negative. Shovel testing was limited due to
disturbance by the drive over the majority of the ROW corridor. Aerial imagery available on GIS and onsite observations were used to map the extent of the structures on the farmstead.
A records search indicated that the property was part of the Josias Randolph survey, granted by the
Republic of Texas in 1838 (Texas General Land Office [TXGLO] 2015). No other information about the
property during the nineteenth century was available. According to LCRA archives, the transmission line
ROW easement was sold originally to the Central Power and Light Company on August 8th, 1929, by C.
H. and Shellie Adams, an African-American couple. Records of the U.S. Census surveys
(www.archives.com 2015) conducted between 1900 and 1940 show a Mr. Charles H. Adams (b. 1888)
residing in Guadalupe County. In 1900 and 1910, Charles Adams is shown as living in the household of
his mother and father, William and Jennie Adams, who owned a farm free of mortgage in Guadalupe
County.
William Adams was a farmer, born in Missouri around 1844, and was married to Jennie Adams, born
around 1851 in Virginia. The couple appears in records as early as the 1870 Census, in which he is listed
as a farmer and Jennie kept house in Guadalupe County. The family appears to have been in Texas since
at least 1860, as the oldest child, 10 year old William, is listed as having been born in Texas. Based on
this information, it is highly likely that this was a family of former slaves, living in Texas, who were

47

emancipated when the Civil War came to an end. By the 1900 Census, William is listed as owning a farm
free of mortgage, upon which he and his family lived, including 12 year old Charles.
By the 1920 Census, Charles H. Adams is shown as married to Shellie Adams (b. 1893), and owning a
farm free of mortgage in Guadalupe County. Considering that his father, William Adams, was listed as
age 67 in 1910, and is not listed in the 1920 Census records, it very possible Charles inherited his fatherâs
farm. By the 1940 U.S. Census, the last year for which records are currently available, Charles and Shellie
Adams are still listed as owning a farm free of mortgage in Guadalupe County, upon which they lived
with their seven children, including 16 year old Shellie Adams.
Census records beyond 1940 are unavailable, and the Guadalupe County Appraisal District website only
lists the current property owner. In a brief phone conversation with LCRA Cultural Resources staff, the
landowner, a Mr. Mergele, stated he had purchased the property between 2007 and 2009 from an elderly
Mrs. Shirley Harris, whom he knew was the married daughter of a Charles Adams. While no Shirley
Adams is listed among Charles and Shellieâs children in the 1940 Census, a sixteen year old Shellie is,
and it is likely she to whom Mr. Mergele refers. Mr. Mergele also stated that his grandfather had known
Charles Henry Adams, and that the property in question was inherited by Mrs. Harris from her father. He
also relayed that the house and outbuildings on the property were related to Charles Adams and Shirley
Harrisâ occupations of the property. No further records were located referring to Mrs. Harris.
Based on the various chains of evidence, it appears site 41GU187 was an African-American farmstead,
possibly dating as far back as 1900, under the ownership of William Adams, and was retained within the
family for over 100 years. As discussed above in the Historical Background, many African-American
families owned farmsteads on Nash, Darst, and Smith Creeks in the area and the Adams family farmstead
seems to be one of these.
The LCRA Cultural Resources team assesses the portion of site 41GU187 situated within the ROW to be
of low research value and no further investigations are recommended. The LCRA Cultural Resources
team recommends the part of site 41GU187 within the ROW is not to be eligible for SAL status. The
removal of the current transmission line structure will have no impact on the site and the proposed new
structure location is well outside the site boundary. The LCRA Cultural Resources team recommends no
special considerations or avoidance measures at this time.

Site 41GU188
Site 41GU188 is a sparse lithic scatter within the T-538 ROW corridor on the alluvial terrace of the north
(left) bank of Nash Creek. At this location Nash Creek is deeply incised, though a crossing has been
created for the truck path along the transmission line (Figure 21). A relatively steep hill overlooks the
creek about 350 m south of the site. A low secondary terrace rises about 50 m north of the creek bank and
the ground continues to rise gently to the north of the site. There are many small tributaries of Nash Creek
in this area. Darst Oil Field lies about 3 km to the east. The soil at the site is Uhland loamy alluvium,
which is of Holocene age, is derived from mixed sources and occurs in level flood plains of central Texas
(Ramsey and Bade 1977). At the northern site boundary, where the second terrace begins, the soil
48

changes to the Crockett series, fine sandy loam (Ramsey and Bade 1977). The soil at this location on the
northern site edge is considerably eroded by animals and vehicle traffic. The vegetation at the site consists
of short and tall grass, prickly pear, and dewberry within the narrow ROW and post oak and yaupon holly
at the wooded edges of the ROW and surface visibility ranged from 5 percent to 100 percent.

Figure 21. Site 41GU188 sketch map.

This site is a narrow lithic scatter approximately 70 m north-south within the narrow 10 m wide
transmission line ROW corridor. Only one surficial artifact, a chert tertiary flake, was observed and all
other observed material came from three positive shovel tests. The first shovel test, ST#62 was excavated
49

at the location of the proposed new structure. All material recovered from ST#62 was from the first level
(0-20 cm bs) and included one good tertiary flake and several possible tertiary and secondary chert chips
and chunks, as well as eleven small pieces of burned chert. The next shovel test was excavated 50 m to
the north, well into the secondary terrace, near where a vehicle and animal path intersected the ROW
corridor. This test was negative as was the next shovel test, which was excavated at the midpoint between
the previous two tests. This test was at the edge of the secondary terrace just a few meters from the only
surficial artifact observed. Two more positive shovel tests were excavated; one, ST#65 (Figure 22),
located 20 m to the south of ST#62 and one more a further 10 m south at the edge of the primary terrace
at the creek bank. ST#65 had cultural material, mostly tertiary flakes and chips, observed down to nearly
45 cm bs. ST#66 had cultural material observed down to 20 cm bs only; mostly secondary and tertiary
flakes and chips.

Figure 22. View of site 41GU188 with archaeologist working at ST#65.

Based on shovel testing and surface survey, the site boundaries were drawn within the ROW corridor
from the creek-side terrace edge to the surficial artifact observed at the edge of the secondary terrace, 70
m to the north. The majority of the prehistoric artifacts were found at the south end on the site in ST#65
and 66. It is likely the site extends east and west outside the ROW corridor along the alluvial terrace but
these areas were not able to be investigated during this project. No time diagnostic artifacts were
observed. Additionally, there are many small chert cobbles in this area but the vehicular traffic along the
ROW truck path has crushed many of them, making it difficult sometimes to distinguish damaged cultural
material from non-cultural chert material.
The availability of chert and the numerous low hills and creeks in this locality would have made it a good
area for lithic procurement and occasional occupation by prehistoric people. Site 41GU188 provides
locational data but the portion of the site in the ROW is otherwise of low research value. LCRA Cultural
50

Resources staff recommends no further investigations at this site for the current project. LCRA Cultural
Resources staff also recommends that the portion of site 41GU188 within the T-538 ROW corridor is not
be eligible for SAL designation. One old transmission line structure will be removed from the north site
boundary and should have no negative impact on site 41GU188. One new structure will be placed near
the site center and should have a negligible impact on site 41GU188. LCRA Cultural Resources staff
recommends no special considerations or avoidance measures at this time.
Site 41CW170
Site 41CW170 is a historic occupation, possibly a farmstead, situated on the north edge of the town of
Luling, Texas (Figure 23). The site appears to date to the early to mid-twentieth century and is 128 ft long
within the approximately 60 ft wide LCRA transmission line ROW corridor. The site sits about half way
up a gently sloping hill about 100 m west of a small drainage to Salt Branch, which itself lies 600 m to the
south. The site is located less than 1 km west of the Salt Flat Oil Field. The soil is Crockett gravelly
sandy loam (Lowther and Werchan 1978). Due to brush clearing and winter defoliation of the vegetation,
visibility at this location was generally quite good.

Figure 23. Site 41CW170 sketch map.

Site 41CW170 is bisected through its center by LCRAâs T-538 transmission line corridor, which is
approximately 60 feet wide. The boundaries of this site are confined to the ROW corridor, which is the
only area able to be investigated by the LCRA in this project, but the site obviously extends north and

51

south at least another 40 ft or so in either direction. Investigations consisted of surface survey, mapping,
and limited shovel testing.
The site is located on North Hackberry Avenue, which is the old Luling to Lockhart Highway. LCRA
records from 1928 show a house measuring 24 ft x 40 ft in plan view located 118 ft south of the
transmission line. A dilapidated structure was observed at that approximate location but not investigated,
as it was outside the ROW. Three structures remain standing within the ROW, all of which are at the
eastern site edge (Figure 24). Beginning at the south and ending at the north site boundaries, the buildings
are described below. There is a 10 ft x 20 ft wooden shed, likely a small shop, as there is a workbench and
some broken TVs (ca. 1970s) inside. Outside the east wall is a scatter of bricks, with each brick
measuring approximately 8 in x 4 in x 2 in, and labeled âACME BRICKâ within the frog. Ten ft north of
the shed is an approximately 4 ft square outhouse with a concrete foundation and bowl and the remnants
of a wooden seat and tar shingles used as siding. Seventeen ft north of the outhouse is a storage building
with a smaller south room, about 13 ft square, connected by a covered breezeway to a larger north room,
about 24 ft x13 ft. Both rooms contained modern rubbish, though the larger room also contained two or
three rows of auditorium-style seating. To the west, near Hackberry Avenue, is a concrete walkway
running north-south, which connects to two steps leading up to a concrete foundation slab raised about 7
inches above the ground surface. To the east of the steps are the remnants of a wall painted bright green
and white. Just north of this foundation slab, at the north site boundary, was a small scatter of red
unfrogged bricks marked âKOOKENâ. The walkway leads to other concrete foundation slabs outside the
ROW to both the north and the south.

Figure 24. View of structures witin the T-538 corridor at site 41CW170.

Outside the ROW corridor and site boundaries, the following features are visible due to the cleared and
defoliated vegetation. These features are included in this report only because of their close association
52

with site 41CW170. A raised slab to the north of the ROW has water and toilet plumbing connections and
a possible Men's Room sign was observed. Where the walkway turns to front the northernmost slabs,
there are two 6 ft tall tubular, round metal poles flanking the walkway. To the south of the site is another
raised concrete slab foundation, partially painted red, 6 feet east of which is a brick cistern. Another
structure outline was visible to the south as a change in vegetation color. Southeast of the site is a trash
scatter with a wringer/mangle style washing machine visible, a style popular from the late 1930s to 1950s.
Southeast of the trash scatter sits the aforementioned structure noted in the 1928 LCRA documents and an
associated smaller structure resembling a chicken coop.
Once the surface was surveyed and the site mapped, LCRA Cultural Resources conducted limited shovel
testing. A shovel test placed in the location of the proposed new transmission line structure was negative.
Another shovel test was placed at the center of the observed features. This shovel test consisted of 6 cm (2
in) of sandy silty loam over 16 cm (6 in) of outside gravel fill over intact clay, where the shovel test was
terminated. A single ring pull tab was observed in the gravel fill layer. Shovel probes indicated the gravel
layer extends across the rest of the site in a rough square centered around the main concentration of
foundation slabs. No other shovel tests were excavated due to ground disturbance.
Site 41CW170 is in a historically black neighborhood of Luling and is on the northwest border of a park
and athletic facilities built by Edgar B. Davis in the 1920s for the use of Luling's African-American
community. Archival research indicates the land is on the Adolphin Floyd survey, which was patented to
Floyd's family in 1841(TXGLO 2015). Floyd himself died defending the Alamo in 1836. No other
records were available regarding the property until the early twentieth century. LCRA records show the
transmission line easement was bought for $10 in 1929 from Ira and Anna Askey, a farmer and a teacher
respectively. The land was granted to the Askeys in 1924 by Anna's father, William Sloan, who, at
various times, was a farmer and a salesman of toiletry articles, according to U.S. Census Records. The
1940 Census records indicate the widowed Anna Askey (age 52) was still living at the location and
renting a room to a young couple. The 1940 Census Enumeration District map of Luling shows the land
as the Sloan tract. 1940 is the last year for which Census records are currently available. According to
Caldwell County Appraisal District records, the current land owner bought the property in 2008 and the
previous owner was Sam Ketchum, a member of another African-American family long residing in
Luling. The current landowner does not live on the property and could not be contacted.
Records, features, and artifactual material indicate a small farmstead or home of an African-American
family beginning in at least the mid-1920s. The structure observed outside the ROW and noted in the
1928 LCRA document likely dates to this occupation. The Kooken bricks may date to the 1920s
occupation as well. The Kooken Pressed Brick Company was formed around 1919, in Ferris, Texas, and
merged with five other companies to form the Ferris Brick Company in 1923 (McKnight 2003). At some
later date, the property seems to have been built up and expanded into a larger complex of buildings. The
gravel configuration may suggest a parking lot surrounding a building or buildings. The standing
structures are all located right at the edge of the gravel and may have been constructed to flank the gravel
lot (or vice versa). The presence of a possible gravel parking lot, a menâs room sign, and bright green
paint also indicates the site may at some point in time have been a public structure, such as a community
center, school, or church; or it may have been a business.

53

Site 41CW170 is roughly 5 percent intact and in poor condition within the ROW. The state of several
likely associated structures outside the LCRA transmission line ROW remains unassessed. The research
value of the portion of the site 41CW170 within the ROW is low, and, thus, that part of the site is
assessed as not eligible for SAL designation as the buildings are mostly gone and there is very little
artifactual material remaining. No further investigations are recommended, within the ROW. Other parts
of 41CW170 outside of the transmission line ROW appear to have an undetermined eligibility for formal
designation as an SAL. During the current upgrade project, a new transmission line structure is slated to
be placed outside the site to the east and an old structure within the site boundaries is to be removed.
Neither activity should have a negative impact on the site. LCRA Cultural Resources staff recommends
no special considerations or avoidance measures at this time.

OTHER AREAS

Site 41CW28 and the San Marcos River Alluvial Plain
Between I-10 and Luling, the T-538 line traverses a deep alluvial floodplain of the San Marcos River for
a distance of 5.8 km. In order to attain a greater depth and more quickly investigate the floodplain, LCRA
Cultural Resources staff used a skid-steer mounted auger in lieu of shovels for subsurface testing, as
described above in the Methodology section. The locations of the 21 auger tests dug on the river
floodplain are shown in (Figure 25).
Prior to the start of the augering, a bifacial tool fragment was noted on a gravel road outside the ROW on
the higher terrace above and to the southwest of the flood plain, but no cultural material was observed in
adjacent transmission line corridor in that area. Additionally, no cultural materials were found in ST#51
which was close to the surface find. Auger tests in the southern half of the river floodplain area had clay,
clay loam and silty clay deposits that were sterile.
The northern part of the floodplain area is within undeveloped City of Luling parkland that was originally
a pecan orchard but has mostly been utilized as an oil field in recent years. Auger Tests #90-93 were
placed at each of the four new structure locations. Three additional auger tests, #95-97, were placed
between the structures and one auger test, #94, was placed where the transmission line comes closest to a
bend in the San Marcos River. Soil profiles were consistent, with a darker brown clay loam down to
around 60 cm bs, over a brown clay loam down to around 140 cm bs, where a slightly lighter brown clay
loam ran to termination depth between 145 cm and 160 cm bs. This is consistent with observations of a
cutbank on the other bank of the San Marcos River near tests #93 and #94 (Figure 26).

54

Figure 25. Auger test locations in the San Marcos River alluvial plain.

Figure 26. Soil profile in cutbank of San Marcos River consistent with auger tests placed in Luling City Park.

It is important to note that the far eastern site boundary for site 41CW28 extends into the transmission
line ROW on the San Marcos River floodplain. The actual Zedlerâs Mill complex at 41CW28 is located
on the north bank of the San Marcos River outside of the transmission line corridor. The only portion of
55

the defined site area that will be impacted by ground-disturbing activities is located on the opposite
(south) side of the river downstream from the mill where Auger Test #90 was excavated. The only artifact
found in Auger Test #90 is a metal object from Level 4 (60-80 cm bs). The artifact is thought to be an
early to mid-twentieth century date that possibly from a tractor or some unidentified type of oil field
machinery rather than an artifact associated with the mill (Figure 27). No other artifacts were found in
nearby Auger Tests #97 or 91 to the southwest. Northeast of Auger Test #90, the remainder of the ROW

Figure 27. Historic metal artifact from Auger Test #90.

between the south site boundary and the river was submitted to surface survey but not subsurface testing
because no other ground disturbances are expected during this project. During the surface survey, several
early to mid-twentieth century bricks were observed but were obviously part of river bank stabilization
fill. North of the river, there is one new transmission pole structure that will be erected just northeast of
the boundary of 41CW28. This location was surface surveyed but no subsurface testing was conducted
because the new structure will be placed in the same location as the old structure, the structure is located
on the man-made embankment of HWY 80, and because there was a risk of striking buried utilities.
Smith Creek Crossing
Smith Creek lies between site 41GU186 to the west and the Malone substation to the east. In this area are
both the main channel and a tributary of Smith Creek. The banks of both of these stream channels are
deeply incised and heavily eroded in the ROW corridor. LCRA Cultural Resources staff surveyed both
channels in case ramped gravel stream crossings are constructed for vehicles to access structure locations.
At the main channel of Smith Creek, LCRA Cultural Resources staff placed a shovel test on both sides of
the creek. Both tests were negative for cultural material. Where the ROW corridor crosses the tributary,
56

the channel is actually several deeply incised and badly eroded channels. No shovel tests were placed in
this area, as surface and cutbank observations revealed that much of the surface sandy loam had been
washed away in high energy floods leaving only pre-Holocene clays to remain.
Luling
Survey within Luling city limits is constrained to only certain areas because most structures will be
replaced in the same location, and because there are very few high probability areas. At the northern city
limits, shovel and skid-steer mounted auger subsurface tests were conducted along the stream channel of
Salt Branch but no cultural material was observed.

SUMMARY AND RECOMMENDATIONS

An intensive cultural resources survey of the LCRAâs proposed T-538 Deer Creek to Luling Electrical
Transmission Line Upgrade Project was conducted by LCRA Cultural Resources staff from January
through March 2016. Seven new sites were recorded, including four prehistoric sites, two historic sites,
and one multi-component site. A portion of previously recorded site 41CW28 was also investigated
(Table 4: T-538 upgrade project sites, types, and recommendations.
).
The four prehistoric sites, sites 41GU184, 41GU185, 41GU186, and 41GU188, were sparse lithic scatters
of chert flakes and occasional burned rock. No tools or diagnostics were observed at these sites. No
avoidance measures or special considerations are recommended for these sites. These sites are of low
research value and the portions of these sites within the transmission line corridor are all recommended as
ineligible for formal designation as SALs...

None

Site 41GU187 is a historic African-American farmstead with material generally dating to the 1930s and
1940s. Though numerous structures remain standing, none are within the project area, nor will they be
57

affected by the project. The foundation and fenceline intruding into the ROW corridor will not be
negatively affected by the project. No avoidance measures or special considerations are recommended for
this site. The portion of the site within the project area is deemed to be of low research value. This site is
recommended to be ineligible for SAL status within the ROW.
Site 41CW170 is the other historic site recorded during this survey. This site is likely a historic AfricanAmerican farmstead dating to the 1920s, where other buildings were constructed at a later date. These
later structures seem to have been of a more public nature, and may have been a community center,
church, school, or business. The project should have a negligible impact on this site. No avoidance
measures or special considerations are recommended for this site. The portion of this site within the
project area is assessed as not eligible for formal designation as a SAL.
Site 41GU183 is the only multi-component site, consisting of a sparse lithic scatter and a sparse historic
artifact scatter. The site was highly disturbed by previous transmission line construction activity, and is
heavily bioturbated. No avoidance measures are recommended for this site. This site is deemed to be of
low research value. This site is recommended as ineligible for SAL status within the project area.
The far southeastern edge of previously recorded site 41CW28 also known as the Zedlerâs Mill site, is
situated within the project area. No significant or potentially significant remains associated with the mill
site were found during investigations within the transmission line ROW. The portion of the site within
the ROW is assessed as not eligible for formal designation as a SAL.
The LCRA Cultural Resources staff considers the T-538 Deer Creek to Luling electrical transmission line
project to be completely investigated. No special considerations or avoidance measures are recommended.
Emergency discovery procedures remain in place, as always.

Project 3.16: Intensive Cultural Resource e Investigations of LCRAâs Proposed T-421,
Gilleland Creek to Elgin Switch Overhaul Project, Travis County, Texas.

By Andrew F. Malof
Principal Investigator

TAC Permit 7531

Interim Report 1.16
Real Estate Services
Lower Colorado River Authority
February 2016
81

INTRODUCTION AND MANAGEMENT SUMMARY

LCRA proposes to conduct an overhaul on the 12.9-mile-long Gilleland Creek to Elgin Switch (T-421)
substation transmission line, located in northeastern Travis County. The project includes replacing 11 of
118 support structures. A map and file review indicated that eight of these are in areas of moderate to high
probability for unrecorded archaeological sites. The high probability areas totaled approximately 4,000
feet in length, for a total project area of about 9 acres (see Figure 30-Figure 33).
A cultural resources survey was conducted by LCRA Cultural Resources staff on February 19, 2016.
Each high probability areas was examined and a shovel test placed at each replacement structure location,
excepting one in a highly disturbed, plowed field with 100 percent visibility.
One previously unrecorded archaeological site was documented. The site is a minor lithic scatter and
minor historic scatter. The site is considered to be fully documented within the project area and is
recommended as ineligible for formal listing as a State Antiquities Landmark.
Given the limited nature of the project, the short format report is used here. Detailed culture history will
be included in the annual report where the final will reside.
No artifacts were collected during the project; project records will be submitted to an accredited curation
facility.
NATURAL SETTING

The project areas is within the Blackland Prairie region of central Texas. The geology is primarily
Cretaceous-aged Navarro and Taylor group marl and a small amount of Eocene-aged Midway group clays
and sands, and a lesser amount of Late Pleistocene terrace deposits (Barnes 1974). Soils in high
probability areas are mapped as Heiden clay, Houston Black clay, and Tinn clay (Soil Survey Staff 2016).
Observed conditions included broad areas of cropland, some pasture, and wooded riparian zones. The
largest drainage is Wilbarger Creek, located at the western end of the project area. Other streams include
Cottonwood, Willow and Little Willow, and Dry Brushy creeks and their tributaries.

RESEARCH DESIGN AND FIELD METHODS

The assessment was designed to identify and evaluate archaeological sites potentially impacted by the
construction project. The only significant new soil disturbance is at transmission structure locations,
where new structures will be placed within about 3 meters of present structures. Therefore shovel tests
were placed adjacent to existing structures in high probability areas. Given the nature of the dense, clayey
Vertisols, tests were excavated to depths of 40 cm. This often was enough to reach an apparent B horizon.
Shovel test and other items of interest were collected on a GPS with typical 3-meter accuracy. Shovel test
82

data is summarized in Table 5. Other records consisted of shovel test profiles, field notes and
photographs.

RESULTS OF INVESTIGATIONS
41TV2505
One previously unrecorded multi-component archaeological site was documented. Site 41TV2505 is a
very sparse lithic scatter with a minor historic component located across the toe of an upland ridge system
overlooking Wilbarger Creek to the east (see Figure 34). It is within a plowed, terraced agricultural field
of Heiden clay that rises about 20 feet above the floodplain. The multiple terraces are as much as a meter
tall, indicating significant ground disturbance. No shovel tests were attempted due to 100 percent
visibility and degree of past impacts.

Figure 28. Setting of 41TV2505 looking west. Note contouring visible at tire tracks.

The prehistoric component consists of two cores, a heavily patinated hard hammer flake, a couple of other
flakes and flake fragments, a thin, late stage biface, and, most notably, a small polyhedral blade core. The
historic component, located at the eastern field edge, consisted of a single piece of solarized glass, a thick
piece of clear bottle glass, and a well and associated windmill. The well is presently constructed of
cement, and is about three feet in diameter. The remains of a brick collar demonstrate its age relative to
the present feature.
The site, while barely registering as such, is interesting for its lithic assemblage. Nearby fields are heavily
littered with chert and quartzite cobbles, so resource availability would not be an issue. The blade core,
however, suggests high mobility, and is most likely an artifact of Toyah phase people. It is probable that
there is a more substantial site located nearby. The historical artifacts merely indicate long-term land
83

usage, most likely starting with the Bohls family, which, according to LCRA records, deeded it to Joseph
and Adele Jaworski in 1936. The site is not considered eligible for formal SAL status.

Figure 29. Polyhedral core from 41TV2505.

SUMMARY AND RECOMMENDATIONS

LCRA proposes to conduct work on the existing T-421 transmission line between the Elgin Switch and
Gilleland Creek substations. The project consist of replacing 11 existing support structures, eight of
which are considered to be in moderate to high probability areas for archaeological sites. A cultural
resources assessment consisting of a map and file review followed by a field survey resulted in the
recording of 41TV2505. The site is a minor prehistoric lithic scatter with a small historic artifact scatter
and field well with windmill. The site is considered to be fully investigated within the project area and is
recommended as ineligible for formal SAL status. It is recommended the project be allowed to proceed as
described.
