Project 4.16: An Intensive Cultural Resource Survey of the Lower Colorado River Authorityâs
Proposed T-544 Altair to Glidden Transmission Line Upgrade Project, Colorado County, Texas

By Daniel J. Prikryl, Steven R. Evans and Matthew E. Larsen

Principal Investigator Daniel J. Prikryl

Texas Antiquities Permit # 7531
Interim Report # 5.16
Project # 4.16
Lower Colorado River Authority
May 2016
92

INTRODUCTION AND MANAGEMENT SUMMARY
The Lower Colorado River Authority (LCRA) is proposing to rebuild the majority of its T-544 electrical
transmission line which extends for a length of 12.2 miles from the Altair Substation to the Glidden
Substation in Colorado County, Texas (Figure 35, Figure 36, and Figure 37). At the northwest end of the
T-544 route, where the line shares an 80 ft wide right-of-way (ROW) with LCRAâs T-113 transmission
line, approximately 2.4 linear miles of the T-544 alignment were surveyed for cultural resources in 2008
with two sites, 41CD157 and 41CD158, being recorded. The portions of these two sites within the
combined T-113 and T-544 ROW were assessed during a cultural resource assessment in 2015 for
maintenance work as not eligible for formal designation as State Antiquities Landmarks (SALs) (Prikryl
et al. 2016:549-553). The remainder of the T-544 ROW has never been surveyed for cultural resource
sites. Where possible, LCRA is proposing to increase the spacing between structures during the rebuild
activities so that many of the new structures will be placed at the locations in between the current
structures. As a result, ground-disturbing activities will occur in locations not previously impacted by the
placements of the original structures. Based on this, a cultural resource survey of the portion of the T-544
ROW not previously surveyed was warranted.
During the period from March through May 2016, the LCRA Archaeology Services staff conducted an
intensive cultural resource survey of high probability areas along the previously unsurveyed part of the T544 route. The survey consisted of pedestrian survey and shovel testing. Personnel who conducted the
fieldwork consisted of LCRA staff member Daniel J. Prikryl (Principal Investigator) along with
contracted archaeologists Matthew Larsen and Steven Evans. The investigation was conducted under
TAC Blanket Permit #7531, which was issued to the LCRA by the Texas Historical Commission (THC)
for Calendar Year 2016. All work was completed in accordance with methods for fieldwork and
reporting as proposed by the Council of Texas Archeologists (1992).
Approximately 55 acres were surveyed during the current investigation. One new cultural resource site,
41CD159, was recorded and assessed. The portion of this prehistoric campsite within the transmission
line corridor as well as the portions of previously recorded sites 41CD157 and 41CD158 within the
corridor are assessed as not meriting formal designation as SALs. The LCRA Cultural Resource staff
recommends that no additional cultural resource investigations are needed and that construction of the
upgrade project be allowed to proceed. Since the project had a âno collection policyâ, there are no
artifacts that will require curation. Project records will be submitted to an accredited curation facility.

ENVIRONMENTAL BACKGROUND
The project area is located in the Gulf Coastal Plains (Fenneman 1938:100-103) in Southeast Texas. The
transmission line is located near the Colorado River with the main north-south running transmission line
segment ranging from 1 to 6.25 km west of the current river channel.
Surface geology at the northern end of the project area consists of Holocene era alluvium and Pleistoceneaged fluviatile deposits associated with the Colorado River channel, while the southern end of the project

93

Figure 35. The T-544 Transmission line, proposed structures, archaeological sites and shovel tests on a section of the Columbus USGS
7.5â Quadrangle.
94

Figure 36. The T-544 Transmission line, proposed structures, archaeological sites and shovel tests on sections of the Columbus,
Alleyton, Rock Island, and Altair USGS 7.5â Quadrangles.
95

Figure 37. The T-544 Transmission line, proposed structures, archaeological sites and shovel tests on a section of the Altair USGS
7.5â Quadrangle.
96

sits on the Beaumont Formation (Barnes 1974). The Beaumont Formation is a Pleistocene formation
composed of clay, silt, and sand with concentrations of CaCO3 and iron oxides, and is noted as being
featureless. The formation also appears to be mostly upland area, and large portions of it lack notable
creek drainages.
According to the Colorado County Soil Survey, soils on the alluvial floodplain of the Colorado River in
the project area consist of Mohat loam, Gad loamy fine sand, Norwood silty clay loam, Bergstrom silt
loam, Wilson clay loam, and Brazoria clay (Soil Survey Staff 2014). On the Pleistocene aged first terrace
rise above the river floodplain, soils consist of a variety of sandy loams and fine sandy loams, and loamy
sands and fine loamy sands, the majority being Telf fine sandy loam, Telfner fine sandy loam, or TelfCieno complex. Approximately 3.34 miles, or 27%, of the transmission line, runs across areas reported by
the county soil survey as gravel mines or pits, and there are various other operations in existence along
the transmission line that are too recent to appear in the soil survey.

Figure 38. View of upper end of McKenzie Creek immediately downstream from transmission line ROW corridor.
Vegetation shown is typical of this area, as is the general width and depth of the drainage.

Colorado County is an ecotone of three ecoregions (Griffith et al 2007), the Southern Blackland Prairies,
the Northern Humid Gulf Coastal Prairies, and the Southern Post Oak Savanna. Additionally, much of the
project area lies in or adjacent to the floodplains and low terraces of the Colorado River. The vegetation
of these ecoregions consists of prairie grasslands including little and big bluestem and yellow indiangrass,
among others, with occasional stands of oak, mostly Post oak. The floodplain of the Colorado River is
97

riparian woodlands consisting of oaks, elm, and pecan. Since European settlement, most of this region has
been converted to pasture and croplands. The waning of agriculture in recent times has left much of the
land overgrown with mesquite and various other acacias. Prehistorically, the fauna of the area would have
been typical of grassland and riparian environments and would have included, among others, deer, bison,
coyote, otter, and various freshwater fish, turtles and birds. The numerous spent gravel pits in the area
collect water, which attracts various waterfowl, and are often put to use as cow pastures.

CULTURE HISTORY
Prehistoric Background
The project area is located adjacent to the boundaries of the Southeast, South, and Central Texas
Archeological Regions (Patterson 1995; Hester 1995; Prewitt 1981). None of the authors of recent articles
on these three archeological regions claim Colorado County as being within their respective region of
interest. Skelton (1977) described the transitional nature of the Fayette and Colorado county area in the
discussion of his excavation work at the Fayette Power Project in nearby Fayette County. Skelton also
noted that J. Charles Kelley (1955) had previously advanced the notion that this area was a transitional
zone in prehistoric times. Specifically, Kelley discussed cultural relationships between coastal and inland
prehistoric groups and suggested that geographically diverse cultural groups may have co-existed within
this area.
Common prehistoric site types in the Colorado County area consist of open campsites, lithic
scatter/procurement sites, and cemeteries. Archeological investigations in neighboring areas have shown
occupation by Native American peoples for at least 13,000 years prior to the coming of Europeans. The
data show that throughout these millennia, prehistoric peoples were nomadic hunter-gatherers who moved
across the landscape exploiting seasonally available plant and animal resources. In terms of a
chronological sequence for these nomadic hunter-gatherer peoples, Pattersonâs (1995:243) proposed
sequence for Southeast Texas, for example, is as follows: Early Paleoindian 10,000-8000 B.C., Late
Paleoindian 8000-5000 B.C., Early Archaic 5000-3000 B.C., Middle Archaic 3000-1500 B.C., Late
Archaic 1500 B.C.-A.D. 100, Early Ceramic A.D. 100-600, Late Prehistoric A.D. 600-1500, Protohistoric
A.D. 1500-1700, and Historic Indian A.D. 1700-1800.
Historic Background
The transmission line passes through the southern part of the City of Columbus, Texas and then extends
southward to the Altair community. Information on these communities and important historical events is
taken from The Handbook of Texas Online and is summarized in the following paragraphs.
According to legend, the City of Columbus was originally the location of an Indian village named
Montezuma. No specific data could be found on this village or its inhabitants. The first Anglo-American
settlers, Robert and Joseph Kuykendall and Daniel Gillehand, all members of Stephen F. Austinâs Old
Three Hundred, moved to the area near Columbus in 1821. In 1823, Benjamin Beeson, another early
settler, established a ferry across the Colorado River at the fledgling community, which became known as
98

Beesonâs Crossing. As early as 1822, the settlers in the area gathered at this spot for elections of local
officials of the Mexican governmentâs Colorado River District.
At the time of the Texas Revolution, the settlement was known as Columbus and had about 25 families.
Although Houstonâs army burned all of the buildings in Columbus during the Runaway Scrape, the town
was soon rebuilt after the Revolution and designated as the county seat of Colorado County in 1836. By
1847, it had 20 houses, 3 stores, 2 taverns, and a blacksmith shop. An early industry was a German cigar
factory that was established in 1840. Cotton production became the major economic pursuit in the 1850s
and continued to be important into the early twentieth century.
By 1880, there were 1,666 farms in Colorado County. However, like many other areas in Texas, the
period after the Civil War was marred by violence. A fresh wave of killings even occurred as late as
1906. Cattle production was also important to the economy, especially in the 1870s and 1880s. In 1883,
the Columbus Meat and Ice Company was established in Columbus. Exploitation of large sand and
gravel deposits became a flourishing industry in the first decade of the twentieth century. By 1910,
Columbus was said to be surrounded by gravel pits. Sand and gravel mining continues to be important to
the local economy today.
After World War II, a large part of Columbusâ local economy began to focus on recreational activities. In
Columbus, this focus centered on historic buildings and âdown-home atmosphereâ. Numerous historic
buildings were restored and the Magnolia Homes Tour was begun in 1961. In the year 2000, Columbus
had a population of 3,916 and 326 businesses. However, in 2010 the population had decreased to 3,655.

PREVIOUS INVESTIGATIONS
There are relatively few previously recorded archeological sites within and around the project area. A file
search showed that in 2008 the LCRA Cultural Resources Team conducted an intensive cultural resource
survey for the portion of this line that extends for a length of 24.4 miles from the Columbus AEP
Substation, which is located in the southwestern part of the City of Columbus, and east of the Peters
Substation. As a result of this work, six new cultural resource sites, consisting of 41CD135-140, and one
previously recorded site, 41CD132, were recorded and assessed. The portions of sites 41CD135-140
within the transmission line corridor were assessed as not eligible for formal designation as SALs and no
restrictions or special conditions were recommended for any of these sites during any future LCRA
upgrade, overhaul and maintenance work activities. Site 41CD132 is a portion of the old elevated railroad
grade of the Buffalo Bayou, Brazos, and Colorado Railroad that was constructed in 1860 adjacent to the
town of Alleyton. The railroad has been previously formally designated as a SAL. Avoidance of this site
is required during any future LCRA upgrade, overhaul and maintenance.
Additionally, during the 2008 LCRA survey of T-113, the LCRA Cultural Resources Team undertook an
assessment of the portion of the T-544 corridor that extends from the Columbus AEP Substation to the
Glidden Substation. Two new cultural resource sites, 41CD157 and 41CD158, were recorded and
assessed within the west end of the corridor between the Glidden and Columbus AEP substations (Figure
1). These sites are discussed in detail in the Survey Results section.
99

Other individually recorded sites in the vicinity of the T-544 line include 41CD60, 41CD120, 41CD121,
41CD124, 41CD154 and 41CD155. Site 41CD60 is a prehistoric campsite located just 180 meters north
of where site 41CD158 is situated within the T-544 ROW. Both of these sites are situated on the
Pleistocene terrace that overlooks the former Colorado River floodplain. Materials found in 1976 when
41CD60 was recorded include two broken arrowpoints, numerous flakes and five cores.
Elsewhere, prehistoric sites 41CD120 and 41CD121 were recorded during the All American Pipeline
Survey in 1986. The sites are situated on a Pleistocene terrace overlooking the former Colorado River
floodplain. They lie 1.5 and 1.6 km east of the T-544 ROW. Both sites are described as small lithic
scatters, although 41CD121 did include some burned rocks. Site 41CD124 is located 2.4 km east of the T544 ROW on a high terrace overlooking the confluence of McKenzie Creek and Colorado River. It is a
multi-component site with a Republic of Texas era historic component containing square nails, porcelain
and glass artifacts along with prehistoric materials dating from the Early Archaic through the Late
Prehistoric.
Sites 41CD154 and 41CD155 are apparently the two sites currently under investigations by the 20142016 Texas Archeological Society Field Schools. No information about the sites is available on the THC
Site Atlas. However, the TAS Newsletter has previously described 41CD154 as a lithic scatter and lithic
procurement site with dense chipped stone lithic debris. Site 41CD155 has been described as a buried
Late Prehistoric campsite on the Colorado River floodplain terrace.
At the Fayette Power Project, a 2,400 acre survey area on the Cedar Creek drainage yielded 10 historic
and 19 prehistoric sites. Most of the historic sites were associated with the historic Biegel Settlement,
which was founded in the mid-nineteenth century as a part of Austinâs Little Colony. Seven prehistoric
and three historic sites were recommended for excavation work. Skelton (1977) subsequently excavated
six of the prehistoric sites. Most of these prehistoric sites turned out to be shallow lithic scatters
interpreted as temporary upland work camps occupied intermittently over the last 8,000 years. At site
41FY74, however, the remains of a single butchered bison were found in association with Late Prehistoric
Toyah Phase artifacts and activity areas.

The closest published large-scale professional investigations consist of those for the proposed Columbus
Bend Reservoir and the Fayette Power Project. At Columbus Bend, which is located on the Colorado
River between La Grange and Columbus north of the western end of the project area, Nunley (1963)
recorded 36 archeological sites within Colorado County. The proposed reservoir was suspended until the
early 1980s when a modified reservoir proposal called the Colorado Coastal Plains Project was surveyed
by New World Research (Keller and Campbell 1984). A 20 percent sample survey led to the recording of
96 cultural resource sites in Fayette and Colorado counties. Site types included prehistoric lithic scatters,
lithic and ceramic scatters, stratified prehistoric sites, and historic sites. This reservoir project was then
cancelled by the Bureau of Reclamation.

100

METHODOLOGY
The methodology for this project consisted of a file search, followed by field survey. The file search
involved research on the THC archaeological Site Atlas, in LCRA internal archives, and of topographical
maps of the area, from both the United States Geological Survey and the Army Corps of Engineers,
dating from 1919 to 1963. The purpose of the file search was to find areas of high probability for
archaeological sites, both historic and prehistoric. Prehistoric high probability areas were determined by
various factors, including: presence of Holocene deposits, landforms, and proximity to water sources.
Historic period high probability areas were determined by noting the relationship of the transmission line
corridor to the towns of Columbus and Altair. Additionally, the locations at which the transmission line
crossed over or near recorded house structures and old roads on early maps were noted. Fieldwork then
focused on these prehistoric and historic period areas with high probabilities for cultural resource sites. A
total of 5.74 linear miles of the transmission line corridor were assessed as having a high probability for
cultural resource sites. Within this 5.74 linear mile area, 40 transmission pole structures will be replaced.
Fieldwork concentrated on these 40 locations with additional consideration being given to several high
probability areas adjacent to stream channels that might be affected by vehicular traffic.
The survey method involved inspecting the ground surface within 15 meters of each of the pertinent
structures, and excavating shovel tests near the structure poles to detect any subsurface cultural material.
In accordance with CTA standards, LCRA Cultural Resources staff excavated shovel tests, 30-35 cm in
diameter, down to either 100 cm bs or to the end of Holocene deposits, in 20 cm levels. Shovel tests were
generally concentrated in areas where the ground will be disturbed by construction. In some areas, local
conditions obviated the need for such tests. Such situations included terraces with numerous gopher
burrows where a sizeable sample of subsurface soils were mounded on ground surface, former and active
rice fields and their surrounding embankments where topsoils had been previously churned, and cutbanks
along creek channels and adjacent gravel pits that provided substantial soil profiles.

101

Figure 39. View of archaeologist examining cutbank/disturbed soils on edge of gravel pit adjacent to transmission
line.

Figure 40. View of archaeologist examining bare ground surface at south end of project area near Altair community.

A total of 50 shovel tests are reported. These include STs 35-50 which were actually dug in 2008 where
the T-544 and T-113 lines share the same corridor at the north end of the project area. All deposits from
the shovel tests were screened in the field using Â¼-inch mesh screens. No materials were collected during
this survey. The soil descriptions and artifact recovery from the subsurface tests are presented in
Appendix 1.
102

In several areas within the town of Columbus, intensive commercial and residential development and road
construction have heavily damaged or destroyed the original soil deposits within the transmission line
ROW to the extent that any cultural resource sites would have been destroyed. No survey work was
undertaken by the LCRA staff in such areas. These areas include of the stretch of transmission line
corridor that extends from the south edge of Interstate Highway 10 north to the Columbus Substation (see
Figure 1). Additionally, the area between STs #4 and 25 is a densely developed trailer park where much
of the transmission line corridor is either covered by asphalt roads, gravel-covered parking spots, small
yards or actual trailer houses (see Figure 1). Another area that could not be investigated is a scrap metal
yard that lies between the east edge of site 41CD157 and Business Highway 71 (see Figure 1). The
presence of dense piles of metal and car parts made survey impractical.
Also of special note is a non-urban area in the southern part of the project area that was covered by
pedestrian survey but was not shovel tested. This area contains rice fields on both the north and south
sides of McKenzie Creek (see Figure 2). Four transmission pole structures will be erected in this area.
This area has been modified to flatten the fields with minor raised embankments being located along the
eastern edges of the fields adjacent to McKenzie Creek. The actual rice fields contained some standing
water that made shovel testing impractical. The embankments along with scattered stretches of actual rice
fields provided adequate ground surface visibility to check for archaeological materials with none being
found.

Figure 41. View of archaeologists excavating shovel test adjacent to road on south edge of Columbus, Texas.
103

RESULTS
The field survey was completed intermittently from March through May 2016. Previously recorded site,
41CD158 was revisited to check the current condition of the site and to further confirm that the portion
within the LCRA transmission line easement is not SAL eligible. Previously recorded site 41CD157 was
not revisited because the existing data indicated that it had already been heavily damaged at the time of its
previous recording. One new site, 41CD159, was recorded and assessed within the project area. These
three sites are described below.
41CD157
Site 41CD157 is a multi-component site with prehistoric and historic materials that is located about 150
meters west of the point at which the double circuited T-113 and T-544 transmission line crosses State
Highway 71 (Business) in the southwestern part of the City of Columbus. The portion of the site that was
recorded in 2008 in the T-113/T-544 corridor extends for a length of 85 meters between existing
Structures 24/6 and 24/5 (Figure 1). Previous work consisted of pedestrian survey and the excavation of
four shovel tests that were assigned the numbers 44 and 46-48 for the current project. Prehistoric artifacts
observed consisted of about 10 pieces of chert chipping debris that were seen on ground surface and in ST
#46 (Figure 42). No prehistoric features or diagnostic artifacts were seen. The historic component at site
41CD157 appears to be an early to mid-twentieth century trash dump. Historic artifacts were found on
ground surface and in three of the shovel tests. The historic materials are mostly nondescript pieces of
clear glass, scrap metal, pipe fragments and brick fragments. Some oyster shell fragments and marine
shell fragments were also seen on ground surface and in several of the shovel tests, but these probably
represent introduced fill for an old road bed.
Site 41CD157 is situated on the northeast edge of a former gravel pit. It is possible that the pit served as a
convenient spot for dumping trash during the early and mid-twentieth century. The removal of terrace fill
for the pit may have destroyed any part of the prehistoric component that extended southward. Overall,
the portion of the site within the transmission line ROW does not appear to have any research potential as
the prehistoric materials are sparse and the historic materials are also diffuse and date to relatively recent
times. Further, no intact features associated with either component were evident. The LCRA Cultural
Resources Team recommends site 41CD157 to be ineligible for formal designation as an SAL within the
ROW. No new structures will be placed within the site during the 2016 upgrade project and therefore no
ground disturbances are expected. The LCRA Cultural Resources Team recommends that all upgrade
activities within site 41CD157 be allowed to proceed without any restrictions or special conditions.

104

Figure 42. A sketch map of 41CD157, showing the shovel tests and current and proposed transmission line
structures.

41CD158
The second site, 41CD158, is located on the terrace slope that is the boundary between the Colorado
Riverâs alluvial floodplain and the higher Pleistocene terrace top to the west. The site was recorded
during the survey of the T-113 upgrade project in 2008. Work consisted of pedestrian survey and the
excavation of four positive shovel tests which are labeled as STs #37-40 for the current project. The edges
of the site were defined by negative STs # 50 and 49 to the east and west. The overall length of the site
within the double circuited T-113 and T-544 ROW is about 100 meters, with existing Structure 25/1
being situated within the west central part of the site (Figure 43). A new transmission pole structure will
be erected about 10 meters east of this structure. The 1981 photorevised version of the Columbus 7.5â
USGS quadrangle indicates that some former sand and gravel quarrying occurred in the past on the
terrace slope where the site was found. This may be reflected by the absence of topsoil in some spots
within the site.
The work in 2008 indicated that site 41CD158 is a prehistoric campsite with observed cultural materials
consisting of about 16 chert flakes and chips, one chert biface thinning flake, one chert core, one burned
quartzite rock and three burned chert chunks. Aside from several chert flakes seen on ground surface, the
majority of the prehistoric artifacts were found in Shovel Tests #38 and 39 on the terrace slope in the
eastern half of the site with the artifacts scattered through topsoil deposits down to 60 cm below ground
surface. No diagnostic artifacts or evidence of features were found. A revisit during the current project
revealed no additional artifacts during pedestrian survey.
105

Figure 43. A sketch map of 41CD158, showing the shovel tests and current and proposed transmission line
structures.

The portion of site 41CD158 within the transmission line corridor was previously assessed as not meriting
formal designation as a SAL due to its diffuse remains, the lack of diagnostic artifacts and features, and
the fact that the site had been previously damaged by gravel quarrying. The revisit did not alter that
assessment. The LCRA Cultural Resources Team recommends the proposed upgrade activities, including
the placement of the new structure, should proceed across the site without any special conditions or
restrictions.
41CD159
This prehistoric campsite is located on the north side of a tributary of McKenzie Creek (Figure 44). The
creek channel in this area has cut down about 20 ft in the terrace creating a prominent ridge overlooking
the creek channel. Previous vehicular traffic on an old road has created an eroded linear depression that
extends from the creek channel northward up the slope into the terrace top in the center of the ROW for a
length of about 25 meters. Soils consist of a 12 to 27 cm thick sandy loam topsoil above a sandy clay B
horizon. The ROW is currently a grassy field covered with short grasses, wildflowers and dewberry
bushes.
Cultural materials were first seen on the eroded terrace edge overlooking the creek channel and on the
eroded slopes of the old road. The materials included chert debitage as well as burned chert fragments that
106

Figure 44. A sketch map of 41CD159, showing the shovel tests, creek crossing, and current transmission line
structure.

Figure 45. Looking south at the portion of site 41CD159 within the T-544 ROW.

were probably hearthstones. Similar materials were found in three shovel tests, STs #5-7. However,
artifact density was low, with ST #5 and 6 each producing just three pieces of chert debitage and ST #7
107

yielding just one piece of debitage. Six pieces of burned chert and two chert chunks were also found in ST
#5 and one such piece in ST #6. All of these artifacts were found in Level 1 of the shallow sandy loam
deposits that went down to depths of 12, 17 and 27 cm in the three positive shovel tests before hitting
basal clay. No other classes of artifacts were found nor were any features or diagnostic artifacts present.
The LCRA staff concludes that the portion of site 41CD159 within the transmission line corridor
represents an undated prehistoric campsite and that, based on the terrace topography, the majority of the
site lies upstream (west) of the ROW. The part of the site within the ROW is assessed as being of low
research value and not eligible for formal designation as a SAL due to the diffuse nature of artifacts in
shallow deposits and the lack of features and diagnostic artifacts. No new transmission pole structures
will be placed within site 41CD159 during the 2016 upgrade project with the closest new structure
placement being planned for an area about 60 meters north of the north end of the site.

SUMMARY AND RECOMMENDATIONS
The LCRA is proposing to rebuild the majority of its T-544 electrical transmission line from the Altair
substation to the Glidden substation in Columbus, in Colorado County. The new structures will be placed
farther apart than the old structures and will, therefore, impact previously undisturbed areas. A file search
led to the determination that 40 new structures are to be placed in areas of high probability for prehistoric
or historic cultural resources and should be surveyed. Survey within the high probability areas consisted
of surface inspection and shovel testing within the immediate area of the new structure locations and at
high probability areas at stream crossings.
The section of T-544 between Columbus and the Glidden substation was surveyed by the LCRA Cultural
Resources Team in 2008, resulting in one new multi-component site, 41CD157, and one new prehistoric
campsite, 41CD158, being recorded in the ROW. The portions of these sites within the transmission line
corridor had been previously assessed as not eligible for SAL listing, but site 41CD158 was revisited
during the current project.
The 2016 cultural resources survey of the previously unsurveyed section of T-544 between Columbus and
Altair resulted in the recording of one new prehistoric campsite, 41CD159, on the north bank of a
tributary to McKenzie Creek.
As a result of the 2016 survey, the LCRA Cultural Resources Team assessed all three sites recorded
within the T-544 ROW to be of low research value and recommends all three as ineligible for SAL status
within the ROW. The LCRA Cultural Resources Team recommends the T-544 electrical transmission line
upgrade project be allowed to proceed without any special considerations or avoidance measures.
As always, standard emergency discovery provisions are in place. Should the construction personnel
uncover any presently unknown buried archeological features during construction work, ground
disturbing activities in the immediate area of the discovery will cease until LCRA Cultural Resources
staff can contact the Texas Historical Commission to assess what, if any, mitigation efforts may be
necessary.

Project 14.16: Intensive Cultural Resources Assessment of LCRAâs Proposed T-260
Transmission Line Sim Gideon to Paige Split Rebuild Project, Bastrop County, Texas

By Andrew F. Malof and Steven R. Evans

Andrew F. Malof
Principal Investigator

TAC Permit 7531
Interim Report 2.16

Lower Colorado River Authority
Real Estate Services
April 2016
122

MANAGEMENT SUMMARY

The Lower Colorado River Authority is proposing to rebuild the T-260 electrical transmission line from
the Sim Gideon Power Plant to the Paige Split (Figure 46; Figure 47). This encompasses approximately
19.18 miles of transmission line with a 100 foot wide right of way, equaling approximately 111.27 acres
of project area. The project consists of removing all existing support structures, which are of lattice tower
construction, and replacing them with single-pole concrete or metal poles.
Although the line has been previously surveyed, those assessments were for right of way maintenance
only, and the previous work consisted of little more than reconnaissance. Therefore, a research design
commensurate with the present project was developed. High probability areas were determined based on
map and file reviews, including historical maps to help determine where historic period sites could be
present.
The project will also require a number of stream crossings, necessitating a United States Army Corps of
Engineers Nationwide 12 permit. All identified stream crossings were assessed due to the potential
presence of the federally-listed Houston toad, as well as the potential for archaeological sites eligible for
listing on the National Register of Historic Places (NRHP). The crossing-specific assessment is included
in this report as an appendix.
Stream crossings were added to standard high probability areas, although in most cases stream crossings
would have been considered high probability. These areas totaled 3.7 miles in length (about 19 percent) or
about 45 acres. Survey standards suggest about 60 shovel test for a project of this size. Seventy were
excavated.
Seven previously unrecorded archaeological sites (41BP942-41BP948) were documented, and one site,
41BP811, was revisited and reassessed. Previously recorded site 41BP674, formally assessed as ineligible
for listings, was briefly examined, but not reassessed. Poorly documented site 41BP3, plotted outside but
near the right of way, was not encountered.
None of the recorded sites are assessed as eligible for formal designations as State Antiquities Landmarks
(SALs) or NRHP status. This is largely due to the preponderance of loose, deep sands, extreme
bioturbation and the resulting lack of vertical or horizontal integrity. Likewise, there is a complete
absence of organic material other than recent charcoal from brush and forest fires. Historical materials
were often found below prehistoric artifacts.
No artifacts were collected during the project; project records will be submitted to an accredited curation
facility.

123

Figure 46. Southern portion of project area with locations of archaeological sites, surveyed areas, and shovel tests.

124

Figure 47. Central portion of project area with locations of archaeological sites, surveyed areas, and shovel tests.

Figure 48. Northern portion of project area with locations of archaeological sites, surveyed areas, and shovel tests.

126

ENVIRONMENTAL BACKGROUND

The project area is within the East Central Texas Plains ecoregion, and crosses the Bastrop Lost Pines, the
Southern Post Oak Savanna, and the San Antonio Prairie as it proceeds from southwest to northeast
(Griffith et al. 2007). Much of the Lost Pines portion of the transmission line had been decimated by
recent forest fires (Figure 49). As the Southern Post Oak Savanna is approached the pine trees decrease in
frequency and are replaced by oak and hickory, sometimes with very dense underbrush, and by the time
the San Antonio Prairie is reached the trees decrease in density and a more open pasture is seen.
Soils are primarily Patilo-Demona-Silstid association sand and loamy fine sand, although the shift to the
San Antonio Prairie corresponds with Behring-Crockett-Heiden association loams, clay loams and clays
(Baker 1979). The sands correspond with the underlying Carizzo, Reklaw and Queen City sand and
sandstone formations, while the Cook Mountain clay formation probably accounts for the transition to the
loams and clays at the northeast portion of the project.
The line crosses a number of drainages, often near their heads, although the central portion crosses Alum
Creek and several of its tributaries. Alum Creek joins the Colorado River about 8 miles to the south. The
extreme northern portion of the line is within the Brazos river drainage system, although there are no
major associated streams.
The area is also notable for presence of the federally-listed Houston toad, which uses the soft sands for
hibernating and the numerous small pools for breeding.

Figure 49. Typical section of right of way. Note burned trees.

PREVIOUS INVESTIGATIONS
At the time of this report, there were 941 previously recorded archeological sites in Bastrop County. The
earliest archaeological investigations in the area appear to have been by Wilson (1930), who conducted
surveys in Travis, Bastrop, and Fayette Counties, though poor documentation of Wilsonâs findings has led
127

to problems relocating the sites that he discovered, as well as limited interpretation of his findings
(Nightengale and Moncure 1996). In 1953, The University of Texas sponsored excavations at 41BP1,
which contained two burials with Late Prehistoric artifacts (Campbell and Jelks 1953). In the 1960s, the
UT Austin Archeological Society conducted several excavations, including an excavation recorded by
Davis (1964), which involved another two burials with Late Prehistoric artifacts, and a variety of other
projects that involved primarily Archaic and Late Prehistoric encampments and lithic scatters. One
possible Paleoindian site, 41BP51, was excavated in this time period as well.
An avocational effort to document the Thunderbird site (41BP78) occurred in the countyâs northern
uplands (Duke 1977). The site appeared to have occupations ranging from the Archaic to the Late
Prehistoric (Nightengale and Moncure 1996).
Dillehay (1979) recorded a survey of an LCRA transmission line corridor that traversed parts of Fayette,
Caldwell, and Bastrop counties (Nightengale and Moncure 1996). Kenmotsu and Freeman (1980)
followed up and recorded eight prehistoric and three multicomponent sites in the countyâs southern
uplands. Brown and Kenmotsu (1980) reported on excavations at one of those sites, 41BP176, and
concluded that the site represented several intermittent occupations buried in a sandy terrace, with one
diagnostic artifact dating to the Late Prehistoric. Brown (1984) looked at another LCRA transmission line
corridor in Bastrop County, and found one prehistoric and one multicomponent site, neither of which
were felt to warrant avoidance or further work. Kotter (1980) surveyed a pipeline right of way for the Sim
Gideon Power Plant, and recorded two prehistoric sites, and again the sites were felt to not warrant
avoidance.
Nash et al. (1996) reported a survey by Espey, Huston &Associates of the T-150 Sim Gideon to
Smithville transmission line, and the area surveyed was approximately 9.43 miles, which is just over half
of the length of the nearby T-260 transmission line. The survey recorded four new sites and revisited four
previously recorded sites. EH&A found that all eight had prehistoric components, while three sites also
had historic components, and that four of the sites, 41BP427, 41BP428, 41BP465 and 41BP466, were felt
to be potentially eligible (of undetermined eligibility by todays terminology) for SAL or NHRP status. All
four of these sites were eventually ruled ineligible for NRHP status in 2005.
Robinson et al. (2001) describe a series of undertakings to complete a comprehensive cultural resources
survey within the modern boundaries of Camp Swift Army Base. Between a 1996 to 1997 survey
sponsored by the Adjutant Generalâs Department of Texas, and a 2000 resurvey of 28 sites and
geomorphological testing by the Center for Archaeological Research of the University of Texas at San
Antonio, it was concluded that there are 169 known sites within present day Camp Swift. Of these, one
site, 41BP138, is considered eligible for NRHP listing, 59 sites are considered âpotentially eligibleâ
(undetermined), and 106 sites were considered completely ineligible. It is worth noting that 41BP138 is a
multicomponent site consisting of a lithic scatter and the remains of a house and wine cellar from an early
Texas vineyard, and that the site was considered eligible for NRHP listing based on the historic vineyard
component, and not the prehistoric lithic scatter.
In subsequent years, LCRA Cultural Resources staff has conducted several brief surveys of transmission
lines in the area for varying transmission line maintenance projects. One of these (Hixson et al 2005)
128

included an attempt to relocate 41BP3, which is plotted just north of the transmission line near its north
end. Apparently the site was reported to archaeologists by artifact collectors, and is plotted in an
approximate location on the Sites Atlas. Shovel testing along the transmission line for the present project
failed to relocate the site, and its exact location remains unknown.

CULTURAL BACKGROUND
Prehistoric Background
The project area is within the Central Texas archaeological region as described variously by various
sources over the decades. The final version of this interim report will be within LCRAâs annual report of
investigations for 2016, which will have a substantial culture history section, and it will not be repeated
here.
Historic Background
Histories of several counties have been compiled by the LCRA staff for historic background information
(LCRA files). According to these records, Bastrop County was settled as early as 1804, when a fort was
established at the location of present day Bastrop, which was then a stop along the Nacogdoches-San
Antonio road. In 1827 Stephen F. Austin received a grant from the Spanish governor, and by 1830 the
town of Bastrop, named for the Baron de Bastrop, was settled. In 1837 the Republic of Texas established
the County of Bastrop. Cotton and lumber were primary industries, and by 1860 almost a third of the
population was slaves. Around 1870 industrial interests were expanding. By the turn of the century
railroads had helped to improve the economy, oil had been discovered, and a major brick manufacturing
plant was in place near Elgin. Camp Swift was established as a training center during World War II, and
in time housed a prisoner of war camp. By 1950 a diversified agricultural base was still the main
economic strength of the county. Agriculture has been Bastrop Countyâs mainstay until recently; as
nearby Austin has continued to grow, so has Bastrop. Many residents of Bastrop now commute on a daily
basis to the stateâs capital.
The majority of the project area is within the southern edge of the original Camp Swift, constructed in
1942 across some 50,000 acres (Robinson et al. 2001). The camp required condemnation of numerous
properties, and thousands of acres of land were cleared, which included the demolition of structures and
in some cases repurposing of building materials. It is telling that the 1936 Bastrop County highway map
illustrates dozens of structures near the project area, but post WWII maps show none at all. Following the
war the majority of the camp was declared to be surplus, and was sold back to various interests, and not
necessarily those who had been previously removed. The result is a greater-than-expected disconnect in
land tenancy, making connections to earlier landholdings less palpable.
In 2007, a final report was issued by the U.S. Army regarding evaluation and cost analysis of risk factors
associated with potential unexploded ordnance and other potentially hazardous materials in the former
Camp Swift (Parsons 2007). Included were map overlays showing various land uses during the operation
of the camp. Although the map legends are somewhat confusing, it appears that the portion of the camp
129

crossed by the transmission line was only lightly used, except for an artillery impact area towards the
northeast end.

RESEARCH DESIGN AND FIELD METHODS
The project was designed to identify and assess known and unknown archaeological sites that are present
within the project area and that might be adversely impacted. Assessments result in recommendations to
the State Historic Preservation Officer as to any individual siteâs eligibility for formal listing as an SAL
and in this case, nomination to the National Register of Historic Places.
Prior to initiating field work a map and file review was conducted to identify high probability areas.
These were based primarily upon topography, hydrology, and locations of structures on historical maps.
Field work consisted of pedestrian survey of high probability areas coupled with subsurface (shovel)
testing when appropriate. Shovel tests were 30-40 cm in diameter and excavated in 20 cm increments
with all fill passed through one-quarter inch screen. Soils across the project area allowed shovel tests to be
routinely excavated to depths of 100 cm or more, and were terminated when they were too deep to dig
further or when subsoils, usually sandy clay, were encountered (Figure 46).
Records included shovel test logs, field notes, field forms, photographs and photograph logs, and GPS
logs. A no-collection policy was maintained throughout the project.

Figure 50. Profiling a deep shovel test.

130

RESULTS
LCRA Cultural Resources staff conducted intensive cultural resources survey work on the T-260 line
from the Sim Gideon Power Plant to the Paige Split during several field visits from March 28th to April
7th, 2016. In the course of that work, seven new archaeological sites were recorded, and one previously
recorded site was retested and assessed. New sites consisted of 41BP942-41BP948. Sites 41BP94241BP946 and 41BP948 were all prehistoric, while previously recorded site 41BP811 and new site
41BP947 contained both prehistoric and historic components.

41BP942
Site 41BP942 sits on top of and off the edge of a high terrace to an unnamed creek drainage (Figure 56).
Soils are mapped as Padina fine sand over the Carrizo sand formation. The right of way is clear of all but
short grass and some short brushy vegetation, and it bordered by thick and tall pine growth to the
northwest, and pine growth that has been partially cleared for housing to the southeast. The site runs for
65 m along the right of way from the terrace edge, and extends across the 30 m right of way.
No cultural material was located on the lower terrace on either side of the drainage. Shovel testing
indicated that 41BP942 is a buried lithic scatter, though a few surface artifacts are present in the siteâs
northeastern portion. The subsurface material is primarily between 80 and 120 cm, and is sparse. Sandy
soils have likely allowed artifacts to descend over time. Material observed consisted primarily of burned
chert, hard and soft hammer flakes and flake fragments, and chert chips. While shovel test 12 did reveal a
small amount of glass and a pull tab, these recent materials are likely related to the occupied home
nearby.
Although a new transmission line structure is planned for placement on the edge of the terrace within the
site, staff feels that the impact of this structure in site 41BP942 would be minimal, due to the siteâs
condition. Because of poor stratification and apparent bioturbation and natural movement within the soils,
site 41BP942 holds very little research potential beyond its locational information, and is therefore
recommended as ineligible for formal SAL status within the right of way. Replacement of the
transmission line structure within the site be allowed to proceed without restrictions.

41BP943
Site 41BP943 sits on a rise straddled by two intermittent drainages (Figure 57). The slope is steeper to the
northeast than to the southwest. Soils are mapped as Padina fine sand of the Reklaw geologic formation.
The right of way is surrounded by primarily pine and post oak trees with short grass in the right of way.
While some of the pine trees are living, most are dead trunks left from the 2012 Bastrop fires. Water
draining along vehicle paths on the slope to the northeast has eroded a channel parallel to the transmission
line in the right of way. The site extends for approximately 115 m and across the 30 m right of way.

131

Site 41BP943 is a buried lithic scatter with some burned chert and quartzite. Cultural material is clearly
mixed, illustrated most clearly by the two diagnostic projectile points found in shovel tests 21 and 24.
Shovel test 21 revealed an Ensor dart point around 50 cmbs (Figure 51), while test 24 to the northeast
revealed a serrated Scallorn arrow point around 90 cmbs (Figure 52). In general, material appears to be
migrating down through the sandy soils towards a stable clay layer. For this reason, the origin of the
burned chert and quartzite present in shovel tests is questionable. While the central northeastern part of
the site seems to have the highest quantity of cultural material, consisting of debitage and burned rock, the
subsurface material abruptly ends at the sites periphery to the south, and decreases with distance to the
north. Both projectile points were unbroken, making the circumstances of their deposition curious. Site
41BP946, across the drainage to the northeast but at the same elevation, is similar in relative density,
though it lacks diagnostic artifacts.
Despite 41BP943âs diagnostic artifacts, severe bioturbation and soil mixing in the site have destroyed the
integrity of the sites deposits, and robbed it of its research value beyond locational data. It is therefore
recommended as ineligible for formal SAL status, and it is recommended that replacement of the
transmission line structure within the site be allowed to proceed without restrictions.

Figure 51. Ensor point from 41BP943.

Figure 52. Scallorn point from 41BP943.

132

41BP944
Site 41BP944 sits on the lower terrace to an intermittent drainage (Figure 57). The soils are mapped as
Padina fine sand over the Reklaw geologic formation. The right of way is clear of most vegetation, and
contains primarily short grass and wildflowers, while the right of way is surrounded by pine trees, some
living, but mostly dead trunks left from the 2012 Bastrop fires. Another drainage runs to the northeast of
the site, cutting in between this terrace surface and the higher terrace surface to the northeast. The lower
terrace top where the site is located is relatively flat, and the site extends for about 50 m back from the
terrace edge, and across the 30 m right of way.
Site 41BP944 was discovered when a shovel test at a new structure location recovered several chert chips.
Subsequent testing along the right of way in 20 m intervals resulted in another test positive for chert chips
and this time a tertiary flake and some miscellaneous chert shatter, followed by two negative tests.
The site appears to be a sparse lithic scatter overlooking the two drainages. A lack of temporally
diagnostic artifacts and stratification, as well as the extremely sparse nature of this lithic scatter give the
site little in the way of research value.. For these reasons, 41BP944 is recommended as ineligible for
formal SAL status. It is also recommended that replacement of the transmission line structure within the
site be allowed to proceed without any restrictions.

Site 41BP945
Site 41BP945 sits atop a high ridge in between several drainages (Figure 57). It is a high terrace to Alum
Creek 260 m to the east, as well as a terrace to a small intermittent drainage to the southwest, and perhaps
formerly a terrace to the drainage on the other side of 41BP944. This land from is eroding in all directions
but north. Soils are mapped as Padina fine sand over the Reklaw geologic formation. The right of way is
vegetated with short grass, wildflowers, dewberry thickets and a few prickly pears. The northwest edge of
the right of way is populated with dense pine, oak, post oak, and hickory growth, while the southeast side
contains the same vegetation but is less dense. The site extends for 175 m across the top and slightly
down each side of the ridge, and across the 30 m right of way.
Site 41BP945 was immediately apparent because of surface artifacts located in pocket gopher backdirt
piles and across the slope in both directions of the right of way. Shovel testing revealed that the densest
deposits of cultural material exist in the siteâs center, and that subsurface material decreases from there in
both directions. Material observed included a variety of soft and hard hammer flakes, a possible
hammerstone, and some burned chert and quartzite. Material is exposed on the slopes because the top of
the ridge is eroding away. The ridge actually appears to be eroding in all direction but north, and the area
to the north may be more stable than the portion of the site presented in the right of way. The site also sits
on the same elevation as 41BP943, and the two sites are similar in relative artifact density and
composition.
Heavy bioturbation and mixing in the sandy soils has unfortunately destroyed any stratigraphy that may
have existed in this site. Lack of temporally diagnostic artifacts as well leaves this site with little research
value beyond its locational data. Site 41BP945 is for these reasons recommended as ineligible for formal
133

SAL status within the ROW, and it is recommended that the replacement of the transmission line
structure within the site be allowed to proceed without restrictions.

Site 41BP946
Site 41BP946 sits on the broad sloping terrace to Alum Creek (Figure 57). The site is about 100 m from
the closest segment of Alum Creek, and 250 m from the part that crosses the right of way. Site 41BP945
sits on the higher terrace to the southwest of the site. Soils across most of the site are Sayers fine sandy
loam, but the southwestern portion of the site is mapped at Padina fine sand. The entire site sits on the
Reklaw geologic formation. Short grass, wildflowers, dewberry, and other brushy plants grow in the right
of way, while the northwestern edge of the right of way is populated with dense pine, oak, post oak, and
hickory growth, and the southeast side contains the same vegetation but is less dense, especially
approaching the creek. The site runs for about 160 m along the transmission line, and spans the 30 m right
of way.
Site 41BP946 was discovered when a couple of flake fragments were observed in gopher backdirt piles.
Shovel testing from the edge of the terrace above the floodplain (only a slight hump in the slope) to the
area preceding the slope of the higher terrace revealed hard and soft hammer flakes, chert chips, a
possible hammerstone, and burned chert. While the material observed closer to the edge of the lower
terrace is certainly an independent site, material in the southwestern portion of the site could represent
more redeposition of artifacts from 41BP945 on the deflating terrace ridge line above. For this reason,
shovel testing was halted in that section of the site, and the boundary was drawn to represent location of
surface material in that area. The site is interpreted as a lithic scatter with some burned chert.
Pocket gophers, deflation, and natural soil mixing have led to poor stratigraphy in 41BP946, and lack of
temporally diagnostic artifacts leave this site with little research value beyond its locational data. Site
41BP946 is thus recommended as ineligible for formal SAL status. While current plans do not call for a
structure to be placed within the boundary of the site, it is also recommended that should construction
plans change, any transmission line construction activities during the T-260 rebuild project be allowed to
proceed within the siteâs boundary without restrictions.

41BP947
Site 41BP947 is located on a southwest facing slope overlooking an unnamed tributary to Alum Creek
110 m to the southwest (Figure 58). While the site appears to be on the first terrace of the creek in that
direction, an artificial soil berm (which was the basis for the siteâs southwestern boundary) runs along
what appears to be the first terrace edge. The site sits in a cleared pasture on the slope, surrounded on the
northwestern edge of the right of way and on the sides of the pasture in all other directions by dense pine
growth. Post oak also makes an appearance in the vegetation surrounding the pasture, but oak, Ashejuniper, and mesquite trees populate the pasture in small clumps. Otherwise, the pasture is filled with
shortgrass and wildflowers. Soils are mapped as Padina fine sand over the Reklaw geological formation.

134

The site spans 160 m along the slope (southwest to northeast), and 95 m from the edge of the clearing
towards the opposing tree line across the pasture (northwest to southeast).
Site 41BP947 was discovered when an area of the right of way was surveyed for remains of a structure
shown directly in the path of the T-260 transmission line on an 1899 USGS map of the area. Pedestrian
survey and shovel testing revealed both historic glass and crockery and prehistoric chips and a flake in the
right of way. A chimney fall was spotted about 70 m from the right of way (Figure 53, and informal
investigation revealed a cut sandstone hearth base with an associated scatter of local red bricks and white
fire bricks, and a variety of historic refuse, including aqua glass, brown glass, blue glass, patinated
window pane glass, salt glazed crockery, several pieces of a cast iron stove, a metal handsaw blade, and
whiteware. A stone-lined well was also discovered some 13 m to the south in a clump of trees. Chert
flakes and chips were observed on the surface and in shovel tests in the right of way across the site, and
are the basis for the siteâs northern boundary. Shovel testing was not conducted in portions of the site
outside of the right of way.

Figure 53. Chimney fall outside right of way at 41BP947.

A subsequent search of deed records at the Bastrop County courthouse, as well as federal census records,
has revealed the following ownership history of the property. The property on which 41BP947 sits was
originally part of a 3,100 acre property granted to an S. S. Beasley by the Republic of Texas in 1838.
While no date was found, a later deed explains that a Franklin Lewis purchased a part of that 3,100 acres,
and in 1886, Franklin Lewisâs heirs split his property between two of his sons, Alexander and L.G. Lewis.
These two men then exchanged some parts of the property again in 1887, and the land held by L.G. Lewis
was listed at 127.99 acres, the same size as the modern property. L.G., along with his wife Ida and their
four children, are believed to have resided on the property. While the circumstances of L.G.âs demise are

135

unknown, his wife Ida is shown as the widowed head of the household on the 1900 census, and in 1902,
Ida sold the property to a John C. Porter.
John Porter, an English immigrant with two Texas-born daughters, is shown in the 1900 census as a
widower who owned a farm free of mortgage. According to census records, Johnâs daughter Grace
married, while Bertha was shown as single and working as a postmaster, and boarding in the town of
Paige. By 1922, Bertha Porter is shown as the property owner in a lease agreement for a county road,
though the 1930 census records show her as a boarder in San Angelo, and still unmarried.
In 1942, the property was purchased by the U.S. Government during acquisition of property for the Camp
Swift Army Base. In 1950, Bertha Porter purchased the land back from the government (minus the rights
to any fissionable materials). Ms. Porter sold the property to a Mr. and Mrs. Mack in 1952, and later that
year, the property was sold to a Bruno Ernst, the grandfather of the current property owner, Mrs. Susan K.
Prihoda. A phone conversation with Mrs. Prihoda confirmed that Mr. Ernst was her grandfather, and that
her family had never lived on the property, but used it for agricultural activities.
The approximate age of the artifacts at the site would suggest late nineteenth or early twentieth century
occupation. This indicates that the house was very likely built or maintained by L.G. and Ida Lewis. Mr.
Porter and his daughters had another farm at the time of purchase, and Ms. Bertha Porter seems to have
boarded in cities most of her life. While it is possible that one or more of the Porters resided on the
property for a time, it seems unlikely that they built the structure in question.
The portion of 41BP947 that is contained in the right of way is sparse and lacking stratification. Heavy
bioturbation and natural movement within the sandy soils have also mixed the historic and prehistoric
components of the site. The prehistoric component is a sparse lithic scatter across the slope, and lacks
diagnostics and is mixed with the historic refuse. For this reason, 41BP947 is recommended as ineligible
for formal SAL status within the right of way, and it is recommended that replacement of the transmission
line structure in the site be allowed to proceed without restrictions.

41BP948
Site 41BP948 is located on a low terrace rise over a 700-m-wide floodplain formed by a tributary to Alum
Creek (Figure 59). The tributary has been dammed and there is a substantial private lake present. The site
is as far as 400 m from the stream channel. Soil are mapped as Padina fine sand over the Queen City sand
formation. The easement is cleared of vegetation other than various grasses, and is surrounded by post
oak woods to the south, while to the north the floodplain is largely open pasture.
The site was found while prospecting for a potential historic house seen on old maps in the vicinity of the
right of way. Shovel test 62 was excavated through clearly recent burned soil with charcoal which then
became pale brown sand. At about 80 cm fire-cracked sandstone was encountered. About 10 pieces of
rock ranging in size from about 5 to 15 cm and weighing a total of 1.3 km was recovered (Figure 54). A
single chip was also seen. These materials were immediately above wet, yellowish mottled sandy clay.
Test 63 was placed about 25 m to the southwest. A burned biface thinning flake was recovered from
136

between 60 and 80 cm and a potlid from the level below. The test was excavated to a depth of 110 cm
where orange clay mottling began.
Because no support structures are planned for the immediate area, shovel tests 64 and 65 were placed
about 140 m southwest and northeast, respectively, at proposed new structure locations. Shovel test 64
encountered sand to about 50 cm where yellow and orange mottled sandy clay was seen. Shove test 65
recovered one split chert cobble and a fire-cracked chert pebble from between 60 and 80 cm. These
materials are not conclusively cultural in origin. Mottled clay was once again present below sand.
Site 41BP948 may represent an open camp, however it is doubtful that the potential feature material is on
an old land surface. It is more probable that these materials were displaced vertically and settled at a
relatively steady rate until they rested upon the dense clay layer. The site is defined as extending to test 65
and ending somewhere between test 63 and 64, a distance of about 250 m. Because there will be no direct
impacts to any feature material, no further investigation was conducted. Given the nature of the remainder
of the sites that were documented, which consistently exhibit a lack of integrity due to extreme
bioturbation in the loose sandy soils, site 41BP948 is recommended as ineligible for formal SAL status
within the right of way.

Figure 54. Fire-cracked rock from 41BP948.

41BP811
Site 41BP81 was recorded in 2006 during an LCRA transmission line right of way maintenance project.
At the time, a single shovel test recovered moderate amounts of fire-cracked rock and lithic debitage, and
the medial section of a dart pint was seen within a disturbed area. The site was recommended as having
undetermined SAL status.
137

The site is located on the outside bend of a deeply (c. 10 m) incised tributary of Alum Creek (Figure 58).
Although the transmission line crosses over the creek, the transmission line access follows the high bank
of the creek, so it does not restrict travel. Soils are mapped as Whitesboro clay loam, although soils
encountered at the site were clearly sand. The underlying geology is the Eocene-aged Reklaw formation.
The cutbank and the numerous rodent burrows and other exposures were examined for cultural material.
All that was seen was two or three pieces of fire-cracked rock, of uncertain origin, along the creek cut.
Three shovel test (47-49) were excavated, the first on the edge of the right of way near the top of a knoll
from which it previously had been hypothesized that prehistoric cultural material may have been
emanating from. It was excavated through 55 cm of sand over reddish sandy clay. A single small (5 cm)
burned rock was recovered from between 40 and 60 cm. The next test was placed immediately downslope
in the centerline of the easement, relatively near where the original 2006 test had been. A solarized bottle
neck was recovered from between 40 and 60 cm, and a crude, thick bifacially worked chert chunk was
recovered from 60 to 80 cm. This test was excavated through sand to a depth of 85 cm where clay was
encountered. The final test was placed on the terrace edge adjacent to the highest portion of the cutbank.
This test recovered a chip from 0-40 cm, two pieces of chert shatter from 40-80 cm, a flake and a chip
from 80-100, cm, and a live .30-06 round from 100-120 cm. The shell was stamped with marks
identifying it as produced in 1942 at the Des Moines Ordnance Plant. It was probably a result of military
activities at Camp Swift. The tests and other observations indicate the site is about 75 m in diameter.
Site 41BP811 did not live up to previous expectations. Prehistoric cultural material is minimal and mixed
with historical materials. The loose sands are not conducive to horizontal or vertical integrity. The site is
assessed as ineligible for formal SAL status within the right of way.

140

SUMMARY AND RECOMMENDATIONS
None of the sites known to be within the T-260 corridor are recommended as eligible for state or federal
level listings. Their presence within loose, sandy, turbated soils minimizes any meaningful research
potential. There was no organic preservation noted, and historical artifacts were often located below
prehistoric artifacts. Prehistoric sites were located on ridges or lobes overlooking streams, and at times
quite distant from them. Most were just a few chips and flake fragments along with fire-cracked chert or
quartzite of inconclusive origin. It is therefore recommended the project be allowed to proceed as
described, with the understanding that emergency discovery procedure will be in place.
As an exercise, the TxDOT-sponsored HPALM model (Abbot 2013; Abbot and Pletka n.d.) was placed in
the project GIS and then joined with the site data. This was a post-survey experiment, and the HPALM
was not used for predictive modeling.
According to the HPALM, a number of GIS-linked variables can be layered and weighted to create an
integrity-based model that provides an opportunity to focus survey efforts on areas more likely to contain
significant, in the regulatory sense, prehistoric archaeological sites. While the author (Abbott)
acknowledges relatively severe limitations for the model in the Austin area, he argues that it is
âreasonably effective at identifying areas where eligible sites are unlikely to occurâ (Abbot and Pletka
2013:8). Other limitations include discounting sites on TPWD lands, due to a different significance
threshold associated with agencies tasked with preservation more than development. Given this, and other
constraints, the testing site population was whittled from some 22,000 sites to 68 eligible across the 11county study area.
The model, however, is focused on landform integrity, and classifies landscapes into 10 separate
categories, based on the potential for deep (> 1 m) and shallow (< 1 m) deposits (Table 6). The
âmoderateâ and âhighâ potentials (scores 5 and 9) are assumed here to mean for both shallow and deep
integrity. Table 7 illustrates the sites in the present project area and corresponding HPALM scores

Nine of the 10 sites have some portion in areas with HPALM scores of 5, two sites in areas with HPALM
score of 9, two in areas of negligible potential (scores of 1 or 2) and one each with scores of 3 or 4.
Remembering that the model does not explicitly predict the presence or absence of sites, but rather the
potential for there to be intact artifact-bearing deposits, the model does not seem to fare particularly well.
It might be expected that at least some of the sites with scores of 5 would have somewhat better
preservation than seen. The two sites that overlap areas with scores of 9 are not appreciably different in
setting or soils than the remainder (although 41BP811 is across a pronounced topographic break).
This small sample supports the provisos and cautions expressed in the HPALM documents, and
traditional behavioral modeling may be a better prospecting tool in this area.
