APPENDIX 1

Stream Crossings along the Sim Gideon-to-Paige Split portion of the T-260 Sim Gideon-toElgin Switch T-260 Rebuild Project
DRAFT Cultural Resource Assessment

Stream crossings and potential adjacent wetlands, where it is expected some sort of fill or other crossing
such as mats will be introduced to allow vehicular movement down the right of way, were assessed
separately from high probability areas, although in many cases these areas coincided. The crossing
locations were obtained from LCRA construction staff, then labeled for field assessment. Eleven
crossings were labeled 0-10, starting from near Sim Gideon and moving sequentially to near Paige Split
(Figure 60; Figure 61). Three additional potential crossings were identified in the field (7a, 11, and Alum
Creek) and were also assessed.
There is an existing access road under the entirety of the transmission line, and the existing streams have
been crossed regularly. At these locations there is typically a highly eroded road bed, often created by
past bulldozing, resulting in excellent visibility. The area between a crossing and the ordinary high water
mark, as evidenced by flood debris and/or topographic relief, was inspected visually, photographed, and if
warranted, shovel tested. In some cases, crossings are not mapped as streams on USGS quads, and are
instead side drainages that have become gullied, and, in the case of Crossing 7a, appear to be the result of
a recent, major flooding event.
The crossings are discussed individually, and are not necessarily jurisdictional waterways, nor are all
within mapped Houston toad habitat. The overall cultural resources survey primarily located prehistoric
sites on high sandy terraces overlooking streams. Apparently, streams in this area are too flood-prone to
support long term human occupation or preserve evidence of land use. None of the crossings was assessed
as potentially impacting significant (National Register of Historic Places or State Antiquities Codeeligible) sites.
Notably, the first mapped drainage out of Sim Gideon was not identified as requiring a crossing. This area
was examined on foot and determined to have a low probability for cultural resources. No shovel tests
were excavated.
Following a field assessment by consulting firm Blanton and Associates for delineation of jurisdictional
waters, three additional locations were identified. These are addressed at the end of this appendix and are
referred to as Waters A, B and C. While all but C were crossed during the field assessment, they were not
specifically addressed as potential crossings, and all three were in low probability areas for archaeological
sites.

145

Crossing 0
Crossing 0 is a north-trending erosional feature that may eventually join a short, unnamed tributary to
Lake Bastrop (Figure 62). The access road southwest of the crossing crosses a long sloping hillside with
excellent visibility within the road cut. On the northeast side there is a flat bench about 50 m from the
crossing. Shovel tests 3 and 4 were placed on this bench after two possible flakes and a few pieces of firecracked quartzite were observed on the ground surface. Both tests were excavated to a depth of 100 cm.
No artifacts were recovered. Because no cultural material was found in either test and because the flakes
were not conclusively cultural in origin, the area was not given a site designation. This crossing is
assessed as having no potential to impact significant cultural resource sites.

Crossing 1
Crossing 1 is a north-trending erosional feature about 65 m south of the mapped head of the drainage
mentioned above for Crossing 0 (Figure 63). The crossing is gently sloped on the northeast side and has a
more pronounced rise to the southwest. Visibility was variably excellent and no artifacts were observed.
Shovel test 5 was placed on the most promising portion of the landform, about 30 m southwest of the
lowest portion of the drainage. It was excavated through loamy sand to a depth of about 25 cm before a
brownish-yellow sandy clay was encountered. No artifacts were present. This crossing is assessed as
having no potential to impact significant cultural resource sites.

Crossing 2
Crossing 2 is across the head of an unnamed south-trending tributary of Alum Creek (Figure 64). To the
southwest the area is low-lying and of generally low probability for cultural resources. The northeast side
is somewhat more stable, with low terraces seemingly more conducive for intact archaeological sites.
Shovel test 8 was placed on the southwest side of the crossing about 30 m from its mapped intersection. It
was excavated to a depth of about 60 cm before sandy clay was encountered. Shovel test 9 was placed on
the edge of the first stable terrace above the stream (Figure 65). It was excavated to a depth of about 40
cm where gray and strong-brown sandy clay was encountered. Testing continued up the slope to the
northeast (tests 9, 10, 11) to where 41BP942 (discussed above) is located. Site 41BP942 is assessed as not
eligible for formal SAL or NR status and will not be impacted by the crossing. This crossing is assessed
as having no potential to impact significant cultural resource sites.

Crossing 3
Crossing 3 is at a south-trending drainage of Alum Creek (Figure 66; Figure 65). It is generally low-lying,
with eroded areas providing excellent visibility. Shovel tests (14 and 15) were placed near the stream at
higher elevations where replacement structures are located, about 140 m equidistant on either side. Both
of these tests were negative for cultural material. This crossing is consider fully investigated and as
having no potential to impact significant cultural resource sites.
146

Crossing 4
Crossing 4 is a highly eroded west-trending side drainage of the mapped stream described at Crossing 3
(Figure 67). The drainage has cut through the ground to underlying clay. Shovel test 17 was placed on the
southwest side of the drainage where there was a suggestion of a stable terrace. It encountered mottled
clay at about 50 cm. Due to the visibility and relatively steep slope on the northeast side no shovel tests
were attempted. This crossing is assessed as having no potential to impact significant cultural resource
sites.

Crossing 5
Crossing 5 is located at the confluence of south-trending Price Creek and a short unnamed tributary
(Figure 68). This is a highly eroded landform with excellent visibility. Price Creek fronts previously
recorded prehistoric archaeological site 41BP674 (discussed above and determined by THC to be
ineligible for NR status) to the northeast. No additional testing was attempted at this site. On the
southwest side three tests (18, 19, and 20) were placed in area of apparent stability. Test 18, located about
150 m from the crossing, resulted in recovery of two pieces of burned, non-cultural chert. The remaining
two tests were negative for cultural material and encountered sandy clay at depths of about 80 cm. This
crossing is assessed as having no potential to impact significant cultural resource sites.

Crossing 6
Crossing 6 is located across the low-lying gullied head of an unnamed south-trending tributary to Alum
Creek (Figure 69). It will likely only require mats for crossing. This was clearly an area of low probability
for archaeological sites, and no shovel tests were attempted. Site 41BP943 was recorded on higher ground
about 110 m to the northeast. This site is assessed as ineligible for formal SAL or NR status and will not
be impacted by any crossing activities. This crossing is assessed as having no potential to impact
significant cultural resource sites.

Crossing 7.
Crossing 7 is across a relatively substantial south-trending tributary of Alum Creek, although in the field
it was observed as probably crossable by mats. Shovel test 25 was placed on an apparent stable terrace on
the southwest side of the stream (Figure 70). It was excavated through 130 cm of strong brown sand and
encountered the water table at 105 cm. There was no recovery. Due to the sloping landform and excellent
visibility in erosional features, the first test to the northeast was placed about 90 m from the stream, where
prehistoric site 41BP944 (discussed above) was recorded. Site 41BP944 is considered ineligible for
formal SAL or NR status and will not be impacted by crossing activities. This crossing is assessed as
having no potential to impact significant cultural resource sites.

147

Crossing 7a.
Crossing 7a is an unexpected and unmapped south-trending tributary to Alum Creek, apparently formed
by a recent flood event, possibly due to blockage of the stream to the east (Crossing 8). A stratigraphic
profile in the bank indicates a lack of soil development (Figure 72). No artifacts were seen in the cuts or
any other surface exposures. Shovel tests 42 and 4 were placed about 45 m northeast and 25 m southwest,
respectively. Both encountered clay at depths below 50 cm. No artifacts were encountered. This crossing
is assessed as having no potential to impact significant cultural resource sites.

Crossing 8
Crossing 8 is a mapped south-trending drainage of Alum Creek that may not be crossable (Figure 73).
Shovel test 38 was placed on the high bank on the northeast side, about 20 m from the stream (Figure 74).
It encountered sandy clay at a depth of about 40 cm. Shovel test 45 was placed about 70 m southwest of
the stream (the low side). This test encountered mottled brown sand below 45 cm. Test 39 (not illustrated)
was placed about 95 m northeast of the stream and encountered red clay below 20 cm. No artifacts were
found in any of the tests. This crossing is assessed as having no potential to impact significant cultural
resource sites.

Crossing 9
Crossing 9 is across a broad, low-lying drainage that meanders in a southeasterly direction towards Alum
Creek. This area may be crossable by mats. Shovel tests 51 and 52 were placed as near the crossing as
topography allowed (25 m to the southwest and 45 m to the northeast, respectively). Test 51 encountered
gleyed sandy clay below 60 cm, and test 52 encountered heavily mottled sand below 20 cm. No artifacts
were recovered or otherwise observed. This crossing is assessed as having no potential to impact
significant cultural resource sites.

Crossing 10
Crossing 10 is across a broad, low-lying west-trending stream that feds a large artificial lake which is part
of the Alum Creek drainage system (Figure 75). An unmapped smaller drainage is located about 30 m to
the southwest and has the appearance of a wetland. The existing road crossing the stream indicates
shallow soils over clay, and cow trails and rodent backdirt piles showed no artifacts. This area may be
crossable by using mats. Although this area is of low probability for intact cultural resources, a single
shovel test (67) was placed between the potential wetland about 10 m southwest of the mapped stream.
The test encountered saturated, mottled and striated flood deposits and was terminated at 50 cm in dark
brown clay. No artifacts were encountered. Test 66 was placed about 100 m northeast of the stream on the
terrace above the floodplain at the location of a new support structure. It was excavated to a depth of 120
cm through brown sand. Five pieces of chert shatter were seen between 40 and 100 cm, although none
were clearly of cultural origin. Notably, a piece of concrete, almost certainly from the existing lattice
148

tower at that location, was also recovered from between 80 and 100 cm. Test 65 was placed about 200 m
to the southwest, where the broad floodplain rises to the next terrace edge. It encountered potential
cultural material (a split cobble and fire-fractured pebble) in sand at about 70 cm. Below this was mottled
yellow and orange sandy clay. This area will not be impacted by crossing activities. This crossing is
assessed as having no potential to impact significant cultural resource sites.

Crossing 11
Crossing 11 is a well-defined but shallow side drainage that feeds a north-trending tributary to Springs
Creek (Figure 76). It is not mapped as a stream and may be crossable by mats. It is in the vicinity of
poorly-defined 41BP3 (see Previous Investigations section). Shovel tests 69-70 were placed on either side
of the drainage. Test 68 was placed about 30 m south of the crossing near the existing lattice structure.
One possible flake or core fragment was found between 20 and 40 cm in gravelly, brown sandy loam over
strongly mottled clay encountered at about 50 cm. Test 69 was placed about 20 m to the northeast of the
drainage and encountered black clay over strongly mottled orange and yellow clay with cobbles at about
25 cm. Given the possible positive recovery from test 68, test 70 was placed about 30 m further to the
southwest. It was excavated through 40 cm of brown loamy sand before sandy orange clay was seen. This
crossing is assessed as having no potential to impact significant cultural resource sites.

Alum Creek
The transmission line crosses Alum Creek where a side flood channel (possibly an old oxbow) joins the
main channel, forming an island on which a structure is located (Figure 77). This area is not identified as
a formal crossing, but may require matting to remove and possibly replace the existing structure. The
vicinity of the stream channel is low-lying and clearly flood prone, and therefore has a very low
probability of containing intact cultural materials. Shovel test 40 was placed about 70 m northeast of the
side channel. It encountered sandy clay below about 70 cm. Shovel test 37 was placed about 75 m to the
southwest of the main creek channel (Figure 78). It was located on a sideslope overlooking a mapped
âmarsh or swampâ where a few pieces of fire-cracked quartzite were observed, but nothing definitively
cultural. The adjacent access road exposures and numerous rodent burrows did not evidence any cultural
material, and the low-lying landform is not conducive to site preservation. This crossing is assessed as
having no potential to impact significant cultural resource sites.

Waters A
This is an apparent gully on a side slope southwest of Crossing 2 (Figure 79). It received windshield
survey during the assessment of Crossing 2 (see above) and is within 100 m of shovel test 8. Furthermore,
no crossings will be needed at this location. There will be no impacts to potential significant cultural
resource sites at this location.

149

Waters B
This is an apparent side drainage to the drainage described for Crossing 10 (Figure 80). It is an area
determined to be of low probability for cultural materials, although it received windshield survey. It is
within 120 m of shovel test 60. There will be no need for a crossing at this location. There will be no
impacts to potential significant cultural resource sites at this location.

Waters C
This is a large pond at the far northeastern portion of the project area (Figure 81). No cultural resource
field assessments were conducted in this low probability area. Furthermore, there will be no crossings of
this pond. There will be no impacts to potential significant cultural resource sites at this location.

Project 15.16: Intensive Cultural Resources Survey of LCRAâs Proposed Blewett Electrical
Substation Relocation Project, Uvalde County, Texas

By Andrew F. Malof
Principal Investigator

TAC Permit 7531
Interim Report 4.16
Lower Colorado River Authority
Real Estate Services
April, 2016

163

MANAGEMENT SUMMARY
LCRA Transmission Services Corporation (TSC) proposes to construct a new electrical substation and
access road on approximately 11.71 acres in southwestern Uvalde County (Figure 82). The substation will
be located adjacent to existing LCRA transmission line T-570. The substation will be accessed by a short
section of new road and by widening an existing private ranch road that follows the transmission line for
about 1.2 km before entering the substation site. Additionally, there will be a 2-3-foot-deep narrow trench
within the transmission line easement that will be used to route a fiber optic line from the highway to the
new substation site. This substation is a redesign and relocation of one that was proposed in 2015 (Malof
2015).
A cultural resources assessment consisting of a map and file review followed by field survey was
conducted by LCRA Cultural Resources staff on April 13 and 14, 2016. Three previously unrecorded
prehistoric sites, Sites 1-3, were documented (Figure 83). All three are lithic scatters with fire-cracked
rock of uncertain origin. Due in large part to a highly disturbed landscape, only one small portion of
41UV501 is considered to possibly have interpretable remains, in the form of a burned rock zone found in
a shovel test at a depth of about 50 cm. However, a lack of organic material or tools of any type severely
limit the research utility. Therefore, none of the sites are recommended as being eligible for formal State
Antiquities Landmark status within the project area.
The project was conducted under the terms of LCRAâs 2016 blanket permit 7531 in accordance with the
Memorandum of Understanding between LCRA and the Texas Historical Commission, which
incorporates Council of Texas Archeologists survey standards. This interim report will be presented in
final form within LCRAâs annual report of cultural resource investigations for 2016. There is no known
or anticipated federal funding or permitting for this project.
No artifacts were collected during the project; project records will be submitted to an accredited curation
facility. It is recommended that the project be allowed to proceed.

NATURAL ENVIRONMENT
The project area is located within the Texas-Tamaulipan Thrornscrub portion of the Tamaulipas-Texas
Semiarid Plain, which is part of the Southern Texas Plains (Griffith et al. 2007). More specifically, it is
described as interior plains and hills with xerophytic shrub and oak forest within an ecotone formed by
the Chihuahuan Desert, Tamaulipan thornscrub and subtropical woodlands and coastal grasslands. Oncewidespread grasslands have been replaced by scrub due to grazing, fire suppression and drought. Much of
the land has been used historically for grazing.
Geologically, the project area is within the Anacacho limestone formation, a Late Cretaceous area of
limestone and marl covered with Quaternary colluvium, alluvial gravel, and sand. Holocene deposits are
not mapped in the project area (Stoesser et al. 2015). Soils are mapped as undulating Olmos and Ector

164

Figure 82. Project area in red on Cline USGS quad.

165

Figure 83. Aerial imagery with project area, locations of archaeological sites and shovel tests.

166

series at the substation location, and Uvalde silty clay loam along the northern half of the access road, and
there is a small area of Valera silty clay (Soil Survey Staff 2015). Ector soils are very shallow stony soils
formed in loamy residuum, and encounter limestone bedrock at a depth of about 45 cm. The undivided
Olmos soils are very similar but tend to be found over caliche. Uvalde soils are very deep soils formed on
terraces on piedmont alluvial plains, although the A horizon is limited to about 40 cm, resting over
successive Bk horizons. Valera soils formed in clayey slope alluvium derived from limestone.
Topographically, the substation site is 200 m from the mapped bank of Turkey Creek, part of a major
stream system eventually feeding the Nueces River. A contour dip corresponds with a mapped geologic
fault, with the substation on the downthrust side, suggestive of groundwater availability. Anecdotally,
springs are present in the area.
Observed conditions showed alternating areas of shallow stony soils (Ector/Olmos) with largely purple
sage cover in relatively upland settings, with Uvalde silty clay loam covered with dense secondary
mesquite growth (Figure 84). Numerous bulldozer push piles are present, and the majority of the
transmission line corridor is shared by an improved, caliche-based road that is intensively used by haul
trucks from the nearby asphalt mines.

Figure 84. View from northwestern portion of substation looking southeast. Note how purple sage
transitions to mesquite woods.

PREVIOUS INVESTIGATIONS
While there are nearly 500 recorded archaeological sites in Uvalde County, very few have been recorded
in the vicinity of the project area. The nearest, 41UV450, is less than 1,000 m to the south at the Turkey
167

Creek crossing of Hwy 2022. The site is described as a lithic procurement locale where the raw material
benefitted greatly from heat treatment. A bit further to the south is 41UV129, an upland exposure of
limestone bedrock with an anthropomorphic petroglyph. Some 8 km to the east are sites 41UV6 and
41UV36, both along the headwaters of a minor tributary of the Nueces River. Site 41UV6 consisted of a
few crude bifaces and other minimally worked tools found at the bottom of a dry watering hole. Site
41UV36 was similar, with a few crude tools in Uvalde gravels.
A cultural resources survey was conducted by Hicks and Company for an LCRA transmission line
running between Del Rio and Uvalde, which included the section that will tie in to the proposed
substation (Miller at al. 2003). The present project area was considered to be low probability and was not
assessed. The survey resulted in the recording of 41UV438 at the Nueces River to the east, and 41UV439
and 41UV440 along Turkey Creek to the northwest. Site 41UV438 was a low density lithic scatter with
possible burned rocks and an apparent hearth. Shovel testing was inconclusive for portions of the site, and
it is of undetermined eligibility status. Sites 41UV439 and 41UV440 were both early twentieth century
trash scatters, and were determined to be ineligible for formal SAL status.
The previous iteration of the Blewett substation was located about one mile to the southwest along the
same transmission line. This project, which was never initiated, resulted in the recording of 41UV499,
which had a few chert artifacts including unifaces, occasional fire-cracked rock, and a historic trash
scatter. The trash was apparently associated with the former town of Blewett, and according to the
landowner, an actual town dump. The site was assessed as ineligible for SAL status Malof 2015).

CULTURAL BACKGROUND
The project area is within the South Texas archaeological region, near its juncture with the Lower Pecos
region (Hester 1995). A regional chronology has been difficult to attain, given the largely surficial nature
of the data and lack of vertical stratification. In general though, the prehistoric cultural chronology
follows that of Central Texas and the Lower Pecos, with Paleoindian through Late Prehistoric and
Protohistoric sites present. This information is covered in detail within the final report that this interim
report will be submitted with.
Uvalde County was named for the Spaniard Juan de Ugalde, who fought Indians in northern Mexico and
Texas in the late eighteenth century (Ochoa 2015a; Starnes 2015). The county was largely unoccupied
until the 1840s, when Fort Inge was established and the road between San Antonio and the Rio Grande
became well-traveled. Indian depredations remained common through the 1850s, and a Wild West
mentality was common throughout the Civil War and reconstruction. Ranching remained the primary
means of making a living, although successful irrigated farms began appearing. Mining of asphalt was
another important industry, making use of bituminous limestone. The county seat was home to John
Nance Garner, who served two terms as vice president to Franklin Roosevelt. Uvalde County has had a
very high percentage of Mexican-Americans, and civil rights issues came to a forefront in the early 1970s.
Ranching, farming and mining remain principal economic drivers.

168

The former community of Blewett is just south of the project area. Blewett was originally known as
Carbonville, probably a company town of the Lathe Carbon Company that in 1888 attempted to extract
bitumen for paint and varnish products (Ochoa 2015b). This unsuccessful operation was followed, in
1912, by an operation that developed limestone asphalt, and was organized by John Blewett Smyth. Also
known as Mine No. 2, Blewett peaked in production in 1927, and by 1940 was in decline. Despite this,
the population in the area may have been as high as 500 when the mines were fully operational.

RESEARCH DESIGN AND FIELD METHODS
The project was designed to identify any archaeological resources present within the project area and
assess their potential for listing as State Antiquities Landmarks. The assessment consisted of a map and
file review followed by a formal field survey. The project area was transected by pedestrian survey
coupled with shovel testing. Twenty-seven shovel tests were excavated, which exceeds the number
suggested for a project of this size. Records included GPS and shovel test logs, photographs and field
notes.
RESULTS OF INVESTIGATIONS

Three sites were recorded, all on relatively low ground near swales that act as minor tributaries to Turkey
Creek. Higher elevations to the northeast and in the central portions of the project area were covered with
shallow, rocky soils, and no sites were recorded in those settings (Figure 85).

1,000 1,050 1,100 1,150 1,200 1,250

Vertical scale in ft, horizontal scale in m

Figure 85. Profile view of project area derived from DEM imagery. Sites 41UV501â41UV503 are
illustrated from left to right.
41UV501
Site 41UV501 is located on the southeastern portion of the substation site, primarily on the southern side
of a low draw that drains toward Turkey Creek, which is about 200 m to the southwest. The sites is about
100 m in diameter. It is within Uvalde silty clay loam that covers the mesquite-filled portion of the
substation site (Figure 86). The site is defined by 15 shovel tests, eight of which (1, 2, 4, 6, 9, 12, 13, 14)
were positive for cultural material. Recovery was minimal (e.g. a single chert chip) to moderate, and in
the case of test 12, the test was halted when possible feature material (fire-cracked limestone) was
encountered. About 17 pieces weighing between 2 and 3 kg were recovered, and more was seen within
the edge and below the bottom of the test. A few pieces of chert debitage were also observed. The
169

remainder of the positive tests had no suggestion of intact cultural materials and were rarely deeper than
50 cm before dense gravels were struck. Notably, no tools or organic material was observed in any of the
shovel tests, other than a few Rabdotus and Polygyra species snails.
Aerial imagery from 2004 shows the site area as heavily wooded in 2004, then largely cleared by 2010,
and presently in state of regrowth. The present road used by heavy trucks was completed sometime
between 2010 and 2012.
The site is interpreted as a largely diffuse and mostly heavily disturbed portion of a prehistoric campsite
that is probably denser to southwest, closer to Turkey Creek. Overall, any research potential appears to be
low within the project area. Site 41UV501 is recommended as ineligible for formal SAL status, although
the area around shovel test 12 will have no direct construction-related impacts.

Figure 86. Site 41UV501, general environment, looking northeast
41UV502
Site 41UV502 is located on the sloping southeastern side of the high stony divide between the substation
site and the highway. It is separated from 41UV503 by a low, short draw that drains into Turkey Creek,
which is about 230 m southwest of the right of way. The site is defined by two shovel tests (18, 22)
located about 250 m apart. It is equally within Uvalde silty clay loam to the southeast and Valera silty
clay to the northwest. The site crosses and then is almost entirely within the road, as well as a large, road
base-covered turnout (Figure 87). The northwestern portion of the site terminates in the shallow, rocky
soils of the upland divide.

170

Shovel test 19, located on the lower portion of the site, recovered a single chert chip in the upper 20 cm,
then two 8-cm fire-cracked rocks in the next 20 cm. Shovel test 22, on the far side of the road and
turnaround, found seven 10-cm fire-cracked rocks between 20 and 40 cm, then a single 10-cm firecracked rock and a piece of chert shatter in the next 20 cm.
The site is interpreted as a minor lithic scatter with some burned rock of questionable origin. There was
no structure associated with the rock, in other words, no appearance of an intact feature, and its presence
may be due to colluvial redeposition from the higher elevation. The site is recommended as ineligible for
formal designation within the project area.

Figure 87. Central portion of 41UV502 looking northeast toward hill crest.
41UV503
Site 41UV503 is immediately to the southeast of 41UV502, on the opposite side of the low drainage
(Figure 88). Soils are mapped as Uvalde silty clay loam. The site is defined by three positive shovel tests
(16, 18, 21) that span about 90 meters. A road bisects the site, which is partially within open grassland
and partially within dense mesquite scrub.
The site was identified by a light surface scatter of chert debitage, including at least three pieces of a
similar, non-patinated white chert, as well as sparsely scattered fire-cracked rock. These materials were
on an open, relatively sandy patch of ground. Shovel test 16, which was placed at this location, was
excavated to 80 cm and recovered a total of 10 pieces of debitage and two fire-cracked rocks. A test to the
southeast was negative. Test 18 recovered a single hard-hammer thinning flake from 20-40 cm, and two
10-cm fire-cracked rocks from 40-60 cm. Test 21, nearest the draw, recovered seven 10-cm fire-cracked
rocks from 20-40 cm, and a single 10-cm fire-cracked rock and piece of chert shatter from the next 20 cm.
171

As with the majority of the burned rocks from the project area, there was no suggestion of an intact
feature, and its origin is questionable. 41UV503 is recommended as ineligible within the project area.

Figure 88. Central portion of 41UV502 looking southwest across drainage near transmission structure at
41UV503.

Discussion
The three archaeological sites that were recorded are very similar, consisting of small amounts of
chipping debris and incidental shatter, as well as a relative abundance of fire-cracked limestone. The
burned rocks are also commonly seen across the surface, where there is enough visibility. Repeated land
clearing events, as evidenced by numerous bulldozer pushpiles and probably accomplished by chaining
and root-plowing at various times, has severely impacted the majority of the project area. The origin of
the fire-cracked rock is unknown, and could be a mixture of prehistoric and historical events. Its
distribution, with the possible exception of shovel test 12 at 41UV501, gives no indication of intact
cultural deposits.
This type of environment is one where chances are good that a close enough examination of any given
area would result in identifying prehistoric or potential prehistoric material. It is telling the higher, stony
portions of the project area did not produce enough artifacts to warrant site designations. The presence of
burned rocks could not be quantified as being of prehistoric origin.
The sites do, however, point to the very likely probability of intact, denser sites closer to the creek, which
appears consistently well-watered, based on various aerial images, as well as observation from the road

172

crossing south of the project area. The challenge would be in finding any pockets of undisturbed soil in
this highly dynamic landscape.

SUMMARY AND RECOMMENDATIONS
A cultural resources assessment of LCRAâs proposed Blewett Substation and access road project resulted
in the recording of three prehistoric archaeological sites, 41UV501, 41UV502, and 41UV503. With the
possible exception of shovel test 12 at 41UV501, none exhibit integrity or diversity of materials that
would warrant further work. The potential feature at shovel test 12, in the opinion of the principal
investigator, does not warrant consideration as a formal State Antiquities Landmark. The area, however,
will be avoided by construction impacts.
LCRA therefore recommends the project be allowed to proceed as described, with the understanding that
emergency discovery protocols will be in place.

Project 17.16: Cultural Resources Inventory of LCRAâs Muleshoe Bend Recreation Area,
Travis and Burnet Counties, Texas

By Andrew F. Malof, Steven R. Evans, and Matthew E. Larsen

Principal Investigator
Andrew F. Malof

TAC Permit 7531
Interim Report 2.16
October 2016
Lower Colorado River Authority

180

MANAGEMENT SUMMARY
LCRAâS Muleshoe Bend Recreation Area consists of approximately 860 acres of designated parkland and
another 414 acres of lands subject to inundation and/or that are contiguous to the park (Figure 89). It is
bisected by the Travis/Burnet County line and is on the south side of Lake Travis, which is formed by the
Colorado River. Originally 1,467 acres in size, 193 acres have been sold at various times, leaving 1,274
acres of land under LCRAâs control. At present there are 44 known archaeological sites on the property
that span most of the prehistoric period as well as historic settlement and use dating back perhaps to the
1850s. The property is notable for its association with Turner Farm, a tenant farming complex operated
from about 1890 to the 1930s, when LCRA acquired the land.
The property has had a number of projects requiring cultural resource assessments and surveys, beginning
in the late 1970s for brush clearing and fence construction, then again in the 1990s and continuing
through recent years as part of a program of inventory assessments for LCRA properties and proposed
improvements. A number of these later investigations resulted in the collection of select artifacts, which
have been temporarily stored at LCRA offices. This report is one in a series that accompanies artifacts
being permanently curated at the Texas Archeological Research Laboratory in Austin.
While not intended for regulatory clearance on any particular project, the report does offer
recommendations for each of the known sites. The document can therefore be used as guidance for future
projects, which can reference the recommendations to identify potential constraints, assess interpretive
value or address broader research questions.
The report is rather traditionally organized, although site descriptions have been grouped by type and
component. This moves, somewhat, the focus from the individual site to broader implications of land use
and resource management, and therefore takes on something of a landscape perspective.

ENVIRONMENTAL BACKGROUND
Muleshoe Bend Recreation Area is located within the Edwards Plateau Section of the Great Plains
physiographic province (Carr 1969:3) and is a part of a northeast-to-southwest-trending terrace that is
situated within a large bend of the Colorado River. A 2006 in-house land management plan (LCRA
2006:14-16) provides information on three environmental/topographic zones present at the park. Data on
these three zones â lowland/floodplain, upland margins, and uplands â is summarized in the next three
paragraphs.
The lowland/floodplain zone extends from the top of the original bank of the Colorado River at an
elevation of 620 ft above mean sea level (amsl) up to 700 ft (amsl). Most of this zone is normally
inundated by the conservation pool level of 681 ft amsl of Lake Travis. Detailed mapping of the river
basin undertaken just prior to the construction of the lake indicates that, within the park area, the
lowland/floodplain terrace is as wide as 950 to 1,950 ft in some areas. The zone consists of deep alluvial
terrace deposits (Barnes 1981). During times of low lake levels, staff examined cutbanks that indicate that
this terrace has 40-ft-deep alluvial deposits in some areas adjacent to the river channel.
181

The upland margins extend from about 700 to 800 ft amsl. In many parts of the park, the base of the
upland margins is marked by moderately steep slopes that rise abruptly from the inner edges of the
lowland/floodplain. The surfaces of the upland margins are draped with Pleistocene-era fluviatile terrace
deposits (Barnes 1981). These terrace deposits are generally less than 4 ft in thickness and include large
volumes of lag gravels.
Topographically, the upland zone extends from 800 ft amsl upward. Upland surfaces have little to no
topsoil. Outcrops of Cow Creek Limestone and Hammett Shale are frequently evident on the ground
surface in upland areas, as are surface exposures of Pleistocene lag gravel deposits (Barnes 1981).
An in-depth discussion of soil types occurring on Muleshoe Bend is difficult, as the current surveys of the
area are split by the Burnet-Travis County line, which bisects the bend (Figure 90). While soils that cross
the county line are mapped as continuous units, soils are mapped as different types on either side of the
line. On the Burnet County side (the western portion of the bend), the soils are mapped as BracketPurves-Doss soils (Dittemore and Allison 1979). The primary soil type in this association is Brackett
soils, at 37%, followed by Purves, Doss, Bolar, Eckrant, Krum, and Real soils, respectively. On the Travis
County side (the eastern portion), the soils are mapped as Brackett Association (Werchan et al. 1974).
Brackett soils make up 63% of this association, followed by Tarrant, Volente, Denton, San Saba,
Pedernales, and Altoga soils, again respectively. These soils are close in description most of the time, but
a discussion of the general soil types on either side of the county gives a picture of the soils that is just as
informative, and less confusing.
Brackett-type soils, which seem to make up much of the upland area of Muleshoe Bend, are described
varyingly as 5 or 6 inches of light brownish gray to paly brown clay loam, over either 12 inches of very
pale brown clay loam, or 9 inches of light yellowish brown clay loam, followed by either more pale
brown clay loam or pale yellow clay loam with scattered limestone chunks and calcium carbonate. The
lowlands around the lake and down to the inundated Colorado River seem to be uniformly shown as
loamy fine sands above clay loam and sandy clay in some places, and sandy loams with gravels above
sandy clay and clay loam in others. While other soil types occur in several place across the bend, most of
the bend can be described by one of these three common profiles.
Vegetation found on the alluvial terraces consists of a predominantly herbaceous community that includes
Bermuda grass, switchgrass, and eastern gamagrass with some buttonbush and mesquite trees. Post oak,
live oak, and Buckleyâs yucca, cowpen daisy, Texas croton, and wooly croton are also found on these
terraces.
In the upland margins, dense thickets of Ashe juniper are the predominant vegetation along with some
live oak, post oak, and mesquite trees. Shrub species including agarita, hog plum, prickly pear, brasil,
Texas persimmon, twist-leaf yucca, and mountain laurel are very common. In some areas, especially at
the locations of former historic house sites, white bush, tasajillo, and prickly pear are very dense.

182

Figure 89. Muleshoe Bend Recreation Area on Pace Bend USGS quad.

183

Figure 90. An aerial map of Muleshoe Bend showing the SSURGO county soil survey data for both Burnet County
(west side of the map) and Travis County (east side of the map).

PREVIOUS INVESTIGATIONS
In the 1930s, when archeologists conducted a reconnaissance survey of the Lake Travis basin prior to the
construction of Mansfield Dam, one archeological site, 41TV250, was recorded in the Muleshoe Bend
tract. Jackson (1938) described it as a small prehistoric campsite with shallow deposits. No excavation
work was recommended at that time. In 1979, Prewitt and Associates, Inc. recorded 16 additional cultural
resource sites in advance of a proposed LCRA brush control project (Prewitt and Kotter 1979). These
consist of sites 41BT147 and 41BT148 in Burnet County and sites 41TV495 through 41TV508 in Travis
County. Site 41TV250 was also revisited during that investigation. In all, Prewitt and Associates recorded
13 prehistoric sites, 3 historic sites, and 1 multi-component site that is primarily historic, but includes a
small amount of prehistoric material. Most of the prehistoric sites were described as upland lithic scatters
and lithic procurement areas. Only four of the prehistoric sites were described as having campsite
materials. The historic period sites all appeared to be former late nineteenth to early twentieth century
house sites. Although the houses had been removed, surface features including cisterns, foundation
stones, rock walls, and root cellars were still evident. In summarizing the historic sites, Utley (1996:64)
later noted that of the four historic sites recorded by Prewitt and Kotter, 41TV499 and 41TV507 appeared
to be the most stable and least damaged. Overall, Prewitt and Kotter (1979) recommended avoidance of
13 of the prehistoric and historic sites during the proposed brush clearing program.
Additional cultural resource survey work was conducted by LCRA archaeological staff intermittently
from 1995 through. Staff investigations in the 1995â1997 era primarily focused on historic period house
sites in conjunction with Utleyâs (1996) historical research. Other fieldwork that focused on prehistoric
sites was conducted during droughts when areas normally covered by the lake were exposed. All of these
investigations were primarily reconnaissance level work, and importantly, did not include any shovel
testing or other types of subsurface probing. There was one survey with subsequent monitoring conducted
in 1995 for composting toilets being installed at 41TV500 and near 41TV495, but surface survey and
monitoring results were both negative (Nightengale et al 1997).
In 2005 and 2006, several surveys were conducted for construction projects at T-Bar-M, a privately-run
camp leased from LCRA. Site 41TV2255 was recorded at this time. LCRA Cultural Resources staff again
took advantage of the low water conditions to monitor several sites along the old Colorado River channel.
Unfortunately, the low water conditions also allowed vandals to damage site 41TV1342, a burned rock
midden at the mouth of Red Bluff Creek. While inspecting the damage, some fragments of large faunal
remains, possibly bison, were collected.
In 2007 and 2008, several surveys including collection were conducted at Muleshoe Bend. These surveys
were part of a capital improvements plan for the Muleshoe Bend Recreation Area, which was reported to
the Texas Historical Commission (THC) in the LCRA Annual Report of Cultural Resource Investigations
for 2008. These surveys also took advantage of low water conditions to survey normally inundated sites
along the original Colorado River channel.
From 2009 to 2013, surveys were conducted as time allowed to monitor the cultural resources of
Muleshoe Bend. Several surveys were conducted to take advantage of low water conditions to examine
terraces along the original Colorado River channel at sites such as 41TV1910 and 41TV1911. The only
185

survey not associated with inventory monitoring during this time occurred at site 41BT343 in November
2012, when the Ridge Harbor neighborhood on the west side of Muleshoe Bend considered moving some
marina slips due to low water conditions.
Due to the sporadic nature of the inventory surveys, the results were not documented as systematically as
might be ideal. As a result, some sites were visited at different times by different teams, and it was not
always clear if a site was being revisited or recorded for the first time. In some cases, intermittent
inundation had dramatically altered a siteâs appearance or visibility, and site descriptions and boundaries
changed based on field notes and GPS points during post-processing. Additional assessments of many of
these sites are certainly warranted.

CULTURAL BACKGROUND

Prehistoric Background
The prehistoric background is typical of the Texas Hill Country. The interested reader is invited to see the
introductory portion of this annual report, and to explore numerous other resources.
Historic Background
Most of the historic background information in the following paragraphs is taken from Utleyâs (1996)
previous historical study of the Muleshoe Bend Recreation Area. A number of local area residents,
including some who lived within the Muleshoe Bend Recreation Area on the historic tenant farms as
children, were interviewed by Utley.
The land on the bend was originally part of three land grants from the Republic of Texas. The land from
the county line to the western edge of the bend was granted to an Isaac Calvet, the land on the eastern
edge in Burnet County was granted to a Fedele Secholzer, and the land in between was granted to an
Allison Williams (suspected to be male). Records obtained from the Texas Land Office via the Original
Texas Land Survey indicate that, after receiving these properties in the late 1840s and early 1850s, all
three original grantees sold them almost immediately. What follows in the records available through the
land office is a short trail of transfers and surveys that seem to indicate that the land was subject to land
speculation, and there is no indication that any of the owners ever resided on the properties.
Although some undocumented historic period activities may have occurred earlier, it was not until the
1870s that sustained settlement of the area began. Muleshoe Bend has traditionally been considered to be
a part of a dispersed, rural community called Haynie Flat, established in 1879 (Thompson and Frasier
1996:38). While this community did not have a commercial center, there was at least one cotton gin in
operation in the early twentieth century (41TV1778). The real focal point of the community was the local
school and nearby cemetery, which are located about 2 miles south of the park. The one-room school was
started in the 1870s and offered only seven or eight grades. Nearby Spicewood High School allowed
students to complete their educations.
186

At Muleshoe Bend, George S. Turner, a prominent rancher, started a sharecropping farm operation in the
late nineteenth or early twentieth century. Following his death in 1903, his granddaughter, Laura
Penelope âNellaâ Turner Evans, inherited this land and oversaw the farm operations until she sold the
land to the LCRA in 1939. Today, the descendants of some of the sharecropper families who were tenants
on the Turner Farm still live in the vicinity of Muleshoe Bend Park. The locations and family names of
the occupants of five tenant farm houses are shown on a 1936 era contour map made by LCRA land
surveyors of the then proposed Lake Travis basin (Figure 91).
The archeological remains of six tenant farmsteads and two unrelated owner-occupied farmsteads are
among the cultural resource sites within the Muleshoe Bend Recreation Area. All houses and outbuildings
at five of these sites were deliberately removed by LCRA when it purchased the Turner Farm over 70
years ago. LCRA allowed one of the farmsteads to remain occupied into the 1950s, and then demolished
that house and its associated outbuildings. In some cases, underground cement-lined cisterns, root cellars,
trash dumps, portions of barbed wire fences, and remnants of house foundations are still discernable at
these sites. Limited numbers of artifacts such as metal fragments and broken glass and ceramics are also
visible on ground surface at the sites.
Cotton farming and its accompanying tenant farming system waned in the 1930s and 1940s across Texas,
at approximately the same time that LCRA was purchasing the Turner Farm to become a part of its Lake
Travis lands. In adjacent areas not bought by the LCRA, cattle ranching became the predominant
economic activity in the following decades along with land leasing for deer hunting. In more recent years,
many areas adjacent to Muleshoe Bend Park have been subdivided for rural residency with most
occupants either retirees or workers who commute to and from Austin each day.

187

Figure 91. Composite of five 1936 topographic maps depicting roads, fences, and houses with family
names. Note cultivated fields to north and east.
188

METHODOLOGY
This report summarizes over 40 years of semi-continuous work at LCRAâs Muleshoe Bend Recreation
Area and contiguous lands. It therefore relies on notes, forms, reports, and other records created at various
times for various purposes (including differing survey intensities), as well as a deep institutional
knowledge of the property. Of the 44 known sites (Figure 92), 15 have been formally assessed by the
Texas Historical Commission as âineligible,â either in their entirety or within a specific project area. One
site, 41TV495, is formally assessed as undetermined, and one site assessed as ineligible is considered by
LCRA to contain undetermined components (41TV504). A large number of the remainder are considered
to be of undetermined status for regulatory purposes (Table 8). LCRA cultural resources department,
however, uses a five-point rating scale: 5-not eligible; 4-unknown eligibility; 3-potentially significant; 2significant; 1-listed, where some sites are clearly not eligible (5), others (3 and 4) need varying amounts
of work to clarify eligibility, some are clearly eligible (2), and others are listed as SALs (1). These
classifications aid in GIS analysis of site distributions, but are not intended to replace the THC review
process for individual ground-disturbing projects requiring review.
The reporting process looks at sites by types, then discusses them as groups (e.g. open campsite;
farmstead), and, rather than go into unnecessary or redundant discussion for each, gives analytical weight
to a site or site type and its relative contribution to the relevant discussion. As a result, some sites are
discussed more than once, emphasizing the pertinent component or category.
This report also serves as the accompanying document for the artifact collections from the property
(Table 9). As with the other records, these artifacts have varying degrees of documentation and
provenience, but help preserve the archaeological record at Muleshoe Bend.
