PREHISTORIC COMPONENTS

There are 40 sites with prehistoric components (Figure 93). These are grouped by perceived function (site
type) which consist of lithic scatter, lithic procurement, limited activity or mixed use, and open campsite.
Lithic scatters
Lithic scatters are locations where chipping debris not suggestive of lithic procurement is present, and
also lack other indicators of specific land use such as tools, food remains, or fire-cracked rock. There are
four such sites at Muleshoe Bend (Table 10).
Table 10. Lithic scatters at Muleshoe Bend.

41BT283
41TV498
41TV505
41TV2291
Sites 41BT283, 41TV498, and 41TV505 comprise small numbers of flakes seen within historical site
boundaries. They probably represent observer bias given that historical materials were present, and so
minor amounts of prehistoric chipping debris were also observed. Site 41TV2291 was recorded by shovel
testing an area of loamy sands and sands, where soils are mapped as Travis gravelly soils, which have an
18-cm fine sandy loam A horizon (Werchan et al. 1974). Comparison of the soil profile and shovel test
data suggest a pocket of redeposition of sandy topsoils. Materials encountered in four shovel tests totaled
about 10 very small flakes and flake fragments, found between 20 and 80 cmbs.
As is often the case, there is little that can be said of such sites. Muleshoe Bend is the type of area that
would likely yield a few artifacts at any given location if examined long enough and hard enough. So
while these sites do help place human presence across the landscape, they offer little in the way of
research potential, and these components are not considered eligible for formal SAL status.

Lithic procurement sites
There are 14 identified lithic procurement sites at Muleshoe Bend (Table 11). Lithic procurement sites are
defined as having a predominance of early stage reduction materials such as large hard-hammer flakes,
cores, and tested cobbles. These are typically within gravel deposits, the locations of which can be
predicted geologically.

192

Figure 93. Distribution of prehistoric components at Muleshoe Bend.

193

Table 11. Muleshoe Bend sites with lithic procurement components.

41BT148
41TV496
41TV498
41TV501
41TV506
41TV1792
41TV2287

41BT347
41TV497
41TV500
41TV502
41TV507
41TV1793
41TV2288

The original geologic maps of Muleshoe Bend show the majority of the eastern part of the landform as
Quaternary terrace deposits; i.e. Pleistocene gravel deposits (Qt) with bands of older Sycamore sand
(Ksy) at higher elevations that abut Cow Creek limestone and Hammett shale (Kch) (Barnes 1981). A
fairly narrow band of Holocene floodplain is mapped around the edge of the bend, and corresponds with
the full lake elevation. More recent imagery has combined most of the Qt and Ksy, with a much smaller
area of Qt apparently âlaggingâ at a higher elevation (Figure 94). The primary difference is that the Ksy
formation is described as a conglomeration of pebbles, gravels, and boulders of early Cretaceous or even
Permian age. The potential for there to be gravels suitable for aboriginal use would be similar, once
gravels separated from the surrounding cement. Personal observation has noted chert boulders in excess
of 30 cm diameter.

Figure 94. Distribution of geological units at Muleshoe Bend.

Of the 14 sites described as having lithic procurement components, six, 41TV496, 41TV497, 41TV500,
41TV501, 41TV1792, and 41TV1793, are fully or mostly within Sycamore sands. Sites 41TV506,
41TV507, 41TV2287, 41TV2288, and 41TV2289 are shown mostly or wholly either in Holocene
194

alluvium or within the lake itself. This is an inaccurate representation, and these sites should be
considered to be either within Ksy or Qt deposits. Site 41BT347 is within mapped Kch, which may have
scattered cobbles in transitional areas between the limestone and the sandstone components. More likely,
this minor site has seen some colluvial movement of cherts from elsewhere, or mapping resolution is not
sufficiently fine grained.
Site 41TV1793 produced two rather unique items; an apparent incised pebble, and an ornamental sword
hilt made of âpot metal.â The latter is single-sided, as if to be displayed on a wall.

Figure 95. Possible incised pebble, obverse and reverse, 41TV1793.

Figure 96. Decorative sword hilt, 41TV1793.

Sites 41BT148 and 41TV502 are both described as multi-use sites, where lithic procurement occurred but
where there is also evidence of other, more generalized activities, i.e. the mixed use site definition used in
this report and defined more fully elsewhere. Both of these sites are within gravel-bearing deposits, but
also have substantial areas within the Cow Creek limestone/Hammett shale formations. The potential
diversity of resources appears represented by diversity in activities. And although the description of the
soils does not fully support numerous cobbles, the majority of the âpureâ procurement sites are within
Travis gravelly soils.

195

None of the lithic procurement components at Muleshoe Bend are particularly noteworthy. The ancient
gravels tend to have dense cortex, and due to millennia of movement are likely to have internal flaws,
making them less than optimal choices for fine tools. They would, however, probably serve well for dayto-day informal or expedient use, and could be exploited either logistically or as part of an activity
embedded in other rounds. None of these components are considered to be eligible for formal SAL status.

Mixed Use Sites
The âmixed useâ or âlimited activityâ site crept into LCRA terminology following several surveys
conducted by Steve Kotter, then of Prewitt and Associates, who referred to sites that were more complex
than simple lithic scatters but did not fully exhibit characteristics of full-blown campsites. They are
typically in upland settings and contain tools, food remains (usually mussel shell), and perhaps some firecracked rock, but not in quantities to indicate intensive use. They might be lookouts, very short-term
camps, probably used for logistically exploiting a particular resource, or might actually represent highly
mobile foraging groups rather than logistically-based collectors (sensu Binford 1981). Eight such sites
have been identified (Table 12).
Table 12. Mixed use sites at Muleshoe Bend.

41BT147
41BT494
41TV502
41TV1777

41BT148
41BT480
41TV503
41TV2255

Two of these sites, 41TV502 and 41BT148, are seen as subareas or palimpsests of tools, flake scatters, or
food remains inconsistent with the lithic procurement also seen occurring. Site 41BT147, when first
recorded in 1979, was described as a scatter of 20â30 mussel shells, one with a perforated umbo, several
tertiary flakes, and scattered fire-cracked rock of uncertain origin. When it was revisited in 1997 much of
the same material was present, but a brush fire had necessitated bulldozing a fire lane through the site,
which is located on the fenceline adjacent to private property.
Sites 41BT480 and 41BT494 are both tool scatters located on lands leased by Ridge Harbor. They are
both relatively near open campsites 41BT343 and 41BT344. Site 41TV480 was located along a deep
drainage and comprised a crude gouge, a large edge-modified flake, and a crude core on limestone
bedrock. No other cultural material was seen. The gouge most closely resembles a unifacial Clear Fork
tool, possibly Early Archaic in age. The site may represent an extraction activity.
Site 41BT494 is located on a high stony terrace overlooking the two campsites. The site consisted of a
small cluster of stone tools consisting of two Archaic-aged dart point fragments, two medial biface
fragments, a small chopper, and an end-and-side scraper. USGS maps indicated small cabins were once in
this area, and it is possible the artifacts are the result of recent collecting. If not, then they also appear to
represent a short-term activity area.
The remaining three sites, 41TV503, 41TV1777, and 41TV2255, are located on lands leased by T-Bar-M
Camp, and are in the vicinity of open camps 41TV250, 41TV504, and 41TV1777. Site 41TV503 was
196

described in 1979 as a thin scatter of cores, interior flakes, scattered burned rock, and a metate fragment,
and fell somewhere between interpretation as a limited activity area and a camp. An attempt to relocate
the site in 1997 was unsuccessful, and it was thought to have deflated, perhaps completely into the lake,
during the intervening period.
Recorded during the T-Bar-M assessment in 1997, 41TV1777 was described as a light scatter of debitage
and scattered fire-cracked rock. Also present were two cores, a battered hammerstone or chopper (perhaps
a crusher?), and a few pieces of mussel shell. The diffuse nature of the artifacts indicated a short-term,
disturbed activity area, and a septic field was subsequently placed over it.
Site 41TV2255 was a very light but very discrete artifact scatter consisting of about 50 interior chips and
flakes, a side-notched dart point, a blade-like edge-modified flake, and a very small antler tine. This last
was not seriously considered to be part of the prehistoric artifact assemblage, but its presence was
intriguing.
Geologically, the sites are fairly evenly split between the Hammett Shale/Cow Creek Limestone and the
Sycamore Sand formations. There is more diversity in soil types than for lithic procurement sites, and
they are found in areas with clay, clay loams, and silty clays as well as limestone rock outcrops. None
were found in the Travis gravelly soils where the majority of the lithic procurement sites were located.
While it is tempting to impose order on these sites, it is difficult to say more than that they represent land
use, but not whether it was for direct support of large riverside camps or the result of small wandering
bands, or some combination. None of the mixed use/limited activity sites retain enough integrity or
diversity of materials to warrant formal listing as State Antiquities Landmarks.
Open Campsites
Open campsites are locations where diversity, and often a density, of materials are present that indicate
repeated or stable use of the site for habitation. There is typically a preponderance of formal and informal
chipped stone tools, ground stone, mussel shell and bone, fire-cracked rock and burned rock features. The
apparent favorable landforms that invite repeated use create an interpretive challenge, and multiple
occupations are often seen across a single surface. There are 14 open campsites at Muleshoe Bend (Table
13).
Table 13. Open campsites at Muleshoe Bend.

41BT343
41TV250
41TV504
41TV1778
41TV1795
41TV1797
41TV1911

41BT344
41TV495
41TV1342
41TV1794
41TV1796
41TV1910
41TV2511

197

Of the 14 sites, all but two, 41TV495 and 41TV504, are located along or very near the original (or
present) channel of the Colorado River. These latter two sites are located close to the full pool elevation
of Lake Travis, and both have minimal recorded information.
41TV495
Site 41TV495 was first recorded in 1979 and described as a multipurpose camp, with debitage, mussel
shell, scattered burned rocks, and hearths. Although partially destroyed by shoreline erosion, it was felt to
have some intact deposits and was recommended for testing. A revisit in 1997 was during a period when
the lake elevation was higher than in 1979, and little material was observed. There are no records that the
site was revisited during later periods of lower lake levels, although it is known that informal visits were
made. Although it is likely this site is destroyed, a formal revisit will be required to confirm this. As such,
the site remains of undetermined eligibility.
41TV504
Site 41TV504 is very similar in setting and recording circumstances. In 1979 it was recorded as a multiple
purpose camp with tertiary flakes, fire-cracked rock, and two possible hearths, partially destroyed by
shoreline erosion with possible intact deposits. It was informally revisited in 1994 and shovel testing was
recommended. As with 41TV495, there is no record of further work. For both sites this is likely due, at
least in part, to the discovery of much larger and more visible campsites, such as 41TV1794, where
attempts at more detailed recording expenditures seemed warranted. Site 41TV504 remains of
undetermined eligibility.
41TV1342
Site 41TV1342 is the most visually intriguing of the remainder of these components (Figure 97).
Unfortunately, it is located at the extreme southern portion of Muleshoe Bend, outside the formal park or
leased areas, and is relatively difficult to access, as it can be reached only across private land or by boat.
The site is located primarily along the west and north sides of Red Bluff Creek, a relatively short, steep
tributary of the Colorado River. It is not clear how close the site is to the original river, however it is
located on an outside bend, and the river appears to be wide and relatively shallow here.
The site was first recorded in 1987 when it apparently was reported to LCRA archaeologists, who
discovered it had been long collected. It was recorded as the âSissy Site,â possibly for Sissy
Hollingsworth, whose family has long lived in the area. Three areas were described along with a large
burned rock midden and several smaller satellite campsites. Area âAâ had Middle Archaic points (Travis,
Nolan through Late Archaic (Montell, Marshall, Ensor) and several bifaces. Area âBâ had only
Pedernales points, while Area âCâ had Late Prehistoric Scallorn and Perdiz points and ceramics, as well
as two beveled knives. The burned rock midden, part of Area âA,â also had five corner-tang bifaces.
Apparently no attempt was made to retrieve the artifacts, some of which (from Area âBâ) would have
come from private property if collected after 1983 when LCRA sold that area.

198

Figure 97. Site 41TV1342.

199

The site was not revisited formally until 2006 when calls came in that the site was being impacted by an
unknown party. The person or persons took it upon themselves, during a period of low lake levels, to
remove cypress stumps and otherwise lower the burned rock midden portion of the site, presumably to
allow easier and/or safer boat navigation, and possibly to facilitate movement of a boat dock further into
the cove once lake levels rose.
Upon arrival at the site it was seen that, indeed, a bulldozer or front-end loader had essentially destroyed
this portion of the site, although it is possible that there are remnants still intact. Visual inspection of the
area noted dark organic soils, redeposited fire-cracked rock, burned clay, probable bison bone, an area of
about seven exposed heaths, a very thin distal portion of a probable a projectile point (possibly a Montell
point), and the proximal section of a probable knife (biface).
Despite eyewitness accounts of events leading up to the siteâs being impacted, LCRA rangers, who
investigated, were unable to connect anyone with the crime, which remains unsolved.
At this same time the ground between Area âAâ and Area âCâ was resurveyed. Areas with multiple
hearths, fire-cracked rock, and groundstone were documented. As Area âCâ was reached, dense firecracked rock, a lighter scatter of burned rock, and a possible shell midden were seen. At least two large
hearths were mapped, one primarily of limestone and one almost entirely of quartzite (Figure 98). An
arrow point preform and fragmentary dart point were also seen, partially supporting the earlier premise
that there is a strong Late Prehistoric component in this area.

Figure 98. Quartzite hearth at Area C, 41TV1342.

Area âBâ was revisited under similar circumstances in 2011, after reports of scraping across a narrow spit
of land exposed during low lake levels. Large amounts of fire-cracked rock, chert debitage, and mussel
shell were observed, with a seemingly buried zone between 30 and 60 cmbs (Figure 99). GPS and
subsequent map and boundary review determined that the majority of this area was located on private
200

property, although the westernmost section of the âpeninsula,â where disturbance was minimal, was
found to be LCRA property. No diagnostic artifacts were found at that time.
Site 41TV1342âs ultimate research potential remains unknown, although its history of local collecting and
unauthorized land modifications have severely compromised portions of it. Despite this, pockets of high
integrity deposits may still exist, and, if so, the research potential would be high.

Figure 99. Mussel shell lens at Area âBâ, 41TV1342.

41TV250, 41TV1778, 41TV1794, 41TV1910, 41TV1911, 41BT343, 41BT344
The remainder of the riverside campsites are quite similar to each other in general composition. Each is
on the edge of the eroding bank of the Colorado River channel and consist of varying amounts of firecracked rock, chipping debris and tools, mussel shell, and bone. In some cases clusters of Rabdotus
species snail are present, but are also seen in non-cultural context, and are presumed at this time to be the
result of natural processes.
Site 41TV1794 is the most extensive of these sites, although its presence on a sloping terrace edge make
it problematic. Site 41TV1910 may be the best candidate for isolating discrete features, as the terrace
edge is higher and much more abrupt.
In general, each of these riverside campsites extend an unknown distance (or none at all) into the terrace
tread. Labor capital has been spent so far in identifying and minimally recording these sites. At the time
of this writing Lake Travis has returned to full elevation after several years of being well below normal.
When lake levels recede, as they surely will, systematic backhoe trenching âbehindâ these sites would be
201

very beneficial in determining what, if any, research value they retain. Until then they have to be
considered of undetermined eligibility.
41TV1794
Site 41TV1794 is by far the largest of the sites documented at Muleshoe Bend, and is mapped as both
concentrations and discontinuous scatters of fire-cracked rock, mussel shell, debitage, groundstone, and
tools (Figure 100). There is also a minor historic component, which is discussed below. When first
recorded in 1996, GPS points requiring post-processing were obtained. These were subsequently lost.
Various revisits over the years also resulted in GPS point records, but the correlations are often vague.
The majority of the observed material is exposed in the sloping river bank at elevations between 20 and
30 feet above the river channel (Figure 101). There is a lower floodplain zone that is rarely exposed, and
when it is, is rarely dry enough to walk across. These may correspond with J. Charles Kelleyâs 40- and
20-foot terraces as described at the Grelle site (41BT1). Kelley suggested that the 40-foot terrace was
responsible, by way of redeposition, for the 20-foot terrace, and that any cultural stratigraphy in the 20foot terrace would likely be reversed.

Figure 100. Site 41TV1794.

202

The site has produced a Nolan point, a Late Archaic dart point fragment, a Scallorn point, a Perdiz point,
and a piece of bone-tempered ceramic, all from the sloping bank (Figure 102; Figure 103). Other items,
not collected, included an apparently incised mano and a perforated mussel shell. This last item was the
first of several that have been seen along the lake shore, where clearly the higher hinge portion of the
mussel shell was in the process of being eroded by sand and water action, eventually creating the
impression of an intentional artifact. These types of shells need clear contextual information before
intentional modification is suggested.
Two hand auger borings and two shovel tests have been excavated on the terrace edge. The locations of
these are poorly known, especially for the two shovel tests. The auger tests were performed with a 3-inch
(c. 10-cm) diameter core with an extendable T-handle. They were excavated to depths of 185 and 205 cm,
and were spaced about 20 m apart. The soil profile of the second, located a bit further inland on the
terrace, had a much greater amount of clay within the soil descriptions. No cultural material was found in
either.
The two shovel test locations are unknown. They were primarily through what was described as brown
silt loam and had minimal recovery, although both had a single tertiary chip between 80 and 100 cm.

Figure 101. Fire-cracked rock along terrace edge of 41TV1794.

203

Figure 102. Perdiz point (left) and Scallorn point from 41TV1794.

Figure 103. Bone-tempered ceramic from 41TV1794. Note partially smoothed coiling.

41TV1910
Site 41TV1910 probably has the greatest detail in recording of the riverside camps. It is located on the far
eastern edge of the bend and is bounded to the south by a pronounced gully. The site extends northward
for a distance of about 300 m. At the southern end was the greatest accumulation of cultural material,
centered in an approximate 25-m-diameter area. This yielded quantities of fire-cracked rock, mussel shell,
various tools, manos, and a massive limestone metate fragment, whichmeasured 33-x-40 cm and was 9
cm thick (Figure 104; Figure 105). A Frio point was recovered from this area.
Beginning about 70 m to the north of this area is a series of features, seen primarily cascading out of the
20-ft-tall river bank. An exception was Feature 9, which consisted of three distinct concentrations of firecracked rock, with the appearance of separate heating features. One consisted of nine large rocks with two
apparent manos. About a meter distant was a concentration of eight large rocks, up to 20 cm in diameter.
Approximately equidistant between the two features was a hearth consisting of about 18 rocks up to 15

204

cm in diameter, composed of sandstone, quartzite, and limestone. These three features may actually
comprise a single feature with only portions exposed.

Figure 104. Fire-cracked rock on terrace tread, south end, 41TV1910.

Figure 105. Large metate at 41TV1910.

The remainder of the features contained varying amounts of rock and other material and were exposed on
the terrace edge. Feature 1 was about 110 cm in length (along the shore) and 70 cm wide (across [down])
the shore). It contained about 22 rocks up to 15 cm in diameter, with a few outlying rocks and another 30
or so that had slumped below the feature. No other cultural material was observed.
Feature 2 was somewhat larger, about 100 cm in length and 140 cm wide. It consisted of about 24 rocks,
mostly between 15 and 20 cm in diameter that were clearly eroding out of reddish silt. This seemed to be
an incipient or maybe single-use feature, as the rocks were not heavily fractured, although some were
seen to have cracked in place. An Early Triangular point was collected from very near this feature.

205

Feature 3 comprised two buried fire-cracked rocks, about 8 cm in diameter, lying roughly 23 cm from
each other (Figure 106). They were well-embedded at about 130 cm below the top of the terrace. There
were three other loosely associated rocks. Feature 4 was similar, and was recorded as three rocks eroding
out of the cutbank.
Feature 5 consisted of about 20 rocks averaging around 15 cm in diameter, clearly fire-cracked. Although
the feature suffered from erosion, the main portion was largely intact and about 30 cm in diameter. A
half-dozen thinning flakes and an early stage biface were closely associated.
Feature 6 was composed of three widely spaced burned rocks, two blade-like flakes, and an early stage
biface. Feature 7 was similar, with 11 rocks seen forming a line down the edge of the cutbank. It was at
this point that debitage became more frequent. This is the most southerly of the recorded features.
Further to the north, Feature 8 consisted of three tightly packed quartzite rocks. It was about 20 m south
of Feature 9, the surface manifestation.
Site 41TV1910 is intriguing in that it appears to hold intact buried deposits. What remains unknown is
whether these materials are simply a veneer or remnant deposit or if they represent additional, substantial
buried materials.

206

Figure 106. Feature 3, buried burned rock and cleaned profile at 41TV1910.

The most likely soil across this setting (now mapped as âWaterâ) is Heaton loamy fine sand, which is
described as an Alfisol with a 50-cm-thick brown fine sand over an eluviated horizon to 76 cm below
where a reddish sandy clay loam Bt horizon is encountered. This last seems to correspond with the
presence of much of the archaeological material at 41TV1910 (and elsewhere along the lake). The Early
Triangular point may help to date this deposit, which J. Charles Kelley, when working upstream at 41BT1
(the Grelle site) recognized as resting below âblack humic siltsâ and above âyellowish gritty clayâ (Kelley
1940:2). Kelley noted artifacts in the overlying humic soils, often at the contact, but not within the lower
reddish silts, although he expected he eventually would, and then did (hearth 5 in Area 49, at 4 ft 10 in
[1.5 m] below the surface and later at least one more). This was referred to as archeological level AD, and
was interpreted as a rapidly aggrading surface with sparse cultural materials. No artifacts, other than a
longbone, thought perhaps to be an atlatl, are described from this zone. Notably, Pedernales and Nolan
points were found in the humic soils above the red silts.

41TV1911
Site 41TV1911 is located just downstream from 41TV1910 and separated from it by a deep erosional cut.
While very similar in appearance, it lacks detailed feature descriptions. When first recorded in 2000 it was
described as an area of fire-cracked limestone, mussel shell and debitage, and only described as being
about 10 m in diameter in size. When revisited in 2011 and 2012 it was seen to be substantially larger,
extending about 80 m onto the shore and about 40 m along the shore. In addition to an increase in
observed material, an apparent hearth was also noted. Much of the material was seen near the terrace edge
opposite 41TV1910, and it may once have been a single locale. This siteâs setting and recommendations
are the same as described above for 41TV1910.

41BT343 and 41BT344
These two sites are located at the far upstream portion of the bend, and are on either side of a bifurcated
canyon system that drains into the Colorado River (Figure 107; Figure 108). Dense to very dense cultural
material has been observed eroding out of the steep banks, consisting of the typical fire-cracked rock,
mussel shell, ground stone, tools, and debitage. There is also a (possible) double historic component at
41BT344 consisting of a farmstead and a riverside foundation (both discussed below in the section on
Historic Components).
Three shovel tests were excavated adjacent to artifact concentrations at 41BT344 in 2000. Although the
records have not been located, the site form at the time indicates there was little recovery, and it was
suggested that the site, in general, was surficial and/or deflated. A dart point reportedly was collected
from the surface, but has not been located within the Muleshoe Bend collections. A field sketch of it or
another point suggests a small-bodied Late Archaic type.
207

Figure 107. Sites 41BT343 and 41BT344.

208

Site 41BT343 was cursorily recorded in 2000, then more thoroughly assessed in 2012. Five shovel tests
were excavated, mostly to depths of 40-60 cm, except for that was terminated at 20 cm when a possible
burned rock feature was encountered (Figure 109). Soils were described generally as yellowish brown silt
loams. Recovery was moderate, with fire-cracked rock, debitage, and mussel shell documented. A
resharpened Andice point was observed, as well as two âcrushersâ, which have been found at other
locations along Lake Travis (Prewitt 1982). Given the preponderance of groundstone, including portable
metates, these are surmised to have been used for pecking metate surfaces. Once again, shovel testing is
inconclusive but not promising. Deep testing is the only way to make any definitive assessment of the
sites.

Figure 108. Site 41BT343. Site 41BT344 is across the gully to the northeast. View to northeast.

Figure 109. Burned rock feature in shovel test at 41BT343.

209

41TV250 and 41TV1778
These are both terrace sites that lack the pronounced drop-off seen at most of the other riverside camps.
Site 41TV1778 is similar to 41TV1342 in that it both are removed from the main portion of the property
and are essentially landlocked. The site is documented primarily as a farmstead (discussed below in the
Historic Component section), but also has a prehistoric component that has little documentation. The site
was visited once during a period of lower lake levels, when it was confirmed that it was the location of
the âBill Johnson placeâ, which had previously been misplotted. At the same time, scatters of fire-cracked
rock, debitage, mussel shell, and presumably groundstone were observed. No subsurface testing has been
done and the eligibility of the prehistoric component of the site remains undetermined.
Site 41TV250 was first recorded in 1938 during the Marshall Ford survey. A. T. Jackson noted it as the
âNella T. Evans placeâ and described it as a small campsite which did not have âsufficient imprintance to
justify digging.â The site was revisited in in 1979 prior to brush clearing activities, and described as
apparently highly eroded, with potential only for buried deposits in the upper portion of the site. In 1997
the site was enlarged to include the historic farmstead (discussed below), and was substantially resized
along the shoreline from what was seen in 1979.
The prehistoric component lies across a two-pronged flat between two short tributary drainages. Cultural
material was present in varying degrees across an eroding edge, as noted in 1979, as well as along the
sloping land that was exposed when lake levels dropped. Cultural material was seen across the surface on
the southwest trending lobe, while to the east, where the land form was steeper, two seemingly separate
(but overlapping) concentrations were observed eroding out of the upper portion of the terrace. The main
difference between the two was a greater number of large flakes to the north than the south.
The site has produced Nolan, Fairland, and Darl points, a strangulated uniface, and a perforated mussel
shell. This site, however, is where a mussel shell was seen on the waterâs edge, with wear on the upper
hinge, which once lightly touched, became perforated. Once again, these artifacts need careful attention to
context, and many in collections may actually be ecofacts.
Other Campsites: 41TV1795, 41TV1796, 41TV1797, 41TV2511
The remainder of the sites in this category have little descriptive information or lack distinctive
characteristics. Site 41TV1795 was originally recorded as a small, discrete lithic scatter. Later summary
notes indicate the presence of fire-cracked rock and possibly a hearth. Sites 41TV1796 and 41TV1797
were both recorded in 1997 and have not been systematically revisited. The first was described as a 10-x10-m area with a few flakes, burned rock and a granite mano. The second was much larger, about 75-x100 m, but only flakes, chips, and fire-cracked limestone are mentioned. Site 41TV2511 received a
trinomial based on a site form derived from descriptions for two GPS points marking tertiary debitage, a
core, scattered burned rock, and a possible small burned rock midden.
Summary

210

In general, these sites are all largely unknown, beyond what has been observed on the surface.
Observations and limited testing at sites such as 41TV250, 41TV1794, 41BT343 and 41BT344 have
suggested there is a low potential for substantial, intact deposits within the stream-side terrace. Others,
such as 41TV1910 and 41TV1911, have a seemingly high but completely untapped potential for useful
further research. At present, these sites are inaccessible, and hopefully protected underneath the waters of
Lake Travis. When water levels recede sufficiently, a program of systematic backhoe trenching would be
of great use to better determine what remains of these sites. All of the campsites are considered to be of
undetermined status.

HISTORIC COMPONENTS
There are six homestead sites associated with Turner Farm and three that are apparently individual
enterprises. There are five sites along the edge of the original river channel, some with substantial
amounts of apparent foundation or chimney rock that may predate Turner Farm. There are three sites in
upland or near upland settings that have no clear associations, although two of these may predate Turner
Farm as well.
The Turner Farm sites have been discussed by Utley (1996), who conducted oral history interviews with a
number of former residents of the farm. For example, in a conversation with G. W. Woods in 1994, Utley
learned that there was a fairly high turnover of tenants and recalled Tatums, Heffingtons, Crumleys,
Trammells, Gilleys, and Hobbs families, not all of which are shown on the 1936 map. The houses were
all similar, âsmall, rough lumber structures consisting of a front porch, two central rooms, and an addition
across the back elevationâ (Utley 1996:35). The rear addition usually served as a kitchen and dining area.
Most of the houses had adjacent below-ground cisterns, hand dug and partially stone lined, with above
ground collars. There was at least one well.
Woods recalled a fair amount of traffic across the farm, as there was a nearby river crossing. Area
families would use the crossing to visit friends and relatives, bring goods to the gin or gristmill, and shop
at the nearby grocery store.
Cotton seems to have been the primary crop, and each family was responsible for selling their own
production, with one-fourth of the proceeds going to the landlord. Corn was delivered to a central
repository, which the foreman of the farm oversaw and then sold when he felt the time was appropriate,
allowing him a portion of the proceeds, although whether from the ownerâs one-third or tenantsâ twothirds is not clear. Pecans were another valuable cash crop, although one-half of the harvest from Turner
Farm went to the owner. Area farmers also had supplementary vegetable crops and stock, primarily hogs,
chickens, turkeys, and milk cows. Harvest and processing periods were communal events, with the
neighbors all gathering to can corn, butcher and smoke hogs (each farm had a smokehouse) and otherwise
assist with the labors of agricultural life. There are seven sites associated with the tenant farming
community (Table 14).

211

Figure 110. Historic components at Muleshoe Bend.

212

Table 14. Turner Farm and two other independent farmstead components and tenants in 1936.

41TV498 - Trammell

41TV508 â R. Tatum - cistern
41TV507 â J Tatum - cistern
41TV1778 â Bill Johnson â cistern?
41TV1779 â Didway - cistern
Other
41BT283 â J. Hays â cistern
41BT344 - C. Hollingsworth - cistern
There is little left at most of these sites, which typically consist of a few foundation stones, household
debris, and often a trash disposal area on a nearby slope. The cisterns were removed from all but
41TV1779 in 2008 (Hixson 2009). This site is perhaps the best preserved of all the homesteads, with a
substantial chimney fall, the well-built cistern which still holds water, and the outlines of lower fields
likely forming garden space, and a likely garage or workshop area.

Figure 111. Cistern at 41TV1779. Note chimney fall behind, to left.

Although Turner Farm was originally considered as potentially eligible for nomination as a historic
district, based particularly on its role in the near-western extent of cotton production in Texas (Utley
1996:84), it was also noted that each of the sites had integrity issues, and that individually they did not
merit formal listings. Sites 41TV498, 41TV507, 41TV508 and most of 41TV499 were determined
ineligible, as were the cisterns at 41BT283, 41TV250 and 41TV1779. By extension, these last three sites
are also considered individually ineligible. There is however, high potential for interpretive purposes, and
it is recommended that LCRA voluntarily continue to manage these sites though avoidance.
Site 41TV1778 and 41BT344 are outliers in the sense that they are removed from the park, and are
instead located on contiguous lands. Site 41TV1778, known as the Bill Johnson place (Utley 1996) but
depicted as the Joe Tatum place on the 1936 map (Figure 112), is located at the southern end of the farm
and was the foremanâs residence. This is where the corn was stored after harvest, and also the location of
a gin and blacksmith shop, and for a short time (1901-1905) was known as Clover. When the gin
machinery was moved so was the place name, but the buildings remained and were used as barns and
213

other storage (Utley 1996; Thompson and Frasier 1996). The site is located on a land-locked low terrace
bench, and is below the normal pool of Lake Travis. It is therefore poorly recorded. There are numerous
foundation stones, large pieces of metal, and possibly a cistern. This site deserves more documentation
and until it can be further assessed should be considered to have an undetermined eligibility status.
Site 41BT344 is depicted as the Hollingsworth place. It is located at the far upstream end of the bend and
is on land leased by a private enterprise. A filled-in cistern is present, but the house location has been
covered by a parking lot and boat ramp. There minimal artifacts. This site is considered to be ineligible.

Unknown Historic Components
There are five sites located along the river edge that have historic components and cannot be directly
associated with known farmstead sites, except, perhaps, at 41BT344, as described above (Table 15). In
general, these sites are relatively minor scatters of historic-aged artifacts in association with limestone
rubble. None of these are depicted on available maps, and the artifact assemblages often seem to predate
Turner Farm occupation. These may be field houses, early settlements prior to institutionalized
knowledge of river flooding, or some other manifestations, including a molasses mill that is very roughly
shown on a sketched map of the area (Fraser 1996:ix).
Table 15. Riverside historic component sites.

41BT344
41TV1794
41TV1912
41TV1913
41TV2510
At 41BT344, the Hollingsworth place, there is a square limestone foundation measuring about 15 feet on
a side. A burned wooden post, possibly a pier, is located nearby. It is situated about 100 m northnorthwest of the house area. About halfway between the house area and this feature is a discrete scatter of
cut nails, solarized glass, a crockery fragment and various bits of rusted metal, near a short cedar post
alignment.
At 41TV1794 there is a discrete scatter consisting of a whiteware, cut square nails, wire nails, a motherof-pearl button, a metal button, a rimfire (.44 Henry) cartridge casing, horseshoes, a frying pan handle and
a few limestone blocks.
At 41TV1912 there is an apparent collapsed limestone foundation that is about 15 feet in length, parallel
with the river. It may have once been supported by piers, or more likely on a now-eroded river bank
surface. Artifacts consisted of a fragment of a bottle panel, white stoneware, solarized glass, blue glass,
and possibly associated bricks.
Site 41TV1913 is very similar to 41TV1912. The apparent limestone foundation is about 12 feet in
length, again parallel with the river. There was a minimal artifact assemblage consisting of a cast iron
skillet handle, a couple of whiteware sherds, some cut nails and bits of rusted metal.
214

Site 41TV2510 had two distinct limestone features. Feature 1 was a large rock scatter from the terrace top
down the slope toward the river. It was about 30 feet in length parallel with the river. Feature 2 was much
smaller, containing about 30 rocks some 6 feet in diameter. It was located 35 feet north (upstream) of
Feature 1, and was at midslope on the terrace edge. It had no associated artifacts. Artifacts in the vicinity
(within or just south) of Feature 1 included several whiteware sherds, a number of pieces of old aqua and
amber glass, cut nails, pieces of a harmonica and an iron skillet.
These sites are all extremely deflated with minimal artifact assemblages. Despite this, these sites
potentially add time depth to the Muleshoe Bend story. Interestingly, the Handbook of Texas states that
the highest concentration of slaves in Burnet County was at nearby Spicewood and Double Horn Creek
(Smyrl 2016). There were relatively few slaves in Burnet County (235 owned by 65 persons in 1860) due
in part to its relatively recent settlement plus the fact that there was little arable land (Debo 1979:98). The
wide Muleshoe Bend floodplain would have been an exception, so it is possible that at least some of these
sites are associated with slaves or recent freedmen. These sites should all be considered to be of
undetermined SAL status.

Other Historic Components
There are three sites with historic components located in upland settings and not associated with Turner
Farm or other known homesteads. One of these, 41TV496, appears to be a twentieth century trash dump
containing metal banding, apparent tractor parts, a Mason jar, crockery and clear glass and is not
considered to be eligible. The other two sites, 41TV501 and 41TV505 are more intriguing.
Table 16. Additional historic components.

41TV496
41TV501
41TV505

41TV501
Site 41TV501 is located on a south-trending ridge overlooking a fairly substantial inlet to the south. The
original recording in 1979 found, in addition to the prehistoric material discussed elsewhere in this report,
a light scatter of glass, metal whiteware and stoneware. In 1996, when LCRA archaeologists revisited the
site, three apparent historic features were recognized, which consisted of varying amounts of tabular rock
(possibly the âburned rockâ observed in 1979) along with metal, ceramics and glass. The largest of these,
Feature 3, was approximately 10 m in diameter and contained perhaps 40 pieces of rock scattered around
a low mounded area. Artifacts within and surrounding the feature included a green salt-glazed ceramic, an
aqua panel bottle fragment, purple and clear glass, a snuff bottle fragment, and, importantly, two cut nails.

215

Figure 112. 1936 map of 41TV1778, previous site of Clover. Note location of gin

216

Feature 2 was a shallow depression in the ground, about 10 feet in length, with a few pieces of tabular
limestone surrounding it. Nearby Feature 1 was a scatter of tabular limestone and sandstone with five
pieces of a cast iron pot or kettle. Two of the pieces had protruding perforated âlugs,â possibly
representing a lid with a bail handle. A pronounced lip was visible on another piece. Marks included âS
No1, and a symbol similar to the upper portion of a Celtic cross, with the letter A in the center and E, R
and Y in each of the three âtabs.â
The purpose of the site remains unknown. It may represent a processing area such as rendering or soap
production removed from a house location. It may be a hunting camp, or it may be a relatively ephemeral
house site. It does appear to predate the Turner Farm occupation, and so may have some connection with
the riverside historic sites. Additional work through systematic shovel testing is recommended.

41TV505
Site 41TV505, largely destroyed, also does not have clear associations with Turner Farm. It was first
recorded in 1979 as a possible chimney fall consisting as a âroughly rectangular pile of limestone rubbleâ
along with historical ceramics and glass, thought to likely postdate the possible house. Even at that time
the siteâs location was popular for camping, and associated disturbances were noted. When revisited in
1997, all that was observed was about five large pieces of limestone forming a right angle within which
was a low, mounded area. There were no clearly associated artifacts, and nearby camping rings seemed to
have incorporated some of the rocks previously reported. Distinctively, however, was a short section of
barbed wire that was collected, and later identified as Scutts (or Scutt) arrow plate, which consisted of a
sheet metal arrow-shaped barb split at the distal end which allowed the plate to be twisted in opposite
directions within two strand wire. It was patented in 1878, according to various sources, although its
period of popularity has not been discovered at this time.
This site, like 41TV501, may represent an activity area or a homesite. The barbed wire sample may
predate Turner Farm, and the site is not depicted on period maps. It is unlikely, but possible, that
systematic shovel testing might reveal more information about the site.
Of these last three historic components, only 41TV501 has reasonable potential to add new information
regarding historic period land use at Muleshoe Bend. The SAL status of the site remains undetermined.
Site 41TV505 is intriguing, but its location on a knoll overlooking the full pool of Lake Travis has
resulted in severe disturbance. It is considered ineligible for formal SAL status, despite the remote
possibility that early (pre-1900) artifacts may be present. Site 41TV496, as an isolated twentieth century
trash dump, is considered ineligible.

217

SUMMARY DISCUSSION
Muleshoe Bend is one of several LCRA-owned parcels of land along the Lake Travis portion of the
Colorado River. Other substantial holdings are found upstream at Lake Buchanan, within the Llano Uplift
region. These areas form a resource pool that can examine diversity in lifeways in the prehistoric past
through the advent of rural electrification. As an example, approximately 13 km of river distance centered
on Muleshoe Bend contain 177 documented archaeological sites over almost 4,000 acres (Figure 113).
Landscape assessments could include aspect, line-of-site between different site types and locations, leastcost or catchment analyses, among many. Unpublished LCRA work includes a comparison of site types
and observed artifact diversity between Muleshoe Bend and nearby Turkey Bend in order to place sites
within Binfordâs (1980) collector/forager spectrum.
Historic settlement can likewise be explored up and down the river. LCRA work has produced various
oral histories and archival documentation examining the modification and intensification of an agrarian
economy over time. The sociological microcosm that was Turner Farm presents a view of cotton
economy on its western fringes of central Texas. Creation of the lakes then changed the social fabric,
eliminating, or at least greatly inhibiting, decades of interactions between the two sides of the river. To
this day, the north side of the river remains largely rural and isolated, while the south side is experiencing
sustained and recently, explosive growth.
While the total artifact sample across all properties is perhaps not statistically significant, future
researchers may find value in a comparison of the curated items. Lake Buchanan artifacts may be
technologically or stylistically different from those found downstream. The density of well-worn manos
on Lake Buchanan can be startling, and bedrock metates have been recorded. Downstream, isolated crude
choppers have been found, often in upland settings. After seeing several, they became referred to as
âbitted choppers,â given that there was an isolated, gouge-like bit on the distal end. While none are in the
Muleshoe Bend collection, they have been noted elsewhere, notably Turkey Bend, and seem to represent
a specialized extraction technology.
Although this is not a regulatory report in the sense of being project driven, it does lay out
recommendations for individual sites, and upon completion of the review of the recommendations by
THC it is expected that a number of the sites described here will fall into the âineligibleâ category. Such
categorizations, however, serve to shape how potential future projects may take form, and whenever
possible the intrinsic value of a site and its relationship to the property will be given appropriate weight.

Project 20.16: An Intensive Cultural Resources Survey of the Lower Colorado River
Authorityâs Transmission Services Corporation Proposed Substation 2-6 (the Lewis Tract)
Project, Williamson County, Texas

By Andrew F. Malof and Steven R. Evans

Andrew Malof
Principal Investigator

TAC Permit #7531
Lower Colorado River Authority
Real Estate Services
May 2016
229

MANAGEMENT SUMMARY
LCRA is currently in the process of purchasing a parcel of land in Williamson County, within one of two
circles that must contain an electrical substation for a proposed new transmission line to be constructed
between existing substations in Leander and Round Rock. The land is being purchased, but no
construction is imminent.
The substation would be between about 6.7 acres in size. It is known as substation site 2-6, is located on
property presently owned by the Lewis family. It is along RR 2243, also known as Leander Road, a bit
east of Ronald Reagan Blvd.
LCRA Cultural Resources staff has completed a cultural resources assessment of the 2-6 prospective
substation location and associated property in Williamson County (Figure 114). The property for 2-6,
owned by the Lewisâs, is currently in use as a construction storage yard and office, and is approximately
28.9 acres in size, though the proposed substation would only take up 6.7 acres of the property. If
selected, the 2-6 site would be the location for a new substation on the proposed LCRA Leander to Round
Rock Electrical Transmission Line.
LCRA Cultural Resources staff physically surveyed 20.9 acres of the Lewis tract on March 1, 2016, and
completed 14 shovel tests in the area of the potential future substation as presently proposed. Shovel
testing was not attempted in the north part of the property, both because the prospective project has no
anticipated impact at this time (although a substation may eventually be constructed there), and because
most of the area has been covered in 1 to 2 feet of fill material. Additionally, a further 7.97 acres, where
the present office buildings are located, will not be affected by the proposed project, and are also
considered to be in a low probability area (Figure 115). One new archaeological site, 41WM1333, was
discovered as a result of the survey.
Site 41WM1333 is a very minor lithic scatter located on the edge and slope of an upland terrace. It is
considered to be fully recorded and ineligible for formal designation a State Antiquities Landmark. It is
recommended the project be allowed to proceed.

ENVIRONMENTAL BACKGROUND
The project area resides in the Edwards Plateau, and is mapped in Oak-Mesquite-Juniper Parks/Woods
(McMahan et al. 1984). This vegetation area consists of Post oak, Ashe juniper, shin, Texas, blackjack,
and live oak, cedar elm, agarito, sumac, and hackberry trees, with Texas prickly pear, curly mesquite, and
Texas wintergrass making up most of the groundcover. Drainages and creeks in the area drain to the
Brazos River system.
The Lewis property sits on Eckrant cobbly clay to the south and Eckrant extremely stony clay to the north
(Werchan and Coker 1983). The Eckrant series soils are described as containing two A horizons of very
dark grey to black clay down to 30 cm, followed by coarsely fractured limestone bedrock. Shovel testing
in the project showed that, for this location, the soil closely follows the profile of 10-25 cm of 7.5YR
230

2.5/1 black clay over 5YR 3/3 dark reddish brown clay. Most shovel tests were terminated at or before 40
cm, and while limestone bedrock was not encountered in any but one shovel test, exposures of the
bedrock can be seen at some places in the project area. All soils encountered were stony or cobbly, and
many were extremely so. An upturned tree observed on the Eckrant extremely stony clay provided
information on the profile at depths below those achieved shovel testing.
Geologically, the property is split between Early Cretaceous Comanchean limestone, shale, and mudstone
in the southern half of the project area, and Early Cretaceous limestone with dolomite and chert in the
northern half of the project area and the rest of the property (Stoeser et al. 2005, Barnes et al. 1992).

CULTURAL BACKGROUND
The project area is squarely within the Central Texas archaeological region as described by various
authors. This background will be presented in the final version of this report (the annual report for TAC
Permit 7531) and will not be repeated here.
Williamson County has a long, diverse history. The earliest known historic occupants of Williamson
County, the Tonkawas, were a flint-working, hunting people who followed the bison on foot. During the
eighteenth century they made the transition to a horse culture and used firearms to a limited extent. Later
decimated by diseases and warfare with Cherokees and Comanches, the Tonkawas were removed from
Central Texas by the 1850s. Lipan Apaches and Comanches were also associated with the area that would
become Williamson County. Before the arrival of Europeans in the area, the Apaches ranged through the
western part of present Williamson County, raiding Spanish missions established on the San Gabriel
River in the eighteenth century. The Comanches arrived in the area in the eighteenth century and lived in
parts of Williamson County until 1838. After they were pushed out by white settlements, the Comanches
continued to raid settlements in the county until the 1860s (Odintz, 2005).
The area was first explored by the Spanish in the late seventeenth century, when Captain Alonso De Leon
sought an alternate route between San Antonio and the Spanish missions in East Texas. Anglo settlement
began during the Texas Revolution and the early days of the Republic of Texas. In 1835, in an attempt to
strengthen the frontier against Indian attacks, a military post was built near the headwaters of Brushy
Creek. The post was abandoned in 1836, when its garrison was withdrawn to deal with the Mexican
invasion. However, two years later in 1838 the first civilian settlement was established by a party of
settlers who built a fort on Brushy Creek.
Several sites along Brushy Creek were settled soon after, but Indian raids kept white settlements from
expanding and a number of the early pioneers were killed by Indians over the next few years. In 1842
many of the early farms were abandoned when Governor Sam Houston advised settlers to pull back from
the frontier due to the Indian attacks in the area. The Indian threat eased after 1846, and part of the influx
of settlers who returned to the area came to the frontier along Brushy Creek and the San Gabriel River. In
1848 Williamson County was established with the city of Georgetown as county seat. Pioneers were
attracted by the abundance of timber and good, clear water, and the inexpensive and extremely fertile
land. The first wave of settlers moved from other southern states, particularly Tennessee, and a substantial
231

contingent came from Illinois. This latter group remained pro-Union and Republican in its political
orientation during the secession crisis (Odintz, 2005).
In the years before the Civil War, Williamson County had moved beyond the frontier stage and was a
populous, agriculturally diverse county. Agricultural pursuits were varied and reflected the countyâs
diversity. Farmers were using the rich blackland soils to grow wheat and corn. Cotton was introduced in
the 1850s but was not an important cash crop for most farmers. The early settlers had found large herds of
wild cattle in the 1840s, and cattle ranching was widespread throughout the county by 1860 (Odintz,
2005).
Political divisions marked Williamson County during the Civil War and Reconstruction. Unionist
sentiment was strong and resolution denouncing secession was adopted by a Texas Constitutional Union
party meeting in 1860. When the war began the majority of the citizens of Williamson County supported
the Confederate cause. While those who remained loyal to the Union fled to Mexico or the North, while
others enlisted in the Union army. Throughout the years of the war Unionists were persecuted and
arrested or hanged. The pattern of violence within the community continued into the summer following
the end to the Civil War. However, the county seems to have been spared much of the political and racial
strife that plagued other Texas towns during Reconstruction. On the other hand, there was a great deal of
crime, much of it violent, in the later nineteenth century. Horse and cattle thieves and some famous
outlaws preyed on citizens, along with family feuds and drunken drawls adding to the toll (Odintz, 2005).
The Civil War caused very little damage in the area, however the county was much poorer in 1870 than it
had been in 1860. Economic recovery was added by the growth of the cattle and sheep industries and a
dramatic expansion of cotton farming. The establishment of Southwestern University in 1873 and
construction of a railroad in 1878 contributed to the townâs growth and importance. Industry prior to the
1900s included limestone quarries; grist, flour, planning, and woodworking mills; cotton gins; brick, flue,
chair, and mattress factories; tin, pewter, blacksmith, saddlery, and shoe shops; a knitting mill, bakeries,
and confectioneries; the Texas Chautauqua Assembly, a bottling works, and an old mill (Scarbrough,
2005). Agricultural diversification during the middle twentieth century (decrease in cotton production and
an increase in corn, sorghum, wheat, livestock, and poultry farming) was followed by significant social
and economic changes, allowing Williamson County to grow and prosper in the twentieth century.

RESEARCH DESIGN AND FIELD METHODS
After reviewing the topography of the property and the anticipated project area, it was determined that
pedestrian survey coupled with shovel testing of areas with appropriate soil depth and conditions was the
best course of action. At the Lewis tract, the area south of the construction yard was surveyed by transects
with 15 meter intervals, and the construction yard was surveyed along its edges where lack of fill revealed
the original ground surface, and numerous recent disturbances provided excellent visibility. Shovel test
locations were initially selected based on the visual topography and soil conditions on the ground, and
later were placed near two areas where surface artifacts had been located, and along terrace edges where
further artifacts would be anticipated. In total, 14 shovel tests were completed within the project area.

232

Shovel tests achieved a mean depth of 22 cm, and all were negative for cultural material. Most were
excavated until a dark reddish brown clay layer was reached, usually below a layer of black clay.

RESULTS
One site was located during the survey of the Lewis tract. Site 41WM1333 is a lithic scatter occurring on
a high terrace to a major tributary to Brushy Creek, and adjacent to a former drainage, with another small
drainage just 130 m to the west (Figure 116). The site consists of just 7 artifacts; 1 thin biface fragment, 1
larges secondary flake, 1 biface thinning flake, 1 patinated tertiary flake, and 3 small tertiary flake
fragments (Figure 117). Shovel testing failed to locate any subsurface cultural material. All but one of the
artifacts was located in a cluster on the terrace edge, with the secondary flake being located on the terrace
slope.
While some chert is available on the property, none of the artifacts appeared to be of that same material.
No temporally diagnostic material was observed, and though the low quantity of artifacts would suggest a
single component, a lack of stratified deposits makes a determination about the number of components
impossible. The material observed was scattered in amongst gravels, some of which may have been
foreign gravels related to later-twentieth century road and building construction. The adjacent road and
former building areas may have concealed or destroyed additional areas of the site.
Given the low quantity of artifacts and condition of the site, LCRA Cultural Resources staff recommends
that 41WM1333 be considered ineligible for formal SAL status. No further work is recommended on the
site, and no restrictions should be imposed on future projects impacting 41WM1333.

SUMMARY AND RECOMMENDATIONS

LCRA Cultural Resource staff completed an intensive cultural resources survey of the proposed 2-6
substation project area and the associated property. One archaeological site, 41WM1333, was located
within the proposed substation project area. Site 41WM1333 is a minor lithic scatter that is recommended
as ineligible for SAL status, and it is recommended that, should 2-6 be selected as the site for the new
substation, the construction be allowed to proceed without restrictions in regards to 41WM1333.
Emergency discovery protocols would of course be in place during construction, and should any new
cultural material be discovered, work in the immediate area of the discovery would halt, and the THC
would be contacted to determine what, if any, avoidance or mitigation efforts would be necessary.
