Project 25.16: An Intensive Cultural Resources Survey of the LCRAâs Proposed Brush Clearing
Project on the North Shore of Lake Fayette, Fayette County, Texas

By Matthew E. Larsen and Steven R. Evans

Andrew F. Malof
Principal Investigator

TAC Permit 7531
Interim Report 6.16
July 2016

239

MANAGEMENT SUMMARY
The Lower Colorado River Authority (LCRA) is proposing to clear brush in fall 2016 on approximately
90 acres of land on the north shore of Lake Fayette (Figure 118), which are co-owned by LCRA and City
of Austin Utilities (COA). These lands lie between LCRAâs Oak Thicket and Park Prairie Parks, which
are connected by a 3 mile long hike and bike trail. Lake Fayette is the cooling reservoir for the Fayette
Power Plant, located on the southwest shore, which was built in the 1970s. As part of that project, LCRA
and COA bought, and now co-own, all the land parcels immediately bordering Lake Fayette.
In 1979, LCRA constructed the 87-acre Oak Thicket Park on the north shore and the nearby 12-acre Park
Prairie Park on the northwest shore near Park, Texas. In 1995, these two parks were connected by a 3mile-long greenbelt corridor and hiking trail running along the shoreline. LCRA cultural resources staff
conducted surface surveys and shovel testing for this project, including a 50-acre tract east of Oak Thicket
Park, and found no cultural material (Nightengale 1995).
In 2015, LCRA Cultural Resources staff conducted an intensive cultural resources survey for a brush
clearing project encompassing 123 acres in and around Oak Thicket Park. The 2016 survey brush clearing
is located immediately to the west of the 2015 brush clearing project. Both of these projects are part of a
comprehensive land management plan by the LCRA to increase the natural diversity of LCRA-owned
lands.
The current vegetative state in the 2016 project area is dense oak-elm woodland and oak-mesquite
woodland, with dense vines of green briar and mustang grape. The target vegetative state is tallgrass
prairie and tallgrass-oak savannah (Figure 119). Brush clearing methods may include: bulldozing brush
before stacking it to burn; mulching with a skid-steer-mounted Seppi mulcher and leaving mulch in place;
and hand-clearing and transporting brush to a safe place for burning.
LCRA cultural resources staff, under Texas Antiquities Committee Annual Permit No. 7531, conducted a
file search and fieldwork including targeted surveys of two areas known to have had historical structures
as well as surface survey of several upland ephemeral streams in the project area. LCRA Cultural
Resources staff surveyed a total of 14.3 acres after determining the other 75.7 acres to be of low
probability for cultural resources.
As a result of the 2016 field work, two new sites were recorded. Site 41FY588 is an early- to midtwentieth century farmstead, but is almost completely inundated by Lake Fayette, leaving only a few
fence posts standing at the shoreline. Site 41FY587 is also an early- to mid-twentieth century farmstead,
where numerous concrete features, as well as fences, livestock pens, and surficial material were observed.
Three shovel tests placed at areas of perceived high use were negative for subsurface material. Survey and
shovel testing adhered to THC minimum survey standards, as described below under the heading
Methodology.

240

Figure 118: 2016 brush clearing project area and locations of recorded sites; USGS topo 1:24,000.

241

Both recorded sites were deemed to be of low research value. Site 41FY587 is recommended ineligible
for formal designation as a State Antiquities Landmark (SAL), while site 41FY588 is recommended to
remain of undetermined eligibility. No prehistoric material was encountered. Due to the negligible
research value of the material recovered, LCRA Cultural Resources staff recommends approving the
entirety of the project area for mechanical brush clearing. While not required, LCRA Cultural Resources
will recommend, however, avoidance or other methods to avoid negative impacts on the sites within the
project area, in accordance with resource management goals on LCRA-owned and managed lands.
During an intensive cultural resources survey, such as this one, conducted under the aegis of LCRAâs
annual permit obtained from the THC, LCRA Cultural Resources maintains a no collection policy.
Project records will be submitted to an accredited curation facility.

ENVIRONMENTAL BACKGROUND
Much of the following Environmental Background is taken from the LCRAâs 2015 brush clearing project
at Oak Thicket Park (Larsen et al. 2015).
Located roughly halfway between Austin and Houston, and just northeast of La Grange, Lake Fayette was
formed in 1978 by damming Cedar Creek and encompasses the creek and several tributaries; the normal
pool level being stable at 391 feet msl. The shoreline has many peninsulas, which are the interfluvial
ridges of Cedar Creek and its tributaries. Additionally, the north shore is an upland interfluvial ridge
separating the Cedar Creek drainage in the south from the Clear Creek drainage in the north (Figure 118).

Figure 119: Previously cleared area in Oak Thicket Park, east of the 2016 project area.

Lake Fayette lies in the interior Gulf coastal plains physiographic region of Texas, as defined by the
Bureau of Economic Geology (1996). This region consists of calcareous marine sediments of the Miocene
242

age Goliad, Fleming and Oakville Formations as well as the Pliocene age Willis Formation (United States
Department of Agriculture, Natural Resources Conservation Service [USDA, NRCS] 2016). The soils
within the project area are mostly Hallettsville fine sandy loam, Straber loamy fine sand and Frelsburg
clay (USDA, NRCS 2016). These soils are on non-aggrading upland landforms and have a shallow
horizon of loam or sand before turning into dense mottled clay 20-35 cm below the surface. As such, they
are unlikely to hold buried, stratified prehistoric deposits. Historic deposits lie, typically, on or just under
the surface.
The historical climax plant communities (Stellbauer 2004, 2005) in these interfluvial uplands are tallgrass
prairie or tallgrass-oak savannah. Historical agricultural use, abandonment, and fire protection have left
the areas within the project area in an overgrown state of woodlands and shrublands with woody canopy
cover ranging from 40-80% with dense undergrowth. Common plant species include post oak, cedar elm,
netleaf hackberry, eastern red cedar, yaupon, greenbriar, prickly pear, silver bluestem, and woody vines,
such as rattan or grape. According to the ecological site description (Stellbauer 2004, 2005) these areas
provide habitat for deer, turkey, coyote, and many others, as well as bison, wolf, bobcat, mountain lion,
and black bear, historically.

CULTURAL BACKGROUND
Much of the following prehistoric and historic background information is taken from the LCRAâs 2015
brush clearing project at Oak Thicket Park (Larsen et al 2015).
Prehistoric Background
The project area is located in a portion of Texas occupied by nomadic, hunter-gatherer peoples throughout
all of the prehistoric era. The project area lies outside any of the traditional archeological regions defined
for the state. This is probably due to the fact relatively few surveys had taken place in Fayette County
when archeological regions for the state were first proposed in the 1950s. Presently, the Fayette County
area is marked as southeast of Prewittâs (1981) Central Texas Archaeological Region, north of Hesterâs
(1995) South Texas Archaeological Region, and slightly west of Pattersonâs (1995) Southeast Texas
Archaeological Region.
More recent data from large cultural resource management projects conducted in Fayette County show the
general area is located on the periphery of many projectile point style distributions, a cultural trait
traditionally used to define prehistoric cultural areas in Texas. However, beginning about 3,000 years ago,
many artifact types of the Central Texas region become common, and for that reason the culture
chronology of this region is utilized for this report. Therefore, a sequence of chronological periods that
borrows from work in Central Texas by Johnson and Goode (1994) and Prewitt (1981) is utilized for this
survey report. From earliest to latest in time, these chronological periods are as follows: Paleoindian (pre
6500 B.C.), Early Archaic (6500 to 3500 B.C.), Middle Archaic (3500 to 2200 B.C.), Late Archaic I
(2200 to 50 B.C.), Late Archaic II (50 B.C. to A.D. 700), Late Prehistoric (A.D. 700 to A.D. 1700), which
is divided into the Austin phase (A.D. 700 to 1250) and the Toyah phase (A.D. 1250 to 1700), and,
finally, Historic Indian (A.D. 1700 to 1875).
243

In their 1975 report detailing the results of the Fayette Power Project survey, Jackson and Skelton divided
prehistoric sites into two categories: upland sites and alluvial terrace sites. Upland sites were near
significant streams, usually near confluences, but elevated enough to avoid floodwaters (Jackson and
Skelton 1975:17). These sites were shallow and eroded and lacked time-diagnostic artifacts (likely due to
modern collectors) but seemed generally to be open campsites with large amounts of debitage. No upland
sites on Cedar Creek were recorded on the ephemeral drainages near the 2016 project area, but rather at
the more significant tributaries farther downstream near the dam.
Alluvial terrace sites were generally identified in cutbanks of streams and exhibited stratification due to
their location on aggrading landforms. These sites may have been attractive as sources of chert nodules
and river cobbles used in tool production and food processing. In these sites, Jackson and Skelton found
âextremely large quantities of thermally fractured and percussion-chipped detritusâ (1975: 18), though
time-diagnostics were only found with the chipped stone and not the burned rock, which they thought
may indicate some separation of activities and tool assemblages. During the Fayette Power Project
survey, time-diagnostic artifacts recovered ranged from the Middle Archaic to the Late Prehistoric Austin
phase.

Historic Background
According to the Handbook of Texas Online (Garrett 2010a) the following history of this part of Fayette
County extends back to the Spanish Colonial era. A Spanish trading route running from the Rio Grande to
southwestern Louisiana called La BahÃ­a ran through the area, crossing the Colorado River at La Grange.
The earliest known Anglo settlers were Aylett C. Buckner and Peter Powell, who settled in the area on the
Colorado River in 1819. The area was further settled by members of Stephen F. Austinâs first colony
beginning in 1822. Another notable settler in the area was Joseph Biegel, a German immigrant from
Alsace-Lorraine who bought a league of land in 1832, which lies just southwest of the 2016 project area.
During the Texas Revolution, settlers of Fayette County played a prominent role and the ferry across the
Colorado River, near modern La Grange and known as Burnamâs Crossing, was burned during the
Runaway Scrape.
Nassau Farm (Garrett 2010b) was established in 1843 in nearby Round Top by the Verein zum Schutze
deutscher Einwandrer in Texas, also known as the Adelsverein, a group of German noblemen who
promoted German immigration to Texas. The farm was a working plantation, including some 19 slaves,
and was originally meant as a base for arriving immigrants but was instead used as âa pleasure retreat for
the officers and as a hideaway from their creditorsâ (Garrett 2010b). The presence of the Adelsverein and
settlement by Germans on the Joseph Biegel league established a strong nascent German presence in the
area.
Prior to the Civil War, the economy of Fayette County was heavily dependent on plantations and slave
labor but, due to the changing agricultural economy during Reconstruction, the large plantations were
divided and sold, mainly to immigrant farmers, most of whom were Germans and Czechs (Garrett 2010a).
The German and Czech community on Joseph Biegelâs League and the surrounding area was known as
244

Biegelâs Settlement. Most of this community was inundated by the construction of Lake Fayette in the
late 1970s.
Immigration increased through the nineteenth century and by 1890 nearly one in four residents of Fayette
County was foreign born (Garrett 2010a). Throughout the twentieth century agriculture remained the
primary driver of the economy, though the emphasis shifted from cotton to cattle after the Great
Depression. Oil and gas leases also developed as a common source of income in the twentieth century.
The 2016 project area sits on a league of land (4428.4 acres) granted to J. M. Hensley in 1832, which lies
immediately north of the Biegel League. The Handbook of Texas Online (TSHA 2010) indicates Hensley
was one of Stephen F. Austinâs âOld Three Hundredâ settlers, and a member of Austinâs colony as early
as 1824. Records indicate Hensley, unlike Biegel, never resided on the tract bearing his name. At least as
early as 1834, the Hensley tract was divided into quarters, each of which was 1750 x 3571 varas and
encompassed an area of 1107.1 acres. From 1834 to the present, land in the Hensley league has been
repeatedly subdivided and sold.
The lands in the 2016 project area were previously owned by the Urban and Hilsher families and were
purchased by the LCRA and COA for the construction of Lake Fayette in the 1970s. Lake Fayette was
constructed as a cooling reservoir for the Fayette power plant, which resides on the lakeâs southern shore.
The LCRA/COA lands include the power plant, the cooling reservoir and the majority of the acreage on
the shore of Lake Fayette. When construction was completed, the Urban tract was almost entirely
inundated and the Hilsher Tract was substantially inundated, leaving only the southern and northern ends
above the water.

PREVIOUS INVESTIGATIONS
As this 2016 brush clearing project is in the immediate vicinity of a 2015 LCRA brush clearing project,
both of which projects are part of LCRAâs land management plan, much of the following is taken from
the 2015 project report (Larsen et al. 2015).
Until the advent of the CRM era beginning in the early 1970s, there were relatively few archaeological
investigations in the area surrounding what is now Lake Fayette. Since the 1970s, archaeological
investigation has usually been tied to construction projects like the Fayette Power Project and the aborted
Cummins Creek lignite mine project, which has led to a marked increase in the number of archaeological
sites. The following discussion of previous archaeological investigations in the area is largely summarized
from Nightengaleâs (1995) report concerning the construction of a greenbelt corridor between Oak
Thicket and Park Prairie parks.
The earliest archaeological investigations in the area were conducted by A.M. Wilson, who surveyed
portions of the Colorado River running though Travis, Fayette, and Bastrop counties in 1930.
Unfortunately, the documentation of Wilsonâs efforts is either lost or insufficient to allow a good
interpretation of his findings. In 1963, John P. Nunley conducted a reconnaissance survey of the proposed
Columbus Bend Reservoir and Colorado River from La Grange to about 20 km upstream of Columbus,
245

resulting in the documentation of 73 new sites. In 1968, Hester and Collins conducted a salvage
excavation of the Frisch Auf! site (41FY42). The site contained six burials, two of which included
identically produced Scallorn points, indicating that they dated to the Austin phase of the Late Prehistoric
period. In 1972, Meir and Hester investigated the Meier Site (41FY59), which had surface deposits of
Paleoindian artifacts.
Throughout the 1970âs, extensive investigations were undertaken to facilitate the construction of the
Fayette Power Plant and cooling reservoir by the Lower Colorado River Authority. Jackson and Skelton
(1975) surveyed 2,400 acres, and documented 19 prehistoric and 10 historic sites. The historic sites
primarily consisted of structures associated with the Biegel settlement. Site 41FY86 is a historic
homestead near the current project area, but not on of LCRA property. It consisted of a log cabin with
hand-adzed floor beams on cedar piers. No artifacts were found in association with the structure. Of the
prehistoric sites, site 41FY74 stands out as a single occupation butcher site of an individual bison dated to
the Toyah phase of the Late Prehistoric. Most other prehistoric sites were shallow lithic scatters
interpreted as temporary upland work camps, and occupied at various times over the last 8,000 years.
In 1991 and 1992, LCRA staff archaeologists, with the help of Texas Archeological Research Laboratory
(TARL) historian Dan Utley and TARL archaeologist David Robinson, completed surveys of several
project areas in the vicinity of Baylor Creek and Lake Fayette in order to facilitate the removal of several
trash dumps, clear a 30 acre area of brush, and remove two structures. The surveys for the trash dumps
and the 30 acre brush clearing project were both negative (Nightengale and Roemer 1995a: 60). The
investigations of the house structures (Nightengale and Roemer 1995b: 72) resulted in the recording of
two new historic sites, 41FY517 and 41FY518. The house at 41FY517 was built in 1907 but had been
extensively remodeled and had fallen into disrepair. The house at 41FY518 was a 1960s ranch house
located where a previous house, constructed around 1907, once stood. Both structures were deemed
ineligible for inclusion on the National Register of Historic Places and for SAL status and were removed.
Throughout 1995, LCRA archaeologists surveyed several small areas around Lake Fayette where well
pads had been proposed (Nightengale and Utley 1995). During one of these surveys, historic material was
noted and flagged for avoidance but the site was not formally recorded and a full investigation was set
aside for the future, as the site itself would not be impacted by well pad construction. This historic
material has been determined to be the same as that seen in the 2016 brush clearing survey and recorded
as site 41FY587. Five negative shovel tests were placed at the well pad site during the 1995 survey.
Also in 1995, a survey was conducted for the construction of a bike path paralleling the lake shore
between Oak Thicket and Park Prairie Parks (Nightengale 1995). A 30 meter wide corridor was surveyed
for the path along its route, including several shovel probes, and no cultural materials were observed. The
proposed 2016 brush clearing project abuts and surrounds much of this path corridor. The 1995 greenbelt
corridor project was the last investigation undertaken in this area until a 2015 LCRA brush clearing
project.
In 2015, as part of its ongoing lands management plan, the LCRA undertook a brush clearing project of
123 acres on lands including and immediately to the west of LCRAâs Oak Thicket Park on Lake Fayette.
Of these, 88 acres had been previously surveyed, leaving two areas of one acre each, and an area of 33
246

acres to be surveyed, all three of which had mapped historic structures. As a result of the 2015 survey
(Larsen et al. 2015), the vicinity of the western periphery of site 41FY86 was visited, two isolated historic
artifact scatters were discovered, and a new site, 41FY586, a historic trash scatter associated with a barn
location, was recorded.

RESEARCH DESIGN AND METHODS
Following a map and file review, it was determined that a research design consisting of identifying and
surveying high probability areas for any unknown archaeological site was appropriate for the project.
Two areas of high probability for historic materials, each approximately four acres in size, were
determined based on historical maps. The remaining 82 acres were determined to be of low probability
for both historic and prehistoric materials based on their location and soil composition. Drainages were
determined to be of low probability for prehistoric materials due to their location in the far uplands and
their ephemeral nature, as has been noted previously (Nightengale 1995:84, Jackson and Skelton
1975:17). A six acre area around the largest mapped drainages was submitted to surface survey to test the
predictive model.
Work was carried out by teams of two to three archaeologists conducting pedestrian survey accompanied
by standard shovel testing of high probability areas, in accordance with CTA standards of one shovel test
per 3 acres on a project area between 11 and 100 acres in size. Locational information was recorded on
GPS with typical 3 m accuracy. Shovel test data was recorded on standardized paper and electronic media
forms. No artifacts were collected.

RESULTS OF INVESTIGATIONS
After it was determined that the project area had a low probability for prehistoric materials, LCRA
Cultural Resources staff focused on two areas of high probability for historic material based on USGS
topo maps and previous investigations (Nightengale 1995). Cultural materials were observed at both high
probability areas, as a result of which two new sites were recorded, site 41FY587 and site 41FY588. Both
of these sites are early- to mid-twentieth century farmsteads.

Site 41FY587
Site 41FY587 is an early- to mid-twentieth century farmstead, approximately 330 ft long east-west and
nearly 330 ft wide north-south at the widest point (Figure 121). The 1981 USGS topo map of the site area
() indicates a short drive off a rural road which no longer exists. At the end of the drive to the east is a
house and across the drive to the west are two barns.

247

Figure 120: House and two barns mapped on 1981 USGS topo map.

The site consists of eight observed concrete features and relatively sparse surface artifacts, the majority of
which are sheet metal fragments ranging from 8 to 24 inches in size and concentrated at two or three
locations. Feature 1 () is a roughly 10 ft x 15 ft concrete foundation with a raised border and a 5 ft x 8 ft
small concrete slab ten feet to the west. At the corners of Feature 1 and in the center on the long axis are
piers of firebrick marked âT.F.B. Co.â There is a scatter of metal at the north corner, including ring pulltab cans, and a sign for RC Cola. Farther west is a fenceline in a very low fence dune with the remains of
a cedar post and barbed wire fence, which incorporates hog wire to the north. These fence remains
continue southwest and branch off to the east. This off-branch separates two small cedar and corrugated
metal animal shelters, of which the northern shelter has collapsed and the southern shelter (Figure 123)
remains standing.
Another small fenceline separates the shelters and runs to a larger concrete foundation feature, Feature 4.
This concrete foundation is divided by raised concrete thresholds into three partitions: two end partitions
of the same size and a larger central partition. Artifacts were observed at both the north and south ends of
the foundation. At the south end was a cobble marked with the number â4â, the purpose of which was not
readily discernable, and which may have been natural. At the north end of the foundation was a scatter of
metal, mostly sheet metal, but including a 1971 Texas license plate. Between Features 1 and 4 is a long 4
ft x 20 ft rectangular concrete slab, designated Feature 2, which exhibits the same orientation along the
long axis as Feature 1. To the west between Feature 4 and the fenceline, sits a round, approximately 6-ftdiameter concrete foundation with a raised collar, designated Feature 3 (Figure 124). This may be the

248

foundation for a silo, but is more likely for a cistern, based on cisterns of similar construction in the area
(Figure 125).
To the east, across the mapped drive, are more concrete features and artifact scatters. Approximately 100
ft southeast of Feature 4 is a concrete windmill pad designated Feature 5 (Figure 127). The bases of the
windmill frame struts remain at the corners of the pad and a date of July 25, 1925 is incised into the
concrete. Roughly ten feet north of Feature 5 is a small scatter of sheet metal and roughly fifty feet
southwest of the feature are a small milkglass sherd and a brown-glazed ceramic insulator for a
distribution power line marked âGE MADE IN USA.â This insulator consists of a hollow stem threaded
into a cap. Under the cap is a rubber gasket and a short length of the line remains protruding.

Figure 121: Sketch map of site 41FY587; well access path and gravel pad to east.

Approximately 25 feet east of Feature 5 is a 10 ft x 15 ft concrete foundation border, designated Feature
6. A shovel probe indicated the concrete is a raised border only with no interior slab. Approximately 3040 ft southeast of Feature 6 is a surface scatter of several pipe fragments, including: one Â½â diameter
threaded pipe protruding from the ground surface, a roughly one foot length of three-inch pipe, and
another length of the same size pipe threaded into a coupling. About 10 ft southeast of the pipe scatter
was a surface depression, which was at first thought to be a collapsed cistern. A shovel probe revealed the
area to consist of pea gravel fill, which may indicate a filled cistern, a large utilities trench of some sort,
or perhaps a septic feature.
Another 60-70 ft beyond the depression is a concrete step designated Feature 7. A whiteware fragment
with a scalloped interior base (Figure 126) was observed two feet southeast of the step. Another 40 ft
249

south of Feature 7 is another concrete step designated Feature 8. This step is of more primitive
manufacture than Feature 7 and appears to have been overturned. The step itself is to one side rather than
centered and appears to have been poured around a large dressed limestone block, using a lower quality
concrete. This was the last feature observed at site 41FY587.

Figure 122: Feature 1 facing southwest; note firebrick pier in the corner.

On-site work consisted of an initial surface survey through dense vegetation of thorny brush and vines to
discover the extent of the site. Once the surficial features and artifacts were recorded, three shovel tests
were placed at 30 meter intervals along the long axis of the site at the areas of estimated highest
likelihood for subsurface artifacts. Shovel test 1 was placed at the center of the concrete foundation
features to the northwest. Shovel test 2 was placed between Feature 5, the windmill pad, and Feature 6.
Shovel test 3 was placed at Feature 7 near the observed surface artifact. The shovel tests were all negative
and revealed the soil to be only about 8 cm of loamy topsoil over dense clay loam.
Once fieldwork was completed, more detailed research was undertaken of the location of site 41FY587
and the land upon which it sits. LCRA transaction records revealed the site to be on a nearly 200 acre
tract of land owned by the family of the late Charles Hilsher, 17 acres of which were purchased by LCRA
in 1975 for the construction of the Lake Fayette cooling reservoir for the Fayette Power Project. Research
into the Fayette County deed records in La Grange enabled LCRA Cultural Resources staff to construct a
history of the land.
Site 41FY587 sits on land originally granted in 1832 to John M. Hensley, a member of Stephen F.
Austinâs colony, from the Mexican government (Texas General Land Office [TxGLO] 2016). As
mentioned above, the Hensley League was divided up and sold by 1834. The title chain is somewhat
unclear after 1832, but the land was purchased sometime prior to 1866 by Christian and Ana Olmstead of
250

New York City, who appear to have been land speculators, as they owned and then sold numerous tracts
in the area at this time. In 1866, William and Henrietta Ratgen bought 79 Â¼ acres (vol. R, p. 401) and 12
acres (vol. T, p. 30) from the Olmsteads including improvements, which were not described. The Ratgens
owned the land for 24 years until they sold it to Ignaz Holub in 1890. Holub owned the land for 17 years,
during which time he donated 1/8 of an acre in the far northwest corner to the Hackebeil School (vol. 64,
p. 84). In 1907, Holub sold the land to Fritz Treybig Jr. (vol. 84, p. 122). The Treybigs were, at this time,
buying numerous abutting tracts in the area. After Treybig died, his heirs sold the land in 1945 to Joseph
LaCoke (vol. 198, p.493) who then sold the land in 1948 to Charles and Albina Hilsher (vol 226, p. 371).
This is the first of several land purchases the Hilshers make, mostly from various Treybigs, to consolidate
a nearly 200 acre tract of their own.

Figure 123: Animal shelter #2, facing west.

Figure 124: Feature 3, circular foundation, facing northwest. Machete in seam between inner and outer sections.

Figure 125: Metal cistern on circular concrete foundation to catch roof water; at house on nearby Urban tract.

Figure 127: Feature 5, Windmill foundation, facing southwest; water pipe in center; incised date is out of frame to
the left.

253

Charles Hilsher (originally HilÅ¡er) was born, according to U.S. Census records, in Bohemia (part of
Austria at that time) and immigrated to the United States in 1901 at the age of eleven. In 1910, he was
recorded as a servant in the Halamicek household. John Halamicek founded the town of Roznov around
1885, naming it after his birthplace (Carroll 2010). Charles married John Halamicekâs daughter, Albina,
in 1912 and they appear to have lived near Warrenton, based on U.S. Census records. By 1940 five of
their eight children were living in Houston and the youngest three children would soon follow. In late
1948, Charles and Albina bought the land on which site 41FY587 sits. They may have lived at the house
at site 41FY587 (Figure 128) for a time, but just over one year later they bought another house and
several acres of land adjacent to and south of their first tract. In late 1949 or early 1950, they moved into
this house at the southern edge of their land just off TX-159 in Park, Texas. The land where the southern
house was located is now LCRAâs Park Prairie Park, though the house was bought by the Hilsher children
and removed in the early 1980s.

Figure 128: House at site 41FY587 facing southwest; note windmill and roof water cistern; to the left is a small
chicken house.

Site 41FY587 consists of what was a second house to the north, with outbuildings. At the time LCRA
purchased the property from the Hilsher family, this house was rented to a farmhand, who managed the
livestock and land for the Hilshers. This house and the outbuildings were removed at an unknown time
after the LCRA purchased the land.
254

According to LCRA records, the house was built in 1935 and was of box construction with a corrugated
steel roof and a foundation of concrete blocks. The house plan is an L-shape, with 3 bedrooms, a hall, a
kitchen and pantry, a bathroom, a screened in porch, and an unfinished second story, except for the
flooring, used as a sleeping area. Feature 6, a rectangular concrete border of unknown function, seems to
have been located near the kitchen, as are the pipe remains. The covered front porch faced west to the
drive and there was a windmill (Feature 5) just to the north of the porch. The house had two steps, which
may be Features 7 and 8. If they are, they appear to have been moved to the south. Behind the house, to
the east, was a round roof water catchment cistern and a small chicken house. No observed features or
artifacts are associated with this area.
Across the drive were two structures, the northern was a barn for the storage of feed and stock, and the
southern was a slightly smaller barn and stock shed. Feature 1, the concrete foundation with brick piers
and a small slab immediately to the west, and Feature 2, a long rectangular concrete slab, appear to be at
the location of the northern barn. According to pictures taken in 1975, the slab appears to be a covered
porch-like area (Figure 129). Feature 4, the concrete slab with three partitions, appears to be at the
location of the southern barn (Figure 130). Feature 3, the circular concrete foundation, does not appear on
any records, but is likely for a cistern catching water off the roof of the southern barn.

Figure 129: North barn at site 41FY587 facing northwest from drive; south barn is out of frame to the left.

255

Figure 130: South barn at site 41FY587 facing northwest from drive near the house.

Finally, there was a stock shed and a granary or crib in the fields several hundred feet to the south, in the
vicinity of which no features or artifacts were observed. The outlines of these buildings are shown in
Figure 131.
Given the dates of construction for the house (1935) and the windmill foundation (1925) it seems
reasonable to assume the house and outbuildings may have been the residence of Fritz Treybig Jr. and his
family. Based on the history of this and surrounding tracts, this may have also been the location of Ignaz
Holubâs residence, though this is highly speculative.
The remains observed during the 2016 survey match well with the historical records. The locations of the
western features including the fences and pens match those of the two barns, including animal pens,
shelters and a cistern for water. The power distribution line insulator matches the mapped end of the drive
and seems a likely location for a distribution pole to the house and maybe to the barns. The pipe scatter,
surface depression, and concrete steps correspond with the house location, and two steps are noted in the
house plan. The lack of any large structures or structural debris, and lack of artifacts generally, indicates
the house and barns were likely removed intact or destroyed and the debris removed. Based on their
location it is likely the steps were pushed aside during the removal of the house. The time diagnostic
materials consisted of several ring pull tab cans, which were common from 1965 to 1985, and the 1971
Texas license plate, as well as the windmill foundation dated to 1925. This corresponds to records
indicating an early to late mid-twentieth century occupation.

256

Figure 131: Layout and outlines of buildings with corresponding observed features at site 41FY587, prior to
destruction; from LCRA archives.

The histories of the families who owned this land fit well into the historical narrative of post-Civil War
Czech and German immigration to the area. Late-nineteenth and early-twentieth century changes in
agricultural practices are also illustrated here, as large tracts of land were subdivided and sold to small
farmers, such as the Holubs and Treybigs. The movement of the Hilsher children away from the country
and into Houston is indicative of the broader mid-twentieth century trend of population movements from
rural areas to cities.
Site 41FY587 is in very poor condition overall. The site is perhaps 1-5 percent intact and overgrown with
dense vegetation. The cultural material present is very sparse and consists almost entirely of smallish
fragments of sheet metal of relatively recent age. The history of this area since 1925, the earliest
verifiable date of occupation, is already very well documented and site 41FY587 provides no new or
valuable information. The sole point of interest is that this land was once owned by Albina and Charles
Hilsher, the daughter and son-in-law of the founder of nearby Roznov, John Halamicek. Taking all factors
into consideration, LCRA Cultural Resources staff assesses site 41FY587 to be of low research value.
LCRA Cultural Resources recommends site 41FY587 be ineligible for formal SAL designation. No
avoidance measures or special considerations are required. LCRA Cultural Resources will recommend,
however, against the use of large mechanical brush clearing methods in order to prevent unnecessary
negative impact to remaining features.

257

Site 41FY588
Site 41FY588 is an early- to late-mid twentieth century farmstead site and is approximately 700 ft long
northeast to southwest and 450 wide northwest to southeast (Figure 132). The site sits on the very tip of a
peninsula jutting out into the Lake Fayette cooling reservoir. The site boundaries encompass observed
artifactual material and mapped structure locations just offshore. LCRA Cultural Resources staff believe,
based on historical USGS topo maps and LCRA archived records, it is likely the site may even extend
several hundred feet farther to the south and southwest. This area is under Lake Fayetteâs maintained pool
level of 390 feet msl, however, and is therefore unavailable for survey.

Figure 132: Sketch map of site 41FY588, including mapped historic structures, now inundated.

The observed material at site 41FY588 consisted of three standing cedar fence posts (Figure 133) trending
east to west and a few clear flat glass fragments next to the east-most fence post. The fence posts had wire
nails in them and the flat glass appeared to be only lightly patinated.
USGS topo maps from 1981 and 1958 indicate the presence of at least a house and a barn prior to
inundation. LCRA archives show a house (Figure 135) built in 1935, a barn built in 1940, a garage or
shop built in 1950, two chicken houses, and several other outbuildings (Figure 134). The five mentioned
buildings all had metal roofs and concrete foundations. The buildings were most likely removed prior to
inundation to prevent them creating an underwater hazard and also because, given the gentle slope at this
location, they would have likely been only partially inundated, leaving the roofs above water. When the
LCRA bought the land in 1975, the farm was described as âthe most versatile farming operation in the
258

area, having cattle, hogs, turkeys, ducks, geese, guineas, fruit trees (peach, plum, and pear), pecan trees,
and timber for use on the farm â¦ and has two very fine, clear water, stock water pondsâ (LCRA
1974:20). At that time, the land belonged to Steve C. and Gladys Mae Urban, who had owned it since
1950, when they bought it from Alvin L. Treybig (vol. 241, p. 445) with the help of the Slavonic
Benevolent Order of the State of Texas (SPJST), in the form of a deed of trust, which is similar to a
mortgage.

Figure 133: Site 41FY588, facing south; two of three remaining cedar fence posts in center; note proximity to water
and washed up debris.

The title goes back to 1832 when John M. Hensley received a league of land, as discussed above. In 1834,
after the Hensley League was quartered, George W. Barnett purchased two tracts: the western quarter
league and a large portion of the eastern quarter league encompassing 731 acres. Barnett appears to be
George Washington Barnett, who was a prominent early Texan. According to the following from the
Handbook of Texas Online (Kemp 2010), Barnett was born in South Carolina in 1793, studied medicine
and moved to Texas in 1834. He was involved in the founding of the Municipality of Washington in
1835, was active during the Texas Revolution, and was a senator in the Republic of Texas. He was killed
by Lipan-Apache while deer hunting near Gonzales in 1848. The records are unclear, but the land seems
to have been divided and conveyed among various members of the Barnett family until 1891.
259

In 1891, the land appears to have left the Barnett family as a result of a court case. By this time the tract
on which site 41FY588 sits had been whittled down to 94 acres. A judgement was rendered in 1891 in the
case of M.H. Parr and Wife vs. Frank Barnett et al. (case no. 4395) regarding the 94 acre tract. Records
indicate that M.H. Parr may have been Milligan Henry Parr who married Sara Evaline Barnett, and that
Frank Barnett et al. may have been their children, Frank, Laura, and Sam, using their motherâs maiden
name. Court minutes did not detail the particulars of the case but the decision of the court was to create a
commission to sell the 94 acres and award one quarter of the proceeds to each of the three Barnetts and
the final one quarter to M.H. Parr and his wife. As a result of this, the land was purchased in 1893 by
Joseph Orsak (vol. 48, p. 77) who then sold it to Frank Orsak in 1894 (vol. 55, p. 423). In 1896, Frank
Orsak sold the land to Peter Kulhanek (vol. 58, p. 4) who owned the tract for 16 years. Either in 1915 or
previously, Kulhanek subdivided the 94 acre tract, and, in 1915, sold 63 5/8 (63.63) acres to Emil and
Mary M. Prasifka (vol. 100, p. 240). According to his World War One draft registration papers, Emil was
working land near Park, Texas, which is most likely these 63.63 acres, but by 1919 he sold the tract to
Edward and Elizabeth Knippel (vol. 109, p. 490) and moved to nearby Fayetteville. The Knippels owned,
and presumably worked, the land for 27 years until 1946, when they sold it to Alvin L. Treybig (vol. 202,
p. 133), who sold it in 1948 to the Urbans, as mentioned above.

Figure 134: Layout and outline of structures on Urban tract prior to destruction and inundation. Unfortunately, what
the structures are (barn, house, etc) were not recorded.

260

It is clear from the title chain that this 63.63 acre tract changed hands many times since 1832. It is
tempting to conclude that all those who owned it for only a short time were land speculators, but the
experience of Emil Prasifka indicates that even a short term owner may have been personally working the
land before deciding to sell it. It appears that the majority of the improvements at site 41FY588 were
made by the Knippels and the Urbans, although these may have built on improvements by previous
owners, who possibly resided at this location. The scant archaeological remains support a relatively
modern early- to late-mid twentieth century interpretation of the site. It is currently impossible, or at least
infeasible, to submit the inundated portions of the site to further archaeological investigations.

Figure 135: Urban house, constructed in 1935, at site 41FY588, facing northeast; house was of box construction
with a metal roof and a concrete foundation.

The 63.63 acre Urban tract, upon which site 41FY588 sits, has, aside from a possible connection to
George Washington Barnett, no other connection to prominent historical figures or events. It is interesting
to note, however, that a Charles Hackebeil was a member of the 1891 commission appointed to execute

261

the decision in the case of M.H. Parr and Wife vs Frank Barnett et al., and that he may have had a role in
the creation of the Hackebeil School mentioned above.
As with site 41FY587 to the northwest, the history of the owners of the land on which site 41FY588 is
located, is illustrative of late-nineteenth century immigration of German and Czech small farmers into the
region. The sparse material observed at site 41FY588 is, however, of a later, mid-twentieth century date
and provides no new or important information into this well-documented time period.
The portions of the site available for survey are in very poor condition, with less than 1 percent of the site
intact. The scant archaeological materials and the well documented history of the area lead LCRA
Cultural Resources staff to assess the exposed portion of 41FY588 to be of little research value. The
current, formal boundaries of site 41FY588 are restricted to the dry land surveyed in 2016, but the
majority of the site lies underwater and therefore unavailable for assessment. This fact leads the LCRA
Cultural Resources staff to recommend that site 41FY588 remain of undetermined eligibility for formal
designation as an SAL, until such time as it can be assessed for materials predating the turn of the
century.
Although the majority of site 41FY588 lies too close to the shoreline to be affected by the 2016 brush
clearing project, LCRA Cultural Resources staff recommend no special considerations or avoidance
measures be required for any area of the site that may be cleared of vegetation.
Other Surveyed Areas
LCRA Cultural Resources staff surveyed areas (Figure 136) around three mapped drainages, totaling six
and a half acres, in order to assess the nature of the drainages and to test the predictive model used in the
research design. Survey confirmed these to be ephemeral drainages on non-aggrading landforms in an
upland setting on sediments of Miocene and Pliocene age. No shovel tests were placed due to ground
surface visibility averaging between 75 percent and 100 percent. No prehistoric materials were observed.
Several cedar fence posts were observed at the largest drainage and were determined to be associated with
a mid-twentieth century farmstead located outside LCRA lands. With no other materials observed, these
fence remains were considered to be an isolated find.

262

Figure 136: Surveyed areas around drainages; these ephemeral upland drainages had shallow soils and excellent
visibility.

SUMMARY AND RECOMMENDATIONS
In June of 2016, LCRA Cultural Resources staff completed an intensive survey in advance of a proposed
brush clearing project set for the fall of 2016 on approximately 90 acres of LCRA/COA owned lands in
Fayette County on the north shore of Lake Fayette between LCRAâs Oak Thicket and Park Prairie Parks.
A file and map search located two high probability areas of approximately 4 acres each to be surveyed,
based on historic structures plotted on USGS topographic maps. Several upland ephemeral streams in the
project area were also subjected to surficial survey for prehistoric material. The total area surveyed
comprised 14.3 acres. The remaining 75.7 acres were determined to be of low probability for both
prehistoric and historic material and were not surveyed.
Background research and previous investigations indicated a high likelihood of recovering historic
artifacts, due to mapped structures in the project area and because of the long history of settlement in the
area by Europeans beginning in the 1830s. Research also indicated a low likelihood of recovering any
significant prehistoric artifacts, due to the upland non-aggrading nature of the area and the relatively long
distance from significant prehistoric water sources, near which prehistoric sites are often found (Collins
2004, Jackson and Skelton 1975, Nightengale 1995:84). As a result of the 2016 survey two new early- to
late-mid twentieth century farmstead sites were recorded, site 41FY587 and site 41FY588.

263

The two areas of high probability for historic cultural materials were determined based on mapped
structures in the project area. The northern high probability area sits between a disused well pad to the
south and a disused gravel lot to the east. Historic material was noted during the 1995 well pad survey
(Nightengale 1995) and flagged for avoidance but not formally recorded. During the 2016 survey of
roughly 3.75 acres in this area, eight concrete features, several fencelines and fence posts, two animal
shelters, and surficial artifacts, mostly sheet metal, were observed. This historic farmstead was designated
site 41FY587 and the site boundaries were based on the extent of observed surface materials. Three
shovel tests were placed in a northwest to southeast line and spaced approximately 30 m apart at the
locations of estimated highest use. All three shovel tests were negative and revealed an 8 cm topsoil A
horizon over a dense clay loam B horizon. Site 41FY587 was determined to be of low research value and
recommended ineligible for formal designation as an SAL. No special considerations or avoidance
measures are deemed necessary at this site.
The southeastern high probability area is mapped right at the shoreline of the Lake Fayette cooling
reservoir and consists of a house and a barn. LCRA Cultural Resources staff surveyed an area of roughly
4 acres between the inter-park trail and the shoreline. At the mapped site it was determined that the
majority of the site lay under the waters of Lake Fayette. Only three cedar fence posts with wire nails and
s small scatter of very lightly patinated clear flat glass was observed at the shoreline. No shovel tests were
placed due to the water-logged soil and the lack of observed surficial materials. Additionally, much
material had washed up on the shore over the years, destroying any clear distinction between any possibly
in situ materials and flotsam.
Due to the observed scant fence remains and the mapped inundated structures it was decided to designate
this inundated historic farmstead as site 41FY588. The site boundary was set as a circle with a diameter of
250 feet, centered on the fence posts, and encompassing the dry land portions of the site. Because the
majority of the site is underwater and unavailable for survey at present, LCRA Cultural Resources
assesses site 41FY588 to be of low research value but recommends it should remain of undetermined
eligibility for designation as an SAL. No special considerations or avoidance measures are deemed
necessary for the present project.
During the 2016 survey, only mid twentieth century historic materials of low research value and
absolutely no prehistoric materials were recovered. Taken in conjunction with previous investigations,
especially the 1995 greenbelt corridor and gas well projects, the 2016 survey resulted in the
recommendation by LCRA Cultural Resources staff that no avoidance measures or special conditions be
required and the brush clearing project should be allowed to proceed using mechanical methods,
including the use of bulldozers and skid-steers mounted with shears or Seppi mulchers. LCRA Cultural
Resources staff will, however, recommend voluntary avoidance or less destructive mechanical clearing
methods at site 41FY587 in accordance with cultural resource protection and management goals on
LCRA-owned and managed lands. There is nothing to avoid at 41FY588.

Project 30.16: Intensive Cultural Resources Assessment of LCRAâs Proposed T-233 Henne-toMcCarty Lane Transmission Line Rebuild Project, Comal and Hays Counties, Texas

By Steven R. Evans and Michael Smith

Andrew F. Malof
Principal Investigator

TAC Permit 7531
Interim Report 7.16
September 2016
Lower Colorado River Authority

269

MANAGEMENT SUMMARY
The Lower Colorado River Authority (LCRA) is proposing a tear down and rebuild of its existing T-233
Henne-to-McCarty Lane transmission line, which runs from the Henne Substation to the McCarty Lane
East Substation in eastern Comal County and southern Hays County, Texas (Figure 137 and Figure 138).
The transmission line is approximately 6.5 miles (10.5 kilometer [km]) in length and occupies a 100-feet
(ft) (30 meter [m]) -wide easement, totaling almost 80 acres. The project will consist of the removal and
replacement of 38 existing support structures. The new structures will be placed within approximately 3
m of the current locations within undisturbed soils. A 30- to 36-inch auger will be employed to drill the
new holes to depths of over 6 ft. Other impacts may involve the modification of the banks of intermittent
streams to improve existing vehicle crossings.
Roughly 1.3 miles (2.1 km) of the 100-ft-wide easement, totaling 15.8 acres, have been surveyed
previously, so a cultural resources assessment was necessary for the remaining distance. The assessment
consisted of a map and file review followed by an intensive cultural resources survey of high probability
areas (HPA), both prehistoric and historic, that could be impacted by construction activities.
Approximately 1.1 linear miles (1.8 km) of 100-ft-wide right of way, totaling 13.7 acres, was identified as
HPA. Nine proposed tower locations are located within these areas, and were surveyed by LCRA Cultural
Resources staff on September 1, 2016, using standard survey and subsurface testing methods. The Texas
Historical Commissionâs minimum state survey standards were not met due to the paucity of soils, high
surface visibility, and the targeting of proposed areas to be subjected to subsurface disturbances.
One previously undocumented archeological site, 41HY533, was recorded during the
course of the survey. Site 41HY533 represents the partial remains of a historic
farmstead likely dating from the late nineteenth to mid-twentieth centuries. The
portion of the site recorded within the right of way is recommended as ineligible for
formal SAL status, while the status of portions of the site outside the right of way
remain unknown.
The cultural resource investigations were conducted under the terms of LCRAâs annual blanket State
Antiquities Permit 7531 in accordance with the Antiquities Code of Texas and the Memorandum of
Understanding between the Texas Historical Commission and LCRA. There is no known or anticipated
federal funding or permitting involved with this project. No artifacts were collected during the project;
project records will be submitted to an accredited curation facility.

ENVIRONMENTAL BACKGROUND
The project area is located in eastern Comal County and southern Hays County, Texas, southwest of San
Marcos, Texas. The project is located within the York Creek and Caney Creek drainages, parts of the
larger Guadalupe River watershed. Elevations within the project area range between 559 and 683 ft
above).

270

Figure 137. The southern portion of the LCRA T-233 transmission line on a 1:24,000 scaled section of the Hunter USGS 7.5â
Quadrangle.

271

Figure 138. The northern portion of the LCRA T-233 transmission line on a 1:24,000 scaled section of the Hunter and San Marcos
South USGS 7.5â Quadrangles.

272

mean sea level. Vegetation in the area is typified by cropland at the juncture of the Blackland Prairie and
the Edwards Plateau (McMahan et al. 1984
Surface geology in the upland areas along the transmission corridor consists of Upper Pleistocene-aged
Pecan Gap Chalk, Upper Taylor Marl, and Fluviatile terrace deposits (Barnes 1974). Soils of the HeidenHouston Black associations are found throughout the project area (Batte 1984). Within the HPAs, these
are dominated by the Heiden, Houston Black, and Tinn series. Heiden clays are nearly level to moderately
steep and are found on base slopes and interfluve shoulders. These dark grayish brown clays formed a
clayey residuum weathered from mudstone and have an A-horizon that extends to a depth of 46
centimeters (cm) below ground surface. Soils of the Houston Black series formed in Cretaceous-age
clayey residuum and are found on interfluves and upland ridges. The A-horizon typically measures 20 cm
in depth, terminating in basal clay. In contrast, Tinn clays occur on nearly level floodplains, having
formed in calcareous clayey alluvium. They possess Holocene-age deposits approximately 46 cm in
thickness.
The T-233 transmission line was originally constructed in 1935, and the 100-ft-wide right of way has
since been modified and cleared of major vegetation. Visible impacts to the project area are
predominantly plowing and terracing of cropland, land clearing around rural residences, and the original
land clearing and ongoing maintenance activities along the transmission line corridor. Although the right
of way is subjected to periodic maintenance, it is currently overgrown with low to medium grasses and
ubiquitous young mesquite bushes

PREVIOUS INVESTIGATIONS
Hays and Comal counties presently have over 500 and 300 recorded archaeological
sites, respectively (Texas Archeological Sites Atlas), which can safely be described as
encompassing all presently recognized cultural periods. Several cultural resources
projects have been conducted within 0.6 miles (1 km) of the current project area.
The most pertinent of these is the 2002 survey of the Henne Spur Transmission Line (T-233) in Comal
and Hays counties conducted by LCRA (Prikryl and Goode 2002). This consisted of intensive cultural
resource survey of select portions of the T-233 transmission line having moderate to high probabilities for
cultural resource sites. Two new sites, 41CM250 and 41CM251, were recorded, both comprising light
lithic scatters consisting of chert debitage observed at surface to a depth of 30 cm below ground surface.
Both sites were determined ineligible for listing on the NRHP or as SALs.
The HPAs covered within the current report have not been subjected to cultural resources investigations.
No sites were previously identified within these HPAs. Table 17 summarizes known archaeological sites
within 1 km of the project area.

CULTURAL BACKGROUND
Prehistoric
The project area is considered to be in the central Texas archeological region (Ellis et al. 1995). Central
Texas prehistory has typically been divided into four main subdivisions, known variably as eras or
periods, which are subdivided further into phases or subperiods. These subdivisions combine to begin to
describe 11,500 years of history. A longstanding chronology of Prewitt (1981, 1985) has been revised by
Johnson and Goode (1994), who utilized new lines of evidence based on climatic and geomorphological
data to redefine period divisions. This can be contrasted with Collins (1995), who indicates climatic
variables at times were somewhat different than those of Johnson and Goode. A more detailed prehistoric
culture history will be with the final version of this report.
Historic
From the late seventeenth through the mid-nineteenth centuries, the present San
Marcos area lay on the Camino de los Tejas and the Camino de Arriba routes of the
Camino Reales (Kings Highways) that connected San Antonio with newly established
settlements in east Texas. The first Europeans to describe the San Marcos area in
much detail were the members of the Espinosa-Olivares-Auguirre Expedition of 1709
(Gonzales 1982). Later, the San Xavier mission and presidio were briefly located at the
San Marcos springs in 1755 and 1756. However, the first serious Spanish attempt at a
more permanent settlement at San Marcos did not occur until 1808 and it ended in
failure several years later (Cecil and Greene 1996:520). Subsequently, permanent
settlement was realized in the 1820s and 1830s when the Mexican government issued
land grants to a number of individuals. For example, in 1835 Thomas G. McGehee
acquired a Spanish land grant and began farming north of San Marcos.
274

Hays County was established in 1848 from land carved out of the existing Travis County, and named for
Texas Ranger Jack C. Hays (LCRA files). The countyâs population was over 2,000 by 1860, and rose to
over 14,000 by 1900. Agriculture and ranching have always been the predominate industries throughout
the history of the county, but beginning in the 1960s educational facilities such as Southwest Texas State
University and Gary Job Corps began playing an increasing role in the economic base of the area. Within
the area of the future Comal County, the short-lived Nuestra Senora de Guadalupe Mission was
established by the Spanish in 1756 (Greene 2016). Later, the area was largely settled by Germans, and
Comal County was formally established in 1846. While the historically dominant industries are
agriculture and ranching, tourism has become a primary industry in the modern era.
Historic site types in the Hays and Comal counties area include home, farm, and ranch-steads, Spanish
military, ecclesiastic, and civil settlements, engineering features, battle sites, industrial sites, as well as
trash dumps and debris scatters (Jurgens 1996:5).

METHODOLOGY
The project was designed to identify and assess archaeological sites that might be
eligible for formal listing as State Antiquities Landmarks (SAL). All work was
conducted under blanket TAC Permit 7531 and terms of the memorandum of
understanding between THC and LCRA, which incorporates CTA survey and reporting
standards.
Following a review of geology, soils, recent and historical maps, and previous investigations, 1.1 linear
miles (1.8 km) of HPA were identified for potential prehistoric and historic sites. Prehistoric HPAs were
based on proximity to natural water resources and topography, and historic HPAs were based on aerial
photographs and historic map research. HPAs were covered by pedestrian survey, with subsurface testing
conducted within 3 m of the existing tower locations within those areas.
Shovel tests were excavated in 20-cm levels to subsoil or a depth of 120 cm, whichever was reached first.
Soils were screened through 0.25-inch mesh unless dominated by clay, in which case they were sorted by
hand. Artifacts were recorded, but were not collected. Instead, artifacts were reinterred within the shovel
test following completion.

RESULTS
Portions of the T-233 transmission line possessing a high probability for encountering cultural resources
sites were surveyed by LCRA archaeologists on September 1, 2016. Four areas of prehistoric HPA and
two areas of historic HPA were identified prior to the beginning of fieldwork. These HPAs were
subjected to surface observation, and the nine proposed transmission line structure locations within them
were given particular focus. Shovel tests were not conducted where stream cutbanks provided clear
profile views of the soil profiles, or where ground disturbance was significant. In total, eight shovel tests
275

were excavated, only two of which were positive for cultural materials. These were located on a newly
recorded historic archaeological site, 41HY533.
41HY533
Overview
Site 41HY533 is located within an historic HPA based on the 1931 update of the 1911 USGS San Marcos
topographic map. The map shows two structures in the area of the site, and a house adjacent to the
transmission line appears to be old enough to be at least one of the structures represented. A later review
of maps indicated that the structures are depicted on the 1911 San Marcos topographic map as well. The
site boundary was generated based on the distribution of surficial artifacts, features, and major ground
disturbance from a buried pipeline (Figure 139). The site measures approximately 60 m north to south and
35 m east to west within the right of way, continuing east and west beyond the right of way boundary.
The site is topographically situated on an upland area, with drainages 145 m to the northeast and 410 m to
the southwest. The right of way has a layer of mulched vegetation on the surface and scattered as much as
10 to 20 cm below surface, indicating that right of way maintenance and brush clearing has been ongoing
for some time. Tall grass and thistle make up much of the rest of the ground cover, leaving low visibility
across much of the site. Mesquite trees are abundant and form thickets along the eastern limits of the right
of way. Soils on the site are mapped as belonging to the Houston Black series of clays (Batte 1984), with
basal soils appearing just below the plow zone.
The site consists of a mortared fieldstone and cement collar to either a well or a cistern (Figure 140), a
small scatter of dry-pressed handmade bricks (Figure 141), a buried scatter of cut nails and flat glass, and
sparse surface material (including a piece of stoneware and a fragment of solarized (amethyst) glass
visible on an adjacent dirt track road. Remnants of a fence exist to the east of the stone collar. The collar
has a soft cement or lime mortar top, in which the date "Mar 1942" is scratched, though it is unknown
whether this represents the initial construction date or the date that the concrete cap was added. The
presence of solarized glass and cut nails indicates a very late nineteenth or very early twentieth century
occupation, and this is supported by the handmade bricks. The site very likely extends beyond the right of
way to the northwest and southeast. Other than the brick and nails, no other structural remnants were
located within or immediately adjacent to the right of way. Aerial imagery appears to show other possible
features to the southwest, and an old structure 60 m to the northwest; however, these lie outside the
present right of way and were therefore not evaluated.
Four shovel tests were conducted, indicating buried material in the immediate area of the stone collar
down to 40 cm below ground surface, but the deposits were also clearly disturbed. The artifacts all appear
to be within the plow zone, which overlies undisturbed pre-Holocene soils. A buried pipeline, which
appears to follow the T-233 transmission line for some distance, has a cement surface component to the
immediate north that appears to have caused major ground disturbance. The siteâs north termination was a
result of this disturbance, and there is a lack of surface material visible beyond it

276

Figure 139. An aerial map of site 41HY533 in the T-233 right of way, showing shovel tests, features, and surficial artifacts.

277

Figure 140. The mortared fieldstone and cement collar in site 41HY533, with a 1 meter scale bar.

Figure 141. Some of the handmade bricks present at 41HY533

278

Archival Research
Deed research indicates that the land is part of the Vanhorn survey, which was originally granted to
William H. Vanhorn (sometimes recorded as Van Horn) in 1838, Mr. Vanhorn being a single male who
arrived in Texas before 1836. (Texas General Land Office, Hays County A-464). In many later deeds, Mr.
Vanhorn is recorded as having the property patented to him in December of 1847. While records could
not be located to indicate who owned the property in the intervening years, in 1869 part of the property is
recorded as having been sold by Elizabeth and Francis Flournoy to a Benjamin Netherland, and the deed
appears to indicate that Mr. Netherland had owned the property before and was repurchasing it from the
Flournoys (Hays County deed records G:556). Another Deed record from 1871 shows that Benjamin
Netherland gave 50 acres (later described as 54.73 acres) to his son, Joseph H. Netherland, and that the
land was part of Benjaminâs homestead (Hays County deed records G:133-134). US Census records
indicate that Benjamin Netherland was born in Kentucky, and was in Kentucky during the 1850 US
Census, but was in Hays County and married to Rebecca Netherland during the 1860 US Census.
The section of the property given to Joseph H. Netherland was bought in 1910 by a Will. G. Barber from
an M. R. Netherland (Hays County deed records v58:293), who then sold it (at an unknown date) to W. C.
Richards (Hays County deed records v110:402-404). The other part of the property in question was sold
in two parts by a J. B. and Belle Netherland to Enoch and Annie Wilson in 1915 (Hays County deed
records v68:332), and to L. G. and Katherine Johnson in 1916 (Hays County deed records v70:335). L. G.
Johnson bought the portion owned by the Wilsons in November of 1916 (Hays County deed records
v70:402). W. C. Richards bought these two portions from the Johnsons in 1920 (Hays County deed
records v81:102), and as mentioned previously, also purchased adjoining property from Will. G. Barber.
Later deeds refer to these three tracts within the property and describe them together as being 170 acres of
land from the William Vanhorn Patent.
In 1935, following a court case against W. C. and Alma Richards by an Iowa based insurance company,
Bankers Life, local Sheriff M. M. Wren seized and auctioned the 170 acres, where it was purchased by
the Bankers life Company (Hays County deed records v110:402-404). LCRA internal files indicate that
the Texas Power and Light Company purchased a transmission line easement on the property from the
Bankers Life Company in 1935, and this easement is the same that is owned by LCRA today. In June of
1940, Bankers Life sold the property to Claude L. and Ola Harris for $20,000 (Hays County deed records
v120:490-492), and they owned the property until 1955 (Hays County deed records v165:634)

Conclusion and Recommendations
The artifactual material would appear to indicate a turn of the century occupation, which would indicate
that the material was deposited while the Netherland Family owned the property, and may even be related
to Benjamin Netherlandâs homestead, or that of one of his sons. The 1942 date on the cement of the well
or cistern would indicate that it was either built or modified when the Harrisâs owned the property.
While 41HY533 is located in the T-233 right of way corridor, there are currently no transmission line
structures within the defined site boundary, and the proposed project does not include placing one within
279

the boundary. While transmission line work crews will pass through the site, there should be little to no
impact to features and deposits. Because of clear disturbance of subsurface deposits and the sparse nature
of the cultural material, the primary research value of the site within the right of way is locational.
Additionally, the archival research has found no indication that site 41HY533 is associated significantly
with any historical event, famous person, or important principle or ideal. For these reasons, LCRA
Cultural Resources staff recommends that site 41HY533 be considered ineligible for formal SAL status
within the right of way. Portions of the site that extend beyond the right of way were not investigated, and
are recommended as being of undetermined SAL status.

SUMMARY
LCRA Cultural Resources staff conducted an intensive cultural resources assessment of 1.1 linear miles
(1.8 km) of HPA in preparation for the proposed upgrade of transmission line T-233. The project operated
within a 100-ft-wide right of way and covered an area of 13.7 acres. The assessment located and recorded
one previously undocumented cultural resource site. Site 41HY533 is a historic site represented by a stone
cistern or well and a thin scatter of domestic and construction debris found on the surface and within the
plow zone. Due to the paucity of artifacts, the poor preservation of site integrity, the shallow soils, and
archival research, the portion of site 41HY533 within the current right of way does not appear to meet the
criteria for designation as an SAL.
LCRA Cultural Resources staff considers the rebuild of T-233 transmission line to be fully investigated.
Because no SAL-eligible sites will be impacted by construction activities staff recommends that all
currently proposed work be allowed to proceed without restrictions. Standard emergency discovery
procedures will be in place, and should any previously undiscovered cultural resources be discovered in
the course of the rebuild, work in the immediate vicinity of the discovery will cease, and LCRA Cultural
Resources staff will be contacted to determine what, if any, mitigation efforts are necessary.

Project 32.16: Intensive Cultural Resources Assessment of LCRAâs Proposed Shaffer Bend
Capital Improvement Plan Project, Burnet County, Texas

Andrew F. Malof

Lower Colorado River Authority
