285

MANAGEMENT SUMMARY
The Lower Colorado River Authority (LCRA) is proposing to encourage the use of the Shaffer Bend
Recreation Area as a destination park. The project will consist of two primary components: the
development of a 22-acre recreation area and the installation of a 1 kilometer (km) (0.6-mile) water
pipeline to the recreation area from the parkâs entrance. Anticipated impacts within the recreation area
will include extensive vegetation clearing, some minimal grading by heavy machinery, and the
construction of 50 concrete RV pads with associated utilities. While the exact depths of impacts have not
yet been determined, they are anticipated to be less than 0.9 meters (m) (3 feet [ft]) below ground surface.
The water pipeline is also anticipated to extend to a maximum of roughly 0.9 m (3 ft) in depth (Figure
142).
Fieldwork was conducted on three non-consecutive days (September 8, 12, and 14, 2016) by a crew of
three to four LCRA archaeologists. Two previously documented archeological sites, 41BT68 and
41BT483, were revisited during the present survey. Only surface evidence of prehistoric site 41BT68 was
found during the current survey on denuded slopes; the majority of the site within LCRA property has
been heavily dissected by drainages. Prehistoric site 41BT483 was relocated, and its boundaries were
extended based on the results of shovel testing; minimal cultural material was encountered, and no
distinct stratigraphic integrity was present. LCRA Cultural Resources staff recommends that both sites be
considered ineligible for formal SAL status within the project area. Portions of the sites extending beyond
the project boundaries were not investigated, and are recommended as being of undetermined SAL status.
The cultural resource investigations were conducted under the terms of LCRAâs annual blanket State
Antiquities Permit 7531 in accordance with the Antiquities Code of Texas and the Memorandum of
Understanding between the Texas Historical Commission (THC) and LCRA. There is no known or
anticipated federal funding or permitting involved with this project. No artifacts were collected during the
project; project records will be submitted to an accredited curation facility.

286

Figure 142. The project area, proposed water line, and archaeological sites on a 1:24,000 scale section of the
Smithwick USGS 7.5â quadrangle.

287

ENVIRONMENTAL BACKGROUND
Landforms and Vegetation
For this report, a two-part division is used for a more detailed discussion of the landform zones found
within the Shaffer Bend Recreation Area project area. These two zones are the lowland/floodplain and the
upland margin.
Lowland/Floodplain Zone
The lowland/floodplain zone extends from the top of the original banks of the Colorado River at an
elevation of about 656 feet above mean sea level (amsl) up to a height of approximately 710 feet amsl.
This zone consists of Holocene-aged alluvium that is generally thought to have been deposited in the last
10,000 years. It may be as thick as 60 feet. The upper portions of the alluvium appear to consist primarily
of silty loam and loam sediments with a surface veneer of very fine sandy loam to loamy sand. The
deeper portions of the alluvial column are a mixture of sand, clay, and gravel. All of these sediments have
been deposited by flooding of the Colorado River.
While no intensive geomorphic investigations that would provide a detailed history of the alluvial terrace
sequence on this section of the Colorado River have been undertaken, some relevant data have recently
been summarized for the Double Horn Resource Area (located on the opposite bank of the Colorado
River). However, determination of the age of the alluvial terrace system at Shaffer Bend is made difficult
by two factors. First, the veneer of a recent fine sandy loam and loamy sand across the surface of the
lowland/floodplain terrace system hampers study of deeper deposits. Secondly, this terrace is also an
actively developing point bar sequence that mostly likely has multiple depositional suites that would
require intensive geomorphologic investigations to unravel. The discovery and dating of archaeological
sites within the lowland/floodplain zone aid in assessing the ages of the point bar depositional sequence.
A silty loam soil has developed on the alluvium except in certain low-lying areas, perhaps former
backwaters, where the soil tends to be more clayey. Within the project area two soil series have been
mapped: the most extensive are the Weswood series soils which cover the northern two thirds of the
floodplain, while the Karnes series soils are confined to the southern edge of the floodplain (Dittemore
and Allison 1979). Underlying the alluvium are Pennsylvanian-aged sandstone and shale of the
Smithwick formation (Barnes 1981). Soils of the Weswood series have a silty loam Ap-horizon of 10 cm,
overlying silty loam B-horizons to a depth of approximately 160 cm below surface. Beneath those, two
deeply buried A-horizons extend to a depth of roughly 200 cm; these are well below the majority of the
current projectâs proposed impacts. Karnes soils, which occur on footslopes or stream terraces, have a 38cm-thick loam A-horizon above a lighter-colored B-horizon with common films of calcium carbonate.
The lowland/floodplain zone understory vegetation includes bushy bluestem, King Ranch bluestem,
Johnson grass, switchgrass, and Southwestern bristlegrass. On the shoreline areas and in areas exposed
during droughts, rattlebush, Lindheimer senna, clammy weed, and cocklebur are present. The overstory
includes pecan, live oak, honey mesquite, Texas persimmon, and Ashe juniper. These vegetation types
were present at the time of the survey.
288

Upland Margin Zone
The upland margin zone is defined as generally extending from 710 to 800 feet amsl at Shaffer Bend.
The base of the upland margin is steepâa 12- to 25-foot hill slope. At the top of that rise, a terrace that
ranges from flat to slightly rising extends inland for a distance of 300 feet on the west side of the tract
before a second steep hill slope that rises an additional 40 to 60 feet is encountered. On the southern and
eastern flanks of the Shaffer Bend tract, the terrace at the top of the base of the upland margins has
variable topography with flat to gently sloping terrain present except in the vicinity of a tributary creek
system where sharp ravines and gullies have been formed by channel erosion.
Top soils are generally shallow clays, stony loams, and occasional patches of sandy loam. Some lag
gravels, mainly chert and quartzite, are present in these soils. Within the project area, the upland soils are
mapped as belonging to the Hensley series (Dittemore and Allison 1979), with shallow (10 cm) loams
overlying dark reddish brown clay. These variable soils support patches of oak woodlands, but mesquite
trees, tasijilo, bee brush, Ashe juniper, greenbriar, and prickly pear are the predominant vegetation in
areas that were formerly cleared for farming and ranching activities. Various grasses and croton are
common understory plants.
Prior Land Usage
The entire project area was under cultivation at one time or another, beginning in the 1850âs up until the
late 1930s. Noah Smithwick mentions in his memoirs of opening up 100 acres of bottomland (Smithwick
1983) and his land grant overlaps with the extreme western part of the project area. An old fence forms
the northern two thirds of the projectâs eastern boundary following the border between two other original
Texas land grants. Today this fence is nearly buried by a high berm built up by plowing and wind-blown
dirt. A more recent fence lacking a berm runs parallel to the older one 390 m to the west. The area in
between these two fences was under cultivation in 1936 when the LCRA commissioned a detailed
topographic map of the basin of what would become Lake Travis. A modern barbwire fence runs
diagonally across this field, constructed by a cattleman who leased the property from the LCRA.
The vegetation type prior to clearing for agriculture is unrecorded but was probably a savanna with
riparian forest closer to the riverbank. After the fields were abandoned the land was leased for cattle
grazing for several decades during which periodic clearing took place to remove opportunistic species.
Recently an almost impenetrable thicket of mesquite, acacia, juniper, prickly pear, prickly ash, and
tasajillo has taken over the old fields.

PREVIOUS INVESTIGATIONS
Areas of the property adjacent to the river channel may have been included in the survey of the Basin of
Lake Marshall Ford (later renamed Lake Travis) conducted by the University of Texas between 1937 and
1940. No sites were recorded at Shaffer Bend during this survey. A total of 10 other field projects have
subsequently been conducted through the years, which are summarized in the following paragraphs.
289

In 1967 Preston Millican, an LCRA land surveyor at the time and an avocational archaeologist, recorded
four sites in the Shaffer Bend tract (41BT49, 41BT67, 41BT68, and 41BT70). It is not known how much
of the Shaffer Bend acreage Millican investigated.
In 1978 an archeological survey was done for an LCRA fence project associated with grazing leases
(Kegley and Black 1978). The survey passed through one of Millicanâs sites, 41BT70, located on a ridge
top immediately east of 41BT67. The archeologists were impressed by the abundance of sandstone
cobbles scattered across the site, some of which were clustered and thought to be hearths. Also, possible
mortar holes were seen in sandstone outcrops. It is not known how much acreage was covered during this
survey either.
In 1979, archaeologists from Prewitt & Associates conducted a survey along portions of the boundary
fence around Shaffer Bend Park (Prewitt and Kotter 1980). They relocated Millicanâs 41BT68 and
assessed it as potentially significant, recommending avoidance or significance testing. Two years later in
1981, additional parts of the LCRA Shaffer Bend tracts were surveyed by Prewitt & Associates for an
apparent brush clearing project during which the Shaffer-Hood Farmstead (41BT177) was recorded along
with two prehistoric lithic scatters (41BT178 and 41BT179) in the upland margins. It is estimated that
these two surveys probably covered about 220 acres.
Historic site 41BT177 was first shovel tested during the 1994 LCRA Teacher Workshop to search for
activity areas in and around the house remains and to date the occupancy of the site. Since little if any
solarized (amethyst) or aqua bottle glass was recovered, the site probably dates to the 1920s and later,
although the cut nails suggest that the house may have been built earlier. Although the investigations were
mainly concerned with the historic component, a small amount of prehistoric debitage was also recovered.
The following year, the 1995 LCRA Teacher Workshop shovel tested around the house at 41BT67,
recovering historic artifacts of similar age to those from 41BT177. It is estimated that these two
workshops investigated a total area of three acres.
During the severe drought in the year 2000, the LCRA Cultural Resource staff surveyed approximately 20
acres along the Shaffer Bend shoreline and in low-lying areas exposed by the drought as a part of their
yearly inventory work. No cultural resource sites were found.
In 2004, LCRA archeologists surveyed approximately 25 acres during additional park inventory work at
Shaffer Bend. They placed three shovel tests near the terrace edge east of the 41BT177 house site and
found fairly dense concentrations of chipping debris with a few stone tools and some burned rock buried
in the cobbly sediments of the terrace. This material must have worked its way down into the sediments
through bioturbation over a long period of time. During that survey, site 41BT416, a prehistoric upland
lithic procurement site, was also recorded.
Until 2004 no prehistoric sites had been recorded in the lowland/floodplain within the meander of the
river, which includes much of the southern portion of the park. Exploratory trenching with a backhoe late
in 2004 detected a diffuse scatter of chipping debris extending vertically from near the surface down to a
depth of 1.7 m (5.6 feet). This site was recorded as 41BT361. Additional shovel testing the following

290

year found that the site covers nearly 3 acres, but artifact frequencies remained low and consisted entirely
of chipping debris.
A second floodplain site, 41BT428, was recorded in 2007 during a 94-acre LCRA brush clearing project
(Hixson 2007). LCRA archeologists used a mechanical auger to test for buried cultural deposits down to
depths of between 100 and 144cm. Most of the cultural material from 41BT428 came from below 0.8 m
and included both prehistoric and historic (late nineteenth century) artifacts.
When lake levels are low, scattered prehistoric artifacts and burned rocks along with modern trash can be
seen on the steep slope of the lowest terrace at the southwestern edge of the park. In 2005, LCRAâs
Archaeology Services staff shovel tested the slope and terrace edge in proximity to these artifacts without
recovering additional cultural material. It appears that the artifacts washed in from upstream, mixing with
burned rock from recent campfires. Backhoe trenching by LCRA archeologists in 2005 for a 3.5-acre
wildlife pond project on this same terrace further downstream encountered no cultural material; it appears
that these low terrace sediments accumulated too recently to contain intact prehistoric material (Hixson et
al. 2006).
In 2011, LCRA archaeologists surveyed the 11 known sites, found in previous investigations, in the
Shaffer Bend area in order to assess their SAL eligibility (Hixson and Prikryl 2012). The prehistoric
components of four sitesâ41BT49, 41BT68 41BT70, 41BT177âwere placed in the LCRA category of
potentially eligible for formal designation as SALs. Prehistoric site 41BT416, a lithic procurement site,
was also listed as potentially SAL eligible because it had not been intensively explored yet. The
prehistoric remains at 41BT178 and 41BT179 were assessed as not eligible for formal SAL designation.
With its buried remains and impressive stonework, the Shaffer-Hood Farmstead (41BT177) represents a
site that has both research and interpretive potential. The historic component is rated as potentially
eligible for SAL designation. The historic component at site 41BT67 is assessed as not eligible for formal
designation an SAL.
Site 41BT361 contains debitage scattered over an 8-acre area from the surface down to 1.5 meters;
however, because no discrete cultural zones or features could be identified during the excavation of 14
shovel tests and a backhoe trench, the site is not considered eligible for SAL designation. Because sites
41BT428 and 41BT451 needed backhoe trenching and more intensive shovel testing, these two sites were
placed in the category of sites potentially eligible for SAL designation at that time.
A 20-acre brush clearing project in 2011 revealed the third floodplain site, 41BT451, which probably
represents a prehistoric campsite, and resulted in the enlargement of prehistoric campsite 41BT70 (Malof
et al. 2012).
Later, in 2013, a cultural resources survey was conducted as a part of a dead tree removal project in the
Camp Creek and Shaffer Bend resource areas (Hixson, 2014). LCRA staff did uncover two additional
archaeological sites, 41BT482 and 41BT483, during the Shaffer Bend survey.

291

CULTURAL BACKGROUND
Prehistoric Background
Over 50 years of archaeological investigations in Central Texas have led to a general understanding of the
regionâs chronology and prehistoric subsistence patterns (Collins 1995). Much of the regionâs chronology
was derived from excavations of deeply stratified terrace sites similar to those found across the river at
the mouth of Double Horn Creek. From at least 11,500 years ago up until the Spanish period (circa AD
1700), the Edwards Plateau and the much of the rest of Central Texas were inhabited solely by huntergatherers. Even in the Late Prehistoric period (AD 700 â 1650) when native groups in other parts of what
is now Texas were making the transition to an agriculture-based economy, the Central Texas groups
continued to rely completely on wild resources. Much of the archaeological record relates to subsistence
activities, the presence of burned rocks from cooking facilities being a notable example. At certain
locations, burned rock fragments have accumulated to the point of forming low topographic features
known as burned rock middens, or locally as âIndian mounds.â
Texas has traditionally been divided into a number of different archaeological regions, each defined by a
set of shared cultural traits, such as projectile point forms. Many archaeologists today interpret huntergatherer cultures in terms of their adaptation to the natural environment, and one criticism of
archaeological regions is that they often encompass more than one environmental zone and so contain
more than one type of adaptation (Ellis et al. 1995). For example, the Central Texas Archeological Region
includes six natural regions, including the Edwards Plateau where the Shaffer Bend Recreation Area is
located.
One cultural trait that is characteristic of the Edwards Plateau, especially the eastern portion, is the
immense size of burned rock middens, some covering several acres. Burned rock middens are found in
other natural regions of the state but the large size of those on the Plateau is unique and suggests a level of
intensity and duration not seen elsewhere.
Historic Background
Settlement of the Shaffer Bend of the Colorado River by people of Old World descent began with Noah
Smithwick, who built a frame in the area in the mid-1850s. Smithwick constructed a mill in the winter of
1857-58 only 0.5 miles downstream from the parkâs eastern property line. Although no homes appear to
have been built before 1900 within the area now encompassed by the park, the extensive floodplain
within the meander of the river was probably cleared and tilled at an early date, conceivably by
Smithwick, who owned much of what is now the western half of the park. He even mentions in memoirs
of opening up 100 acres of bottomland (Smithwick 1983).
Two farmsteads were built within what is now the park sometime in the early twentieth century. One of
these is the Shaffer-Hood Farmstead (41BT177) built on the edge of the high Pleistocene terrace in the
upland margin zone overlooking the floodplain. This location afforded easy access to the agricultural
fields but was high enough to escape most floods from the river. The site was referred to as the Abe Hood
Farmstead by local informants whose oral histories were recorded in the 1990s (Utley 1994). Hugh
292

Abraham âAbeâ Hood bought the property two years before his death in 1924, but the farmstead was built
by a previous landowner, perhaps E. L. and Theresa Kusenberger, who held the property between 1903
and 1906, or J. H. Shaffer, who was the owner from 1903 until 1922 when it was sold to Hood. Since the
building is depicted on the USGS Topographic map with cultural features in existence in 1909, the house
was most likely built between 1903 and 1909. Shovel testing near the house foundations recovered cut
nailsâa type of nail superseded by the wire nail by 1900âso it is possible the house was constructed in
the late 1800s when large chimneys were more common on rural homes.
The Shaffer family reacquired the farmstead sometime after 1924 and eventually sold it LCRA. The
house was occupied up until the time LCRA took over ownership, but LCRA officials considered it
barely livable. No obvious trace of the house structure remains today although most of the massive stone
chimney is still standing, as well as an enormous stone cistern. The cistern is a bell-shaped underground
reservoir 20 feet deep and built of stone with a circular collar rising 2.5 feet above the ground surface. It
is connected to the house by a stone-lined drain with a filtration system that received rainwater from the
gabled roof. Utley (1994) notes the high degree of craftsmanship involved in the construction of the
fireplace and cistern and suggests that Primus Lewis, a locally-renowned stone mason and former slave,
was responsible for the work.
The second historic house site, Meredith Farmstead, is located in an area where the terrace is not quite so
prominent but still rises noticeably above the floodplain. This farmstead included a house, a log barn, a
log crib, and a concrete dipping vat. The farmhouse lacks the stonework seen at the Shaffer-Hood
Farmstead and appears to be of early twentieth century construction. Utley (1994) describes its
architecture as displaying both Victorian and bungalow features, which he notes is a transitional style
common on farm houses in the early 1900s. Only wire nails were used in its construction, again
suggesting a twentieth century date for the house. Two structures are depicted in the area on the (1909
cultural) USGS topographic map although these buildings could be the barn and crib, which would mean
the house was built later.
The farmstead is named after J. A. and Elizabeth Meredith, who were the owners from 1925 until it was
sold to LCRA in 1939. The Merediths acquired the property from heirs of Abe Hood, whose family had
owned the property since 1888. The house and two out buildings (barn and crib) were probably built late
in the tenure of the Hood family.
The only standing structure at 41BT67 besides the concrete dipping vat is the log crib, although it has
recently begun to collapse. The log barn was dismantled in the 1980s or early 1990s and taken to the
Jordan-Bachman Pioneer Farm in Austin, where it was reconstructed as a dog-run style house. The
farmhouse has now completely collapsed on top of its stone piers. Utley notes that it was built with
balloon framing and covered with board-and-batten siding.

METHODOLOGY
The project was designed to identify and assess archaeological sites that might be eligible for formal
listing as State Antiquities Landmarks (SAL). All work was conducted under blanket TAC Permit 7531
293

and terms of the memorandum of understanding between THC and LCRA, which incorporates CTA
survey and reporting standards.
The shovel test program was executed following THC standards, with at least 1 shovel test placed per 2
acres. Shovel tests were excavated in 20-centimeter [cm] levels to pre-Holocene levels or a depth of 120
cm, whichever was reached first. Soils were screened through 1/4-inch mesh unless dominated by clay, in
which case they were sorted by hand. Within drainages, exposed cutbanks were examined in lieu of
shovel testing. Artifacts were recorded and photographed, but were not collected; these were reinterred
within the shovel test following completion.

RESULTS
LCRA archaeologists conducted an intensive pedestrian archaeological survey of 100 percent of the
proposed project area on September 8, 12, and 14, 2016. In total, 20 shovel tests were excavated within
the boundaries of the proposed recreational area, and four were placed along the length of the proposed
water pipeline right-of-way (Figure 143). The investigations also referenced five shovel tests excavated
during prior surveys (Hixson, 2014); two were located along the project areaâs southern margin and three
were excavated during the original documentation of site 41BT483. The total of 24 new shovel tests
exceeds the THCâs minimum survey standards. The northern 375 linear m of the water pipeline right of
way were not subjected to shovel testing due to slopes greater than 20 percent, a lack of soil deposition,
and high surface visibility. A possible chert core and primary flake were noted on the slope forming site
41BT68âs northern margin in disturbed contexts, but no cultural resources were observed north of that
point Two previously recorded archaeological sites, 41BT68 and 41BT483, were revisited.
41BT68
LCRA archaeologists revisited site 41BT68, a prehistoric scatter of lithic debris and burned rock, which
was originally recorded in 1979 (Prewitt and Cotter 1980). It extends across a low terrace at an elevation
of 700 ft amsl and continues up a low ridge to the north to a maximum elevation of approximately 290 ft
amsl. The site begins north of a seasonal drainage and is bisected by a smaller intermittent drainage near
its center. Although not visible on USGS topographic maps, field observation noted that the two are
connected by a roughly north-south drainage running parallel to a modern fence line. This drainage has

294

.

Figure 143. An aerial map of the project area with shovel tests from the current and prior projects shown.

Heavily dissected all but roughly 5 m of the ground surface east towards the fence (Figure 144). The soils
within the site area are mapped as belonging to the Hensley association, undulating. At the time of survey,
295

ground surface visibility was less than 30 percent due to the presence of low grasses and leaf cover from
scattered hardwoods.

Figure 144. Site 41BT68, facing south. Fence line is at left, and a drainage is at right.

The original recorders of the site noted scattered lithic debris within the plowed field to the east of the
fence line, but the site form does not detail any subsurface artifacts or features below the plow zone
(Atlas). During the current project, LCRA archaeologists placed only two shovel tests within the
boundaries of site 41BT68 due to the heavy disturbance of the drainage to the west and the narrow width
of intact soils east to the fence line. Extremely compact soils and dense roots precluded shovel testing
below 50 cm below ground surface; both tests proved negative for cultural materials. The cutbanks of the
drainage were also examined, but no artifacts or features were observed in the profiles.
The only observed artifacts within the site boundaries within the right-of-way were a possible chert core
and a single primary flake. These were observed on the surface of the slope at the northern margin of the
site. Soils within that portion of the site belong to the Hensley soil series, which consists of very shallow
loams over basal clays at a depth of approximately 10 cm.

296

The current survey found only surface evidence of site 41BT68 within the project right-of-way in areas
with no soil deposition. No traces of artifacts or features were encountered in the lower portion of the site,
either on the surface or within the nearby cutbanks. As a result, LCRA Cultural Resources staff
recommends that site 41BT68 be considered ineligible for formal SAL status within the pipeline right-ofway. Any portions of the site that may extend beyond the right-of-way were not investigated, and are
recommended as being of undetermined SAL status.
41BT483
Prehistoric site 41BT483 was identified in 2013 (Atlas) and revisited during the current survey. At an
elevation of 710 ft amsl, the site occupies a gently sloping terrace above a deeply incised drainage
immediately to the east. An existing pedestrian trail runs along the eastern edge of the site, and the
difference in elevation to the west suggests the path may have been graded during vegetation clearance.
The local soils are mapped as belonging to the Weswood series of silty loams (Dittemore and Allison
1979). Various hardwoods form the dominant vegetation, and low grasses within the understory greatly
limited ground surface visibility at the time fieldwork was performed.
The initial investigation of site 41BT483 entailed surface observation and the excavation of three shovel
tests (Atlas). Lithic debitage and a few burned rocks were found in exposed areas on the ground surface.
All three shovel tests were positive for cultural materials, with materials comprising chert flakes, mussel
shell, and burned rock fragments (Figure 145). Two shovel tests yielded a single flake and a mussel shell
each, and the third produced 5 flakes and flake fragments. All were found within the upper 40 cm of soils,
though deeper excavation was precluded by the density of tree roots. No SAL eligibility was determined,
and deeper testing was recommended due to the presence of roots (Atlas).
During the current survey, nine shovel tests were placed to further define the siteâs horizontal and vertical
extent, with four producing cultural materials. These positive tests expanded the site boundaries roughly
25 m to the south and 10 m to the north of its originally recorded limits. The site is bounded to the north
and south by shovel testing, on the east by a drainage, and to the west by the project limit. As currently
defined, site 41BT483 measures approximately 100 m from northwest to southeast and 75 m from
southwest to northeast, with a surface area of roughly 1.5 acres.
In general, shovel tests encountered sandy or silty loams extending to depths of over 120 cm below
surface, consistent with the description of the Weswood series of soils (Dittemore and Allison 1979).
Each of the four positive tests encountered a single chert flake at depths between 20 and 80 cm, and one
yielded a distal biface fragment near the terminal depth of the test (Figure 4). Fragments of mussel shell
were found within two of the positive shovel tests, and small pieces of possibly burned rock were present
in two tests as well. Artifacts were noted and reinterred upon test completion.

297

Figure 145. Representative artifacts from site 41BT483: chert flakes, mussel shell, biface fragment.

The current program of shovel testing at site 41BT483 produced only a sparse prehistoric artifact
assemblage within four positive shovel tests placed across the site. The tests were excavated to depths of
over 1 m, with all but one artifact appearing below 80 cm below ground surface; these ranged in depth
between 20 and 80 cm, with no apparent vertical stratigraphic integrity. While fragments of burned rock
and mussel shell were present within some of the tests, there was no evidence of any intact cultural
features either within the tests or on the ground surface. For these reasons, LCRA Cultural Resources staff
recommends that site 41BT483 be considered ineligible for formal SAL status within the project area.
Potential portions of the site extending west of the project boundary were not investigated, and are
recommended as being of undetermined SAL status.

SUMMARY AND RECOMMENDATIONS
LCRA Cultural Resources staff conducted an intensive archaeological assessment of a 22-acre plot and a
contiguous 1 km water pipeline right-of-way within the Shaffer Bend Recreation Area. Two previously

298

recorded sites, 41BT68 and 41BT483, were relocated. The portions of both sites within the project area
and right-of-way are recommended not eligible for listing on the SAL.
As planned, the majority of the improvements will not extend below 0.9 m in depth and should not
disturb the possible buried A-horizons typical of the Weswood soil series. A proposed restroom facility is
anticipated to have deeper impacts, although the exact location has not yet been determined. Once its
position is established, LCRA recommends testing and/or monitoring within that area. With that
exception, LCRA Cultural Resources staff considers the proposed recreation area and pipeline right-ofway to be fully investigated. Because no SAL-eligible sites will be impacted by the majority of the project
area, LCRA staff recommends that all currently proposed workâwith the exception of the proposed
restroom facilitiesâbe allowed to proceed without restrictions.
Standard emergency discovery procedures will be in place, and should any previously undiscovered
cultural resources be discovered in the course of the rebuild, work in the immediate vicinity of the
discovery will cease, and LCRA Cultural Resources staff will be contacted to determine what, if any,
mitigation efforts are necessary.

Project 35.16: Intensive Cultural Resources Assessment of LCRAâs Proposed T-542
Transmission Line Overhaul Project from Luling to Harwood, Caldwell and Gonzales Counties,
Texas

By Michael Smith

Michael Smith
Principal Investigator

TAC Permit 7531
Interim Report 9.16
December 2016
Lower Colorado River Authority
307

MANAGEMENT SUMMARY
The Lower Colorado River Authority (LCRA) is proposing an overhaul of a segment of its existing T-542
Cuero to Luling transmission line. This segment composes the northern section of the T-542 line, running
from the Luling substation in Caldwell County to Structure 379 in the vicinity of the Harwood Substation
in Gonzales County, Texas (Figure 146-Figure 148). The transmission line is approximately 9.7 miles
(15.6 kilometers [km]) in length and occupies a 100-foot (ft) (30 meter [m]) -wide easement, totaling
almost 120 acres. The project will consist of the removal of existing support structures and their
replacement with 79 proposed structures. The new structures will be in placed in new locations, and a 30to 36-inch auger will be employed to drill the new holes to depths of over 6 ft. Other impacts may involve
the modification of the banks of intermittent streams to improve existing vehicle crossings.
This segment of the T-542 transmission line has not been subjected to prior archeological investigations,
so a cultural resources assessment was necessary. The assessment consisted of a map and file review
followed by an intensive cultural resources survey of high probability areas (HPA), both prehistoric and
historic, that could be impacted by construction activities. Over 50 percent of the project areaâ5.2 linear
miles (8.4 km) of 100-ft-wide right of way, totaling 63 acresâwas identified as HPA.
Forty proposed tower locations are located within these HPAs, and were examined by LCRA Cultural
Resources staff on three occasions in November and December, 2016, using standard survey and
subsurface testing methods. In areas where deep alluvial soils were mapped, a skid steer equipped with a
40-cm-diameter auger was used to reach greater depths through clays. The Texas Historical
Commissionâs minimum state survey standards for linear surveys were not met due to the paucity of soils,
high surface visibility, and the targeting of specific proposed structure locations to be subjected to
subsurface disturbances. One new site, 41GZ252, a prehistoric lithic scatter, was recorded during the
survey; the portion of the site within the current right of way does not appear to be eligible for designation
as a State Antiquities Landmark (SAL).
The cultural resource investigations were conducted under the terms of LCRAâs annual blanket State
Antiquities Permit 7531 in accordance with the Antiquities Code of Texas and the Memorandum of
Understanding between the Texas Historical Commission and LCRA. There is no known or anticipated
federal funding or permitting involved with this project. No artifacts were collected during the survey; all
field records will be submitted to an an accredited curation facility.

Environmental background
The project area is located in southern Caldwell and northern Gonzales counties between the towns of
Luling and Harwood, Texas. It lies at the junction of the Blackland Prairie Area to the northwest and the
Claypan Area to the southeast, moving from undulating prairies to gently undulating and rolling
topography, which is moderately dissected (Alvarez 2016:90). Originally, the Blackland Prairie was
mainly a grassy plain; today, much of it has been turned into grassy pastureland, with various hardwood

308

Figure 146. The T-542 transmission line, proposed transmission structure locations, and high probability areas shown on a 1:24,000 scale section of the Luling and Harwood
USGS 7.5â Quadrangles.

309

Figure 147. The T-542 transmission line, proposed transmission structure locations, and high probability areas shown on a 1:24,000 scale section of the Harwood USGS 7.5â
Quadrangle.

310

Figure 148. The T-542 transmission line, proposed transmission structure locations, and high probability areas shown on a 1:24,000 scale section of the Harwood and Sandy Fork
USGS 7.5â Quadrangles.

311

timbers occupying the bottomlands. Within the Claypan Area, or Post Oak Savannah, hardwoods
dominated the original vegetation, along with a variety of grasses (Inglis 1964; Alvarez 2016:113).
Geologically, the project area is underlain primarily by Eocene-age formations that developed in the
Tertiary period. From west to east, these formations are the Wilcox Group, Carrizo Sand, and the Reklaw
Formation (Barnes 1974). The Wilcox Group is composed mostly of mudstone, with quantities of
sandstone, lignite, and ironstone concretions also present. Carrizo Sand is characterized by medium to
very coarse grained sandstone, which is friable and thick-bedded. The Reklaw Formation comprises thinto massively-bedded sandstone and clay ranging in thickness from 50 to 80 feet. Additionally, there are
small areas of recent alluvium, which have accumulated in the floodplains of the Salt Branch and the
confluence of Plum and McNeil creeks.
The upland topography is frequently dissected by narrow drainages and swales, the majority of which
hold water only intermittently and seasonally. Among the larger drainages of the area, from west to east,
are the Salt Branch, Plum Creek, McNeil Creek, and Mule Creek. These water sources all feed into the
San Marcos River to the south, which in turn flows into the Guadalupe River Basin and on to the Gulf of
Mexico.
The T-542 transmission line crosses numerous mapped soil types (Table 18) (Lowther and Werchan
1978; United States Department of Agriculture, Natural Resources Conservation Service [USDA, NRCS]
2006). With few exceptions, the upland soils tend toward sands and sandy loams. These range from
shallow to very deep, frequently containing pebbles and cobbles of chert or sandstone, the latter of which
is dark red and abundant in this area (Baumgartner and Vollentine 2016). The floodplain soils within the
project area have the ability to possess buried A-horizons, and are typically considered to have a medium
to very high probability for harboring buried cultural resources (Abbott 2013).

The T-542 transmission line, previously numbered T-543, was originally constructed around 1959, and
the 100-ft-wide right of way has since been modified and cleared of major vegetation. Visible impacts to
the project area are predominantly the clearance of land for providing pasturage and the original
construction and ongoing maintenance activities along the transmission line corridor. The majority of the
right of way appears to have been subjected to periodic maintenance, although small portions are
overgrown with thick grasses and shrubs.

PREVIOUS INVESTIGATIONS
Prior to this survey, Caldwell and Gonzales counties had 170 and 251 recorded archaeological sites,
respectively (Texas Archeological Sites Atlas [Atlas]), which can safely be described as encompassing all
presently recognized cultural periods. No prior cultural resources projects have been conducted within the
immediate vicinity of the transmission line, which was constructed around 1959. Only five previous
projects lie within 1 km (0.6 miles) of project area.
LCRAâs T-173 transmission line, south from Harwood to Gonzales, was surveyed by the Texas
Archeological Survey in 1981 (Kenmotsu 1981). Intersecting the eastern terminus of the current project
area in Harwood, T-173 parallels T-542 south from Harwood to Gonzales. The 1981 survey was located
completely within Gonzales County and recorded five prehistoric sites. These sites (41GZ175â179)
consisted of highly dispersed scatters of chert cores and reduction debris, with one, 41GZ175, producing
some fragments of burned rock. While sites 41GZ176 and 41GZ179 have been designated of

313

undetermined eligibility and ineligible for SAL status, respectively, no formal determinations for the
other sites are listed in the THCâs Atlas.
In 2005, LCRA surveyed the northern extent of the T-173 transmission line, which lies north of and
roughly parallels T-542 from Luling to Harwood (Hixson et al. 2006). This survey identified three
previously unrecorded cultural resource sites, 41CW98â100. Sites 41CW98 and 41CW99 represent
primarily surface scatters of chert debitage and burned rock, and were determined ineligible for
designation as SALs. Site 41GZ100 possessed a prehistoric component represented by chert
manufacturing debris and a Scallorn point, and a historic component comprised of scattered domestic
debris and a sandstone well; this site was determined to be of undetermined eligibility (Table 19).
LCRA also conducted a survey of the T-538 transmission line from Deer Creek to Luling in 2016 (Larsen
and Prikryl 2016). This project crossed a small section of previously known 41CW28 (discussed below)
and identified seven new archeological sites (41GU183â188, and 41CW170), none of which were found
eligible for designation as SALs. Only site 41CW170, a possible homestead dating to the early to midtwentieth century, is located within the vicinity of the current T-542 survey area. The portion of the site
within the transmission line right-of-way, consisting of three standing structures, two concrete slabs, and
a cistern within, has been found to be ineligible for SAL designation.
The remaining two projects were performed in association with stabilization efforts on the San Marcos
River at Zedlerâs Mill (41CW28) just south of Luling (Quigg and Meyers 2003; Burden 2013). These
focused on the SAL-eligible 1874 gristmill and dam-related structures from the early to mid-twentieth
century.
A review of the Atlas indicates that there are no previously recorded archeological sites, SALs, NRHPeligible sites, or other known cultural resources within the area to be affected by the proposed
construction along this section of T-542. In total, five cultural resources sites and four cemeteries are
located within 0.6 miles of the project area (see Table 18). Of those not discussed above, prehistoric site
41CW8 was recorded in 1971 and described as a âlarge camp.â Details within the site form are few, but
observed materials included lithic debitage, a Pedernales projectile point, and a possible Darl or
Yarbrough point (Atlas). None of the sites identified within 1 km prior to the T-542 survey will be
impacted by the current project.

CULTURAL BACKGROUND
Prehistoric Background
The project area spans the southeastern margin of the Central Texas archeological region (as summarized
by Perttula 2013), and the northeastern margin of the South Texas Archeological Region (as defined by
Hester 1995). Ellis et al. (1995) note the inaccurate homogenization that occurs when writing about
ecologically diverse regions such as these. Indeed, at various times the Central and South Texas
archaeological regions share diagnostic artifacts with their neighboring regions. A framework based on
research in both regions comprises the basic chronological divisions of Paleoindian, Archaic, Late
Prehistoric, and Protohistoric. A more detailed account of the regional prehistory will be included in the
final version of this report.
Historic Background
The history of the area through which LCRAâs T-542 electrical transmission line runs, goes back to the
beginnings of major Texas settlement in the early to mid-nineteenth century. The area was at the center of
the Texas Revolution in 1836 but faded in prominence during the latter half of the century. In the
twentieth century oil played a significant role in the area. The majority of the following discussion is
summarized from the descriptions of Gonzales and Caldwell counties in The Handbook of Texas Online.
Gonzales County
The area of todayâs Gonzales County was originally a part of a Mexican land grant to Green C. DeWitt in
1825 (Baumgartner and Vollentine 2016). The town of Gonzales, the first Anglo settlement west of the
Colorado River, was established at the confluence of the San Marcos and Guadalupe rivers as the capital
DeWittâs Colony (Roell 2016). Following attacks by Native Americans and a brief unsanctioned
resettlement near the mouth of the Lavaca River, a number of settlers returned to the site of Gonzales in
1827. The colony grew quickly and attracted over 100 families within a few years. Subsequently, in 1831
the Mexican government provided the population of over 500 with a six-pound cannon to protect against
Native American raids. After delegates from the colony were sent to the Consultation of 1835, a gathering
of Texan representatives, the Mexican government sent troops to take back the cannon at Gonzales. The
first armed encounter of the Texas Revolution was sparked by the colonistsâ resistance (Baumgartner and
Vollentine 2016). Following the battle at the Alamo, Sam Houstonâs troops retreated to the Colorado
River, burning the town of Gonzales during their flight, which became known as the Runaway Scape.
One of the Republic of Texasâ original counties, Gonzales County was established in 1836 occupying the
same area of DeWittâs Colony (Baumgartner and Vollentine 2016). Returning settlers obtained land
grants, and their farms and ranches produced cotton and corn as the countyâs principal commodities. After
Texas was annexed by the United States, Gonzales County was split to form eight additional counties.
From 1850 to 1860, the countyâs population swelled from 1,492 (including 601 slaves), to 8,059
(including 3,158 slaves). During this time, the cattle industry was one of the countyâs primary economic
mainstays, along with corn and cotton.

315

In the latter half of the nineteenth century, the population grew steadily, peaking at over 28,000 by 1900.
The cattle industry increased dramatically, and agriculture continued to be dominated by cotton and corn.
The arrival of the railroads significantly increased growth, leading to the formation of towns such as
Harwood, which was founded in 1874 and quickly became a center for rail shipping (Townsend 2016).
Eventually, the overdependence on cotton as a cash crop led to a rise in tenant farming, from 40 percent
in 1890 to over 66 percent by the 1930s (Baumgartner and Vollentine 2016). With the Great Depression
the population of the county declined sharply in the 1940s and then steadily throughout the next few
decades. Although it has recovered somewhat, its current population of approximately 20,000 people is
far below its earliest peak.
Caldwell County
Caldwell County was originally part of DeWittâs Colony, later Gonzales County, as discussed above.
Located over 15 miles northwest of the town of Gonzales, settlement in this area began slightly later, with
the majority of land grants made during the early to mid-1830s (Smyrl 2016a). Following the Runway
Scape and the defeat of Native American raiders at Plum Creek in 1840, settlement of the area increased
quickly. By 1847, the growing population petitioned the United States government for the establishment
of a new county, which was granted a year later. The town of Lockhart became the county seat of the new
Caldwell County.
The economic and social history of Caldwell County so closely resembles that of its parent, Gonzales
County, that it will not be discussed here in much further depth. The cattle industry dominated the local
economy until the turn of the century, when cotton displaced it as the leading commodity. The railroads
were similarly a great driving force in the countyâs economic growth and population increase. Although
settlement had already begun along Plum Creek in the 1840s, it was not until 1874 that the town of Luling
formed around the terminus of the Galveston, Harrisburg and San Antonio Railroad (Smyrl 2016b).
The discovery of oil in 1922 resulted in a new industry that changed the countyâs economy to one that
was no longer almost completely cotton-dependent. While the new resource helped to somewhat cushion
the effects of the Great Depression, the countyâs economy was still hard hit, and the population declined
by nearly half to 17,222 by 1960; it has since rebounded, reaching almost 40,000 in 2014 (Smyrl 2016a).

METHODOLOGY
The project was designed to identify and assess archaeological sites that might be eligible for formal
listing as SALs. All work was conducted under blanket TAC Permit 7531 and terms of the memorandum
of understanding between THC and LCRA, which incorporates CTA survey and reporting standards.
Following a review of geology, soils, recent and historical maps, and previous investigations, 5.2 linear
miles (8.4 km) of HPA were identified for potential prehistoric and historic sites. Prehistoric HPAs were
based on proximity to natural water resources and topography, and historic HPAs were based on aerial
photographs and historic map research. HPAs were covered by pedestrian survey, with subsurface testing
conducted within 3 m of the proposed tower locations within those areas.

316

Shovel tests were excavated in 20-centimeter (cm) levels to subsoil or a depth of 120 cmbs (cm below
surface), whichever was reached first. Soils were screened through 0.25-inch mesh unless dominated by
clay, in which case they were sorted by hand. Artifacts were recorded, but were not collected; instead,
artifacts were reinterred within the shovel test following completion.
Additionally, in areas with deep alluvium and high potential for buried deposits, LCRA Cultural
Resources staff used a skid-steer-mounted auger to excavate subsurface tests 40 cm in diameter, in 20-cm
levels and to a depth of 120 cm to 140 cmbs. Auger tests were placed near proposed new structure
locations. As with the shovel tests, all deposits were screened in the field using 0.25-inch mesh screens.
No materials were collected during this survey.

RESULTS
Portions of the T-542 transmission line possessing a high probability for encountering cultural resources
sites were surveyed by LCRA archaeologists on November 30 to December 1, December 9, and
December 15, 2016. Six areas of prehistoric HPA and two areas of historic HPA were identified prior to
the beginning of fieldwork. These HPAs were subjected to surface observation, and the proposed
transmission line structure locations within them were given particular focus, supplemented with shovel
testing. Of the original 40 proposed structure locations situated within HPAs, six were ruled out for
examination in the field based on slope, lack of soils, or previous disturbances. Shovel tests were not
conducted where stream cutbanks provided clear profile views of the soil profiles, or where ground
disturbance was significant. The skid steer and auger were used where Holocene soils appeared too deep
or clayey to excavate by hand. In total, 37 shovel tests were excavated, of which 3 were positive for
cultural materials. Two of these were located on a newly recorded prehistoric archaeological site,
41GZ252, and the last proved to be an isolated find. Six tests were excavated within alluvial settings with
the skid steer, all of which proved negative for cultural materials. No features or associated artifacts were
found within the areas identified as historic HPAs.
41GZ252
Setting and Description
Site 41GZ252 is a newly recorded prehistoric site located within an open pasture on a low upland rise
overlooking Mule Creek, which lies immediately to the east (Figure 149). Situated on a level to gently
sloping landform, the site spans an elevation of roughly 380 to 390 feet above mean sea level. Soils across
the site are mapped as belonging to the Silstid series of fine sands (USDA, NRCS 2006), whereas
Waelder soils, frequently flooded, occupy the creek basin. Vegetation within the existing transmission
line right of way consists of low, cut grasses; mixed hardwoods and Ashe junipers occupy the areas
beyond the cleared right of way and line the banks of Mule Creek.
While the ground cover afforded low surface visibility, ubiquitous gopher holes were present within the
area; these provided high visibility within mounds of previously subsurface soils displaced by

317

bioturbation. Modern disturbances to the site consist of the clearance of vegetation for grazing, as well as
the construction of the transmission line and a two-track road along the siteâs eastern margin.

Figure 149. Overview of site 41GZ252 towards Mule Creek, facing east.

Work Performed
The site was initially located by surface observation and examination of the bioturbated soils, which
exposed a light scatter of lithic materials within the right of way (Figure 150). Archeologists performed a
general walkover of the site area in order to identify the extent and nature of surface finds. Artifacts
within redeposited soils were observed across the entire width of the right of way from the creek to the
vicinity of an existing transmission structure, roughly 75 m to the west.
In order to test soil depth and determine the horizontal and vertical extent of the site, four shovel tests
were placed within the right of way. Two shovel tests were placed in areas with a high density of surface
materials or advantageous topography. These yielded chert artifacts primarily between 20 and 60 cm
below ground surface. Soils consisted of fine sands to depths of 90 and 100 cm, with gravels increasing
with depth. The remaining two shovel tests were excavated at the proposed structure location and 15 m to
the west. Both tests proved negative for cultural materials, thus defining the western margin of site
41GZ252. The encountered soils were fine sands and sandy loams overlying sandy clay at a depth of
approximately 55 cm.
Based on surface observation, shovel testing, and existing limits (i.e., transmission right of way and Mule
Creek), the siteâs extent was determined to be approximately 75 m east-west by 30 m north-south. It is

318

likely that the site continues to the north and south beyond the LCRA right of way, but these areas were
not examined.

Figure 150. A sketch map of site 41GZ252

Artifact Analysis
All of the artifacts recorded at site 41GZ252 were observed either within soils displaced by gopher
activity or within shovel tests. Vegetation at the time of survey precluded the ability to determine whether
any undisturbed surface manifestation is present. Artifacts noted within the rodent burrows included a
thin biface fragment, 4 secondary flakes, 7 tertiary flakes, and a possible fragment of burned limestone.
The biface fragment was snapped across the middle, and measured roughly 3 cm in length, 4 cm in width,
and 1 cm in thickness; there were no clear indications of a diagnostic type.
Shovel Test 27 produced 3 tertiary flakes and flake fragments, a core fragment (5 cm in diameter), and 4
pieces of chert shatter. With the exception of a flake in Level 1, all artifacts were found within Levels 2
and 3, ranging from 20 to 60 cm in depth. Similarly, Shovel Test 28 yielded three tertiary flakes and 1
piece of burned chert shatter in Levels 2 and 3. No temporally diagnostic artifacts were observed, and no
artifacts were collected.
Conclusions and Recommendation
Site 41GZ252 appears to represent a prehistoric open campsite of indeterminate age and cultural
association. The sparse lithic assemblage lies within sandy soils that have obviously been impacted by
319

bioturbation, likely precluding stratigraphic integrity. The siteâs lack of features and diagnostic artifacts
likewise suggest that the site does not have the capacity to yield meaningful information to the study of
Texas prehistory. As a result, LCRA Cultural Resources staff recommends that the portion of site
41GZ252 within the transmission line right of way be considered ineligible for formal SAL status. Any
portions of the site that may extend beyond the right of way were not investigated, and are recommended
as being of undetermined SAL status.
Isolated Finds (IF)
Small numbers of chert debitage were found at two loci within the project area. Because of the sparsity of
materials, these were considered to be isolated finds rather than intact archeological sites.
IF 1
IF 1 is situated on a low toeslope roughly 220 m west of site 41GZ252, overlooking an unnamed
drainage. Three flakes were observed on the surface within soils displaced by rodent burrows over a
distance of roughly 100 m; thorough surface examination noted no other artifacts or features. Two shovel
tests were excavated in undisturbed locations; these encountered sandy loams to a maximum of 65 cmbs,
but yielded no cultural material.
IF 2
IF 2 was located on a low rise above the floodplain between bends of the Salt Branch. One positive shovel
test produced a single tertiary flake at a depth of between 60 and 80 cmbs, as well as several small chunks
of chert of questionable manufacture. Four additional shovel tests were placed on either side of the
positive test to the east and west along the 100-ft-wide right of way. All of these proved negative for
cultural materials. A single chert tertiary flake was observed on the surface, located within backdirt from
a gopher hole; despite the high ground surface visibility and a thorough walkover, no other artifacts or
features were detected.

SUMMARY
LCRA Cultural Resources staff conducted an intensive cultural resources assessment of 5.2 linear miles
(8.4 km) of HPA in preparation for the proposed overhaul of transmission line T-542 between the Luling
substation and Structure 379 in the vicinity of Harwood Substation. The project operated within a 100-ftwide right of way and covered an area of approximately 63 acres. The assessment located and recorded
one previously undocumented cultural resource site. Site 41GZ252 is a prehistoric site represented by a
light surface and subsurface scatter of lithic tool production debris. Due to the paucity of artifacts, the
poor preservation of site integrity, and the lack of temporally diagnostic indicators, the portion of site
41GZ252 within the current right of way does not appear to meet the criteria for designation as an SAL.
LCRA Cultural Resources staff considers the overhaul of this T-542 transmission line segment between
Luling and Harwood to be fully investigated. Because no SAL-eligible sites will be impacted by
construction activities staff recommends that all currently proposed work be allowed to proceed without
320

restrictions. Standard emergency discovery procedures will be in place, and should any previously
undiscovered cultural resources be discovered in the course of the rebuild, work in the immediate vicinity
of the discovery will cease, and LCRA Cultural Resources staff will be contacted to determine what, if
any, mitigation efforts are necessary.

Project 36.16: Intensive Cultural Resources Assessment of LCRAâs Proposed T-198 Yoakum to
Cuero Transmission Line Overhaul Project, Dewitt County, Texas

By Steven R. Evans and Valerie Werfel

Andrew F. Malof
Principal Investigator

TAC Permit 7531
Interim Report 11.16
December 2016
Lower Colorado River Authority
Real Estate Services

329

MANAGEMENT SUMMARY
The Lower Colorado River Authority is proposing to replace 39 transmission line structures along its T198 electrical transmission line, which spans 13.8 miles in between the cities of Yoakum and Cuero in
Dewitt County, Texas (Figure 151-Figure 154). The structures are currently H-frames with wooden poles,
and the 39 selected structures will be replaced with new structures of identical construction within 10 feet
of their current locations.
The T-198 right of way corridor has never been surveyed for cultural resources, having only been
subjected to right of way maintenance in recent years. Since new ground disturbance will be generated by
the removal and replacement of structures, an intensive cultural resources survey of the affected structure
locations was conducted from November 8th to November 18th, 2016. While modification of stream
crossings is not currently planned, several major crossings were examined to determine their condition, as
well as assess the potential presence of cultural resources.
Approximately 11.15 miles of the T-198 transmission line was driven, and given a basic assessment via
windshield survey. This would account for a total of 137 acres out of 167 acres of right of way easement.
An estimated 2,049 meters, or 1.27 miles, of pedestrian survey was conducted, totaling 14.8 acres.
There were no previously recorded sites within the T-198 right of way, and no new archaeological sites
were recorded in the course of this survey.
No artifacts were collected during the project; project records will be submitted to an accredited curation
facility.

ENVIRONMENTAL BACKGROUND
The project area is located in the northern corner of DeWitt County, just west of the city of Cuero and
extending into Yoakum. It is within a section of Southern Blackland Prairie that dissects Southern Post
Oak Savanna. (Griffith et. al 2007). The Fayette Prairie intersects the much larger Northern Blackland,
and is distinguished by differing geology, soil and land use (Griffith et. al 2007:64). For example, the
Houston Black soils (Vertisols) that are commonly found in the northern prairie are largely absent in the
Fayette Prairie, and Mollisols are common. The present project crosses Mollisols, primarily in stream
valleys, and Alfisols (soils with little time for development) in the uplands. Soils are about equally split
between clay or sandy clay loam (Cuero, Silvern-Ellen, Meguin, Denhawken-Elmendorf , Trinity, and
Degola series soils) and fine sandy loam or loamy fine sand (Crockett, Catilla, Straber and Tremona
series) (Soil Survey Staff, 2014). Table 20 lists the various soils and their basic characteristics.
T-198 stretches across the Guadalupe and Lavaca River basins and crosses three major streams from the
south to the north, Irish Creek, Willow Prong, and Little Bushy Creek. Vegetation in the area is a
complex mix between wooded areas and pastures which include native grasses with scattered clumps of
trees. Post oak trees dominated, but other species included blackjack oak, water oak, winged elm,
hackberry, and yaupon are present.

Controlled fires as well as other factors such as drought, herbivory, and competition from grasses
restricted shrub and tree growth and maintained the savannah. Since the early 1800s, the suppression of
fire, and soil disturbance and land clearing practices by farmers and ranchers have resulted in a higher
density of smaller trees and thicker undergrowth of vegetation, especially yaupon (Texas Parks and
Wildlife, 2016)

PREVIOUS INVESTIGATIONS
At the time of this survey, there were 336 archaeological sites recorded in Dewitt County. LCRA recently
conducted two surveys in the vicinity of the T-198 transmission line. Project 24.14, which surveyed the
T-541 transmission line from the Lost Creek substation to the Cuero Hydro substation, and Project 28.14,
which surveyed the Cuero Hydro substation to the Hochheim Tap. The T-541 Survey revisited one site,
and recorded five new sites, none of which were eligible for SAL listing within the right of way. The T541 transmission line runs for 6 miles in a generally north to south direction, and is just under 1 mile from
the city of Cuero. The survey of T-543 revisited three sites, and recorded three new sites, all of which
were recommended as ineligible within the right of way. The T-543 transmission line (now a portion of
the larger T-542 transmission line) runs for approximately 11 miles from the Cuero Hydro substation, two
miles northwest of Cuero, to the Hochheim substation, in a generally north-northwest direction.
335

A review of LCRAâs internal cultural resources files, as well as the THCâs online Archeological Site
Atlas, revealed that no cultural resources surveys had been conducted on the T-198 right of way. In fact,
the area through which the transmission line runs is relatively void of recorded archaeological sites, there
only being two within 2 km of the right of way. Site 41DW268, which lies 90 m east of the LCRA right
of way, just north of where it crosses the portion of the Southern Pacific Railroad adjacent to Big Brushy
Creek, is a historic foundation complex that appears to be related to the railroad. The site form makes no
suggestion of the SAL eligibility of the site. The other site, 41DW246, is a historic cabin known as the
May-Hickey House, or the May/McBride Cabin, and is a National Register property. The site is mapped
1.9 km from the T-198 right of way.
Despite the lack of recorded sites in the immediate vicinity of the T-198 line, the southern 3.45 miles of
the right of way runs through the Cuero I National Register District (Figure 155). The Cuero I National
Register District is approximately 57,000 acres, and extends from Cuero in the south to Gonzales in the
north (Figure 155). The district was created in 1974 in response to a proposed reservoir project that was
never completed (Fox et al. 1974). It contains 358 known archaeological sites, of which 245 are
prehistoric and 113 are historic. As stated earlier, only two known sites are mapped within 2 km of the
transmission line, and neither are within the Cuero I National Register District.

CULTURAL BACKGROUND
Prehistoric Background
The project area is located near the northeastern edge of the South Texas Archeological Region as defined
by Hester (1995). Ellis et al. (1995) note the inaccurate homogenization that occurs when writing about an
area as diverse as south Texas. Indeed, at various times south Texas shares diagnostic artifacts with the
Southern Plains, central Texas, the Lower Pecos, and the Coastal Plains regions. The prehistory of south
Texas is not clearly delineated, but the basic chronological divisions of Paleoindian, Archaic, Late
Prehistoric, and Protohistoric provide a framework. A more detailed account of south Texas prehistory
will be included in the final version of this report.

336

Figure 155. The Cuero I National Register District shown on a topographic background at 1:250,000 Scale

Historic Background
Several early Spanish expeditions passed through the area of Dewitt County, the first being that of Alonso
de Leon in 1689 during his fourth attempt to locate La Salleâs fort. De Leon probably followed existing
Indian trails, and several later expeditions followed his basic route on their way to the coast or to the
Caddo region in east Texas.

337

The routes either blazed or followed by these expeditions eventually became part of a network of roads
and trails used by Spanish officials and citizens to traverse the province. The first Spanish routes from
San Antonio to east Texas and Los Adaes followed the network of roads known as the El Camino de los
Tejas, or the Upper Road, but from the 1720s onward, travelers shifted their routes southward, probably
due to threats from Apaches and Comanches. The new route became known as the Lower Road, which
ran southeastward from San Antonio to the Cuero area, and turned to the north to join the Upper Road
east of the Brazos River. Although both trail systems remained in use, most official traffic followed the
lower road until the closing of the Los Adaes mission (NPS 2011). Near Cuero, the Lower Road crossed
the Guadalupe River.
Starting in 1750, a new route into Texas from Mexico was established through Laredo, running to La
Bahia and then up the San Antonio River to San Antonio. After 1755, a branch from the Laredo Road was
extended northeastward to connect with the Lower Road in the Cuero area. None of these roads crossed
the area which no makes up the T-198 right of way.
In 1825 the Mexican government granted permission for Green DeWitt and James Kerr to establish a
colony between the Lavaca and Guadalupe Rivers (Roell 2010a). Early population clusters in the colony
included the Irish Creek Settlement near the present town of Verhelle, and the Upper Cuero Creek
Settlement near present-day Cuero. After independence from Mexico, DeWitt County was established in
1846. The county seat was moved frequently during the first four years but eventually settled on Clinton.
In the 1840s and 1850s many German immigrants chose to settle in the areas west and south of Irish
Creek. At one point half the county population was German.
The town of Cuero was founded in 1872 as a midway point during construction of the Gulf, Western
Texas and Pacific Railway between Indianola and San Antonio (Roell 2010b). Cuero grew rapidly, helped
in part from refugees from Indianola after it was destroyed by a hurricane in 1875. A year later, Cuero
replaced Clinton as the county seat.
Cuero continued to grow and prosper, with increasing industry and livestock exports, including the
budding Cuero turkey industry (Roell 2010b). Residents dealt with the lawlessness of the late 1800âs by
forming a quasi-military group called the Home Protection Club, which assisted local law enforcement. In
the 1920âs, the local dam on the Guadalupe River was the largest in the state, and generated electricity for
the town. The turkey industry took off, and the residents started the Cuero Turkey Trot, a parade where
the birds were herded through the town to the dressing plant. This later evolved into the Cuero Turkey
Festival, which is still held annually. Oil was not exploited in the area until 1929, despite years of
speculation in the town.
The town continued to grow through the Great Depression, and hit 5,474 residents by the mid 1940âs. The
townâs primary export remained cattle, but chicken surpassed turkey as the second highest export around
this time. The townâs population peaked in 1969, at 7,800 residents, and dropped to just 6,751 in 2000
(Roell 2010b).
The town of Yoakum sits on the Lavaca-Dewitt county line, and was originally granted to John May in
1835 (Orozco Vallejo 2010). May used the property as a gathering point for cattle to be driven up the
338

Chisholm Trail. In 1887, the San Antonio and Aransas Pass Railway was built through the area, and the
town was laid out and named after the rail line manager, Benjamin F. Yoakum. By 1896, the town had
boomed, thanks to the rail line, and boasted a cotton mill, three cotton gins, a cotton compress, a bank, an
ice factory, several churches, general and specialty stores, and a school system with 700 pupils. The
population was 3,000 in the 1896, but boomed to 7,500 by 1914. In 1919, resident Carl Welhausen started
Tex-Tan, a tannery and leather shop that would later become the Tandy Corporation.
The town continued to be supported by the railroad and the tomato and leather industry (Orozco Vallejo
2010), but dropped in population, hitting 4,733 in 1940. The town experienced growth after this, and
reached 6,148 in 1984. The population was 5,731 in 2000.

METHODOLOGY
This survey was designed to locate and record cultural resources within areas to be affected by the
proposed overhaul project. High probability areas were generated based on topography and distance from
water. A comprehensive map search failed to locate maps of significant age to generate historic high
probability areas based on mapped structure locations.
Structures proposed for replacement that fell within high probability areas were subjected to pedestrian
survey for 15 meters in all directions, and one shovel test was excavated within 10 feet of the structure.
Stream crossings were selected for survey based on how they looked on both USGS topographic and
aerial maps, with shallow drainages and those with clearly visible crossings not being as thoroughly
investigated, and drainages that looked well incised or otherwise possibly requiring bank modification to
cross were given special attention.
Of the 39 structures being replaced, 28 fell within areas selected as having high probability for prehistoric
cultural resources, and eight stream crossings were selected for survey.

RESULTS
A total of 26 shovel tests were completed during fieldwork on November 8th, November 15th and 16th, and
November 18th, 2016. Thirty-one structures were surveyed via pedestrian survey, another six were
surveyed via windshield survey, and all eight stream crossings were surveyed, all of which appeared to
have good crossing points in place, or otherwise would not need to be crossed because of alternate access
routes. Four structures (5/4, 5/5, 7/5, 9/2) were reassessed in the field as having low probability based on
either slope or clear disturbance. One structure, 5/2, was reassessed in the field as having high probability
for cultural resources, and was subsequently shovel tested. Structure 5/6, which is considered to be in a
high probability area, was not tested due to sudden onset of inclement weather. However, there were
several pocket gopher backdirt piles surrounding the structure, and no cultural materials were observed in
any of these. Further, several structures in similar settings to 5/6 were tested along the T-198 line, and
lacked cultural materials. LCRA Cultural Resources staff considers 5/6 adequately surveyed. No new

339

archaeological sites were recorded as a result of this survey, and with the exception of an isolated find, IF
#1, the survey was completely negative for Cultural Resources.
IF #1
Isolated find #1, or IF #1, is located just south of structure 13/4, approximately 2 km northeast of the
Cuero Substation where the T-198 line originates. Pedestrian survey located a tertiary flake fragment and
a possible polyhedral core 10 to 20 meters south of the structure and around a dirt track road that runs
along the eastern side of the right of way. Shovel tests 1 through 3 were excavated around the structure
and area of the finds, but no conclusive cultural materials were observed. A possible flake fragment was
observed in shovel test 1, however this was dismissed because of the high content of randomly crushed
and shattered chert debris in all three shovel tests. LCRA Cultural Recourses staff does not feel that the
material represents an archaeological site, but rather two randomly deposited or displaced artifacts.

SUMMARY
An intensive Cultural Recourses Assessment of the proposed T-198 Overhaul project was completed
between November 8th and 18th, 2016. Locations of proposed structure replacements that fell within high
probability areas, or were otherwise assessed as high probability in the field, were surveyed within 15 m
of the structures, and shovel tested when necessary. Out of a total 13.8 miles of transmission line, 11.15
miles (135 acres) were driven and surveyed via windshield survey. A total of 14.8 acres of right of way
were surveyed via pedestrian survey, and 26 shovel tests were conducted, assessing 37 of the 39
structures to be replaced. No new archaeological sites were recorded as a result of this survey, and no
SAL-eligible cultural resource sites will be affected as a result of work. LCRA Cultural Resources staff
recommends that all work proposed for this project be allowed to proceed without restrictions. Standard
emergency discovery procedures will be in effect.

Project 37.16: Intensive Cultural Resources Assessment of LCRAâs Proposed Bergheim
Substation Upgrade Project, Comal County, Texas

By Steven R. Evans and Valerie Werfel

Michael Smith
Principal Investigator

TAC Permit 7531
Interim Report 10.16
December 2016
Lower Colorado River Authority
347

MANAGEMENT SUMMARY
The Lower Colorado River Authority (LCRA) is proposing to build an addition onto the AEP-owned
Bergheim Substation, which sits just off of Blanco Road, 1.6 miles south of Highway 46, in Comal
County, Texas. The existing substation is the intersection point of four LCRA transmission lines: T-238,
T-329, T-389, and T-493. Property adjacent to the AEP property is being acquired for the new substation
addition, the planned footprint of which will be approximately 23.2 acres. The subsequent cultural
resources investigation for the substation upgrade was conducted within this known project area, although
the area being purchased has recently risen to approximately 53.4 acres. The total purchase area was not
surveyed, and there are no anticipated impacts outside of the surveyed project area.
Following a map and file review, a cultural resource survey was conducted on December 7th, 2016. No
known archaeological sites are present within or adjacent to the work area, and while three isolated
artifacts were observed, no new archaeological sites were recorded in the course of this investigation. A
total of 15 shovel tests was excavated across the project area, exceeding the CTA survey standards for the
acreage. Much of the project area also consisted of very shallow or exposed limestone bedrock, and
visibility was consistently high.
The project was conducted under the terms of LCRAâs 2016 blanket permit 7531 in accordance with the
Memorandum of Understanding between LCRA and the Texas Historical Commission. This interim
report will be presented in final form within LCRAâs annual report of cultural resource investigations for
2016. There is no known or anticipated federal funding or permitting for this project.
No artifacts were collected in the course of this survey. Field Documents for this project will be submitted
to an accredited curation facility. It is recommended that the project be allowed to proceed.

ENVIRONMENTAL BACKGROUND
The project area is located in the western corner of Comal County, just west of the city of Bulverde. It is
within a section of the eastern Edwards Plateau that takes up most of central Texas. The Edwards Plateau
is distinguished as being an elevated region formed by marine deposits of shales, limestones, sandstones,
and dolomites 100 million years ago when the region was covered by ocean during the Cretaceous Period.
The western portion, where the project is located, is a relatively flat elevated plateau (Texas Parks and
Wildlife Department [TPWD] 2017). The present project area crosses soils that are equally distributed
between Denton silty clay soils (DeB) on the western half and Brackett- Rock complex soil (BtD) on the
east (Soil Survey Staff 2017). Table 1 lists the basic characteristics of these soils. Much of the area is
heavily deflated, and consists of either exposed bedrock, or pockets of around 10 cm of soil atop the
bedrock. Other areas display moderate soil disturbance due to the existing substation located on the
southeastern edge of the project area. The Bergheim Substation is located within the San Antonio River

348

Figure 156. The project area, purchase area, and existing substation location on a 1:24,000 scale section of the Bergheim USGS 7.5â
Quadrangle

349

Basin, and is to the east of Kelly Creek. Vegetation in the area is dominated by low quality browse, forb,
and grass plants such as Ashe-juniper and red berry juniper (cedar). Live oak, shin oak, Texas oak,
blueberry juniper, mesquite, lotebush, yucca, prickly pear, persimmon, hackberry, catclaw, prickly ash,
bumelia, and sumac species are also common in the area. (TPWD 2017)
