PREVIOUS INVESTIGATIONS
An internal file review, as well as a review of the THCâs online Archaeological Sites Atlas, showed that
the Bergheim substation has never been previously assessed for cultural resources. The Atlas further
showed that the nearest prehistoric site, 41CM282, is just over 3 miles to the west of the project area. An
upland open campsite, 41CM282 was determined to be ineligible by the THC in 2006. Site 41CM206, a
12-x-14-foot stone chapel known as the Draessel Chapel, is the closest recorded historic site at 1.85 miles
northeast of the substation. Site 41CM206 has not been given an official eligibility ruling.

METHODOLOGY
This investigation was designed to locate and assess any cultural material present in within the project
area. The entire project area was surveyed via pedestrian survey. This was accomplished by having four
archaeologists (Andrew Malof, Mike Smith, Valerie Werfel, and Steven R. Evans) walk 30-meter-wide
transect paths across the project area, in a north-south orientation. Shovel testing was then conducted in
areas where isolated artifacts had been observed, and where enough soil was present.

RESULTS
Survey work was conducted on December 7, 2016 (Figure 157). Much of the project area consisted of
extremely shallow soils over limestone bedrock, exposed bedrock, or dense gravel lacking soil. A total of
15 shovel tests was excavated, and all were negative for cultural material. Four isolated artifacts were
located. These artifacts, a flake, a small biface fragment, a projectile point (Figure 158), and a complete
biface (Figure 159), were not within sufficient proximity to each other to be considered a site. All four
were subjected to a more intense pedestrian survey in the immediate vicinity, and had a shovel test
excavated in adjacent soil. After the survey had been completed, it was learned that a large buried
350

waterline had been run across the area, and potentially exposed the projectile point and flake, which were
within about 30 m of a large back dirt pile.
A set of concrete building remains were also discovered during pedestrian survey. A review of internal
transmission line plans from past transmission line construction projects revealed the remains to likely be
those of a former substation in the same spot (Figure 160). The remains consist of concrete curbs and
blocks with exposed rebar, and appear to be relatively modern. The aerial plan on which the substation is
visible dates from 1979, though the age of the aerial image is unclear. LCRA Cultural Resources staff do
not feel that these concrete remains are significant, nor that they warrant recording as an archaeological
site.
While the area shown as the project area was originally all that was to be purchased, it is still the only
area in which ground disturbing activity is planned. If plans shift and require ground disturbance outside
of this project area, further survey will be necessary in any portion of the purchased area to be affected.
The decision to purchase the larger area encompassing the project area was made after the survey was
completed, otherwise further survey would have been conducted. Under the current project plans, LCRA
Cultural Resources staff are confident that no SAL eligible cultural resources will be affected, and
recommend that the project be allowed to proceed within the project area without restrictions.

351

Figure 157. An aerial image of the project area showing shovel tests, isolated artifact locations, the remains of a former substation, AEPâs existing substation, and a buried water
line previously installed through the project area.

SUMMARY
LCRA Cultural Resources Staff conducted an intensive archaeological assessment of the 23.2 acres of a
53.4-acre plot that are to be affected by the Bergheim substation addition. Because no archaeological sites
were recorded, and what cultural material that was observed was surficial and isolated, LCRA staff
recommends that all currently proposed work be allowed to proceed without restrictions.
Standard emergency discovery procedures will be in place, and should any previously undiscovered
cultural resources be discovered in the course of the addition, work in the immediate vicinity of the
discovery will cease, and LCRA Cultural Resources staff will be contacted to determine what, if any,
mitigation efforts are necessary.

Part II. Negative Surveys Less Than 5 Acres in Size, Transmission Line Maintenance
Projects, and Projects Cleared through File Searches.
During calendar year 2016 there were 15 projects that were cleared internally through memos
from LCRAâs Cultural Resources staff to project managers. These efforts included five electrical
substation projects, four transmission line projects, one vegetation management project, three
park maintenance and development projects, a waterline, and the automation of a canal system.

357

Project 2.16 T-184 Overhaul Project
County: Caldwell
Type of Investigation: File Search and Field Survey
Date of investigation: April 21, 2016

The Lower Colorado River Authorityâs (LCRA) Cultural Resources staff has completed a cultural
resource assessment for the proposed T-184 Overhaul Project which extends from the Lockhart
Substation to the Luling Substation in Caldwell County, Texas. The overhaul will include the
replacement of 21 H-frame structures with new H-frame structures. Additionally, one existing 3pole steel structure will be replaced near an existing lattice tower structure. Generally, all new
structures will be erected within 10 to 15 feet of the existing ones.
Cultural resources staff conducted a search of internal files, as well as of the Texas Historical
Commissionâs Site Atlas. The file search showed that there are nine previously recorded cultural
resource sites within the T-184 corridor. The file search indicated that one of the proposed pole
replacements lies within the boundaries of previously recorded site 41CW122. The file search
also suggested that previously recorded site 41CW9 is situated near two other proposed pole
replacements. Last of all, the file data indicated that all previous LCRA fieldwork within the
transmission line corridor had been conducted for line maintenance. Such cultural resource work
is generally restricted to areas having existing brush and trees within the right-of-way that will
require clearing. Additionally, such work does not typically include intensive, deep shovel
testing to search for buried sites. Overall, it appeared that a cultural resource survey for the
currently proposed project was warranted.
On April 8 and 15, 2016, LCRAâs Cultural Resource Services team field surveyed all 15 pole
structure locations thought to have any potential to impact cultural resource sites. The work
involved recording any unrecorded cultural resource sites and revisiting all previously recorded
sites that might be affected and assessing their significance. The survey method involved
inspecting the ground surface within 15 meters of each of the pertinent structures, and excavating
shovel tests near the structure poles to detect any subsurface cultural material. A total of 17
shovel tests were completed, with only structures 32/2 and 36/7 receiving no shovel tests, both
due to a lack of soil depth and gravelly surface. One new cultural resources site was discovered,
site 41CW171, and two previously recorded sites, sites 41CW9 and 41CW122 were revisited and
re-assessed.
41CW9
This previously recorded prehistoric site is located on the second terrace above the combined
Plum Creek and Dry Creek floodplain. Dry Creek, which is the closer channel to the site,
358

currently lies 700 meters west of the site (Figure 1). A smaller intermittent tributary creek is
situated about 100 meters northeast of the site. Although the one existing transmission pole
structure situated within the site will not be replaced, it is possible that LCRA vehicles may travel
over the site to replace structures to the immediate north and south of the site. For this reason,
41CW9 was briefly revisited during the field survey.
The site is described as a âknapping siteâ on the original site form that was filled out by
avocational archaeologists Orchard and Kelly of the South Texas Archeological Association in
1974 apparently during a general survey of portions of Caldwell County. Review of the original
site form indicates that the 1970âs era landowner, Mrs. Buelah Liscombe, had collected 25 to 30
projectile points from the site. The projectile points included several Paleoindian points of the
Dalton and Plainview and Golondrina types along with some of the Pedernales and Tortugas
types. Information on the form also suggests that the site extends further eastward to the area
where a house was located, perhaps the Liscombe home.
During the revisit, cultural materials consisting of about 20 pieces of chert chipping debris and 2
cores were visible at the designated site location on a highly eroded terrace top. No diagnostic
artifacts or features were found nor were any definite burned rocks or other artifact classes such
as bone, shell or pottery seen. Within the transmission line corridor, topsoils appear to be almost
totally absent except at the east end of the site. The erosion is probably due to previous cultivation
and root plowing.
Based on the previous site form and the current field observations, the site appears to be a
prehistoric lithic scatter/lithic procurement site that dates from the Paleoindian Period through the
Late Archaic. No shovel testing was undertaken during the current investigation due to the lack of
topsoil. The LCRA Cultural Resources Team assesses the part of the site within the transmission
line corridor as not eligible for formal designation as an SAL. No special precautions are
recommended for this site during the upcoming overhaul project.
41CW122
This prehistoric lithic procurement site was originally recorded by the LCRA Cultural Resources
Team during a partial survey of the T-184 due to upcoming maintenance activities on the line in
2008. The site is located on the second terrace of Sixmile Branch which lies 200 meters northeast
of the site as measured along the T-184 corridor. Topsoil at the site is a thin deposit of Queeny
gravelly loam. Chert, quartzite and petrified wood gravels are thickly strewn across the surface of
the site (Figure 2). A large amount of the gravel material is chert.
The original recorders noted that chert debitage, most of which had cortex suggestive of early
stages of reduction, was present across the two lobes of the terrace in an area measuring 100
meters on a northeast

359

Figure 1. Distant view of 41CW9 located on terrace top in vicinity of transmission pole structure.

Figure 2. Archaeologist inspects chert debitage on surface of site 41CW122.

to southwest line by 50 meters on a southeast to northwest line. A few chert cores were also
evident. Patina was noted on some of the artifact surfaces. The site was described as having little
depth and most of the chert cobbles on the slopes appeared to be small. A pile of flakes and cores
were found near the existing LCRA pole structure within the site. The pile was probably left by
360

relic hunters, and their collecting of the site might explain the absence of bifaces and other tools.
Overall, the portion of site 41CW122 within the transmission line corridor was assessed by
LCRA as not eligible for formal designation as a SAL.
Since the one existing pole structure within the site is to be replaced during the current project,
the site was revisited on April 8, 2016 (Figure 3). The revisit confirmed the previous description
and assessment of the site. Although chert debitage and cores are present, no diagnostic artifacts
or features were seen during the current visit. At the current pole location, the terrace lobe is quite
small and the north end of it slopes down very steeply. Since these physical conditions suggest
that LCRA personnel will need to approach the terrace lobe from areas outside the ROW to the
west, that area was surveyed during the current investigation. Occasional chert chipping debris
related to 41CW122 was seen in the area west of the ROW but as with the area within the ROW
no diagnostic materials or features were apparent. The LCRA Cultural Resources Team continues
to assess the site as not SAL eligible, and recommends that replacement of the pole structure be
permitted to proceed.

Figure 3. A sketch map of site 41CW122.

Site 41CW171
Site 41CW171 extends from a ridgetop, down a north facing slope for 50 m and down a south
facing slope for 115 m, for a total of 150 m within the 30 m right of way easement (Figure 4;
Figure 5). The site is situated on Fett gravelly soils, but has been deflated down to dense gravels
on the slopes. Vegetation surrounding the site includes post oak, elm, mesquite, and some Ashe361

juniper, with short grass, younger mesquite trees, and prickly pear and tasajillo cactus in the right
of way. While the drainage to the north is more substantial, the drainage that abuts the site to the
south has a shallow channel and drains into a stock pond. A road has been built across the top of
the ridge running east to west across the site.
Site 41CW171 was discovered while shovel testing the location of structure 36/5. Shovel Test
#11 which was excavated to a depth of 40 cm through sandy loam topsoil and well into
underlying dense gravel revealed a variety of flakes and flake fragments, as well as a core and a
few pieces of burned sandstone and quartzite. Shovel Test #12 was subsequently placed 17 m
south of the first, and was terminated at 18 cm due to heavy gravel, with only 2 flakes and 2 flake
fragments observed. A general lack of soil depth and clear presence of surficial cultural material
precluded further shovel testing.
Site 41CW171 has been interpreted as a lithic procurement site. Chert cobbles are abundant
across the surface of the site, and cores and debris from several stages of lithic reduction are
present as well, but burned rocks were not observed in sufficient quantity to indicate camping
activities. Most of the site is surficial. The northern boundary was determined by a sudden lack of
artifacts on the deflated surface, and the southern boundary by the drainage in that direction.
While chert cobbles exist to the south of that drainage, no more cultural material was observed on
the other side, and a negative shovel test had already been completed at structure 36/6âs location
50 m to the south of the drainage, where soil depth resumed.
Unfortunately, the crest of the ridge is currently covered by Another Fine Mess Lane, a public
gravel road. Surface inspection indicated that that area would have held the best soil depth, and
perhaps the most

362

Figure 4. A sketch map of site 41CW171

Figure 5. A photo from the drainage on the southern end of Site 41CW171 looking north at structure 36/5
on the ridge.

363

intact deposits. However, the sites surficial nature and disturbance, as well as a lack of diagnostic
artifacts or discrete deposits, leave the site with little to no research value beyond basic locational
and compositional data. For this reason, LCRA Cultural Resources staff recommend that the part
of site 41CW171 within the transmission line ROW be considered ineligible for formal SAL
status, and that the replacement of structure 36/5 within the site be allowed to proceed without
restrictions.

Summary and Recommendations
LCRA Cultural Resource staff completed a survey of locations along the T-184 electrical
transmission line where existing structures will be replaced. One previously recorded site,
41CW122, contains a proposed replacement structure within its boundaries. Additionally, LCRA
vehicles may travel over previously recorded site 41CW9 during work on nearby structure
replacements. Both sites were originally recommended as ineligible for formal SAL listing, and
both were assessed as remaining illegible. One new site, 41CW171, is a lithic procurement site
assessed as ineligible for formal SAL status as well. It is recommended that removal and
replacement of all proposed structures be allowed to proceed without restrictions. Additionally,
the investigation indicates that vehicular traffic can be allowed across site 41CW9. The project
may therefore proceed without additional cultural resources coordination, with the understanding
that if unexpected buried artifacts are encountered during construction, then ground disturbance
will halt in the area of discovery until LCRAâs Cultural Resource Services staff can be notified
and the find evaluated. The results of this investigation will be reviewed by THC within LCRAâs
Annual Report of Cultural Resource Investigations for 2016.

Addendum to Cultural Resource Assessment for the LCRAâs proposed T-184 Overhaul
Project, Caldwell County, Texas
The Lower Colorado River Authorityâs Cultural Resources staff recently completed a cultural
resource assessment for the proposed T-184 Overhaul Project which extends from the Lockhart
Substation to the Luling Substation in Caldwell County, Texas. After the completion of this
assessment, LCRA transmission line engineers added 3 more structures to those being replaced.
These three structures, structures 35/4, 36/1, and 36/3, were all in high probability areas, as
identified in the file search and map review conducted for the other structures being replaced
along this line, and subsequently needed to be surveyed for cultural resources (Figure 161. A map
of the three new replacement structure location with the shovel tests shown.).
On May 5th, 2016, survey of the three structure locations was conducted. This process included
surveying the area of the new structure locations via pedestrian survey, and digging a shovel test
at the new structure location. No cultural resource sites were encountered in this process.
The three new structure locations are considered fully surveyed. Therefore, work replacing the
three structures may proceed without restrictions. Standard emergency discovery procedures are
in effect, and if any previously unknown cultural resources are encountered in the course of work,
ground disturbing activity in the immediate area of the discovery must cease, and Cultural
Resources staff should be contacted at extension 6714.

365

Figure 161. A map of the three new replacement structure location with the shovel tests shown.

366

Project 5.16 Muleshoe Bend Waterline
County: Burnet
Type of Investigation: File Search
Date of Investigation: January 15, 2016

LCRA proposes to reroute an existing water line that serves the park host site at Muleshoe Bend (Figure
162). The waterline will be about 150 feet in length and will be placed within 1-foot-wide by 1-foot-deep
trench.
Although the line is within an area technically surveyed for the park host site, it is also within the mapped
boundary of prehistoric lithic scatter 41TV502. The site was first recorded around 1980 by Steve Kotter,
then revisited and enlarged by Andrew Malof in 1997. The site consists of scattered lithic debris, possible
fire-cracked rock, and a few tools. There is little to no recognizable patterning or distribution.
The portion of the mapped site was visited by an LCRA staff archaeologist on January 14, 2016. The
trench will run through gravelly upland soils. No artifacts were seen along the proposed pipeline route,
although a few flakes were visible some distance to the south.
Given the very limited nature of the project and the fact that no artifacts were visible on the shallowsoiled surface, the project was given internal clearance, with the understanding that if any artifacts are
encountered they will be marked internal staff and any additionally-needed assessments will be
conducted.

367

Figure 162. Location of project area on Spicewood USGS quad.
368

Project 10.16 Camping loop at Turkey Bend Recreational Area
County: Burnet
Type of Investigation: Survey
Date of Investigation: February 16, 2016

In response to an Environmental Checklist, a cultural resources survey of a proposed new Turkey Bend
camping loop was performed by LCRA Cultural Resources staff on February 9, 2016 (Figure 163). The
checklist states that there will be eight camping pads constructed with wood borders infilled with
decomposed granite, a fire ring, a grill stand, a shade shelter, and an anchored picnic table. The pads will
be located on the outer edge of an existing road loop and place at semi-regular intervals over a distance of
about 350 m. For the purposes of the assessment the area of potential effect is approximately 3 acres.
Upon arrival it was found that decomposed granite had been compacted upon the existing ground surface
with an approximate 1-foot-diameter (30 cm) concrete-filled hole and anchoring chain in each. Holes for
sign posts were also visible, and were about 6 inches (15 cm) square to a depth of 12-18 inches (30-40
cm).
A single shovel test was placed adjacent to each pad site, which were numbered in the field as 1-8,
beginning at the northeast then following the layout clockwise (Figure 164). Tests were initially
excavated to a depth of one meter, but based on consistency of soil (loamy sand and sandy loam) they
were later excavated to depths between 60 and 70 cm, well below the maximum depth of anticipated
impact. Two tests were positive for cultural material; a single flake fragment in the upper 20 cm at Pad 1
and a single small chert chip between 60 and 80 cm at Pad 2. The recovery at Pad 1 caused a second
shovel test to be placed on the opposite side of the pad, and it was negative. The relative lack of artifacts
did not warrant listing as an archaeological site, and suggests that there are no intact archaeological
deposits in the upper 70-100 cm of soil at the location of the camping pads. Therefore, there is no need for
additional cultural resource assessment for the project as described. This project will be reported to the
Texas Historical Commission under TAC Permit 7131 within the annual report for 2016.

Project 13.16 FEMA Hazard Mitigation Program at Lost Pines North Area/Camp Swift/Lake
Bastrop
County: Bastrop
Type of Investigation: Survey
Date of Investigation: February 26, 2016

In February 2016, the Lower Colorado River Authorityâs (LCRA) Cultural Resources staff conducted a
file search and field survey for a proposed brush clearing project encompassing 174 acres on the north
shore of Lake Bastrop. This project is a component of the Federal Emergency Management Agencyâs
(FEMA) Bastrop County North Lost Pines Hazardous Fuels Reduction Project, which encompasses a
total of 4,664 acres, 1,825 acres of which are in the area of potential effects (APE), and includes funding
from FEMAâs Hazard Mitigation Grant Program. This project is a response to the massive 2011 wildfires
which affected the region.
The project is intended to reduce understory vegetation, and thereby the risk of large wildfires, while
leaving pine and hardwood trees standing. The project area falls entirely within protected Houston toad
breeding habitat, which precludes ground disturbances and has the benefit of protecting most cultural
resources within the APE as well. Trees would be cut at ground level and the stumps would not be
removed. Removed vegetation would be mulched and left on the ground to a depth of no more than two
inches. No vegetation will be removed within 30 feet of standing structures. Additionally, archaeological
sites eligible or potentially eligible for the National Register of Historic Places (NR) will enjoy further
protections under section V, paragraph 16 of the projectâs Record of Environmental Consideration (REC).
Vegetation will be hand cut within a 50 meter buffer of site centroids and site 41BP351 will be hand cut
within the entire site boundary. Only man-powered equipment will be used to cut and remove vegetation
in these areas.
LCRA Cultural Resources staff reviewed the project documents, including the REC, and conducted a file
search of LCRA internal archives and the Texas Historical Commissionâs (THC) Texas Historic Sites
Atlas. The LCRA Cultural Resources team concurs with the Hazardous Fuels Reduction Project
recommendations within LCRA lands, with the exception of site 41BP348 (Figure 1).
Site BP348 is the remnants of a portion of World War II era Camp Swift. This site was first recorded in
1990 during a survey for a proposed Conservation Training Facility for the Texas Parks and Wildlife

372

Figure 1. Lake Bastrop, Texas 7.5â USGS map showing the location of site 41BP348.
Department. The site was recorded as portions of the motor pool for the âWake Islandâ cantonment,
which was an area for non-divisional troops separate from the rest of Camp Swift. The site was
recommended to be of undetermined eligibility for both State Antiquities Landmark (SAL) status and
inclusion on the NR. In 2006, site 41BP348 was listed by the THC as ineligible for inclusion on the NR
and designation as an SAL, but the Cultural Resources staff at the LCRA believes this status change to
have been made in error. As a result, site 41BP348 is not currently listed as eligible or potentially eligible
for the NR or designation as an SAL, the site is currently not protected by the mitigative actions
recommended in the project proposal, and the site is currently at risk for negative impact during this
project, potentially violating both state and federal regulations.
In order to address this and avoid non-compliance with both state and federal law, LCRA Cultural
Resources staff visited site 41BP348 to relocate and assess the site for eligibility for designation as an
SAL or placement on the NR, as well as to develop recommendations for brush clearing methods to
protect the site.
In the course of two days of intensive survey, LCRA Cultural Resources staff redefined the site
boundaries and created an accurate map (Figure using on the ground measurements, GPS points, and GIS.
The following features were located and mapped: 1 standing structure, 26 concrete structure foundations,
9 non-structural features incorporating concrete, and 5 other features, which are 2 earthen features and the
373

remnants of 3 roads. LCRA Cultural Resources staff was able to determine the function of most of the
features by consulting a 1943 map of Camp Swift.
The southern features are indeed associated with the motor pool and include the foundations of a grease
rack with attendant oil houses, wash racks, motor repair shops, oil houses, a fuel pump house and fuel
pumps and store houses. Additionally, the standing structure was labeled as a gas company building and
is of unknown function. A small structure foundation six feet west of the standing structure is also of
undetermined function. Other features included a raised rectangular area of earth, likely the remnants of a
store house location; a depression behind the pump house, likely where a fuel storage tank was removed;
and the remains of three roads, one of which separates the motor pool area from the residential areas.
The northern features are associated with residential structures for the soldiers stationed at the âWake
Islandâ cantonment. The residential area consisted of officer barracks at the northwestern edge of the
cantonment. East of the officersâ barracks were support buildings including storage, recreational, and
administrative structures, as well as the infirmary. The main body of the sub-camp consisted of enlisted
menâs barracks. This area was divided into six units. Each unit accommodated four companies, except the
eastern unit, which only had three companies. Each unit was divided into quadrants, each of which
housed one company. Each company quadrant included six barracks structures, a mess hall, a lavatory,
and a company administration and storage structure. The barracks appear to have been raised pier-andbeam temporary structures and are now completely absent. The mess hall structures were likely of similar

Figure 2. Sketch map of site 41BP348 with features and revised site boundary.
374

construction, though none were located on what is now LCRA property. The lavatories and company
administration and storage structures were constructed with concrete foundations. Five company
administration and storage structure foundations and four lavatory foundations were located on LCRA
property.
As a result of the 2016 survey of site 41BP348, the LCRA Cultural Resources team feels the 2006
determination of the site as ineligible for inclusion on the NR and designation as an SAL was made in
error and recommends the siteâs eligibility for inclusion on the NR and formal designation as a SAL
should remain undetermined, i.e. potentially eligible. The LCRA Cultural Resources relayed its
recommendations about 41BP348 in an email to the Texas Historical Commission, FEMA, and Bastrop
County on February 24, 2016. All parties agreed with LCRAâs recommendation that the siteâs eligibility
status should be changed to the undetermined category, and special conditions should apply to this site as
follows. Similar to site 41BP351, the areas surrounding the structure remnants should be hand cleared of
vegetation but areas within the site boundary yet far enough removed from structure remnants may be
mechanically cleared (Figure 3). Special care should be taken within the area designated as a grease rack.
This area contains approximately 400 small steel brackets embedded vertically in concrete, which may
pose a hazard to both equipment and to the safety of personnel. LCRA Cultural Resources staff will flag
avoidance areas on-site in advance of vegetation clearing activities.

Figure 3. Site 41BP348 with hand clearing zones in orange and the danger zone marked in red.

Project 16.16 T-260 Paige Split-to-Elgin Switch Transmission Line Overhaul Project
County: Bastrop
Type of Investigation: Survey
Date of Investigation: May 11, 2016

The Lower Colorado River Authorityâs (LCRA) Cultural Resources staff has completed a cultural
resource investigation for the proposed T-260 Paige Split-to-Elgin Switch transmission line overhaul
project (Figure 165). The portion of the T-260 transmission line between the Paige Split and the Elgin
Switch is 35.68 km (22.17 mi) long, and contains 31 structures proposed for replacement.
Cultural Resources staff conducted a file and map search, which concluded that the majority of the
section of line in question had not been previously surveyed for the presence of cultural resources. The
map search resulted in the creation of high probability areas for both historic and prehistoric materials.
These high probability areas were then compared to the 31 structures being replaced, and 19 structures
were selected for field survey. One additional structure was added based on observations in the field.
Field survey on this section of the T-260 line occurred on April 26th and 27th, and May 5th, 2016. Each
structure considered to be in a high probability area was surveyed via pedestrian survey, usually for at
least 20 m in either direction and across the 30 m right of way easement. When possible, a shovel test was
then excavated as close to the new structure location as possible (usually within 3 m of the location on a
GPS).
A total of 19 shovel tests were excavated during field work, covering 18 of the 20 selected structures.
Structures 10/7 and 18/5 were not tested because of standing water at their locations. All shovel tests were
negative for cultural resources. In the course of investigating high probability structure locations, an
isolated find and the remains of an historic barn were encountered, neither of which are considered to be
at risk from the current project.
The first of these was a small scatter of flakes on the periphery of the right of way, 35 m northwest of
structure 16/5. The scatter consisted of two definite flakes and some associated chert debris on a deflated
surface (Figure 166). A shovel test was excavated in the area to the southwest of this scatter where soil
resumed, in addition to at the structure location. No subsurface material was observed, and Cultural
Resources staff did not feel that such a small scatter warranted site status. In addition, its location in the
periphery of the right of way means that it will likely not be affected by transmission line construction.
The historic barn remains appeared to be early to mid-twentieth century in origin (Figure 167). Despite
the apparent age of its design, no associated artifact scatter was observed. The structure remains are
located within the edge of the right of way, but are separated from the functional portion of the right of
way by a fence. It is not believed that the structure will be affected by construction, but Cultural
Resources staff does recommend avoiding the structureâs remains. The transmission line structure
376

adjacent to the historic structure, structure 13/6, is not among those being replaced at this time. If
construction threatens to disturb this structure in the future, however, a more thorough investigation
should be completed.
All proposed structure replacement locations considered to have high probability for cultural resources
have been surveyed. Work replacing these structures may proceed without restrictions in the currently
proposed areas. If additional structures are to be replaced, or construction requires otherwise unplanned
ground disturbing activity, LCRA Cultural Resources staff would need to be contacted to assess affected
areas. Standard emergency discovery procedures are in effect, and if any previously unknown buried
artifacts are discovered in the course of work, ground disturbing activity in the immediate area of the
discovery should halt, and Cultural Resources staff should be contacted to determine what, if any,
additional investigations are necessary.

Project 21.16 Leander to Round Rock Station 2-7 reconnaissance
County: Williamson, Travis
Type of Investigation: Survey
Date of Investigation: May 20, 2016

On April 25, 2016, at the request of project personnel, I conducted a reconnaissance-level cultural
resources assessment of the Moore tract, also known as the location of potential electrical
substation for the proposed Leander to Round Rock transmission line currently under
consideration by the Public Utilities Commission of Texas. The substation site is mapped as just
under 5 acres on an existing 5 acre parcel. This is presently a residential house and associated
wooded land owned by the Moore family (Figure 169). The land is being purchased, but no
construction is imminent.
The Moore parcel is in Edwards limestone formation. It is partially within Crawford clay which
has a dark brown A horizon to 30 cm over slickensides to a depth of around 70 cm where
indurated limestone is encountered. At lower elevations, and largely corresponding to an
intermittent stream, is Georgetown stony clay loam, which has an 18 cm dark brown A horizon
over reddish brown B horizons before limestone is encountered at around 90 cm. The stream is
part of the headwater system of Dry Fork, that eventually drains into Brushy Creek.
The reconnaissance consisted of a pedestrian survey of the entire tract, less the portion
immediately surround the existing house, which appears to be of 1980s construction. No shovel
tests were excavated. Instead, all ground exposures were systematically examined for evidence of
historic or prehistoric artifacts that would indicate an archaeological site is present.
The wooded area south of the house can be roughly divided between a low eroded area to the
southeast of the stream, and a pronounced, non-alluvial terrace rise to the northwest of the stream
(Figure 170). Soils were observed to be shallow and very stony, with tabular chert and limestone
common across the surface.
A single hard-hammer tertiary flake was observed on this higher ground. Given the nature of the
soils, the underlying geological formation, and the presence of the single observed artifact, it is
the professional opinion of the investigator (Malof) that there is a moderate probability that there
are enough artifacts to warrant designation of an archaeological site somewhere on the property.
He is also of the opinion that the potential for there to be a site that might be considered eligible
for State Antiquities Code listing is negligible. There are therefore no cultural resource concerns
with the purchase of the property.

Project 22.16 Automation of 11 gates within LCRAâs Oyster Lake canal system
County: Matagorda
Type of Investigation: File Search
Date: November 11, 2016

LCRAâs irrigation division is in a process of automating 11 existing gate structures on the
existing Oyster Lake canal system located in Matagorda County. The work consists of removing
concrete boxes or wooden structures at the smaller gates and replacing them with a pipe, header
and gate, and making slight modifications on larger structures with multiple gates to mount the
automated lifts.
This project is a continuation of a multi-year project. In 2010, Bureau of Reclamation funding for
11 check gate structures in the same region resulted in project review under Section 106 of the
National Historic Preservation Act. The review included a brief history of irrigation in the region
and what was known about the canal system. The State Historic Preservation Office (SHPO)
determined âNo Historic Properties Affected: Project May Proceed.â
In 2013, eight structures referred to as the Van Vleck area gates were proposed for similar work,
and shortly thereafter, also in 2013, another five gates were modified, also in the Matagorda area.
In both cases, SHPO determined that no historic properties would be affected. Furthermore, both
of these latter projects were not subject to Section 106, and were exempt from State Antiquities
Landmark consideration due to the fact that none of the structures are listed on the National
Register of Historic Places (NHRP).
A file review of the present project indicates that none are listed on the NHRP, nor within an
NRHP district, and that no archaeological sites are within the vicinity of any of the structures. All
work will take place within canal embankments or channels, which have all been previously
disturbed, and as such do not require archaeological survey.
In order to maintain a record of the structures being modified, the following information is
presented. The 11 structures being modified are known as Cedar Tree, Citrus, Fonden, Franzen,
Head Collegeport, Head Duffy, Oyster Lake #1, #2, # 3 #4, and Possum locks (Figure 171-Figure
179; Oyster Lake 1 and Possum not shown). They are associated with the following canals or
laterals: Ashby, Collegeport, Duffy, Duke, Green, Letulle, Nuclear, Oyster Lake, Slough Ranch,
and Wilkerson.
According to irrigation division records, six of the eleven structures were constructed in the
1990s or later. At least one of the larger structures is inscribed â1952,â which is consistent with
what is known for the other projects, although it is possible some structures may date to the
1930s. It remains unknown when the actual canals were built, as LCRA acquired them in about

is keyed by main canal, laterals, bridges, and timber or concrete locks. All structures from the

Project 26.16 Lake Bastrop North Shore Pavilion Project
County: Bastrop
Type of Investigation: Survey
Date: July 6, 2016

The Lower Colorado River Authorityâs (LCRA) Cultural Resources staff has completed a cultural
resource assessment for the proposed pavilion project at the Lake Bastrop North Shore Park
(Figure 183). The proposed pavilion will be approximately 30x40 ft with a concrete slab base,
and have a 60 ft sidewalk extension and a 140 ft waterline trench run to it (Figure 184). While the
physical construction area of the pavilion and associated sidewalk and waterline will consist of
barely 0.10 acres, the project design has outlined a 0.66 acre work area. This includes a former
road that runs from an existing paved parking lot to the pavilion location. This road will be used
to move vehicles and equipment to and from the actual pavilion site.
This assessment consisted of a file search and field survey. The file search consisted of a check of
the Texas Historical Commissionâs computerized Site Atlas and the LCRAâs internal cultural
resource files. The file search indicated that the nearest recorded cultural resource site to the work
area, 41BP858, is approximately 190 m to the north-northwest, and is a small historic home site.

pavilion project. LCRAâs internal cultural resources files indicated that several projects were
surveyed in North Shore Park and its vicinity, although none in the direct area of effect, and these
surveys indicated a lack of observed prehistoric archaeological material in the park area.

location and location of the proposed sidewalk and waterline (Figure 184). Shovel test 1 was

though modern trash was observed down to 50 cm. Shovel test 2 was terminated at 19 cm when
an asphalt and gravel layer was encountered. No archaeological material was observed on the
surface or in either shovel test.
LCRA Cultural Resources staff concludes that no cultural resources will be affected by the
proposed pavilion project. Therefore, the proposed work may proceed as currently planned.
Standard emergency discovery procedures are in effect, and should any presently unknown
cultural resources be encountered in the course of work, work in the immediate area of the
discovery should cease, and LCRA Cultural Resources staff should be contacted at extension
2753 so any further assessment can be considered.

Project 27.16 T-546 emergency work
County: Colorado
Type of Investigation: File Search
Date: July 7, 2016

The Lower Colorado River Authorityâs (LCRA) Cultural Resources staff has completed a cultural
resource assessment for the proposed emergency work on the T-546 Transmission line in
Colorado County (Figure 185). In May 2016, the T-546 conductor became entangled with an
adjacent distribution line pole due to high winds. Following the incident, it was recognized that
the line does not meet current National Electric Safety Code (NESC) clearance requirements at 12
structure locations. In order to correct this and bring the line into NESC compliance and prevent
future incidents involving the conductor material on the T-546 transmission line, 11 structures
need to be replaced with taller structures, two structures will need to be added, and clearing of tall
vegetation will need be conducted around four structures.
A file search and map review of the T-546 transmission line was conducted on July 6th, 2016. The
file search consisted of a review of the Texas Historical Commissions online Texas
Archaeological Site Atlas, which showed that no archaeological sites have been recorded on or
adjacent to the transmission line. A review of LCRAâs internal files shows that while the line has
not been surveyed for cultural resources before, a prior file search and map review was conducted
for maintenance and brush clearing activities, and outlined three avoidance areas. Emergency
work will not be conducted in any of these avoidance areas.
Of the structures of being replaced, 3/6, 3/10, and 9/9 are all in low probability areas away from
creeks or drainages. Structures 3/5, 3/7, 9/8 and 9/10, which are the adjacent structures to 3/6 and
9/9, will have vegetation near the line cleared to meet the NESC standard. Of the 8 other existing
structures that need to be replaced, 5 are adjacent to a raised canal to the south and a road to the
north, and 3 are inside of a gravel mine. Of the two areas where structures need to be added, one
of the areas is in between the canal and road mentioned before, and the other is adjacent to a
paved road and an agricultural field. LCRA Cultural Resources staff does not feel that any of
these structures require survey, being in areas with either low probability, heavy disturbance, or
both.
Therefore, the proposed work may proceed, as currently planned. Emergency discovery
procedures are in effect, and should any previously unknown cultural resources be encountered in
the course of work, work in the immediate area of the find should cease, and Cultural Resources
staff should be contacted at extension 2753, to determine what, if any, additional assessment may
be necessary.

The Lower Colorado River Authorityâs (LCRA) Cultural Resources staff has completed a cultural

approximately 8.2 kilometers (5 miles) northeast of the city of Crane, Texas. Due to the discovery

Project (Karbula et al, 2002), the decision was made to investigate the entire Arco Tap property
for cultural resources in order to facilitate any current or future planned work on the property.
Subsequently, a file search and field survey were conducted.
A review of LCRAâs internal files concluded that only the eastern edge of the project area had
been previously surveyed for cultural resources. The THCâs online Archeological Sites Atlas
showed one previous investigation within 4 km and no archaeological sites within 5 km of the
project area (Karbula et al, 2002). A review of current maps of the area showed that previously
recorded sites were found in similar terrain and drainage areas.
Subsequent intensive field survey of the Arco Tap property, which took place August 28, 2016,
revealed no trace of any cultural resource sites. The property is approximately 5 acres in size and
consists of undisturbed fine sandy soil with approximately 80% ground surface visibility. Nine
shovel tests were excavated across the project area. All of them terminated at 90 cm below
ground surface or deeper (Figure 2), and all proved negative for cultural materials
Because no cultural resources sites were discovered during the survey, work may proceed at the

The Lower Colorado River Authorityâs (LCRA) Cultural Resources staff has completed a cultural

Project 31.16 T-215/232 Brenham to North Chapel Hill Transmission Line Overhaul
County: Washington County
Type of Investigation: Survey
Date: October 20th, 2016

The Lower Colorado River Authorityâs (LCRA) Cultural Resources staff has completed a cultural
resource assessment for the proposed T-215/232 Brenham to North Chapel Hill Transmission Line
Overhaul Project in Washington County (Figure 190-Figure 192). The overhaul will include the
replacement of 24 structures along 9.13 miles of transmission line, in addition to vegetation clearing and
potential modification of stream crossings. All new structures will be erected within 10 to 15 feet of the
existing ones.
Cultural resources staff conducted a file search of internal files as well as the Texas Historical
Commissionâs (THC) online Archaeological Site Atlas. Results of this file search indicated that the T-215
and T-232 corridor had not been previously surveyed, nor had any cultural resource sites been previously
recorded within the right of way. Since new ground disturbance will be generated by the proposed project,
a cultural resources assessment of potentially affected areas was conducted.
Areas containing high probability for prehistoric and historic archaeological deposits were generated
utilizing various historic and modern maps, as well as the THCâs online site atlas. These high probability
areas (HPAâs) were then cross referenced with proposed structure replacements. Structures proposed for
replacement that fell within an HPA were subjected to pedestrian survey for 15 meters in all directions,
and shovel tested when necessary. All historic HPAâs were subjected to pedestrian survey. Streams along
the transmission line were also surveyed for cultural resources, in case transmission line crews need
create or modify crossings.
On October 11 and 12, 2016, LCRAâs Cultural Resource Services team field surveyed the 11 structure
locations that fell within HPA. A total of 17 shovel tests were completed and one new cultural resources
site, site 41WT84, was recorded. Two of the stream crossings encountered were assessed as requiring
modification to cross.
The first crossing was located between structures 20 and 21 and, and the second between structures 3/4
and 3/5. Both had accessible cut banks, and profiles were shaved and examined. Both contain deep
alluvial soils. Crossing one showed no artifacts on ground surface, but recent trash was observed within
the cutbank at about 50 cmbs (cm below surface), and historic refuse was observed in the creek bottom.
While the refuse in the creek bottom was assessed as washout from another area, crossing one clearly has
potential for deeply buried archaeological deposits. No Artifacts were observed on the surface or in the
cutbank of crossing two, but a shaved profile showed deep alluvial deposits. As such, it is recommended
that both crossing be avoided. If this is not possible, Cultural Resources staff should be contacted.
406

41WT84
Site 41WT84 was discovered while surveying a structure location shown on a 1930 USGS map of the
area. Shovel Tests #3 through #8 were excavated to an average depth of depth of 40cm through
predominantly sandy and clay loam topsoil (Figure 193). Site 41WT84 has been interpreted as a historical
trash scatter and buried midden due to the nature of the artifacts and their deposition.
The central portion of the site appears to be a 20-30 cm thick layer of historic refuse dating from around
1920's-1960's, shallowly buried across a slope above a drainage. The layer is tightly compacted and
appears to have been formed and compressed by heavy machinery. Artifacts observed were multiple
shards of aqua, cobalt, green, amber, colorless, and milk glass as well as various pieces of metal, and
multiple pieces of whiteware and transferware ceramic. Bricks with at least five different makersâ stamps
were observed in the area.
The property was owned by a woman named Mariah Waddell, who left it to the city of Brenham in her
will in 1938 (internal easement files). An online file search resulted in little information about Ms.
Waddell, other than that she was possibly African American and may have had the maiden name Bilbow.
The artifacts appear to be too many and too late in age to be solely from the marked structure on the 1930
USGS map. Starting on the 1963 USGS map, the structure is gone, and three gravel pits are shown in the
area just north of the site, where the house would likely have been. One of these gravel pits is shown on
maps until the 1990's. It seems likely that refuse from the gravel mining operation, and possibly the
original house as well, were buried and compressed, intentionally or unintentionally, during earth moving
activities for the gravel pits. Since the property was city owned at least through 1983, when LCRA
purchased its easement, another possible explanation was that the city used the gravel pits and the land to
dispose of trash and bury it until the 1960's. Artifacts are strewn about the ROW and adjacent pasture,
with the most material being centrally located within the site.
No work is currently anticipated within 41WT84, as the site contains no transmission line structures.
However, due to the age and nature of the deposits, the site is considered to have little to no research
value beyond its locational data, and is recommended as ineligible for formal SAL listing within the right
of way. Should future projects threaten to destroy the site, further investigation into the sites origin may
be warranted.
Summary and Recommendations
LCRA Cultural Resource staff has completed a Cultural Resources assessment of the proposed T-215/232
transmission line overhaul. One new Cultural Resources site, 41WT84, was recorded, but is
recommended as ineligible within the right of way. Two surveyed stream crossings, between structures 20
and 21, and 3/4 and 3/5, were assessed as having high potential for deeply buried archaeological deposits,
and are recommended for avoidance. If avoidance of these is not possible, LCRA Cultural Resources staff
should be contacted for further consultation.
The project may therefore proceed without additional cultural resources coordination, with the
understanding that if unexpected cultural resource materials are encountered during construction, then
407

ground disturbance will halt in the area of discovery until LCRAâs Cultural Resource Services staff can
be notified and the find evaluated. The results of this investigation will be reviewed by THC within
LCRAâs Annual Report of Cultural Resource Investigations for 2016.

Project 34.16 Sherwood Shores Substation Upgrade
County: Comal
Type of Investigation: Survey
Date: December 21, 2016

The Lower Colorado River Authorityâs (LCRA) Cultural Resources staff has completed a cultural
resource assessment of the proposed Sherwood Shores Substation project (Figure 1). The project
area measures approximately 390 meters along T-187 and is located within southwestern Burnet
County, approximately 10 kilometers (6.21 miles) northwest of the city of Marble Falls, Texas.
Due to its proximity to a prehistoric archaeological site about 0.6 km to the southwest of the
project area, the decision was made to investigate the new structure locations for cultural
resources in order to facilitate any current or future planned work on the property. Subsequently,
a file search and field survey were conducted.
A review of LCRAâs internal files concluded that the substation portion of the project area had
been previously surveyed for cultural resources, but not the areas of new structure locations. The
THCâs online Archeological Sites Atlas showed one previous investigation within 0.6 km.
Review of current maps of the area showed that previously recorded sites were found in similar
terrain and drainage areas.
Subsequent intensive field survey of the Sherwood Shores property, which took place December
6, 2016, revealed no trace of any cultural resource sites. The property is approximately 390
meters in length and consists of undisturbed gravely sandy loam with approximately 20 percent
ground surface visibility. Two shovel tests were excavated in proposed new structure locations.
Both of them terminated at 40 cm below ground surface or at the water table (Figure 2), and both
proved negative for cultural materials
Because no cultural resources sites were discovered during the survey, work may proceed at the
property without restrictions. Should any currently unknown artifacts or cultural features be
encountered in the course of work, work in the immediate vicinity of the find should cease, and
LCRA Cultural Resources staff be contacted at extension 2753 to assess what, if any, further
investigations are necessary.

quadrangle.

Figure 195. An aerial map of the project area, showing the fieldwork completed.

418

Project 39.16 Reconnaissance of potential substation sites 1-8 and 2-8, Williamson
County
County: Williamson
Type of Investigation: Field Survey
Date of Investigation: March 15, 2016
On March 15, 2016, a cultural resources reconnaissance-level survey was conducted at potential
substation locations 1-8 and 2-8 along proposed segments of a new transmission line, that once
selected, will run between existing substations in Leander and Round Rock (Figure 196).
Location 1-8 is located within a Williamson County-owned park along Sam Bass Road (Figure
197). At present it is used in part as a portion of a disk golf course. Much of the parcel is scrub
brush and cactus. It is located on an upland divide over Edwards formation limestone and
Crawford clay and Georgetown stony clay loam clays. Georgetown soil have an 18 cm clay loam
A horizon while Crawford soils have a 30 cm A horizon. Observed soils were dark clay loams
with concentrations of both tabular and nodular chert, and exposed limestone in places.
The area was systematically transected. Visibility was poor in the northern brushy areas and good
to excellent in the more southerly sections. Soils were clearly shallow. Only two areas with
artifacts were seen. On the eastern edge about three probable flake fragments were present in an
exposure of chert cobbles and limestone bedrock. On the western edge was in isolated large hard
hammer flake that was used either as a flake core or, more probably, as a gouge-like tool (see
Figure 200).
Elsewhere there were numerous pieces of exposed, fractured chert, often patinated, although
usually very thick and lacking characteristics of purposeful reduction. They are likely the result of
repeated fracturing through fires and/or heavy equipment. While shovel testing is recommended
prior to ground disturbing work, it is unlikely that an archaeological site is present, and if one is,
it is highly unlikely it would be of any regulatory or scientific significance.
Location 2-8 fronts Brushy Creek immediately off of Ronald Reagan Blvd (Figure 198). It is over
Fredericksburg Group marl and Denton silty clay loam, which have a 33 cm dark brown silty clay
A horizon. Approximately one-third of the area has been used as a laydown for road construction
materials, and as a result has been scraped to a depth of probably 30-40 cm. The southern section
that abuts Brushy Creek is covered with dense juniper growth with varying visibility due to duff
cover and slope wash from the laydown area.
The non-disturbed portions of the area were systematically transected. The southern edge of the
parcel has a decaying cedar-and-barbed wire fence, and in places has remnants of a stone fence or
low wall. This may be associated with a property to the west, where a relatively old red barn and
outbuilding were observed.

419

Elsewhere, there were several areas where tertiary chert chipping debris was seen, notably at
southeastern corner of the property. Careful examination of exposed ground found flakes and
chips broadly scattered across the wooded area. Fire-cracked limestone was also present, although
typically as a cobble with a single fracture plane, rather than âfist-sizedâ pieces typically
associated with feature material. There was no evidence of burned rock concentrations nor any
areas of raised ground suggestive of burned rock middens.
Two diagnostic artifacts were seen. One was a piece of solarized (purple) glass, located near the
northern edge of the wooded area. Nearby was a carefully-crafted Ensor dart point, made on a
piece of fairly low quality material (Figure 200).
The area was recorded as archaeological site 41WM1375. About 70 pieces of debitage and 30-40
fire-cracked cobbles were seen. Despite the creek-side location, this is at best a slowly aggrading
landform, and may actually be more subject to erosion and deflation. While the site still needs
systematic shovel testing prior to any LCRA-related ground disturbance, it is unlikely that the site
has either a historic or prehistoric component that would have regulatory or scientific
significance. Site 41WM1375 is recommended as having undetermined SAL status because of a
lack of shovel testing.
The two properties were not purchased, and thus no further work was conducted.

Project 6.16 McKinney Roughs Equestrian Camping Area Development
County: Bastrop
Type of Investigation: File search and Survey
Date of Investigation: January 28, 2016
LCRA proposes to further develop an area of the McKinney Roughs Nature Park presently used as an
equestrian parking loop and trail head (Figure 201). There are currently two proposed designs for the
development (Figure 202 and Figure 203). Both are within an area previously surveyed (Malof 2006) and
determined to be within an old gravel quarry, with the exception of a service road which will be rerouted
to the west. The rerouted service road will be approximately 1700 feet in length and crosses an area that
could not be demonstrated as having been previously assessed, and during a reconnaissance of the project
area appeared to be in relatively undisturbed soil. The previous survey resulted in the recording of site
41BP675, a lithic scatter determined to be ineligible for formal SAL status. This is the only known site
that will be impacted by the project.
A field assessment of the proposed new service road was performed by LCRA Cultural Resources staff on
January 28, 2016 (Figure 204). Eighteen shovel tests were excavated in undisturbed portions of the
service road route, which had previously been uploaded into a GPS.
The testing revealed a shallow sandy loam and sand over dense gravels and mottled clay across the
service roadâs path. Shovel test 4 encountered a single flake fragment, and subsequent testing of the
surrounding area located a hard hammer flake in shovel test 14. Five additional tests in the area failed to
locate any more cultural material. These two artifacts appear to be isolated occurrences and were not
assigned a trinomial.
Cultural material was also encountered in shovel tests 8 and 10, and the proximity of these tests to
41BP675 resulted in a slight extension of the siteâs boundary and a revisit form to TARL.
Given the disturbed nature of most of the project area, and the results from this survey, LCRA Cultural
Resources staff recommends that the development be allowed to proceed without restrictions. Standard
emergency discovery procedures will apply.

Project 7.16 Acquisition of Brady South substation from the City of Brady
County: McCulloch
Type of Investigation: File search
Date of Investigation: April 14, 2016
LCRA proposes to purchase and perform minor equipment upgrades to the existing Brady South
substation located in the town of Brady (Figure 205). The substation is approximately 1.5 acres in size on
a 3 acre tract. There will be no new ground disturbance other than for a new 100-foot-long access road
and gate within the existing substation fence (Figure 206).
A review of the Sites Atlas shows no recorded archaeological sites at or near the project area. Historical
maps available through the USGS websites do not indicate previous structures there and the location was
outside city limits in 1921, according to Sanborn Fire Insurance maps.
Given the overall low probability for intact archaeological sites to be present and the very limited scope
of the project LCRA recommends that no field assessment is needed, and that the project should be
allowed to proceed.

Project 9.16 Upton Electrical Substation Project
County: Upton
Type of Investigation: File search
Date of Investigation: March 11, 2016
The Lower Colorado River Authority (LCRA) is proposing to construct the new Upton Electrical
Substation on a 3.55 acre tract in Upton County, Texas (Figures 1 and 2). The proposed substation will
be built immediately east of an existing electrical substation that is owned by ONCOR, a private electrical
power company. LCRA will utilize an existing access road that extends along the north boundary of the
proposed substation site for the proposed project (Figure 3). The access road is 9.1 meters (30 ft) wide
and extends for a length of 246 meters (810 ft). LCRA will own the land where the substation will be
constructed and, therefore, a cultural resources review under the Antiquities Code of Texas is required.
Since there is no federal funding or permitting associated with the project, a review under the National
Historic Preservation Act is not applicable.
A file search conducted for the project showed that there are no previously recorded cultural resource
sites within the proposed project area. The file search showed that LCRAâs T-441 electrical transmission
line, which runs through the existing ONCOR Upton Substation, was surveyed for cultural resources in
2002 when LCRA purchased the line from Texas Utilities and then upgraded it (Karbula et al. 2002). The
portion of the transmission line corridor in and around the existing ONCOR substation and LCRAâs
proposed new substation were rated as low probability areas and were not field investigated. The low
probability rating was due to the long distance any water source. The closest previously recorded site is
41UT43, which was recorded during the 2002 survey about one mile west of the proposed substation.
This site lies on the west side of an unnamed creek at a distance of 60 to 210 meters from that creek.
The currently proposed LCRA substation lies approximately 550 to 730 meters southeast of the same
unnamed creek where 41UT43 is located. Additionally, substation is over 900 meters northeast of the
closest mapped playa lake feature. The LCRA Cultural Resources Team concludes that the proposed
LCRA substation tract has a low probability for any prehistoric cultural resource sites.
The 1970 Lindsey Ranch, Texas 7.5â USGS map shows a windmill symbol along the access road corridor
and a water tank to the north. According to LCRA Real Estate Services personnel, no windmill is
currently present and the elevated water tank lies to the north of the access road as shown in the
background as shown on the Figure 3 photograph. Additionally, a pump jack currently used to pump
water from a well to the tank is visible even further north of the water tank as also shown on Figure 3.
Based on the location of the pump jack, the former windmill depicted on the topographic map may have
been misplotted and may have once stood in the vicinity of the pump jack.
Based on the file search data concerning prehistoric resources and the information about the water pump
and water tank, the LCRA Cultural Resources staff does not feel that a field survey is warranted. LCRA

436

staff concludes that no cultural resource sites eligible for formal designation as State Antiquities
Landmarks will be affected by the proposed substation project.
We request your concurrence with our assessments and recommendations for this project. If you need
any additional information, contact me at (512) 730-6714.

Project 11.16 Reroute of existing transmission line T-134 near Moulton
County: Lavaca
Type of Investigation: File search
Date of Investigation: April 14, 2016
LCRA proposes to provide electrical service to a new substation that will be built and owned by the
Guadalupe Valley Electric Cooperative (GVEC). The new substation will be located immediately
adjacent to LCRAâs existing T-134 transmission line. In order to service the substation the line will make
a 90 degree turn towards the station then another 90 degree turn into the substation from the north. It will
the same thing when it exits the substation to the south (Figure 207). Up to six support structures may be
necessary.
Soils are mapped as Carbengle loam, which has a 30 cm A horizon over B and C horizons, and Frelsberg
clay, which as a 20 cm A horizon over B horizons. The underlying geology is Oakville sandstone.
A review of the Sites Atlas shows no archaeological sites are recorded at the project location. A review of
the detailed 1898 USGS maps shows no structures. Topographically the area appears to have a low
potential for prehistoric archaeological sites.
Given the limited nature of impacts associated with the project and the negative background results,
LCRA Cultural Resources staff recommends the project be allowed to proceed as described, with
standard emergency discovery procedures in place.

Project 18.16 Proposed Water Pipeline at Travis Countyâs Pace Bend Park on LCRA Land
County: Travis
Type of Investigation: File search
Date of Investigation: May 9, 2016
Travis County is proposing to construct a new water pipeline from an existing water well to their park
office complex at Pace Bend Park (Figure 208). The park is on LCRA property, but the land is leased to
Travis County, and Travis County operates the park. Travis County is proposing to place the new pipeline
in an existing electrical distribution line right of way owned by the Pedernales Electrical Co-op (PEC).
The water pipeline would be 2 inches in diameter, and would extend approximately 890 m (2,920 ft)
between the well and the office complex. The pipeline would be laid in a 3-4 ft deep trench excavated by
a mechanical trencher. The PEC distribution line right of way has already been cleared of vegetation, and
the only ground disturbing activity involved will be the trenching within this right of way (Figure 209).
A file search conducted by LCRA Cultural Resource staff found that an intensive cultural resources
survey of the park was conducted by Espey, Huston and Associates in December 1984 (Glander 1985a).
The report states that âvirtually 100%â of the park land above the 680 ft contour (full pool level of Lake
Travis) was surveyed (Glander 1985a: 1). The survey recorded 31 archaeological sites and 33 isolated
finds, and appears to have been diligent and thorough.
Despite having been intensively surveyed for cultural resources, no archaeological sites are currently
recorded along the water pipelineâs proposed path. In fact, the closest that the pipelineâs path comes to an
archaeological site is 230 m (755 ft) from 41TV962, a prehistoric lithic scatter on an upland rise near the
lake shore.
Based on these findings, LCRA Cultural Resources staff has concluded that no State Antiquities
Landmark eligible cultural resource sites will be affected by the proposed work, and recommends that the
trenching and placement of this water pipeline by Travis County be allowed to proceed without field
investigations or restrictions to construction. Standard emergency discovery procedures will be in place,
and should any previously unknown cultural resources be encountered in the course of work, ground
disturbing activity in the immediate area of the discovery will halt until LCRA Cultural Resources staff
can contact the Texas Historical Commission to assess what, if any, avoidance/mitigation efforts are
necessary.
We request your concurrence with our assessments and recommendations for this project. If you need
any additional information, contact me at (512) 730-6714.

Project 33.16 Fayette Power Plant Bus Tie Substation Improvement
County: Fayette
Type of Investigation: File search
Date of Investigation: September 28, 2016
LCRA Transmission Services Corporation (LCRA TSC) proposes the FPP Bus Tie Project to increase the
capacity at two existing substations, referred to as FPP Yard #1 and FPP Yard # 2 within the Fayette
Power Project plant area. Potential ground disturbing portions of the project consist of the teardown and
partial realignment of an existing 0.26-mile-long transmission line between the two substations, and
construction of a new, parallel transmission line, also approximately 0.26 miles in length. Four
replacement support structures located outside of the existing substations will likely be required.
A cultural resources assessment of the project was conducted by LCRA Cultural Resources staff on
September 27, 2016. The assessment consisted of a map and file review. A search of the Texas
Archeological Sites Atlas indicated there are no recorded archaeological sites within the project area.
Although the Atlas does not illustrate an assessment at the precise project location, there have been
numerous studies prior to construction of the plant in the 1970s and subsequently for larger projects such
as ash disposal ponds.
Furthermore, the project is in areas that have undergone numerous disturbances over the decades, and the
potential for significant archeological sites to be present is negligible. Therefore, LCRA recommends that
the project be allowed to proceed as described without further cultural resources consultation, with the
understanding the standard emergency discover procedures will be in place.

Project 38.16 Emergency Cultural Resource Survey of 41BP951
County: Bastrop
Type of Investigation: File search
Date of Investigation: November 17, 2016
The Lower Colorado River Authority (LCRA) is currently conducting maintenance and replacing structures along
the existing T-181/T-229 transmission line in Bastrop County, Texas. LCRA Cultural Resources staff reviewed
the project documents and conducted a file search of LCRAâs internal archives and the Texas Historical
Commissionâs (THC) Online Archeological Sites Atlas. No recorded sites were within the proposed project area
(Figure 1); however, when work commenced, an underground cistern (Figure 3) was discovered and damaged by
the work crews attempting to clear brush near the treeline of the right of way (ROW). The cistern is now
damaged, missing its collar, and is considered a hazard. Work on that section of ROW was immediately halted
until a cultural resources field survey could be conducted.
The field survey, conducted by Valerie Werfel and Steven Evans on November 17, 2016, confirmed the location
of the cistern and revealed a nearby burned trash scatter, which consisted of historical glass and metal fragments,
as well as burned sandstone and brick, and was recorded as 41BP951. The site is located at the top of a small hill
and is approximately 63 meters long and 35 meters wide. A search of Bastrop County deed records was conducted
to establish land ownership chain for the parcel; however, due to unclear deed records of ownership, a precise
chain of deed could not be determined. Because the cistern is constructed primarily of manufactured red brick and
concrete, it is believed to have been built around the 1920s or 1930âs and William Gerbert may have owned the
parcel at the time. This date range is supported by the artifacts present. It was stated by the current landowner that
the cistern and trash pile may have been a part of a nearby barn site; however, no remnants of a barn or any type
of structure were found by Cultural Resources staff.
Due to high ground visibility, the majority of the analysis was done by pedestrian survey with a total of six shovel
tests, all of which were terminated at 40 cmbs or at a clay horizon. Moving from west to east (Figure 2,) the tests
were found to include various pieces of glass, ceramic, metal, and brick fragments in decreasing amounts. Shovel
Test 6 was located at the base of the slope of the hill and was excluded from the site boundary as only a single 1cm2 shard of amber glass was recovered and thought to be run-off scatter from the burn feature.
The site appears to be isolated from any current standing structure or visible structure remnant. It is recommended
as ineligible for formal designation as a State Antiquities Landmark within the ROW, with the area outside of the
ROW being of unknown eligibility. Due to the current work being done on the ROW and the relatively recent
historic construction of the site components, it is recommended that the cistern be filled in for safety of the work
crews and others.
We request your concurrence with our assessments and recommendations for this project. If you need any
additional information, please contact me at 512-578-2753.

PART IV.

Projects Monitored

During the 2016 calendar year, four projects were monitored by archaeologists. These included file
searches and field monitoring for each project, including the monitoring of 1) transmission line
construction near prehistoric site 41GL321; 2) installation of park lights in the vicinity of various known
sites at White Rock Park; 3) electrical line trenches at the Lane City vertical pump station; and 4)
numerous LCRA properties for the FY17 vegetation management project.

453

Project 8.16 Results of T-120 Transmission Line Construction Monitoring at 41GL321
County: Gillespie
Type of Investigation: Construction Monitoring
Date of Investigation: January 22, 2016
On November 18, 2015, the Texas Historical Commission (THC) agreed that archaeological monitoring
at site 41GL321, located on the south side of the Guadalupe River just south of Fredericksburg (Figure
210), was an adequate method for assessing potentially important prehistoric archaeological deposits at
the location of transmission support structure 18/6 (letter from LCRA to THC dated October 27, 2015).
Pre-construction shovel testing had identified a likely McKinney Plain ceramic rim sherd of Caddoan
origin (from 0-20 cmbs) and a small piece of central Texas Leon Plain pottery (from 40-60 cmbs), as well
as numerous pieces of lithic debitage (flakes), mussel shell, and a thin biface that may have been a large
arrow point fragment (from 40-60 cmbs). Given the relative uniqueness of the rim sherd, it was collected
for further analysis, and subsequently the second ceramic piece was also collected. No other collections
were made; all other artifacts were replaced within their respective holes.
Structure replacement occurred on January 22, 2016. When Cultural Resources staff arrived, the two
poles of the H-frame were ready to be removed. The more westerly of the two poles was removed first,
and was pulled straight out of the ground by way of a crane and tethers. There was minimal ground
disturbance as a result. A truck-mounted auger was then used to clean out and enlarge the holes, from
about 18 inches (45 cm) to about 28 inches (70 cm). The auger removed approximately 2 feet of soil at a
time (60 cm) to a final depth of just over 8 feet (2.4 m), which was then sifted through standard quarterinch hardware cloth (Figure 211). Approximately 60 percent of the soil from each 2-foot zone was
screened.
Following communication with the landowner a 100 percent artifact collection was decided upon. Artifact
recovery was minimal, and is summarized in Table 22. No organic material such as charcoal or bone was
observed, and only a single snail (Rabdotus sp.) was seen. There were a few chips and flakes indicative of
tool manufacture and maintenance, and one edge-modified flake and a possible edge-modified core
fragment were recovered. Possible fire-cracked rock was later determined to be naturally-shaped chert
and cherty limestone. No more ceramics were seen.
The most distinctive items were a large round chert cobble that had been split, with edge battering on one
side. It is suggestive of bipolar reduction. Another large flake from either a well-reduced core or a large
biface was present, but is unusual in that the ventral side has abnormal concave curvature, and may have
two opposite platforms, so may also be the result of bipolar reduction. It appears to have been heat
treated. Also recovered were a few pieces of black chert shatter, including a crude pebble, which
contraindicate a previous hypothesis of non-local (Round Rock area) chert being present.

Representative soil samples were collected from each of the 2-foot excavation increments (Table 23), and
