The archaeological monitoring was useful in that it demonstrates that the land form is relatively intact.
The thick bed of well-sorted small gravels indicate periods of high-velocity stream flow, and increasing
clay content with depth suggests a fairly stable environment allowing downward clay movement.
Subjectively, there is about 30 cm of an upper soft loam, followed by 60-70 cm of silt loam. Gravels
become very dense between about 1 and 1.7 m, and decrease below that point. Coarse sands were
encountered briefly at the very bottom, about 240 cm (8 feet). The greatest potential for intact cultural
zones is therefore within the upper meter or so of soil, and perhaps at depths below about 2 meters.
Definite subsoils were not encountered, so total soil depth remains unknown.
Artifact recovery during the monitoring was disappointing, and much less than found in previous shovel
testing. While possibly due to previous auguring, it is more likely that the present structure location is
simply in a less dense portion of the site.
All collected artifacts have been returned to the landowner who will either keep or dispose of them, or
return them to LCRA under Deed of Gift to the Texas Archeological Research Laboratory (TARL) in
Austin.
The overall significance of 41GL321 remains undetermined. If future LCRA work involves significant
new ground disturbance, then additional assessments should be considered.

458

Project 12.16 Monitoring of White Rock Park Playing Field Lights Installation
County: Fayette
Type of Investigation: Construction Monitoring
Date of Investigation: March 29, 2016
In March 2016, the Lower Colorado River Authorityâs (LCRA) Cultural Resources staff has completed
monitoring for cultural resources for the White Rock Park Playing Field Lights Installation project in La
Grange, Fayette County. White Rock Park is a La Grange city park located on land owned by the LCRA
and leased to the City of La Grange (Figure 1).
The City of La Grange completed improvements to White Rock Park consisting of installing four light
pole structures at the combination soccer and football field located between the Colorado River and the
park baseball diamonds (Figure 2). The project involved excavating a trench approximately 70 cm deep
and 20 cm wide from an electrical distribution junction 200 m north of the field to run electrical conduits
to the four light poles to be installed. The trench runs 21 m from the electrical junction to the park road
where it stops, then is picked up on the other side of the road, 40 m farther south, where it runs along the
margin of a baseball diamond for 100 m before branching into two trenches. From this branching, one
trench runs along the northern margin of the combined soccer and football playing field and the other
trench runs along the eastern margin of the field to the southeast corner, where it turns and follows the
south field margin. The total length of all trenching was 431 m. The approximately 21 m segment running
underneath the park road was not trenched but rather horizontally bored to avoid trenching and repaving
the road. The four light pole structures were placed in holes 3 ft (1 m) wide and augered to 14 ft (4.3 m)
below the ground surface. Four additional holes approximately 1 m wide and 3 m long were excavated by
a backhoe to allow repair to several PVC french drain pipes and a PVC water pipe damaged during
trenching.
The cultural resources investigations included a file search and field monitoring of the trenching and
augering. The file search consisted of a check of the Texas Historical Commissionâs (THC) computerized
Site Atlas and the LCRAâs internal cultural resource files. The file search indicated the existence of two
previously recorded sites, 41FY519 and 41FY520, both of which were recorded during a 1992
investigation (Caran et al 1992).
The 1992 survey and backhoe trenching were contracted by the LCRA to the Texas Archeological
Research Laboratory (TARL) to investigate two river access locations on the Colorado River; one in
Columbus in Colorado County and one in La Grange. The purpose of the survey was two-fold. First,
TARL sought to

459

Figure 1. Section of the La Grange East, Texas 7.5â USGS map showing the project area and the location
of site 41FY519.

460

Figure 2. White Rock Park map showing the locations of project construction elements. Previously
recorded site 41FY519 is marked by red colored circle.
Record any archaeological sites and assess the potential impact to them from the construction of park
facilities associated with the river access points. To aid in the investigation, TARL employed a geologist
to investigate the Quaternary geology of the Colorado River channel, the purpose of which was to
potentially increase the ability of archaeologists to define areas of high probability for buried prehistoric
461

cultural resources with better precision. As mentioned, the only sites recorded in La Grange were
41FY519 and 41FY520.
Site 41FY520 is a small historic Jewish cemetery, with burials ranging from the 1860s to the 1930s. This
site is located 100 m west of the 2016 project and is just outside White Rock Parkâs western boundary.
This site is not affected by the 2016 project and will not be discussed further.
Site 41FY519, is a 60 m by 60 m open campsite buried 165 cm to 200 cm below the surface. According
to the report (Caran et al 1992), cultural material was observed in three of six backhoe trenches
excavated. The negative trenches defined the east, north, and west site boundaries, while the Colorado
River defined the south boundary. Reported material observed in the backhoe trenches included burned
rock, burned chert, imported rock and shell, and mussel shell. Trench No. 1 had the highest amount of
material observed, as well as one possible hearth feature. No time diagnostic artifacts were observed but a
soil sample was taken from Trench No. 1 and the organic fraction was radiocarbon dated to
approximately 3000 years before the present. Geological investigations determined the site to lie on a
slightly raised ridge or levee situated on the first terrace of the river. A second terrace was observed
approximately 600 m north of site 41FY519. The 1992 investigations recommended site 41FY519 remain
of undetermined eligibility for both the National Register of Historic Places and for formal designation as
a State Antiquities Landmark.
Since the currently proposed project area had not been trenched or shovel tested during the 1992 survey,
and because it lies immediately north of the site 41FY519, LCRA Cultural Resources staff decided to
monitor all earth moving tasks associated with park light installation at White Rock Park. Given the depth
of the previous finds at 165 cm to 200 cm below the surface, LCRA Cultural Resources staff deemed the
augering of the holes for the placement of the light pole structures to have the highest potential to expose
buried cultural materials, especially at the southwest pole location, which is nearest the 1992 excavations.
LCRA Cultural Resources staff monitored all excavation activities during this project. Staff monitored the
excavation of the entire electrical conduit line trench for cultural materials. Because the trenching was
conducted with a ditch-witch machine, it was not physically possible for the staff to enter the trenches.
However, samples of the trench back dirt were screened through Â¼ inch mesh screen at approximately 20
m intervals and whenever staff observed possible cultural material. Additionally, staff cleaned and
recorded seven measured soil profiles along the course of the trench monitoring. Profiles #1 and 2 at the
south end of the park closest to the current river channel showed a sandy A horizon topsoil similar but not
as thick as that on the levee feature where site 41FY519 was previously found. Profile #2, which was the
closest to the boundary of 41FY519, had a 55 cm thick sandy topsoil above a sandy clay (Figure 3).
Further east and northward, Profiles #3-8 had clay loam topsoil that is dissimilar to soil profile where
41FY519 occurred. Staff did not monitor the horizontal boring under the park road because the bit
pushed the earth aside rather than removing it. As a result, there was no excavated earth to screen.
One unifacial expedient tool fragment and a small amount of possible burned rock were observed in the
trench back dirt nearest site 41FY519. No other cultural materials were observed nor were there any
indications of a definable cultural zone in the trench wall.

462

Figure 3. Soil Profiles #2 and 7.

463

Finally, staff monitored the augering of deep holes for the four light poles. The holes were approximately
1 meter (3 ft) in diameter and 4.3 meters (14.3 ft) deep and were excavated in levels of 60 cm or less.
Staff measured the depth of each level, screened samples (approximately one 5 gallon bucket) from each
level through Â¼ inch mesh screen, and created a soil profile of each auger hole using the samples from
each level. At depths greater than the previously dug ditch witch trenches, the soil profiles of the auger
holes had clay or clay loam, to a depth of 1.5 m to 3 m below surface, overlaying sand and rounded river
gravel, which became coarser and larger as the depth increased. These deep profiles exhibited generally
well sorted sediments, fining upwards from large gravel to sand to silt and clay, as is expected of an
aggrading alluvial terrace. No cultural materials were observed.
In summary, the installation of lights for the playing fields at White Rock Park in La Grange, Fayette
County, involved the excavation of deep augured holes for the light poles and the excavation of a shallow,
narrow trench to run electrical conduits to the poles from an electrical junction 200 m to the north. LCRA
Cultural Resources staff monitored all earth excavation activities due to the close proximity of site
41FY519 and the high potential for cultural resources on this deep alluvial terrace of the Colorado River.
Observed cultural materials consisted solely of one unifacial tool fragment and a small amount of possible
burned rock near the previously recorded site. LCRA Cultural Resources staff also used the opportunity to
observe the deeper geology of the alluvial terrace.
Although no new sites were recorded during the 2016 project, the alluvial terrace upon which White Rock
Park is located retains the potential for significant, deeply buried, stratified sites, which could have a very
high research potential. LCRA Cultural Resources staff recommends the eligibility of site 41FY519 for
both the National Register of Historic Places and for formal designation as a State Antiquities Landmark
remain undetermined. LCRA Cultural Resources staff also recommends any future ground disturbing
activities conducted in White Rock Park should be assessed for cultural resources.

464

Project 24.16 Monitoring of exploratory trenching for electrical lines at the Lane City vertical
pump station
County: Wharton
Type of Investigation: Construction Monitoring
Date of Investigation(s): June 13th and October 17th, 2016
During Cultural Resource investigations for the LCRAâs Lower Basin Reservoir Project at Lane City
(Prikryl et al 2014), a historic vertical irrigation pump plant site, site 41WH119, was tested in areas of
anticipated work for the reservoir project (Figure 212). The Pump plant site is considered to be of
unknown potential for listing on the National Register of Historic Places (NRHP) due to its important role
in the start of rice farming in the area. Areas tested for the reservoir project were considered ineligible for
NRHP listing, although the rest of the site, including a 1928 pump building, a 1949 pump building, and
remains of the southern wall of a 1901 pump building exposed on the bank, is still considered to have
unknown eligibility. Recent construction plans include the digging of trenches for new electrical lines in
areas of the irrigation pump station not previously investigated with subsurface testing.
On June 13th, 2016, LCRA Cultural Resources staff monitored two exploratory trenches being dug in an
effort to locate the existing underground utility lines in between the two existing pump buildings (Figure
213). The method used, dubbed âpotholing,â involved probing the ground with a shovel to a depth of
about 1ft, then allowing a small excavator to scrape away a larger area to the same depth. This method is
repeated until the desired depth is met. Two such trenches were monitored because of their location in
untested and potentially significant areas. Trench 1 was excavated in between the 1949 pump house and
the 1928 pump house (Figure 214). The total depth of the trench was 121cmbs (centimeters below
surface). A total of 7 bricks and brick fragments of varying color and size, 1 heavily burned brick
fragment, a 10cm x 23cm cast iron metal fragment, a square headed bolt approximately 3.5in long, and 2
nails approximately 60D were observed. The hole showed a profile of about 40cm of very dark grey clay,
followed by a 10cm layer of light yellowish brown sand, a 50cm layer of very dark grey clay with
multicolored mottling, and more light yellowish brown sand below that.
Trench 2 was excavated in between the modern pump building and the Colorado River, and reached a
depth of 127cmbs, with no cultural materials observed. It contained a similar soil profile, with 20cm of
very dark brown clay loam, followed by 17cm layer of dark yellowish brown sandy clay loam, 14cm of
yellowish brown sandy loam and sand, and the same very dark grey clay with multicolored mottling
below that. It should be noted that the pump buildings all housed vertical pumps, requiring deep subfloors
where pumps are/were located. Given the location of the original 1901 pump building in between and
underneath the two standing structures, observed soils are understandably heavily disturbed and likely
partially made up of foreign materials. None of the materials observed are considered significant, and no
remnants of the 1901 building foundation were located.

465

On October 17th, 2016, staff returned to monitor the excavation of trenches to run new electrical lines to
the 1949 pump building and the 1970âs storage building adjacent to the two pump houses (Figure 213).
These trenches were dug to an average depth of 95cm, as the electrical lines were to be laid at a target
depth of 3ft. Trench 3 was excavated from an electrical junction box to the end of a buried concrete âduct
bankâ. Around 15 bricks and brick fragments were observed, including a small cluster of 6 bricks around
55cmbs, though these appeared jumbled and lacked mortar (Figure 215). Three chunks of bricks mortared
together were observed in this trench, but all three were isolated and from different areas and depths
within the trench. Trench 3 was terminated because of need to change the angle of the trench.
Trench 4 was excavated from the end of trench 3 to the far edge of the 1928 pump building. Trench 4
almost immediately encountered a feature of what appeared to be stacked bricks (Figure 216). Though
many of these were aligned, many were also at odd angles, and none were mortared together. Some of the
bricks had trace amounts of mortar on them, but most did not. The cluster appeared to be about 30cm
thick, 160cm long, and of unknown width, though the deposit seemed to run most of the width of the
trench, but did not continue into the north wall of the trench. The bricks were surrounded and mixed in
with a layer of brown sand. The typical profile for Trench 4 was 30 to 45cm of brown silt and foreign
gravel (from the dirt road above the trench), 20 to 25cm of brown clay, and approximately 45cm of very
dark brown clay with mottling. The brick layer with sand appears in between the brown clay and very
dark brown clay. Approximately 50-80 more bricks and brick fragments of varying color and texture were
observed either alone or in small groupings of 2-3 bricks throughout the rest of the trench.
Trench 5 was excavated from the rear corner of the 1970âs metal storage building to the electrical
junction box where Trench 3 had begun. This trench encountered bricks throughout and with a similar
density to that observed in Trench 4, with around 50-100 brick observed in backdirt piles and side walls.
Trench 5 also encountered a feature similar to the one encountered in Trench 4 (Figure 217). This cluster
of bricks was about 110cm long, 30cm thick, and extended across most of the trench without entering the
opposing wall. The bricks again appeared to be stacked, however no mortar or mortar residue was
observed on any of them. Once again the cluster was surrounded by a brown sand, and had a 45cm brown
clay layer above and a very dark brown to dark gray clay with mottling below.
One explanation for the clusters observed during the monitoring is that the high quantity of bricks,
lacking traces of mortar, represents fill material. The original pump building would presumably have had
a significant subfloor like those of the buildings that succeeded it, and the canal head and canal route have
changed to reflect the pump building location since the switch to the 1949 pump building. Both the 1901
building and the old canal would have left sizable voids in the ground surface, and the brick may
represent part of the fill used to level the surface out. None of the bricks in the cluster resembled a wall or
foundation, as they lacked mortar, faced the wrong directions, and did not span the complete distance of
the trench. The trenches excavated should have encountered the subsurface portions of the 1901 building
if any existed north of the portions exposed on the river bank, and as none were encountered, it must be
assumed that these portions of the walls and foundation of the 1901 building are either not intact or
potentially no longer present.
No archaeologically significant materials were observed during the monitoring of trenching at 41WH119,
the Lane City Pump Plant. The two brick cluster features are considered to be likely fill material, and
466

while their origin is unknown, they do not represent part of the architecture of the 1901 pump building.
This Project is considered complete.

472

MANAGEMENT SUMMARY
The Lower Colorado River Authorityâs (LCRA) Natural Resource Conservation (NRC) department
completed vegetation management work on seven LCRA properties on Lakes Buchanan and Travis
between December 2016 and March 2017 (Figure 218). Due to a compressed window of work and the
long history of brush clearing surveys on these properties, a series of file searches, short resurveys, and
monitoring of the clearing work was conducted. A total of approximately 849 acres were cleared as a part
of FY17 vegetation management. All file searches, short resurvey data, and monitoring data are recorded
within this monitoring report. Because the majority of planning and start of work fell in 2016, and
because the majority of work was completed by the time LCRA staff began compiling the 2016 Annual
Report of Investigations, this project is treated as a 2016 project.

PREVIOUS INVESTIGATIONS
Many of the LCRA owned properties along Lakes Buchanan and Travis have been subjected to multiple
cultural resource surveys for the specific purpose of brush clearing. This began with TAC permit 157,
which was used from 1978 to 1979 to conduct brush clearing, boundary fence, and other lands
management type surveys across LCRA lands (Table 24). LCRA internal project files are not well
indexed from 1989 to 2000, though it is not believed that much, if any, work was conducted by LCRA
Cultural Resources for the purpose of brush clearing during that time. An extensive effort was made by
Staff Archaeologist Bruce Nightengale during portions of that period to inventory the Lake Buchanan and
Lake Travis lands. All of the affected properties were included in those inventory efforts.
During the following 16 years, in which LCRA Cultural Resource files are well-indexed, many surveys
have been conducted in advance of vegetation management efforts. The table below lists them by their
LCRA project number (Table 24).

METHODOLOGY
This monitoring regime was designed to allow LCRA NRC staff and contractors to clear overgrown areas
of the seven properties concerned within a highly restrictive schedule. In most cases, much of the clearing
area was either along existing roads or fire breaks which had been cleared multiple times in the past, or
had otherwise been cleared in prior years, and had reverted to an overgrown state. Often in these areas,
despite dense vegetation, signs of prior clearing, such as thick mulch or brush piles, were visible.
An intensive file search was first conducted on each property in order to determine what areas had already
been surveyed for brush clearing, and what sites would fall into FY17 clearing areas. Sites with either
unknown or eligible SAL status (as per Texas Historical Commission [THC] determinations listed on the
online Archeological Sites Atlas) were restricted to hand clearing methods only, or at least where the
unknown or eligible component was located within the site. Sites with ineligible SAL status (again, as per
the THC) were evaluated to determine what level of protection was warranted, based on current site
condition and depth. Sites without formal eligibility determinations from the THC were treated as having
unknown eligibility, and were thus restricted to hand clearing methods. There were no SAL-listed sites
within the clearing areas. Contractors and NRC staff were also asked to take care to avoid isolated historic
features present on the properties, such as picket fence segments, and any potentially undiscovered
historic features.
Areas considered to be under-surveyed were re-examined. This consisted of pedestrian survey, and in the
case of Double Horn North, shovel testing of a small area. Once staff had determined what areas would be
suitable to be cleared by what methods, flagging was hung in short intervals around the areas in question.
On some of these flagging trips, NRC staff, and on one occasion a contractor, accompanied the Cultural
Resources team in order to gain a comprehensive knowledge of both flagging locations and restrictions.
In all cases, prior to the start of work, clearing crews and their supervisors were briefed on cultural
resources and the flagging hung at the particular property, along with other environmental and water
quality restrictions.
The majority of work conducted was completed by contractors, though NRC staff also completed some
work with in-house staff and equipment. The equipment types and methods of use utilized during FY17
vegetation management activities, as well as their impact to soil and archaeological sites, are addressed
below.
Hand Clearing
Hand clearing methods were allowed at nearly any location on the properties, but because of the time and
expense associated with this method, it was often not used. Areas designated as hand clearing only were
often left alone entirely, but were sometimes cleared. The method involves manually cutting brush and
trees with chainsaws and machetes. Some of the contractors would mulch vegetation removed using this
method, while some would stack and burn the resulting brush. In both cases, it was made clear to hand
clearing crews that neither mulching nor burning could be done within these hand clearing zones; brush
needed to be removed from these areas by hand, and burned or mulched in areas designated for Seppi

476

mulching or bulldozing. Often hand clearing crews only cleared understory or fresh growth in previously
cleared areas and firebreaks and roads.
Seppi
Seppi is a brand of brush clearing and mulching equipment, but the term has become the vernacular for a
variety of brands of similar machines of varying sizes. Here, and to the crews working during the FY17
vegetation management project, a Seppi refers to a large tractor type machine with a boom and attachment
(Figure 219). The attachments vary somewhat, but consist of either chains or metal disk blades that rotate
at high velocities, and effectively shred anything with which they come into contact. This machine is
effective at destroying brushy vegetation and mulching it at the same time, with relatively little impact to
soil surface. The only negative impacts of the machine on archaeology consist of the soil disturbance
from the machineâs large tires and weight, and the layer of mulch laid down, which effectively eliminates
surface visibility until the material decomposes. The mulch does help prevent return of brushy vegetation,
and reduces the impact of the heavy machines; but some minor soil disturbance nonetheless occurs with
use of these machines. The depth of this disturbance is estimated at 1-6 inches, depending on soil
conditions and weather. For this reason, they were restricted from use in most sites of unknown or eligible
SAL status, and sites lacking formal determinations. Known sites that were deeply buried, such as those
at the south side of Shaffer Bend, were subjected to Seppi work, as the deposits would not be disturbed by
the machinesâ minor soil disturbance.

Figure 219. A Seppi type machine working at Gloster Bend. Note the resulting mulch in the foreground.

477

Skid Steer
LCRA and its contractors have frequently used skid steers for vegetation management. They are highly
versatile, and have a variety of attachments available. In the past, these attachments have often been small
Seppi flail mulcher attachments. For FY17 vegetation management, the skid steers being used had shear
attachments, which, as the name suggests, are like large hydraulic scissors for cutting vegetation at its
base. While these machines are lighter and more versatile than the large Seppiâs, their signature skid
turning method can cause disturbance of around 1-6 inches. In areas where Seppi work was allowed, skid
steer work was also allowed, and vice versa.
Bulldozing
While bulldozers are commonly known to generate massive disturbances, LCRA Cultural Resources staff
have observed several bulldozers in action on LCRA lands, and have found that disturbances can be
minimized with the right practices. The practice commonly used by NRC and their contractors is called
grubbing. Rather than using the width of the blade to push trees over, moving large amounts of soil, the
operator uses the corner of the blade to first penetrate below the base of the tree, and then force it upward
and out of the ground. Smaller brush is still pushed out of the ground using the blades full width, and the
loose brush is then corralled into wind rows, or long rows of brush along the exterior of the clearing area.
When an area is finished, these rows of brush are usually pushed back into a more central portion of the
clearing area and burned (Figure 220). The burning process can create enough heat to generate burned
rock, and thus the area where brush is burned is confined to bulldozing areas. Bulldozing was not allowed
on any sites with unknown or eligible SAL status, or sites lacking SAL determinations. Further, only a
handful of the ineligible prehistoric sites, and none of the known unmitigated features of the historic sites,
were exposed to bulldozing. Despite better practices, bulldozers still commonly disturb soils down to
about 6 inches to 1 foot (ft), and in wet conditions or on loose substrate, much deeper.

478

Figure 220. A section of Muleshoe Bend cleared by bulldozer. Note the burn pile in the middle left, and bulldozer in
the distant right.

The end goal of the clearing is to remove dense brushy vegetation and non-native species of vegetation, in
order to return these areas to a more natural state. The intention of the NRC department is to maintain
these cleared areas with low impact methods, such as prescribed burns, in order to prevent their
overgrowth in the future.

Figure 221. An area of Gloster Bend exemplary of the natural habitat LCRA is trying to achieve.

479

RESULTS
LCRA NRC coordinated work on seven LCRA properties: Canyon of the Eagles, Cedar Point, Shaffer
Bend, Double Horn, Gloster Bend, Grelle, and Muleshoe Bend. The cultural resources work and
restrictions, and vegetation management methods employed at each of these properties is described
below.

Canyon of the Eagles
Canyon of the Eagles is an LCRA park property located on the northeastern side of Lake Buchanan
(Figure 222). The area in which vegetation management took place was cleared via a file search. The area
in question had been well surveyed by a 1996 inventory effort (Kotter et al 1996), and contains no known
archaeological sites. The work on this small 31.2-acre area will be conducted by NRC staff, and utilize
bulldozing, a skid steer with a shear, and a combination of shredding and burning to dispose of materials.
This work will not be monitored due to the extent of prior investigation. Emergency discovery procedures
will be in effect.

Cedar Point
Cedar Point is an LCRA park property located on the northwestern side of Lake Buchanan (Figure 223).
All but 27.3 acres of the 110.3 acres proposed for clearing had survey reports that could be located. The
small area which was not clearly surveyed was subjected to pedestrian survey on December 6th, 2016.
Soils appeared shallow, and there was plentiful evidence of prior brush shredding present. Based on this
survey, it was determined that Seppi use would be acceptable, as they would cause no more damage than
had already been done during past brush clearing. No archaeological material was observed during the
pedestrian survey. The only vegetation management methods used at Cedar Point were Seppis and hand
clearing crews. Fencing was replaced along existing fence corridors along some parts of the park
boundary.

Shaffer Bend
Shaffer Bend is an LCRA park property on the north side of Lake Travis (Figure 224). The entirety of the
vegetation management area is considered to have been previously surveyed. Three sites, 41BT428,
41BT451, and 41BT483, have buried deposits felt to be at risk from bulldozing, and thus were restricted
to skid steer work and hand clearing. Sites 41BT49, 41BT67, and a small portion of the strip of 41BT70
being cleared are considered at risk from any type of mechanical clearing, and were restricted to hand
clearing only. Site 41BT361, a lithic scatter determined by the THC to be ineligible for SAL listing, is
buried between 30 centimeters below surface (cm bs) and 170 cmbs, and consists of chert debitage with
no features. It is felt to neither be at risk for damage from bulldozing, or warranting protection, and thus
no restrictions were placed within the site. A total of 210.8 acres of land were cleared at Shaffer Bend.

480

Double Horn
Double Horn is an LCRA property located on the south side of Lake Travis (Figure 225). Brush
management work for the south side of Double Horn Creek was surveyed and approved in LCRA report
25.15, but was not completed. As such, the work was completed as part of the FY17 vegetation
management plan. Work in this area was monitored, specifically to help guide bulldozers along the
boundaries of areas restricted to bulldozing. The portion of 41BT170 and all of 41BT172 will also be
subjected to skid steer work for those areas that were approved in the 2015 report.
Double Horn north only had a 10.9-acre area proposed for clearing (Figure 226). This area had been
surveyed as part of an inventory survey in the winter of 1999-2000, though the extent of the survey was
unclear in field notes from the time. As such, a 6-acre portion of the 10 acres that was in question was
resurveyed on December 28th, 2016. Thirteen shovel tests were placed within the 6-acre area, and located
a small and seemingly sparse buried lithic scatter, recorded as 41BT517. Two positive shovel tests located
the site, and the boundary of the site was subsequently drawn to reflect the possible area of deposits, as
some areas were inaccessible from the thickness of the brush. Work was then monitored, as several passes
of the bulldozer were watched, including a path along 41BT517âs boundary. As the bulldozer passed
along the siteâs southern end, clearing the terrace edge while archaeologists were present, four pieces of
burned rock were exposed. Subsequently, two more shovel tests were excavated closer to the terrace edge,
and both were terminated at over a meter (m) due to manual testing limits. The terrace is composed of
sandy loam, and appears to be over 1.35 m deep. Both tests were negative, but the possibility of deeply
buried deposits seems high. The burned rocks were exposed near the opening to the burrow of some kind
(likely a small to medium mammal based on the holes), and may have been brought up from below by
these burrowing activities. While 41BT517 was treated as having undetermined SAL status during work
because it lacked a THC eligibility determination, it is also now recommended as being of undetermined
SAL eligibility by LCRA Cultural Resources staff, and would require deep testing methods to determine
anything more.
Once brush had been pushed into piles by the bulldozers, the âwind rowsâ were surveyed again to ensure
that no unknown archaeological material had surfaced during clearing, and to prevent such unknown
deposits from being exposed to the brush burning. Nothing was located, and burning was allowed to
proceed.
Between Double Hornsâs northern and southern portions, a total of approximately 73 acres were cleared
as a part of the FY17 vegetation management work.
Gloster Bend
Gloster Bend is an LCRA park property located on the north side of Lake Travis (Figure 227). The crew
working at Gloster Bend only used Seppis and hand clearing crews. Most of the areas being cleared had
been previously cleared, consisting of fire breaks and road corridors. The Seppi machines were allowed to
work up to 15 ft from the roadway within 41BT475, beyond which crews hand cleared brush and
removed it to the Seppi area for shredding. This is because prior clearing had been completed in that 15-ft
corridor several times, and it was felt that the Seppis would not further damage the small corridor.
481

Portions of sites 41TV481, 41TV482, and 41TV2273 were flagged and marked as hand clearing only
areas, because of historic features at the sites. 41TV2278 was also marked as hand clearing only within
the clearing zone, due to prehistoric deposits of undetermined eligibility. All of these sites were given a
brief resurvey during flagging on November 3rd, and the work areas were monitored twice in the course of
work to ensure restrictions were being followed. No issues were observed, and no new material was
discovered during the work. A total of approximately 133 acres were cleared.

Grelle
The Grelle Recreation Area is an LCRA park property located on the south side of Lake Travis (Figure
228). The work crews at Grelle included Seppiâs and hand clearing crews. Much of the clearing area at
Grelle consisted of previously maintained road corridors and fire breaks, as well as areas that had been
cleared in the past. The only sites of concern at Grelle were 41BT158 and 41BT159. Site 41BT158, a
small historic foundation, was easily relocated and flagged for hand clearing only. Site 41BT159, which
in internal LCRA GIS files is mapped as extending much further than on the THCâs Online Archeological
Sites Atlas, was resurveyed via pedestrian survey before clearing. Areas of the site that were deflated were
cleared for Seppi work, while areas with soil were flagged for hand clearing. Both sites were revisited
after clearing was completed, and removal of vegetation within the hand clearing zone revealed several
more historic foundation features, as well as more prehistoric material. A site revisit form was filed for
both sites, and the current boundaries updated with TARL to reflect their current known extent.
Two sites, 41BT53 and 41BT54, are shown on the Archeological Sits Atlas as being within the current
project area. Both of these sites were removed from their location within the LCRA internal GIS database,
after a review of the 41BT53 site form and surveys of the area revealed that they could not be where they
are mapped. These sites are both supposed to be below the lake level, and are mapped as on a slope where
no sign of them exists. In the case of 41BT53, it is believed by staff that the site could have been a portion
of 41BT1, mistakenly identified in the 1962 survey and excavation. Site 41BT54 has no listed site form,
but is mentioned in the 41BT53 site form as being 100 yards east of 41BT53. The fence line described in
the site form is unknown to LCRA staff as well. A brief consultation with TARL recovered another
sketch map not listed on the THCâs Archeological Sites Atlas, and which would indicate that the sites are
actually south of Grelle on the Pedernales River. TARLâs physical topographic site maps show the two
sites on the side of the peninsula at Grelle, but farther into the channel and not on the peninsula itself.
LCRA staff will make an effort to locate these sites at Grelle during the next major drop in lake levels,
but it is suspected that they are not within the cove where they are mapped at TARL, and possibly not on
LCRA property at all.
Work was visited and monitored twice during the clearing. A total 85.5 acres were cleared.

482

Muleshoe Bend
Muleshoe Bend is an LCRA park property located on the south side of Lake Travis (Figure 229). The
work crew at Muleshoe consisted of two bulldozers. Sites 41TV496, 41TV501, and 41TV1779 were
flagged for hand clearing only. Sites 41TV499, 41TV505, and 41TV507 all had discrete historic features
within them that were flagged for hand clearing. A cistern at 41TV507, and one of two cisterns at
41TV499, were not flagged for protection, as they had been previously removed and mitigated, and all
that remains of them is portions of the bottom of the collars (Hixson 2008). Sites 41BT347, 41TV 498,
41TV500, 41TV502, and 41TV2291 are all lithic scatters considered ineligible for SAL listing. Site
41TV498 also has a historic component, consisting primarily of a couple of dressed limestone
foundations, two trash scatters, and some ranching features; however, these have been determined
ineligible, and are considered by Cultural Resources staff to be disturbed and lacking integrity at this
point, as a result of prior, less well controlled, brush clearing events. Site 41BT147, which is listed on the
Archeological Sites Atlas as undetermined, was cleared for bulldozing because it is considered destroyed.
The site is suspected to extend beyond LCRA property, and any portions beyond the known may still be
intact; however, the portion on LCRA property was destroyed by an emergency bladed fire break installed
some time before 1997 (Malof et al. 2016). Work was monitored at Muleshoe Bend during three separate
trips to ensure that bulldozers remained outside of flagged areas. A more comprehensive post clearing
survey is planned to assess any newly exposed cultural resources, though Muleshoe Bend is considered
one of the best surveyed of all LCRA lands. A total of approximately 205 acres were cleared.

491

SUMMARY
Through a series of in-depth file searches, short resurveys, and monitoring of clearing activates, LCRA
Cultural Resources staff have allowed the LCRAâs NRC department and their vendors to clear 849 acres
of LCRA park land and resource area, using a variety of methods. Clearing was conducted from
December, 2016 to March, 2017. This project has helped enable the NRC department in furthering their
goal of responsible land management, while ensuring appropriate avoidance of cultural resource sites.
NRC staff plan to maintain the areas cleared in recent years using regular and low impact methods, such
as prescribed burns, in order to prevent the need for intensive clearing such as this in these areas in the
future.
A resurvey of select portions of cleared areas will be conducted over the next several months, in order to
locate and assess any undiscovered sites exposed by clearing. Sites where a significant change was noted
during work, such as an expansion of the site or change in the state of the site, will have revisit forms
submitted to TARL. No SAL-eligible sites or sites of undetermined eligibility were adversely affected as
a result of the brush clearing work discussed in this report.
